# CARRY THE FIRE DOWN

*a novel*

---

> *What gets carried down, and whether anyone alive can refuse it.*

---

# Chapter 1 — The Run Report

The call came in at 4:11 a.m. and the dispatcher was a girl named Roxanne who had only been on the desk three weeks and was good. She gave it to us clean. Two-vehicle, possible entrapment, I-75 southbound just past the London exit, KSP en route, one ALS rig requested, weather clear. I was driving. Boyd was on the bench in the back finishing the coffee I had bought him at the start of shift. I keyed the mic and said *Medic Fourteen responding* and laid into the accelerator and Boyd swore at the spill that opened across the front of his uniform and that was the last unimportant thing either of us said for an hour.

The night was thin and cold and the moon was up. I-75 above London at four in the morning is mostly trucks. We came up on the wreck five minutes out. A KSP cruiser had it staked with flares and the trooper was already in the road waving us in past a jackknifed Peterbilt. The Peterbilt looked all right. Its trailer was square across two lanes and the driver was on his feet beside it, hatless, his hands at his sides like he had set down something he could not pick up again. Past him, nosed into the cable barrier on the median side, was a Camry the color of a wedding cake. The front end was folded up to the windshield. The windshield was a web.

I went to the trooper. *Driver in the Camry,* he said. *Eighteen years old. Pinned. Conscious. Breathing. Just her. Coming from her grandmother's in Corbin. Truck driver said she came across the lanes like the wheel slipped out of her hand.* He looked at me and added, *She's been talking. She wants her mama.* I said *Copy* and went to the car.

She had a clean face. That was the first thing. Her face was clean and her hair was the dark loose color of fall water and there was no glass in her eyes and her mouth was bleeding from a split lip and not from inside. I crouched at the driver's door. The door was crushed into her hip and the dash had come down across her thighs and the steering column had pulled forward on impact and was now an inch from her sternum. *Hey,* I said. *Hey. Look at me. I'm Della. I'm a paramedic. What's your name.*

*Madison,* she said.

*Madison. Good. How old are you, Madison.*

*Eighteen.*

*Good. You stay with me, Madison. I'm gonna check you over. Tell me where you hurt.*

*My legs,* she said. *My legs are gone.*

*Your legs are still there. I can see them. They're underneath the dashboard, sweetheart. They've gone to sleep on you. That's all. You let me look.*

I got the penlight out and lit her over. Pupils equal. The light caught the slick on her forehead and I could see it was sweat and not the other thing. I took her wrist. Pulse 124. A little thready at the radial but there. She was breathing twenty-six a minute and it was costing her some. I leaned in and put my left hand flat on her chest above the steering column for nothing but the touch of it, and I felt her take a breath that lifted my palm an inch, and I said, *Madison. I'm gonna stay right here with you while my partner brings the saw and the cribs. We're gonna get you out of this car. You stay with me. Don't you go anywhere.*

*I won't,* she said.

I told Boyd to call for fire and another rig. He had already called. I told him to bring the KED and the c-collar even though I knew he was already coming with them. The trooper was at my shoulder now and I told him to back the Peterbilt driver off and have him sit in the cruiser and not look. The trooper went. I took my hand off Madison's chest and got the c-collar around her neck without moving her head and slid the KED behind her against the seat-back and she made a small sound. *Easy,* I said. *Easy. Almost done with the easy part.*

The fire engine came in three minutes. The lieutenant on it was a man I had worked with for nine years named Trent Pickle and he was the best cutter in eastern Tennessee and he had a son in junior high and he said, when he came up to the driver's window, *Della. Tell me where to start.* I said, *Posts. Roof off. We need to get her out the top because the dash is on her femurs and I want her flat on the board before anything moves below her waist.* He nodded once and went and the saw started up at the rear posts and the noise came down on us like weather.

I stayed at the window with my hand on Madison's wrist and the other on her shoulder. She closed her eyes. *Open them,* I said. *Look at me.* She opened them. *Tell me about your grandmother.*

*She makes soup beans,* Madison said. *On Sundays.*

*What kind of cornbread.*

*Skillet. With bacon grease.*

*That's the right answer,* I said. The roof was coming off. *You keep going. Tell me what else.*

She told me what else, in pieces, while Trent cut. She told me about the soup beans and the cornbread and her grandmother's dog whose name was Banjo and who could not climb steps anymore and had to be carried in and out at night. She told me her mother was a nurse at Baptist in Corbin and she had not wanted Madison to drive home this late because the deer were out, and she, Madison, had said she'd be careful and had drunk a Mountain Dew at the Speedway in Williamsburg and had been doing seventy-two, only seventy-two, and the wheel had pulled at her like a thing alive and she had not known what to do. I told her she had done the thing you do. I told her *you put it in the wall on the median side and you stayed in the car and you kept breathing and you waited and we are here now and you are going to be all right.* I did not know if she was going to be all right. I knew she was alive in this minute and that was the run I was on. I knew the femoral was probably intact because she was still pink and still talking and the column had stopped two inches above her belt and not through it. I knew her right tibia was almost certainly broken because of the angle her knee was making against the bottom of the dash. I knew none of this mattered for the next seven minutes, which were the minutes during which we would get her out.

Trent peeled the roof. Boyd came in behind me with the long board. The second rig had pulled up and the medic on it was a younger guy named Echols I had trained up myself, and he came right in and put a line in her arm one-handed in the position he could get to, and we ran her a bag of saline TKO because her pressure was holding and I did not want to flush her into the cold. We cribbed the dash off her thighs in seven minutes. She screamed once and apologized. I told her not to apologize. We got the board in. We got her out. The femur was fine. The right tibia was the way I had thought it was. We did not lose her on the road and we did not lose her on the bay floor at the Knoxville ER and the trauma team had her up and intubated and rolling by the time my radio said *Medic Fourteen, available* and I keyed back *Medic Fourteen, available* and Boyd and I walked out of the ambulance bay into the gray of false dawn and the air was cold and smelled like the river.

We rode back to the station. Boyd was driving now. I was in the bench seat. I was writing the run report on the laptop with the slow methodical patience the job teaches you because if you write it tired and wrong it will come back at you in a deposition in two years. *Eighteen-year-old female. Two-vehicle MVC. Restrained driver, frontal impact, intrusion to dash, pinned with column displacement.* I wrote it. *Initial GCS 15. Pulse 124 weak at radial, BP 110 over 72, respirations 26 shallow, pupils equal and reactive. C-collar and KED applied prior to extrication.* I wrote it. *Extrication completed at 04:38 by Knoxville Fire Engine Six.* I wrote it. *Patient transported in supine position, full c-spine precautions, IV access via the left antecubital, 200 cc NS infused en route. Vitals stable for transport. Patient remained conscious and verbal throughout.* I wrote *verbal* and stopped and went back and wrote *conversant* instead because *verbal* was what you wrote about a man who would not stop yelling and *conversant* was what you wrote about a girl who told you about her grandmother's dog. The difference is not in any textbook. The difference is in everybody who has ever written one of these.

Boyd parked the rig in the bay at Station Fourteen and shut it off and sat for a second with his hands on the wheel and said, *Della. That was a good one.* I said *Yes it was.* He got out and went inside to start coffee and I climbed down and got the hose off the wall and stood with it in my hand for a long minute before I bent and turned it on and washed the blood off the toes of my boots and off the threshold of the bench-seat door where Madison's right heel had bled onto the kick plate when we lifted her in. The water ran red and then pink and then the color of nothing, and it ran down the concrete bay floor toward the drain in the center, and I watched it go.

My phone was in the breast pocket of my uniform shirt and it had been buzzing against my chest for I did not know how long. I had felt it during the run as a thing far away and I had not looked. I shut the hose off and coiled it back onto the wall and pulled the phone out. Three missed calls. Two from a number I did not have saved. One from my mother.

I looked at my mother's name on the screen for the time it took the bay door to come down on the photoelectric. I had not seen the name on this screen since June. I had not seen it on a screen before five in the morning in my life.

I called her back.

She picked up on the first ring. *Della.* That was all. Her voice was the way her voice was when she had something to tell me she had not yet decided how to tell. The voice she had used in 2007 in the hospital chapel when she said *They could not get him back.* The voice she had used in 2005 when she said *He is asking for you.* When my mother loses her preface I know what the sentence will be.

*Mama,* I said.

*Della.* She breathed in. *They got Wade.*

I did not say anything for a count I would not be able to give you, and then I said, *Who has him.*

*Sheriff's.*

*For what.*

There was a pause. There was the wood-fence pause of a woman who was at her kitchen table and looking out the window above the sink at the line where her yard ran into Verna Hicks's pasture and where past Verna's pasture you could see, if the light was right, the back ridge above what we called Sorrow Hollow.

*Murder,* she said.

The word came down across the line and across the seven hours and across the years and laid itself on the concrete floor of the bay at Knoxville Fire & Rescue Station Fourteen at five-thirty-eight in the morning on the twelfth of October.

*A man name of Tully Renfro,* she said. *They found him up the holler. Della. He was found up Sorrow Hollow.*

The bay door had finished its travel. I heard it set into the rubber. I heard Boyd in the kitchen laughing at something on the radio. I heard the refrigerator across the bay cycle. I heard, in my own head, a thing my grandfather had said to me in his bedroom in April of 2005 with his oxygen tube laid across the bedsheet like a small white snake and his hand on the bone of mine and his one good eye on me and his voice the dry careful voice of a man making sure of a thing.

*Della. Honey. Listen.* My mother on the line. *He didn't do it. He didn't.*

*Mama,* I said. *I am leaving here in an hour. I'll be there by suppertime.*

*I told him I would call you. I had to. I had to call somebody.*

*You called the right person. I'm coming.*

*Della.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

She did not say what came next for a second and then she said it. *Della. I haven't heard your voice since Annie Couch's funeral. I have missed it.*

*I know,* I said.

I would think later that this was the sentence she had been keeping for me — that she had not allowed herself the *I miss you* across all the years because pride had been the only thing she had to set against my absence, and that the wreck of her boy had cracked her open enough to spend it. I would think a lot of things later. In the minute on the phone in the bay at Station Fourteen I only said *I know* and *I'll be there by suppertime* and *Hang up and try to sleep. There is nothing for you to do for an hour. Try.* And she said *I will try,* and we ended the call.

I stood with the phone in my hand and looked at the toes of my boots, which were the only clean part of me. I looked at the drain in the center of the bay, which had been there since before I was born and would be there after the building was condemned. I thought *the second-to-last funeral she means is Annie Couch. The last one was my granddaddy's, four days after, alone at the grave at noon. She is counting them off from when I started coming back. From the last time she could call me by name and have me come.*

My hands started to shake.

It came on slow, the way it does when it is not adrenaline. Adrenaline tremor is fast and fine and gone in five minutes. This was bigger. This was the long-arc shake of a thing in a person that has been packed inside her for twenty years and has just been told by a kitchen-table voice at five-thirty-eight in the morning that it is going to have to come out. My hands shook in a way I had not felt them shake since I was twenty-one and home from college and standing at my granddaddy's bed listening to him give me, with great care, the shape of a thing he wanted me to know in case anybody ever came asking. I had filed the thing under *old man's deathbed talk.* I had filed it carefully. I had not opened the file in twenty years. I had not had to.

I went out of the bay through the side door and stood in the parking lot. The sun was coming up over the river the way the sun comes up over the Tennessee at this latitude in October — slow, pink, with a cold green underlight. The shift change crew was pulling in. Vehicles I knew. A diesel I had ridden in a hundred times. I lifted my hand to a guy named Hamp who waved as he passed and Hamp waved back and did not look at me twice because Hamp had been a paramedic for twenty-three years and he knew the look of somebody who needed a minute and had brought herself outside to take it.

I stood in the parking lot and watched the river. I had been twelve years on this job. I had been twenty-two years gone from the place I was about to drive back to. I had not been home since Annie Couch's funeral in the spring of 2020 and before that, only the once, four days late, for the man whose voice had just risen up off the concrete floor of the bay behind me and walked back into my life.

My hands shook. I held them out in front of me and made myself look at them. They were a paramedic's hands. They were the hands of a person who ran toward emergencies as a way of controlling them. They were the hands of a person who had, all her professional life, done the work, written the report, and walked away clean.

The phone in my other hand had not lit up again. My mother had hung up and was trying, in her own kitchen at the end of Bear Branch, to do what I had told her to do, which was to try to sleep for an hour. She would not sleep. She had not slept the night before either.

The sun cleared the bridge. The river under it ran the color of brown sugar in old whiskey.

I put the phone in my pocket. I went back inside. I told Boyd I needed to go. He looked at my face and did not ask. I told the captain I had a family emergency in Kentucky and I needed the next four shifts off and would call him from the road. He said *Take what you need, Combs.* I went to my locker and changed out of the uniform and put my street clothes on. I took my radio off and laid it on the bench. I picked up the spare key to my truck off the hook beside the door because the one I usually used was in the wash with the uniform.

I stopped, at the door, and turned and looked at the bay. I looked at the rig. I looked at the drain in the floor.

Then I went out and got in my truck and pointed it north on I-75 toward the Cumberland Gap, and I did not stop until I was over the line.

---

# Chapter 2 — The Long Drive

I have driven I-75 north out of Knoxville a hundred times in my life, and most of them I drove because I wanted to be somewhere that was not where I was. This was the first one in twenty-two years where I was driving because of somewhere I did not want to be going. The difference is in the body. The shoulders sit different. The right hand falls off the wheel a lot at sixty miles per hour and you have to keep putting it back.

I left the station parking lot at six-fourteen and I was past the Caryville exit by seven and across the Cumberland Gap a little after eight-thirty. The morning was the cold clear kind October gives you the back end of, with the sun in the rearview the whole climb, and the leaves on the Tennessee side at peak — red maple, yellow tulip poplar, the persimmons just turning. I noticed it the way a person who has worked night shift for twelve years notices things. I noticed it because it was there. I did not feel anything about it.

The Tacoma drove well. I had topped the tank in Knoxville at a Pilot off Lonas because I did not want to stop in London. London is the town where I used to stop on the way in to college, and London is the town where I used to stop on the way out, and London is also the town just south of where the trooper had staked the wreck at four eleven this morning, and I did not want London under my wheels twice in one day. I made it through to the Jellico exit on the dregs and then I let myself top off again.

I crossed into Kentucky the way I have always crossed into Kentucky, which is by going under a green sign that says it and by feeling the road change, an inch, in a way no one but a person from this stretch could verify. The pavement here is a darker, blacker patch. The state seal goes by. The sun moved from my back to my left as I curved up out of Cumberland Gap and aimed for Pineville on KY-25E. The country opened. The ridges came up. The lowering down of my own breathing began.

I did the math on myself the way I would have done it on a patient.

*Forty-one-year-old female, ETA two hours from a death in the family that has not yet been pronounced. Pulse not taken. Respirations elevated. Last meal coffee. Last sleep four hours yesterday afternoon. Hands shook for nine minutes in a fire-station parking lot at five forty-six a.m. local. Patient denies acute distress.*

The run report does not allow you to lie. You have to write what you saw, and the language carries a certain quiet authority about what it has been asked to carry. It is the only register I have ever been able to be honest in. I had asked a girl named Madison about her grandmother's cornbread three hours ago and now I was driving toward a kitchen that smelled like skillet cornbread on a Sunday I would not eat in.

There were trucks at every turnout. There are always trucks at every turnout on this road. Coal trucks not so much anymore — the strip sites had taken most of the deep mines down to skeleton crews when I was in high school and there were not enough loaded haulers on the road by 2005 to fill a parking lot — but log trucks, gravel trucks, dump trucks for the road work that has never finished and never will. I pulled into the Marathon outside Pineville at eleven forty-three and parked at the pump farthest from the door and went in for a banana and a coffee. The woman behind the counter looked at me and looked at the bills I held out and looked at me again and rang me up without saying anything. I was wearing my street clothes — jeans, a long flannel, boots that had been hosed two hours ago — and I had washed my face in the sink at the station, and still she knew. I was a Combs. She did not know which one. She knew the kind of face.

I sat in my truck in her parking lot and ate the banana. It had no taste. It had the texture of banana and the smell of banana and it crossed my tongue like a piece of paper. I drank a quarter of the coffee, which was too hot and then too cold, and I read the front of the Marathon. Pumps. *Kerosene available inside.* A handwritten sign that said *No fishing buddies in the bathroom please.* A Blackridge Coal Holdings sticker on the lower right corner of the door, the kind they used to put up everywhere in the nineties to remind you whose checks paid for the asphalt. The corners of it were curling.

I sat there long enough that a man in a Toyota pickup pulled up next to me and gave me the small lifted finger off the wheel that means *you all right there.* I gave him back the small lifted finger that means *yes, thank you.* He went in for his cigarettes. I started the engine.

KY-92 leaves 25E at a Y you have to know to make, and I made it without looking. I had not been on this road since the spring of 2020 and my body knew it before my eyes did — the slight gravel pull at the gore, the way you set the wheel for the bridge over Cumberland River two hundred yards on. The river was low. October had been dry. I drove across it without slowing and the prose in my head said *the brown carrying brown,* and I thought *don't,* and the prose in my head said it again, *the brown carrying brown,* and I let it.

Twelve miles after the river I came up on the Blackridge Coal Holdings sign at the turnoff to the strip site road. It is a big sign — about the size of a king sheet hung from a pole — and it has been replaced twice in my lifetime, and the new one looks the same as the old one and the old one looked the same as the one before. *Blackridge Coal Holdings,* it says. *Energy. Community. Future.* They added *Future* in the late nineties. They had been wanting it the whole time and finally said it.

I thought about Hollis. It was the first time I had let myself think about him by name in I did not know how many years. The thinking was the kind you do not invite; it came up out of the seat. He had worked thirty-one years for those people. They had taken his lungs and his back and his oldest brother. He had retired in 1979 with a packet of paperwork that gave him benefits the union had bled to win and the company had bled to keep from him. He had said about the company exactly twice in my hearing. He had said it the first time the day after I graduated high school, in 2002, when I told him I was going to UK and was going to study biology and become whatever I became, and he had said, *Honey, you go and don't come back unless you mean it.* He had said it the second time on a porch in 2003 when I was leaving for Lexington for the last time, and he had said, *Della. If you ever come back here for a long enough reason, look the company in the face and don't blink.* And he had clapped me on the shoulder the way a man claps a man and had not hugged me, because the hugging had been done in the kitchen with Vera who was twelve years in the ground and with Ladonna who was just then standing at the screen door pretending to read the paper. The clap on the shoulder was for the road.

The strip site is not visible from KY-92 except at one place, a half-mile straight where the road bends past a clearcut, and when I came up on it the work lights were on along the high contour because the day shift was running. From this distance — five miles east as the crow flies — they look like a string of yellow beads laid along the ridge. They look almost pretty. I have never said this out loud. I have never had occasion to. You drive past the bend and then the trees take it back and you can pretend for the next twenty miles that the ridge is the same ridge it used to be.

I had not eaten properly in something on the order of twenty-two hours when I came over the last rise into the valley that holds the town of Caldwell. The light by then was gone behind the western ridge and the eastern face was still copper. There is a place on KY-92 about a mile out of town, just before the first stoplight, where the road comes up over a knoll and the whole valley sits below you, the four stoplights blinking in sequence and the steeple of the in-town Methodist white against the dusk, and a person driving in with twenty-two years of not having seen the valley like this can be forgiven for taking her foot off the gas without meaning to. I took my foot off the gas. The truck coasted down toward the IGA.

Stoplight one. The Caldwell road heading north into the hollows on my right, the trailer park south on my left. *Don't take it,* I thought. I did not mean the trailer park. I drove on.

Stoplight two. The courthouse on my right, sandstone, lit at the front from underneath by a single bulb that has always made the building look like it was telling a ghost story to itself. The Methodist on my left. *They were married in there in '49,* I thought, *and Wade and I were baptized in there both, the same font.* My foot did not come off the gas this time. I made the green light.

Stoplight three. The IGA on my right. I needed milk and bread and a toothbrush. I had brought neither. I pulled into the lot.

The IGA at six-something on a Sunday in October is not quite empty. There were maybe twenty cars. I parked at the end of a row, three spots over from a green Ford F-250, late nineties by the body lines, with a Blackridge sticker on the back window over the rear glass slider. The truck was clean for its age. It had a black brush guard on the front and tow hooks I do not own a vehicle with, and the bed had a tool box and a length of cable looped on a hook and a five-gallon jerry can strapped against the side wall. There was no one in it. I clocked it without knowing why I was clocking it. The medic eye does that. You walk a scene and you note: a needle on the carpet that may matter; a man in the doorway with a thumb keeping his weight; a sticker on a truck. You move on. You note it. Later you may be glad you did.

I went in. Inside it was bright in the way the IGA has been bright since 1986. I bought milk and bread and a toothbrush and toothpaste and a bag of peanut M&Ms, and the woman ringing me up was somebody I had gone to school with — Lynnie Profitt, two years behind me, sister of the woman who runs the Mountain Laurel — and she looked up at me and her face did something I was not in the mood for, and she said, *Della Combs,* low, and I said *Hi, Lynnie,* and she said *honey, I am so sorry about Wade,* and I said *thank you,* and we did not look at each other again until she gave me my change, and I was out the door inside of two minutes.

The green F-250 was still there. I sat in the cab of my truck with my groceries on the seat next to me and looked at it. I looked at the sticker. I looked at the brush guard. I tried to make the truck do something for me. It just sat. Whoever owned it was still inside the store, buying his Sunday-night thing, and was going to come out and get in it and drive off, and I was probably never going to see him.

I started my engine. I backed out. I drove past the truck on my way out and read the plate. KY. The numbers and the letters. I said them to myself once. I made the right out of the lot onto KY-92 and I did not look back.

Stoplight four. Old Mine Road off to my left, climbing east. The sealed portal of Mine 4 was eight miles up that road. I did not look. I had not looked at it in twenty years and I was not going to look at it tonight.

The Mountain Laurel sits on the south side of KY-92 a half mile east of the IGA, on the way out of town, and it is the kind of place that was built in 1962 and has been almost the same building since. Twelve units in a single row, doors painted dark red, parking right outside each door. A neon sign at the road. The *L* in *Mountain Laurel* has been burnt out since I was eleven. They have not fixed it. They have replaced the *O* and the second *N* in my lifetime, and once a whole tube in the word *Motel*, but never the *L*. I do not know if it is sentiment or that it costs more to fix.

Mrs. Profitt was in the office. She is seventy-three years old and she is the aunt of the woman who had just sold me the M&Ms, and she has run this motel for thirty-eight years, and when I came in she looked up from a crossword she did with a ballpoint and said, *Della. I figured.* I said, *Hi, Mrs. Profitt.* She did not get up. She slid me a key on a green plastic diamond keychain with the room number embossed. Number seven. *I put you on the back side,* she said. *Quieter. You eat any breakfast.* I said *Maybe.* She said, *Biscuits at five-thirty whether you do or you don't. Help yourself.* I thanked her. She went back to the crossword. *Honey,* she said, without looking up, *your mama's been worrying. You might could call her.* *I will,* I said. I went out.

Room seven was at the back. I parked. I carried the milk and the bread and the M&Ms in, and I took my duffel out of the bed and brought it in, and I shut the door behind me and turned the deadbolt and stood in the dark of the room for a count of ten before I felt around for the lamp. The lamp was a brown ceramic thing on a particle-board dresser and it took the bulb a second to come on. The bedspread was a mauve floral. The TV was a small Sanyo bolted to a wall arm. The Bible was on the nightstand. The air smelled the way every motel room in this part of the country smells: a faint propane, a faint cigarette that has not been smoked in this room in thirteen years but is in the carpet, a faint Pine-Sol from the housekeeping cart.

I put the milk on top of the air conditioner because there was no refrigerator. I unwrapped the toothbrush and laid it on the counter by the sink. I sat on the edge of the bed and I called my mother.

She picked up on the second ring this time and her voice was upright, which meant she had not slept. *Della.* *Mama. I'm here. I'm at the Mountain Laurel.* She was quiet a second longer than I expected and then she said, *I figured you'd be.* I did not know if she meant the motel or the second longer. *I am tired,* I said. *I am going to come up in the morning.* *All right,* she said. *Eat something.* *I will.* *I made a stew.* *Save me some.* *I will save you some.* Another small pause. *Wade will keep tonight,* she said. *The visiting hours are seven to ten. You go up at seven.* *Yes, ma'am.* *Good night, Della.* *Good night, Mama.*

I hung up. I sat on the bed in my clothes and I did not get under the cover. The parking-lot neon came through the curtain in a long red bar across the carpet and onto the foot of the spread. I made myself eat half the bread out of the bag. I made myself drink the milk straight from the jug. I lay back on the cover with my boots still on and I closed my eyes.

I saw the IGA parking lot. I saw the green truck. I saw the sticker — Blackridge Coal Holdings, in their little white-and-gold script across the green — and I saw the curl at the corner of it. I saw the brush guard. I saw the cable in the bed. I saw the jerry can strapped against the side wall, and I asked myself *what does a man take a five-gallon jerry can with him on a Sunday for,* and I asked myself *what does any of this have to do with my brother,* and I asked myself *why am I lying here thinking about a truck.*

I did not know. I had clocked it. I had noted it. I was going to think about it later because I had thought about it now.

I turned onto my side. The neon bar moved up the spread and across my shoulder. I let myself think one thing on purpose, which was that I had driven for ten hours and I was in the town of Caldwell and I had not yet gone three more miles up Bear Branch to my mother's house, and I was not going to tonight. The night was the membrane. I was going to stay on this side of it until the sun came up.

The strip site was burning at this minute on the high ridge east of town. I could not see it from the back of the motel. I knew it was there. The work lights along the contour. The yellow beads.

I slept four hours. I do not remember dreaming.

---

# Chapter 3 — Wade

I woke at four-eleven on the nose because that is what twelve years of working a forty-eight-hour rotation has done to me. The sun was not up. The neon bar was off the spread because the office had killed the sign at three. I sat up on the edge of the bed in the dark and listened to my own breathing for a minute the way you listen to a patient's, and I put the breathing where it belonged, and I got up.

The Mountain Laurel's water comes on cold and stays cold for a count of about fifteen, and you learn this at five o'clock in the morning by getting in too soon. I stood in the shower with my forearms against the tile until the heat caught up and then I made it as hot as it would go and I let it run on the back of my neck for the length of a song I would not have admitted I was hearing. *Just a closer walk with thee. Grant it, Jesus, is my plea.* The hymn was not in my head because I had chosen it. It was in my head because the part of me that was awake at four-eleven without an alarm was the part that grew up sitting two pews from the front at Bear Branch Methodist on Sundays with Vera before she died and with Ladonna after. The body keeps the hymnal even when the rest of you has lost it.

I got out and dressed and went down to the office. Mrs. Profitt was in there in her housecoat at five thirty-six with a sheet pan of biscuits and a butter dish and a Mason jar of strawberry preserves, and the coffee in a glass urn on the warmer behind her, and she looked at me and said *Sit there a minute,* and I sat at the little card table by the window, and she brought me a biscuit on a paper plate without putting the preserves on it because she had not asked and was not going to assume, and a cup of black coffee with the grounds in the bottom of the styrofoam. *Eat,* she said. *You're a hair under what I want you at.* I ate. The biscuit was good. The coffee was a kind of coffee. I drank it.

She did not talk to me much. She told me there was rain coming Thursday and the maples were a week ahead of last year and old man Brashear had died on Friday and the funeral was Wednesday at the Methodist in town. She told me Becca Couch was working dispatch at the jail this rotation. *Becca's a good girl,* she said. *Was a sweet child. Lord, I remember her at the parade in '94 with that little tambourine.* I said *I'll be at the jail at seven.* She said, *You eat one more biscuit and you can be at the jail at seven.* I ate one more biscuit. I left her two dollars on the table for the preserves I had not used, and she pretended not to see me do it, and I went out to the truck.

The town at six on a Monday was the gray of a town that has been awake for an hour already. The lights were on at the Brass Lantern and you could see the women through the window already up at the counter. The Phillips 66 was open. I drove past it without looking. The courthouse parking lot was three-quarters empty because the staff doesn't come in until eight, and I parked under the same locust tree Wade and I used to fight over for the swing in the summer of 1998 when our father would drag us into town because he could not get a sitter. The locust was older. The swing had been a frayed rope in 1998 and the rope was gone now. The branch the rope had hung from was still there.

The jail is a basement annex on the north side of the building, accessed by an outside concrete stair that goes down past the gas meter and into a steel door with a buzzer above the handle. I pressed the buzzer at five before seven. The buzzer made a sound like a wet sneeze. The lock thunked. I pulled the door open and went in.

The lobby was a square of vinyl tile with a row of orange plastic chairs against one wall and a glass partition at the far end with the receptionist behind it. Behind the glass was Becca Halsey — Becca Couch now — and she looked up from a printer that was eating a piece of paper and she stood up so fast she knocked her swivel chair against the wall, and she came around the partition and she hugged me before I had time to put my hands in a place where she could hug me, and she said *Della,* into my shoulder, and held on for a count of three, and then let go, and stepped back, and looked at me with eyes that had been her mother's eyes too, exactly.

*Becca,* I said.

*I figured you'd be here at seven.*

*Mama told me seven.*

*I figured Ladonna would tell you seven. Come on back.*

She walked me back behind the partition to a sign-in book and showed me where to put my name and my driver's license number and the time. She did it like a child handing me a school worksheet she had filled out for me already. I wrote *Della Combs.* I wrote my number from memory because it has not changed since I was nineteen. I wrote *07:00.* She took the book back and looked at the page and said, *I am sorry about your brother. I am sorry about my mama. I am sorry I haven't called. Lord, Della, I have meant to call you for five years.*

*Becca,* I said.

*I know,* she said. *I know.*

She had a Wal-Mart hair clip holding her bangs back and a wedding ring with a chip diamond and a hospital lanyard around her neck with what looked like her son's school picture clipped to it. She had been the prettiest girl in our class. She still was. She had cried for a count I did not give her and then she had stopped, and now she was the woman behind the partition.

*Annie was a good mother,* I said. *I should have called.*

She made a small low sound. *Mama loved you for coming. Don't you carry that.*

She walked me to the visitation room. The walk was a short concrete hall with a strip of fluorescent above and a row of cell doors on the right behind a second steel door that I did not see opened. The visitation room was the size of a walk-in closet, with one plastic chair on the visitor side and a counter and a slab of plexiglass scratched with twenty years of names and pen ink and somebody's idiot heart, and a handset on a coiled cord, and a fluorescent strip above the door that was a quarter of one length brighter than the other three-quarters because somebody had replaced a bulb. *Sit,* Becca said. *He's coming. They'll knock on the wall when he goes back.* She closed the door behind her.

I sat. I held the handset. I did not put it to my ear. I looked at the scratched plexiglass and I made myself a sentence the way you make a run-report sentence at the start: *Subject is the visitor's brother, white male age 36, in custody pending preliminary, charge homicide, last seen by visitor in summer of 2021 at a roadside cookout, visitor is the subject's older sister.* I felt the medic settle a sixteenth of an inch lower into the chair.

A door clanked on the far side. He came in. The deputy with him was a kid I did not know — Pickett by his name tag — and he led Wade to the seat on the other side of the plexiglass and did not lock the cuff to anything, which surprised me, and stepped back to the door and stood there. Wade sat down. He looked at me a second through the scratched glass before he picked up the handset. He picked it up with his left hand. His right was the one that always shook when he was tired and the right was at rest in his lap and the resting was a small visible vibration the size of a cell-phone alarm.

*Hey, Dell,* he said.

His voice through the handset was a kind of nine-volt buzz with him under it.

*Hey, baby,* I said. I had not called him *baby* in twenty years. He blinked.

*I figured Mama'd call you,* he said. *I told her not to.*

*She did anyway.*

*She would.* The mouth corner moved. *You drive in last night?*

*Got in around seven. Stayed at the Mountain Laurel.*

*You didn't go up to the house?*

*I'm going up after I leave here.*

He nodded. He looked at me through the plexiglass with the kind of careful attention that is what a man does in a jail when his sister has driven seven hours and he does not know yet what she has driven seven hours for. His eyes were my eyes — the same gray-brown nobody in the family but me and Wade have, that Vera had in the one photograph of her at twenty-five — and they were under-rested in a way that you cannot fake. He was thirty-six. He looked forty-two. His hair was cut short by somebody who was not a barber and his cheek had a half-healed scrape on it the size of a pencil eraser. He was thinner than the last time I had seen him, which had been at our cousin Anne Marie's wedding in August of 2021 in a side yard in London, where he had eaten three pieces of cake and danced with our mother to a song I am not allowed to name.

*Dell,* he said. *I didn't do it.*

*Wade.*

*I didn't.*

*Okay.*

He waited for me to say something else and when I did not, he laughed, low. It was a real laugh. It was a Combs laugh, the dry kind, the kind that comes out from under a thing.

*That fast,* he said. *Okay.*

*I said okay.*

*I know what you said.* He set the handset on his shoulder a second to scratch his cheek with the wrist of his right hand, and I watched the right hand and pretended not to. He brought the handset back. *You're a paramedic,* he said. *You're trained to believe the patient when they tell you what's wrong with them.*

*I'm trained to believe the body.*

*Fair.*

*Wade. Just tell me.*

He sat a second. The deputy behind him scratched his neck and looked at the floor. Wade did not look at the deputy. He looked at me.

*The dead man's name is Tully Renfro,* he said. *He was fifty-one. He was a land man for Blackridge. They found him up Sorrow Hollow on Tuesday morning. He'd been there a day and a half. Mr. Earl Whitt found him walking that beagle. I have a trail cam picture of my truck a half mile from the body two hours before the ME said he died. I have a hammer in the truck that the ME said is what they hit him with. They have not got blood on the hammer. They have not got blood on me. They have what they have.*

*Wade.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*Were you up there.*

He went very still for the third of a second it takes a tell to leave a face if a face is going to give the tell. Then he dropped his voice low — and I knew, because I am his sister, that the dropping was him about to tell me a true thing — and he said, *Yes. I was up there. I did not kill him.*

*Why were you up there.*

He held my eye through the plexiglass.

*I went up to see about a thing,* he said.

*What thing.*

He shook his head, small.

*Wade.*

*A thing,* he said. *I'm not gonna say what.*

*You'd better say what.*

*Dell. I'm not gonna.*

I gripped the handset and made myself not bear down on the plastic. The handset is the same kind the public schools used for the office phones in 1992. They are stronger than they look. I set my jaw. I felt my own right hand — the hand that does not shake at four-eleven when an eighteen-year-old's column has come down across her thighs — settle into the steady that is its work.

*Okay,* I said.

*Okay,* he said.

We sat. The fluorescent above the door buzzed in its faint bright-three-quarter way. The deputy shifted his weight.

*Tell me how you got there,* I said.

*Drove up the trace from the church. Parked at the turn where the laurel bends. Walked the half mile in. I was up there forty minutes maybe. I came back down. I drove home. I ate a frozen pizza and watched the second half of a Vols game and went to bed.*

*You see anybody up there.*

He looked at me too steady. *No,* he said.

*Wade.*

*No, Dell.*

I let it sit. I was going to come back to it later because there are kinds of *no* that get bigger if you let them be alone for an hour or two. I went a different way.

*Granddaddy ever take you up there,* I said.

His face moved. Not much. The shape around the mouth went soft and then went back. He had not been ready for the question. He should have been. We are Hollis's grandchildren and we know what we have been taken up which holler by which old man, and that knowing is part of how we recognize ourselves to each other across a piece of plexiglass twenty years late.

*Yeah,* he said. *He took me up to Sorrow Hollow a bunch of times when I was little. I was — I don't know — six the first time. Seven. He showed me where to set traps. He showed me the seep at the back. He drank out of his hand and made me drink out of mine and said it was the coldest water in the county. He told me it was Combs land before it was anybody's. He said *don't tell your daddy I brought you up here he'll have my hide.* I never told daddy.*

I was very still.

*Did he ever take me,* I said.

Wade looked at me. *I don't know, Dell. I always figured he had.*

He had not. I would have remembered. I was older than Wade by four years and the years between six and ten are years you keep with a man like Hollis, and I had not been taken. The not-taking was a thing I was going to put down and pick up later. I put it down.

*Wade,* I said. *Listen to me. I have a lawyer named Hannah Soyars I am meeting at noon. I am going up to the hollow before that. I am going to your mama's after that. I am not going back to Knoxville until this is done. You hear me.*

*I hear you.*

*You are going to have to tell me what you went up there for.*

*I know what you want me to do.*

*Wade.*

*I know, Dell.*

We looked at each other through the plexiglass. He set the right hand on the counter on his side, palm down, and pressed his fingertips against the glass, and held them there. The shake stopped, the way the shake stops in a man's hand the second he gives it a thing to do. I put my left hand up on my side and pressed it flat against his. The plexiglass was the wrong temperature between us. He kept his hand there for the count of three. Then he took it down.

*Visit's about up,* he said.

The deputy had not moved.

*I'll see you tomorrow,* I said.

*Don't come every day,* he said. *It uses up Mama. They'll let me call from the room twice a week. I'll call.*

*Wade —*

*Dell. Don't come every day.*

I nodded. *Wade. Are you eating.*

*I'm eating.*

*Are you drinking.*

*Eight months tomorrow.*

*Eight months as of —*

*Tomorrow.*

*Wade,* I said, *I love you.*

He looked at me as if I had hit him. He did not say it back. We do not say it back, in this family. We say *I know,* and then we sit. He said, *I know.* We sat. The deputy gave him a soft *Combs,* and he set the handset on the counter and stood up, and the deputy walked him out, and the door clanked, and I was the only person in the room.

I did not cry. I went out. Becca was at her partition and she looked up and saw my face and saw the no-cry on it, and she stood up again and said, *Della. You want to step in the bathroom for a minute.* I said, *No. Thank you. I am going to step into the parking lot for a minute.* She nodded and let me go.

Outside the air was a cold that had given up its frost. The rail of the concrete stair had been white at five-thirty and was wet at nine. The town had come awake. There was a county truck idling at the curb by the courthouse front, and two men in coveralls on the courthouse lawn picking up sycamore leaves with rakes the size of brooms, and the smell of woodsmoke from somewhere up the holler that meant somebody was burning brush early before the rain on Thursday. I stood at the top of the stair with my hand on the rail.

Wade was protecting somebody. The somebody was not Wade. The somebody had to have been up the hollow with him or had to have been up the hollow before him or had to be a person Wade was loyal enough to to take a charge. I had a short list before I had unlocked my truck. The list felt wrong before I sat down in the cab.

I had been brought into this case eighteen hours ago, and I had a brother who would not say what he had gone to see about, and I had a piece of plexiglass between me and a fact, and I had a hollow up Bear Branch I had not been to since 1999 and a grandfather who had taken my brother to it and not me. I had a sticker on a truck in an IGA parking lot in my back pocket as a yellow note.

I sat in the cab with the engine off and made myself a sentence. *Patient pending, charge homicide, family member medic on scene refusing handoff.* It was not a clinical sentence and I knew it. I said it out loud anyway. The truck did not have an opinion.

I started the engine. The clock on the dash said 8:46. Hannah Soyars was meeting me at the Brass Lantern at noon. The light was up. The road was open.

I was going to walk into Sorrow Hollow first.

---

# Chapter 4 — Sorrow Hollow

The trace up to Sorrow Hollow leaves Bear Branch road a quarter mile past the church, on the right, and you have to know it is there because the county quit grading it in 1981 and the laurel has been working at the opening ever since. There is a turnout at the trace head about the size of a single-wide trailer's parking pad, and I pulled into it at twenty after ten on a Monday morning that was colder than the radio had said and clearer than I had a right to expect.

I parked under a black gum that had dropped half its leaves on the hood already in the time it took me to walk to the church and back. I had stopped at the church first because the church was on the way and because I was going to need a minute before I went up. I had stood in the cemetery for about three minutes and read the four stones I had come to read — Hollis 1924–2005, Vera 1928–1991, Wendell 1958–2007, my grandmother's mother Eliza 1898–1947 — and I had walked back to the truck. The leaves underfoot had been the color and consistency of old pennies.

The trace climbs steadily for the first quarter mile and then bends east and steepens. I walked.

I had been up here four times in my life — twice at seven and eight with my grandfather, once at sixteen on a dare with two girls, once at seventeen after a breakup. I had not been on this trace since.

I was older than my body remembered. I was breathing harder by the second bend than I would have at twenty-five. The medic in me wanted to take her own pulse and then made fun of the medic in me for wanting to. The grade settled at the long flat near the top, where the trail follows the contour around the back of a knob before it drops you into the hollow itself, and that was where I felt the cold air change.

I came around the last bend. The flat opened below me. I stopped at the lip of the trace and stood there.

The hollow is a fold in the ridge, the shape of a hand cupped on a table. The fold is bounded on the north by a sandstone outcrop that comes down at about a sixty-degree slope into a face maybe thirty feet high, and a person who knew what to look for could see the dark wet patch low on that face where the seep comes out of the rock. The flat in front of the outcrop is mostly laurel and rhododendron, with three or four big tulip poplars that put the whole place into a green dimness even at the back end of October, and a stand of mixed oak going up the western slope, and at the back of the flat to the west of the seep — set off, alone — was the white pine.

I had not let myself think about the pine. It was there. It was big the way Hollis had told me it was when I was seven and he had said *this tree was here before any of us and it will be here after,* and I had said *how do you know,* and he had said, *because trees and you, what's left of you, are different timekeepers, honey.* The trunk was four feet across or close to it. The bark was the gray-brown plate of a white pine that has been on a slope long enough to forget what it was once. I looked at it from the lip of the trace. I let myself look. Then I looked at the rest of it, because I had not driven up Bear Branch to look at a tree.

The deputies' tape was strung in a sloppy rectangle around the flat at the base of the outcrop. They had run it from a laurel trunk on the south to a small dead beech on the east to a stake hammered into the leaf duff on the north to another laurel on the west, and the tape had sagged on the east side already and was lying in the leaves. *Sheriff's Office Crime Scene Do Not Cross.* The yellow was the kind of yellow you see at night from a long way out. In the morning under the canopy here it was a yellow that did not belong to anything else in sight.

I came down to the lip of the tape and stood at it. I did not cross. I had no reason to. I knew what I was looking at without putting my feet on it.

The body had been ten feet from the base of the outcrop, on the patch of beaten laurel near the seep. The deputies had walked it heavily — I could see the boot marks in the duff and the broken laurel where they had brushed in and out — and the patch of dark on the leaves where the head had been was visible from where I stood. Not red. Brown by now, four days on, and a little black. I had seen this kind of patch in my line of work in three different driveways and one parking lot. You learn its shape. The shape is always the same, give or take the slope of the ground.

I let my eye walk the rest of the scene. I stood there with my hands in my sweater pockets and I did what I do, which is to take an inventory in the medic's order — surfaces, sources, anomalies, exit. There were no surfaces to interpret here that had not already been interpreted. The deputies had cleared the duff in a circle around where the body had been and had not bothered to put it back. The cleared circle was the size of a kitchen table.

I stood at the tape for what was probably a minute and a half. I had not yet sat down. I had not yet let the place have me in any way I would have been willing to write a report about. I was still upright and assessing.

Then I let my eye go further out. The seep. The seep was running. It made the sound a seep that has been running since before the Civil War makes, which is a sound below the sound of anything else, a sound you stop hearing if you do not pay it the favor of attention. It came out of a band of darker sandstone halfway down the outcrop face and fell about three feet onto a basin of flat stones somebody had laid in there a long time ago. I had been told, at seven, by my grandfather, that the stones in the basin had been laid by him and his brother in 1947, before Asa died. I had been seven and I had not held the year. I held it now.

The basin held about a gallon of water at any given moment and then the water spilled out the low side and ran down through a narrow cut in the laurel and joined Big Cane Creek a hundred yards below. The water in the basin was the color of nothing. I knew without thinking how cold it would be on my hand. Fifty-four degrees in any season. Hollis had told me that too.

I moved my eye left from the seep. The laurel went heavy in the bend toward the rhododendron thicket on the east slope, where Pap had buried Calvin Tull in 1961 — I did not know this then, of course, that morning at the tape; I knew the place felt heavier when my eye went that way and I assigned the feeling to nothing in particular. I moved my eye back. Up the outcrop. Down the outcrop. Across the flat. Across the cleared circle of duff. Past the patch of dark on the laurel. Past the second laurel a little further east.

Past the flat stone.

The flat stone was a slab of sandstone the deputies had walked around six times that I could count without bending in. It was the size of a coffee-table top and it lay flush in the leaves about ten feet east of the cleared circle, with one corner sticking up where a root had pushed it, and a smooth face on top from a thousand years of rain and snow and the small flat boots of a thousand years of small flat creatures. The top of the stone was clean in a way the surrounding leaves were not, because somebody — a deputy, an animal, the wind — had wiped a swath across it, and in the middle of the wiped swath, dead center on the flat face, was a piece of cannel coal.

I did not move for a count I would not give you.

The piece was about three and a half inches across. It was glossy in the way cannel coal is glossy — a black with a wet shine, lighter in the hand than ordinary bituminous, the kind you can polish on the cuff of a sleeve and see your own face come up out of it dim and brown. It sat on the rock the way a thing is set, not the way a thing falls. It was square to the long axis of the stone, two inches from the high edge and four from the low edge, and the face that was up was the smoothest face and had been wiped to a dull shine I could see from where I stood. Nobody had bagged it. Nobody had photographed it. The yellow tape did not go around it. The deputies had walked past it six times.

I stood at the lip of the tape and looked at the piece of coal on the stone and the world fell out from under me by the foot.

I have stood at a great many crime scenes in twelve years of being a paramedic in a city where the fire department goes everywhere first. I have stood at the four corners of a tarp-covered shape on a four-lane and watched a state trooper write a number on the asphalt with a piece of yellow chalk. I have crouched over a body in a kitchen and assessed for warmth at the carotid because the dispatcher had not been told he was already gone. I have not, in any of those scenes, lost my legs under me. I lost them at the lip of the tape at Sorrow Hollow on the morning of the thirteenth of October at — I checked, later, on the clock in the truck — ten forty-six.

I made it to a log six feet off the trace and I sat. I put my head between my knees the way you put a fainter's head between her knees, and I breathed for ten breaths in and out by count, and I did not throw up.

I had carried something for twenty years and I had carried it folded so small I had stopped knowing what shape it was, and the second piece of coal on the rock was the corner of the folded thing coming open. I had not asked it to come open. It had not asked me. It was open.

*The coal on the chest means the man broke a vow to the rock. The coal on the rock means somebody else saw it done.*

I had not let those sentences exist in my head in twenty years. They existed in my head now. They were in my grandfather's voice — the dry careful voice, the one good eye, the oxygen tube — and they were as clear as if he were sitting on the log next to me with his Lays-potato-chip-can ashtray on his knee, which he had not done since I was about ten because he had not smoked from 1988 forward. They were clear. They had been there the whole time, packed.

A piece of coal on the chest of a fifty-one-year-old land agent named Tully Renfro four days ago. A piece of coal on a flat rock ten feet off the body, missed by the deputies.

I sat on the log and I did the math the way I did the math on Madison in the Camry two days ago. *Forty-one-year-old female. Pulse not taken. Sitting in the leaves at the lip of the crime-scene tape on what is functionally her own land. Possessed of a piece of information neither the sheriff nor her brother's lawyer knows. Just discovered a second piece of evidence material to the case which the sheriff's office failed to identify. Family member of the accused. Estranged from the place for twenty-two years. Last meal a biscuit at five thirty-six a.m.*

I breathed. I straightened up. The two crows that had argued me onto the trace were now somewhere down the slope toward the creek, arguing softer. The water in the seep ran the way water runs that has been running since the world was young.

I stood up. I made my legs hold. I walked back to the tape. I made myself look at the stone again, and the piece of coal on it. I did not cross. I did not touch.

I looked at the piece for the count it would take a person standing back of me to read this sentence. I made a small careful inventory in my head — its size, its color, its placement, the wipe of the stone underneath, the two inches from the high edge. I would write it later, in my hand, in the small spiral notebook I had bought at the IGA last night and put in the truck. I would write it the way I would write a run report. I would write *cannel coal, 3.5 in across, glossy, placed square to long axis of slab, surface of slab wiped within 12-in radius of placement, evidence visually similar to industrial-grade cannel sometimes used in heating in this area pre-1960, placement deliberate.* I would write it.

I walked to the seep. I did not cross the tape, but the seep was outside it; the rectangle they had run did not extend to the back of the outcrop. I crouched at the basin. I let my fingers go into the water. I had known the temperature already. The water had a stillness to it — the small constant motion of seep water that fools you into thinking it is still — and the cold went up my forearm in a band. I cupped a little water in my hand and brought it to my mouth.

The water tasted the way it had tasted when I was seven and my grandfather had handed me a tin cup he had handed his brother. It tasted like the rock it had come out of. I had no other thing to compare it to. I took a second small sip out of my palm. I shook the water off my hand. I stood up.

The pine was at my back. I had not gone to it. I would not go to it today. I would go to it on a different day, and I would put my hand on its bark, and I would tell it, in a way I would tell no one else, what I now knew. Not yet. The pine had been there before me and was going to be there after me and was not in a hurry.

I walked out of the hollow.

The trace coming down feels shorter than it did going up. The body is going downhill and the body forgives the slope. I came down through the same bars of light and the same dry-wet crunch and the same crows and the same laurel, and by the time I came around the last bend before the trail head my breathing was not what I would have called slow, but it was honest. I came out at the turnout at eleven thirty-eight. The black gum had dropped six more leaves on the hood while I was up there. I brushed them off. I sat in the cab with the door open and one foot on the running board, and I took out the small spiral notebook from the glove box, and I wrote — in the order I had walked it — every observation I had made at the flat.

Then I wrote, at the bottom of the page, in the same hand:

*Coal on a rock ten feet off the body. Cannel. Three and a half inches. Placed.*

I closed the notebook. I sat looking at the cover of it for a count.

Hannah Soyars was meeting me at the Brass Lantern at noon. I was going to be in town with thirty minutes to spare if I went now. I was going to need to decide on the drive in what I was going to say to her and what I was not. I had a second piece of cannel coal on a flat rock in a hollow whose name had been on the page of my life for twenty years without my consent. I had a brother who would not say what he had gone up there to see about. I had a grandfather's voice in my head telling me what the coal meant.

I had not told a soul any of it, ever.

I started the engine.

I drove down Bear Branch toward town and the truck took the curves the way a truck takes curves it has not made before. I did not turn the radio on. I let the gravel sound under the tires for a quarter mile and then the blacktop, and I made the right onto the Caldwell road and then the right onto KY-92, and I drove past the IGA and past the Phillips 66 and past the courthouse and into the Brass Lantern lot, and I parked, and I sat with the engine off for the count it took the heat in the cab to start to fade.

I had decided. I was not going to tell Hannah at this lunch about the coal. Not yet. I was going to meet her, and I was going to listen, and I was going to take the file Cornett was going to hand me at three, and I was going to drive up to my mother's after, and I was going to put my head on a pillow that smelled like my own childhood, and I was going to sleep, and tomorrow morning I was going to tell Cornett about the rock and let his deputy go bag it. And then I would tell Hannah at the diner that evening that I knew what the coal meant.

The plan held me for the count of about two minutes. Then I got out of the truck and went in to meet a woman I had never met, who was going to be on my side, and I started doing the work.

---

# Chapter 5 — The Sheriff and the Lawyer

The back booth at the Brass Lantern — the one in the corner under the framed photograph of a 1957 high-school basketball team — was open. A woman was already in it: two glasses of water on the table, a manila accordion folder set up on its short edge against the wall, a yellow legal pad with a pen across it, a half-eaten dinner roll on a saucer that she pushed at me without saying hello.

*Della Combs. Hannah Soyars. Eat the roll, I had them bring it for you because Becca told me you came straight here from the jail.*

I sat down. I ate the roll.

She was small, dark-haired, charcoal suit jacket over a white blouse. Thirty-two years old by the file, and her face had that thirty-two-going-on-fifty set of women who go into capital defense in the second-poorest district in their state.

*Lily,* she said, without looking up, *bring her a cobbler.*

*I'm not hungry,* I said.

*You're going to be in an hour.* She slid the pen aside and folded her hands. *I have eaten in this diner three days a week for the last seven years and I know what the energy curve looks like after a jail visit and an empty stomach. Cobbler. Peach. Take it home if you have to.*

Lily set the cobbler down and refilled both coffees and went away.

*Tell me what he said,* Hannah said.

I told her. I did it the way I would have told it to a partner on a run. I gave her the chronology of the visit — *arrived 06:55, brother in scrubs and cuffs, deputy was a kid name of Pickett, brother stated victim's name and basic facts of the case, brother stated he was up in Sorrow Hollow that afternoon, brother stated he did not kill Tully Renfro, brother stated he went up to* — and I stopped, and I said, *to see about a thing. He would not say what thing.*

Hannah wrote a single short line in her pad with the pen. *Mm.*

*He also said,* I said, *that he did not see anyone up there.*

*Did you believe him on that.*

*No.*

*Good.* She looked up. *Della, I do not have to tell you what the position of an honest defense attorney is in this state when the client is partly lying. I am going to talk to him this afternoon. He will tell me what he is going to tell me. He may say more or he may say less, but I want to know what he gave you and what he held back, because the held-back tells me where to push.*

*He said he was up there forty minutes. He said he came down and drove home and ate a pizza and watched the second half of a Vols game.*

*He did watch the second half. There's no game data subpoena needed for that, his phone metadata puts him on the couch at six p.m. The Vols lost to Alabama, by the way, which I am taking as a separate insult.*

I almost smiled. I picked up the spoon. I cracked the crust on the cobbler. The cobbler smelled the way you wanted it to.

*Eat,* she said.

I ate. We sat for ten seconds in the kind of small silence between two professional women who have decided on a working tone in the first minute and will not be returning to it.

*Tell me about the case,* I said.

She told me. She had been court-appointed three days ago. She had been to see Wade twice already. She had been at the arraignment Friday afternoon, where Wade had pleaded not guilty in a tone of voice she said sounded like a man reading from an instruction manual for an oscillating fan, and bail had been denied because the charge was capital and the Commonwealth had argued, plausibly, that the defendant was a Caldwell County resident with diminished ties due to recent unemployment and had a sister out of state and a brother-in-law who lived in North Carolina, and there was no one who could meaningfully co-sign for him in the kind of money the court would have wanted. The arraignment had taken eleven minutes. The preliminary hearing was set for the eighteenth of November. The grand jury, if it went, was on the second of December.

She told me Tully Renfro had been fifty-one, divorced, one grown daughter in Pineville, twenty-three years with Blackridge Coal Holdings. A land agent — the man who came to your door with a folder under his arm and tried to get you to sign over what your great-grandparents had not had the foresight to keep out of a 1907 broad-form deed. By her sources, a skilled and slightly cruel practitioner.

*The autopsy report I have is preliminary,* she said. *The full one will be in by Wednesday. Want the cliff notes.*

*Yes.*

She pulled a sheaf out of the manila accordion. She turned it so I could read it upside down at speed, which I did.

*Blunt-force trauma to the left temporal region. Three impacts, the third probably post-mortem. Skull fracture with displacement; epidural hemorrhage. Pattern of injury consistent with a rock or a hammer-shaped tool. Killer struck right-handed. Killer was likely shorter than the victim, given the angle of the wounds and the slight upward trajectory of the impacts. Tully was six-two. So we are looking for somebody under six-two, who used their right hand, and who was within arm's length of a man six-two in a clearing the size of a kitchen.*

*Anything on the hammer.*

*The Commonwealth claims the roofing hammer in Wade's truck bed is type-consistent with the wounds. Type-consistent is a phrase doing a lot of work in their filing. The hammer was clean. No blood. No tissue. No hair. ME has not committed to it being the weapon. Della, no blood means no blood. The press doesn't print the no.*

She set the autopsy back in the folder and pulled a second sheet.

*Here is the thing they have not put in the paper. On Tully Renfro's chest, between the second and third rib on his left side, when the deputies arrived, was a piece of cannel coal. Four inches across. Glossy. Placed.* She let her eye stay on me long enough for the medic in me to consider holding still. I held still. *That detail has been withheld from the public. The Commonwealth's reasoning is that they want it to be a fact only the killer would know, in case anybody decides to confess later in a holding cell to a deputy who happens to take notes. I am sharing it with you because you are family and because I am going to need you. You are not to share it with your brother or with anybody else.*

*Understood.*

*Della.* She put the sheet down. *Does that mean anything to you. The coal on the chest.*

I held still. I had decided in the cab.

*No,* I said.

My hand was on the handle of the coffee cup and did not move. My eyes did not move. The framed basketball team on the wall behind her was the 1957 team and the boy in the center holding the trophy was a boy whose name had been on my grandfather's tongue at every reunion, and I gave the place in my eye where his face was the work of looking at it.

She looked at me a beat too long for me to be sure she had bought it. Then she made the small chin-down nod that lawyers and physicians and a certain kind of preacher make when they are going to allow a sentence past their ear without challenging it because they have not yet decided how to set the trap. She slid the sheet back into the folder.

*All right,* she said. *Let me tell you what I am going to do this afternoon and what I am going to need from you tomorrow.*

She told me. She was going to the jail at two and she would have an hour with Wade. She would push him on the *thing* he had gone up to see about, and she would push him on whether he had seen anyone, and she would push him on whether anyone was with him at any point, and she would push him on his alibi for the morning of the killing, which was that he had been in his own kitchen with coffee and the back door open looking out at the dog, but had no corroborating witness because he lived alone now and the dog could not testify. She said she would call me from the parking lot after.

She was going to need character witnesses for the preliminary. She needed three. She would put my mother first because the mother of an accused in this county is given a hearing even by the magistrates who have decided in their heart that the accused is guilty. She would put on a foreman from Wade's old crew who would testify to his work history at Blackridge before the back injury — *yes, that company,* she said, *and yes, that is going to be one of the points of pressure later* — and she would put on the pastor of the Bear Branch Methodist if he would do it, which she thought he would.

*I cannot put you on as a character,* she said. *Sister, paramedic from out of state, not a current resident. The Commonwealth's attorney is a former classmate of mine name of Brett Hambrick and he will object to you and the magistrate will sustain. You are more useful to me out of the box.*

*I figured.*

*Della. Out of the box doesn't mean off the field. It means you go where I can't and you bring me what I can use.*

I nodded.

*One last thing,* she said, *and then I have to be in the truck by twelve fifty. The Commonwealth is going to ask the magistrate at the prelim to bind this over to a grand jury. They have a thirty-percent-circumstantial case at best on what I have seen. They have motive — Tully had been pressuring Wade's mother for over a year on the Bear Branch mineral rights, and the Commonwealth will frame Wade as a son with a grievance — and they have the trail-cam image at the trace head and they have the hammer. They do not have a weapon with blood on it. They do not have a confession. They do not have a witness. They have a victim killed with a signature object on his chest that they think they can keep out of the public eye long enough to use it as bait. Their case is a sand pile. Sand piles can hold a man down for a long time before they erode. I am going to spend the next thirty-five days kicking sand off the pile.*

*And I am going to.*

*You are going to find me whoever Wade is protecting. Because he is. He is protecting somebody. Both of us know it. He has decided his life is the right thing to spend to keep them out of this and he is wrong about that, and I cannot tell him so because I am a lawyer and what I tell him is privileged and is not going to move him. He's not going to listen to me. He is maybe going to listen to you. We work that question together.*

She closed the accordion folder and stood up. She left a twenty on the table.

*The cobbler is on me,* she said.

*Hannah.*

*Yes.*

*Thank you.*

She gave me a small one-quarter smile.

*Don't thank me yet,* she said. *Be here at eight tomorrow morning. I will have a draft witness list and the rest of the autopsy. You go see Cornett at three.*

She went.

I sat in the booth for ten minutes more. I ate the rest of the cobbler. I did not taste it. I paid for the cobbler. I left the twenty for Hannah. I went out.

I had two and a half hours until Cornett. I sat in the truck in the diner lot and called my mother to tell her I would not be up tonight after all but I would be up tomorrow for sure. She was quiet for the count of three and then she said *all right, Della,* in a voice that was the voice of a woman who had set a table and was going to have to take a plate off. I told her I had a file coming from the sheriff at three and I needed to read it through. She said *yes, all right,* and then she said *bring me a quart of buttermilk when you do come,* and I said *I will,* and she hung up before I could say more, which was the kindness it was meant to be.

I drove the long way around — out the Caldwell road and back in via the old church loop — and I made myself look at the Phillips 66 on the way past. The spot was nothing. It was a piece of ground a man had died on eighteen years ago, in a truck, with a needle in his right arm, while a woman who had loved him went on watching television at her kitchen table four miles up Bear Branch because he had not come home and she had stopped letting it surprise her.

I drove on.

The courthouse parking lot at two forty-five had filled. I circled twice before I got a slot. I knocked on the door that said SHERIFF AVERY CORNETT in white block letters.

*Come in.*

Cornett was at his desk. He was tall and slow and gray. He half-stood from the chair the way an old-school southern man does for a woman entering an office, and he gestured at the chair across from him.

*Della. Sit. I have a file for you. It is what I can give you. There are things in it I have redacted because they are not mine to give. I have written my office number on the inside of the folder for if you have questions.*

*Sheriff.*

*Avery,* he said. *I worked with your daddy in the spring of '79 in a road crew the year I was off from college. He kept me from breaking my back. I'm Avery to a Combs.*

I nodded.

He pushed the folder across the desk. It was a manila with three rubber bands around it and the words *RENFRO, TULLY, HOM, 2025-10-05* in marker on the tab. He pushed it the careful way you push a hot pan.

*The ME's full report comes Wednesday,* he said. *The crime-scene photos are in there. The crime-scene narrative is in there. The trail-cam image is in there. Your brother's interview transcript is in there — both interviews, the one Friday morning and the one Friday afternoon. The hammer report is in there.* He paused. *The thing about the coal is in there but it is sealed in a sub-envelope inside that I have not opened to give to you. You will see it. I do not need to see you read it.*

*All right.*

*Della.* He sat back. He laced his fingers across the front of his department-issue button-up over a small belly that had grown since he was a Lexington detective. *I want to say two things to you, plain. The first is that I do not believe your brother killed that man. The second is that what I believe does not at this hour matter, because the Commonwealth's attorney has all the discretion in this and he has decided Wade is a good defendant for a case that needs to be charged before the news cycle takes the wind out of it. I cannot release Wade. I cannot put my finger on the scale of the prosecution. What I can do is not block you from doing your work, and I can give you what I have.*

*I appreciate it.*

*One more thing.* He looked at me a long moment with the kind of look an older policeman gives a younger investigator, and I let him. *My daddy was a deputy in this county under Caleb Honaker in the sixties. He worked the Calvin Tull missing-persons case in '61. He is in a nursing home in Williamsburg and he is eighty-four and his memory is patchy but it is patchy in a way that has stripes through it that are very clear. If you ever decide you need to talk to him about what he remembers of any of it, I will call ahead.*

I held his eye.

*I have not asked him about that case in forty years,* he said. *Not because I did not want to. Because I did. I came home in '04 and I took this job and I have not asked him. You may decide you want to.*

*Wendell Cornett,* I said.

*Yes ma'am.*

*Your daddy was a deputy under Sheriff Honaker.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*All right,* I said. *I will keep it in mind, Avery.*

*Do.*

I stood. I tucked the folder under my arm. He did not stand. He gave me a small careful nod from the chair. I went out.

In the truck I set the folder on the passenger seat and laid my hand on it for the count it took the heat in the cab to settle, and I did not open it. The afternoon had turned. The light was the long copper light October gives you for an hour before the sun goes behind the ridge. I had a motel room a half-mile east on KY-92 and the lamp on the dresser and an unwrapped toothbrush, and I had a manila folder with three rubber bands around it on the seat next to me, and I had a folded thing in my chest that I had not yet decided to open. Bear Branch had been three miles away for twenty-two years and it could be three miles away for one more night.
