I started the engine. I drove past the IGA — the green truck was not in the lot — and past the Phillips 66 a second time today, and I made the left into the Mountain Laurel lot and pulled around to room seven, and I sat in the cab for a count of ten before I picked up the folder and went in.

---

# Chapter 6 — What Hollis Said

Room seven was where I had left it. The bedspread was still mauve. The neon sign had been turned on a quarter hour before I came in and the red bar lay across the carpet again at the same angle as the night before, and the curtain breathed in the dry air the room blower pushed at it from the corner under the window. I shut the door behind me. I set the manila file on the dresser next to the brown ceramic lamp. I sat on the edge of the bed.

I did not open the file.

I sat on the edge of the bed for what was probably half an hour, not moving and not thinking in any organized way. The medic in me was running through Hannah's chronology and re-mapping it against the visit at the jail and the lip of the tape at the hollow and the placement of the second piece of coal on the stone, and the medic in me was very busy and very useful, and the medic in me was also a fence I was using to keep a child of seven from running across the road of my chest. I had been good at this fence for twenty years. I was good at it now.

I got up. I unbuckled my boots. I put my boots side by side at the foot of the bed in the way that you do in a house your mother trained you to set your boots in even when the house is a 1962 motel room. I took off my flannel. I lay back on the cover in my undershirt and jeans. The neon bar was across my middle. The blower in the corner cycled off. The room was very quiet in the way a small motel room off a highway can be quiet, with the highway as the floor of the quiet and not its opposite.

I closed my eyes.

I let myself remember.

I had not let myself remember in twenty years, and the body, when you let it, does not give you the memory in the order you went through it. It gives you the order it has kept. The first thing was my mother's hand on my shoulder at three in the morning. The second thing was the smell of the back-bedroom carpet — a low wool that had been in the room since I was small — and the third was the rattle of the oxygen tube against the bedframe when he turned his head to look at me. The fourth thing was that his one good eye was a kind of glass-colored brown in the bedside lamp and I had thought as a girl that it was the same color as the small marbles that came in the wooden box my great-uncle had once given me. I had not thought about those marbles in thirty years.

It was April of 2005. I was twenty-one and three months. I was finishing my senior year at UK and I had driven home for the long weekend because my mother had called me on the Thursday and said, *Della, come home now, the man is going.* The man was Hollis. He had been declining since November. He had been declining the way men of his time and his place and his profession decline — slowly, with grace, refusing to lay down longer than the doctors made him, until the body just stopped letting him stand. He had been at home the whole time. Wade was seventeen and in his last semester of high school and he had been sitting up with him most nights for six weeks already, in the chair by the bed with a paperback of Larry McMurtry in his hand and a quart Mason jar of water on the floor, and my mother had been in and out, and my father had been at work or had been the other place he went to when he was not at work, which was a place none of us asked him about because none of us wanted to be told. My grandmother Vera was thirteen years in the ground. The room my grandfather was dying in had been his room and her room and before that it had been her father's room, and before that it had been a room he had walked into in 1929 when he had finished building the house with his own hands.

My mother had woken me up at three. She had not knocked. She had come into my old room — the back bedroom upstairs that is now Wade's, but was mine then — and she had put her hand on my shoulder. She had said *Della. Granddaddy's asking for you.*

I had pulled on a sweatshirt over my pajamas and a pair of socks and I had gone down the back stairs barefoot in the socks because the wood at three in the morning of April in Caldwell County is too cold for bare feet, and I had crossed the kitchen — past the table, past the propane heater, past the refrigerator and the hymnal on top — and I had gone through the front room into the small bedroom off the front room where Pap had died in 1968 and where my grandfather was dying now.

The room had two lamps. He had wanted them both on for the last two weeks because the dark had started to be a thing to him that it had not been when he could still walk down the hall to the bathroom. Wade was in the chair with the paperback closed on his knee and his head on the wing of the chair. He had not gotten up when I came in. He had nodded at me in the way a boy of seventeen who has been awake for sixteen hours nods. He had pointed with his chin at the chair on the other side of the bed. I went around. I sat. I took the hand he held out to me.

His hand. The catcher's mitt of it. The skin had become paper. The bones had become what bones become when the body has decided that bones are no longer load-bearing furniture. He had had hands a man could hang a tire from. Now they were the hands of a man whose hand I had to take with both of mine to make sure I was not hurting him.

He looked at me. His one good eye in the lamplight was the marble color. The blind eye was the eye it had been since 1953, milky at the edge, but it had been part of his face so long it was not a thing you noticed. He had not spoken when I sat down. He looked at me for the count of about ten and then he said my name.

*Della.*

*Granddaddy.*

*Sit a minute.*

*I am sitting.*

*Sit closer.*

I came in. He pulled my hand toward him by an inch, and I felt the strength that was still under the paper — a small bright strength, the strength of a man who had been a strong man so long that the dying had not yet been able to find the bottom of it — and I came in.

*Della. Honey. Listen.*

*I'm listening, Granddaddy.*

He did not look at Wade. He had set the room up, I understood later, so that this conversation would happen with Wade in the chair on the other side too asleep to hear it. Wade was breathing the deep regular breath of seventeen at three in the morning at the bottom of a long week. The paperback would not move on his knee for another forty minutes. My grandfather knew the rhythms of a young man on watch the way he knew the rhythms of a roof crew on the third day of a long shift, and he had timed it.

*I want you to know the shape of a thing,* he said. *I am not making confession. I do not want it on you that way. I am telling it to you the way you tell a thing to a person you have to tell because somebody might come asking some day and you will need to know what to say and what not.*

*All right.*

*You hearing me, honey.*

*I am hearing you.*

He breathed. The oxygen hissed. The tube went up and over and around his ear and into the small clear apparatus on the bedside table. He did not look weak when he was breathing in. He looked like a man with one good eye on a hill in the morning before the air had moved.

*There was a man,* he said. *His name was Tull. Calvin Tull. He was a land man for Blackridge, back when they were called Blackridge Mining and Coke. He had been a land man since before I went underground. He was a tall man. Six foot, easy. He had a sound to him you could hear coming down a hall. He had two ways with him — a way for men he wanted to like him and a way for men he wanted to be afraid of him — and his eye did not change between them, only his mouth.*

*All right.*

*Della.*

*Yes sir.*

*In nineteen-fifty-nine,* he said, *we struck. I was thirty-five years old. They put me in Harlan jail for six weeks on a charge that was not my charge. I would not name the man whose charge it was, because the man's wife had asked me not to, and the man was already a dead man by his own lungs and was just then not knowing it. They let me out when the arbitration came in. They put me back on the crew because they had to. They fired me first to make a point and they hired me back. There was a note in my file about me. I never saw it. I have heard of it. It said something about how I would come back for what they had done.*

*All right.*

*That September,* he said, *Asa was killed.*

The way he said *Asa* — soft, with the stress on the first syllable the way a man says the name of a brother he buried at twenty-three when he himself was thirty-five — I had not heard him say in my life before. He had not, that I had ever heard, said the name of his older brother in my presence. The name had been in the air in our house in the same way a window in a wall is in the room without being a topic. It was a fact of the house. It was the name on the second stone in the cemetery behind the church.

*Asa was killed in a roof fall in the McCreary section of Mine Four,* he said. *He was twenty-three. There were three other men killed with him. The section had been cleared as safe two weeks before. The man who had cleared it had had an inspector's note on his desk and had decided the note was wrong. The man who had cleared it was Calvin Tull.*

He paused. He had not been speaking faster than a man with his lungs could afford, and the pause was him not catching his breath but choosing the next sentence with the kind of care a man chooses a sentence with when he has been thinking about the sentence for forty-six years.

*Della,* he said. *In nineteen-sixty-one, in July, I took a man up Sorrow Hollow on the pretext of a boundary dispute. I struck him on the side of the head three times with a piece of seam rock. I killed him. I left him there. I buried him after. They never found him.*

He held my eye.

I was twenty-one. I had been awake fifteen minutes. I was holding the hand of a man I had loved my entire life and had never, in those years, suspected of any act of harm done to any other human being. The world inside his bedroom rotated on an axis I had not been told about until the sentence and the sentence rotated it. He held my eye.

*I am telling you this so that if somebody ever comes asking,* he said, *you will know what to say and what not.*

*Granddaddy.*

*The man broke a vow. He killed Asa by ledger. He killed my brother by a piece of paper and a sentence on it. I gave him back what he had given the rock. I left a piece of cannel coal on his chest. The coal on the chest means the man broke a vow to the rock. The rock has taken him back. That is what it means.*

He pulled my hand to him a hair. He looked at me with the marble eye.

*The coal on the rock means,* he said, *somebody else saw it done.*

He held me on that sentence the way a man holds a child on a sentence. He said it once. He did not repeat it. He did not explain it. He said it, and then he turned his head a small fraction to the wall, and he breathed twice, and he closed his eyes for so long I thought he had gone, and then he opened them and he said, *Now you know. You don't carry it any further. You set it down where it lay. You don't tell anybody, not your mother, not your daddy, not your brother over there, not your Lord. You hold it because it is a thing in our family and a thing in our family is not a thing for anybody outside. You hear me, honey.*

*I hear you, Granddaddy.*

*All right.* He squeezed my hand a quarter pound. *Honey. You'd better go on back to bed.*

I sat with him. I did not go back to bed. I sat with him until five-fifteen when Wade woke up and stretched and said *Dell,* in a voice rough with sleep, and looked at me across the bed, and saw something in my face he could not read, and was too young to ask about, and went out to use the bathroom. While Wade was out my grandfather looked at me again, and he said, *Della. You did good. You sat.* I said *Yes sir.* He closed his eyes. He breathed in. He breathed out. He did not breathe in.

I sat with him for the next twenty minutes by the clock without moving while the body went the way the body goes. I had been at the bedsides of three patients who had died in twelve years of my professional life and I had not, after, been able to remember the exact texture of the going. I remembered his exactly. The chest got smaller. The hand under mine went, by stages over the count of ten minutes, from the temperature of a hand to the temperature of a room. I did not call for my mother. I sat. When Wade came back I told him. He sat down in the chair and put his head in his hands and did not move for a long time.

My mother came down at six. She had not heard him go. I told her. She stood at the foot of the bed and looked at her father, and then she went and washed her face at the kitchen sink before she made any calls.

I drove back to Lexington that afternoon. I was a senior. I had a thesis to defend in three weeks. I packed a small bag and I drove out the Caldwell road and onto KY-92 and out to I-75 and south into a state I had never lived in and a city I had not yet learned to love, and I did not return for the funeral, which was on the Wednesday following, and which I was not at. I was not at it for a reason I have not, in twenty years, given anyone an honest sentence about. The reason was that I could not stand the idea of standing at the grave of a man I had loved my entire life with the sentences he had given me in my mouth, in the presence of his wife's sister and his neighbors and his pastor and his oldest crew and his daughter, and being asked to say *amen* to any words spoken over him while I held the sentences. I drove back four days later, at noon, and stood at the fresh grave alone, and said nothing, and got in the truck and drove back to Lexington again. I graduated summa cum laude six weeks later. I started in on my paramedic certification in the fall. I did not come home again for any reason for two and a half years, until my father overdosed in the parking lot behind the Phillips 66 station, and I did not come home then either.

I filed what my grandfather had told me under *old man's deathbed talk.*

I had filed it carefully. I had built the file with care. I had given the file a shape and a weight and a small dry pleasant-to-the-touch surface in my mind, and I had set the file on a shelf in a closed cabinet in a closed room in a closed wing of the building of my life, and I had locked the wing, and I had walked away.

I had not opened the cabinet for twenty years.

It was open now.

The first sentence — *the coal on the chest means the man broke a vow to the rock; the rock has taken him back* — was in me whole. It had been in me whole at the lip of the tape at Sorrow Hollow this morning. It had risen up out of the leaves before I had a chance to keep it.

The second sentence — *the coal on the rock means somebody else saw it done* — was in me partially. I could see its shape. I could see its border. I could see, in the dim light of the wing of the building I had just unlocked, that there had been more around it. He had said it once. He had not explained it. There had been a small space after it, a beat, the way you set a comma in the air, and in that beat there had been something — a follow-on sentence, a clarification, a small precise word — and I could not, lying on the bedspread of room seven of the Mountain Laurel Motel at three twenty-six in the morning of the fourteenth of October, recover what the something was. I tried. I held the dim of the room and I closed my eyes and I went back to the chair on the side of the bed and the marble-brown eye and the paper hand, and I tried, and the sentence after the *somebody saw it done* was not where I had left it. It had moved. I was not going to find it tonight. I knew this the way I knew, in the cab earlier, that the no in Wade's *no I did not see anybody* was the wrong size. I was not going to find it tonight. I was going to find it.

I lay on the cover until the parking lot started to brighten. I heard the soft thump of Mrs. Profitt putting the biscuit pan on the warmer in the office. I heard the engine of the first county road-crew pickup coming into the lot for the breakfast they were not technically allowed to take from her and that she gave them anyway.

I got up. I put my boots on. I rolled the flannel up under my arm. I picked up the manila folder off the dresser and tucked it under my arm too, unopened. I stood at the window for a minute.

The day was the gray that the day is for the half hour before the sun clears the eastern ridge. The parking lot was filling. The Phillips 66 across the highway had its lights on. The road crews would be on KY-92 for the next twelve hours. The men in them were the cousins and brothers of men I had grown up with, and a few of them — three or four — were men I had grown up with.

I had a quart of buttermilk to buy and three miles to drive. I was going to drive them, this morning, and I was going to my mother's house, and I was going to sleep there tonight in the bed I had left when I was nineteen. I had not decided this. I had been decided to do it by the sentence I had refused to remember at the lip of the tape and could now not unrecover.

I went out. I stopped at the office for a biscuit and Mrs. Profitt looked at my face and said nothing. I ate the biscuit standing. I went out to the truck.

The sun was just clearing the ridge as I turned out onto KY-92, and the light came across the windshield as a bar the same shape as the neon bar had been on the cover of the bed. I drove west the half mile to the IGA. The green truck was not in the lot at six forty-three on a Tuesday morning, and I bought a quart of buttermilk and a small wedge of yellow rat cheese, and I came back to the truck, and I sat with the buttermilk between my knees for a count of three, and I started the engine, and I made the right out of the lot onto KY-92, and then the left onto the Caldwell road, and then the right onto Bear Branch, and the truck took the road up.

---

# Chapter 7 — The Box

I came up the last quarter mile of Bear Branch slowly, in second gear, both hands on the wheel and my jaw set against the gravel. Hollis used to say the county had not so much built the road as agreed in 1957 to stop fighting it.

The Bear Branch Methodist sat to my right at the church bend in the morning light, shut up the way a country church is shut up on a Wednesday morning. Half a mile past it, Verna Hicks was on her porch with her broom; she lifted her hand off it and waved, and I waved back, and I drove on. The wave was the wave you give a person you have unfinished business with that you both know is going to be finished.

The Combs place sits at the very end of Bear Branch where the road becomes a logging trace and then becomes nothing. I came up the last rise and the house was where it had always been. The dogwood at the corner of the porch was bare. The barn was a darker gray than it had been the last time I saw it. A pile of split locust under the kitchen window. A pair of muddy boots upside down on a board over a nail by the step. Wade's work, both of them.

Ladonna was on the porch.

She was in a dress. She was sixty-seven and she was in a dress on a Wednesday morning at quarter to eight at the end of Bear Branch and there is no person reading this who lived in a household with a woman of her generation who does not understand what that meant. The dark blue one with the small white flowers she had worn to my high-school graduation in 2002. Her grandmother's pin at the collar. The brown shoes she walked church in.

She had been in the dress, I understood, since Monday morning.

I parked at the side of the house under the maple I used to climb. I sat in the cab for a count of five. I made myself take a breath. I picked up the quart of buttermilk and the wedge of cheese off the seat next to me and I got out.

She stood at the top of the porch steps with her hand on the rail and watched me come up.

*You made it,* she said.

*I made it.*

*You eat anything.*

*A biscuit from Mrs. Profitt.*

*That is not anything.* She took the buttermilk out of my hand and turned and went in the screen door and held it for me with her hip and said over her shoulder, *Come on.*

I came on.

The kitchen was the kitchen. I knew it was going to be. There is a way the body knows a kitchen even when the body has not been in the kitchen for five years, and the body knows the kitchen by smell and by ceiling height and by the way the floor under the foot of the table dips toward the propane heater. The propane heater was ticking. The woodstove in the front room was banked. The galvanized sink had a single coffee cup and a single small bowl in it from a breakfast Ladonna had eaten alone, and the dishtowel was over the faucet folded twice. The hymnal was on top of the refrigerator.

I clocked it without putting any weight on it. I had been clocking the hymnal on top of the refrigerator since I was four years old. The hymnal had been there since before I was born. It was a small thick book, leather-bound, the leather worn through to the cloth at the spine, with a brown elastic band wound around it once. The title on the spine was faded almost gone but you could read it if you looked: *Cokesbury Worship Hymnal, 1938 Methodist Edition.* It had belonged to Vera's mother. It had belonged to Vera. It had belonged, after Vera, to my mother. I had picked it up in the past — at five, at eleven, at sixteen — and looked through it and put it back. The elastic band, I noticed for the first time at forty-one, was a kind of band you can buy in a packet of ten at any drug store and that holds, when it gives up, a piece of paper inside.

I did not look at the hymnal twice. I set the cheese on the table.

*Sit,* Ladonna said.

I sat. The chair I sat in was my chair — wooden, the color of strong tea, with a rush seat my mother had re-rushed in 2011. I put my hands on the table.

The table was Pap's table. He had built it in 1929 out of the same poplar he had used for the kitchen floor that lay under it now. The top had been refinished four times. The brace underneath had small marks on it from sixty years of feet, including mine. I read the grain with the side of my hand without meaning to. My mother set a cup of coffee in front of me. She set a piece of toast next to the cup. The toast had butter on it. I had said I had eaten.

*Eat the toast,* she said.

I ate the toast.

She sat across from me. She did not pour herself anything. She folded her hands on the table the way she had folded them in church for sixty years, with the right one over the left and her thumb resting flat. The wedding ring of her mother on her right hand, the wedding ring of her own on her left. Small hands. The hands of a woman whose hands had been small since she was nine and had been the hands that had run the substitute-teacher schedule of a county school district for thirty-one years and had laid out a thousand funeral lunches and had at the same time written every check the household had needed written since my father died.

*Wade's arraignment was on Friday,* she said.

*I know.*

*The hearing is on the eighteenth of next month.*

*I know.*

*Nine days,* she said.

*Twenty-eight,* I said. *Today's the fifteenth.*

She tipped her head a half inch toward me. *Twenty-eight by your math,* she said, *but the lawyer-girl told me Friday on the phone we have nine days of useful preparation. After that, it is what it is.*

I did not say anything.

*I have made a list of who can speak for him,* she said. *I have made a second list of who would not. I have made a third list of who would say they would and then not show. I have spent three days on these lists and I would like you to look at them when we get to it, which is not now.*

*Mama.*

*Yes.*

*I am sorry it took me three days to come up this driveway.*

She did not look at me. She looked at the brace of the table.

*Della,* she said. *I am not going to spend the morning making you sorry. We have got too much to do.*

I waited.

She drew in a small breath. *The lists are in the front room. The pastor will speak. Roy Slone — Cal Slone's cousin — was Wade's foreman at the strip site and will speak if Cal asks him, which Cal will. The third one I have not decided. We will decide it. I told the lawyer-girl I would speak. She told me I would be a good witness because I am a mother and because I cannot help being plain. She is right.*

*She is right.*

*I do not like her.*

*Why.*

*She is too thin.*

I laughed once, a short dry one. She did not. The corner of her mouth went up an eighth and she made it stop.

*You will tell me what Wade said this morning at the jail and I will tell you what he said yesterday at the jail to me, and we will compare. He is keeping a thing from both of us. I have not pried because I have raised two children and I know when a man is going to be moved by prying and when he is not. He is in the not. We work around it.*

*Yes.*

*Della.* She lifted her eye to me. *Have you eaten anything that was not a biscuit since Sunday.*

*A piece of cobbler at the Brass Lantern yesterday.*

She did not move for a count of two. Then she stood up.

*I am making you breakfast,* she said. *Do not argue with me.*

She made me breakfast. Bacon, two eggs over medium, toast, grits with butter. She set the plate in front of me and sat down across and watched me eat. She cut her own piece of toast into four and ate it one piece at a time with her left hand. The propane heater ticked. I cleaned the plate.

*All right,* she said. *Now we talk.*

We talked. She told me what Wade had told her at the jail Sunday afternoon, which was almost word for word what he had told me Monday morning except she said that he had cried twice — once when she asked him whether he was eating and once when she told him she had washed his good shirt and would bring it to him for the prelim — and I had not made him cry. I told her what I had been told by Hannah and what I had been told by Sheriff Cornett, and I did not tell her about the second piece of coal on the rock, and I did not tell her about the cabinet in the wing of the building I had opened at three in the morning. Some things are for later in the day. Some things are for not yet.

When we had walked through it she sat back. The clock above the sink — the same clock that had been above the sink since 1979 and had had its batteries changed by Wade approximately every six months — read ten of nine.

*Mama,* I said. *I want to ask you something and I want you to answer me straight.*

*All right.*

*Granddaddy's papers.*

She did not move for a count of three. Then she nodded a small careful nod.

*Where did they go.*

*To the historical society in Pikeville.* Her voice had the careful even register it had had when she had told me at twelve that the dog had not run off but had been hit by a truck on KY-92, the same hour I'd missed the bus. *I gave them to the historical society twelve years ago, Della. They came to me. They were doing a project on the strike. I gave them what I felt was theirs to have. You weren't here to ask.*

*Mama.*

*You weren't here to ask, baby.*

I sat with that. I had not been there to ask. I had been in Knoxville. I had been at the start of a job I would do for twelve years. I had been seven years away from her porch fall, which I had also not been there for. The math of my absence was the math. I let her say it.

*What was in them,* I said.

*His pay stubs from forty-eight to seventy-nine. The notice of the firing in fifty-nine. The arbitration agreement. Three letters from Asa to him from the camp in Tennessee where Asa worked for a summer when they were boys. The deed paperwork on this house — the surface part of it, anyway. A photograph of the union meeting in Hazard in fifty-eight. A photograph of him with the crew on the day they brought up the last car out of Mine Four in seventy-nine. Two handwritten pages of notes on a job he was doing at the church in nineteen sixty.* She paused. *That is what was in them.*

I held her eye.

*That is the file at the historical society,* I said.

*Yes.*

*Was that all of his papers.*

She did not answer the question for the count of about four. She looked at the brace of the table again.

*The file at the historical society,* she said, *is thin.*

She did not look up.

*I see,* I said.

*I did not give them everything.*

*I see.*

*I am not giving you everything today either, Della.*

She said it without any heat. She said it the way a woman tells the truth at her own kitchen table when she has decided in advance that the truth is going to be told one piece at a time and the woman holding the pieces is the one who decides which piece and when. She was not lying. She was withholding. I had not understood the difference until this hour of this morning, sitting at this table with this woman whose hands were folded on the brace mark Pap had cut into the underside in 1929.

*Mama.*

*Della. Honey. There is a hearing in twenty-eight days. There is a lawyer who has to know what she has to know to keep your brother out of prison. There is information I have, and there is information your granddaddy had, and there is information I am keeping for reasons that are mine, and at the right hour I will hand to you what I have, and you will tell me which of it the lawyer-girl needs to know. Until then I am asking you to sit with not knowing.*

*Mama.*

*Della. Please.*

I sat with not knowing.

The clock above the sink ticked. The propane heater ticked. The refrigerator cycled. My mother set her hands flat on the table to indicate the conversation had ended. The hymnal sat. I did not pick it up. I had a small steady understanding that the hymnal was the thing my mother was talking about and that it was on the refrigerator in plain sight where it had been since I was a child, and that I was not, this morning, going to take it down.

*All right,* I said.

*All right,* my mother said.

We sat. The morning came on.

I helped her with the dishes. I sat at the kitchen table most of the afternoon with the manila folder open in front of me and a yellow legal pad my mother brought me out of a drawer in the front room. I read the file through twice. I made a small list of names — Earl Whitt; Sheriff Cornett's father Wendell in the nursing home; the foreman Roy Slone; my cousin Lem, whom I had not seen in nine years. I sat with the list.

My mother made supper. We ate it. She did not ask me what was in the folder. She did not look at it. She had read it once already, I understood, on the day Hannah had given her a copy at the jail; she had read it once and she had decided to read it no more. She sat across from me and ate her soup, and we did not speak much, and the clock ticked, and the hymnal sat.

After supper she said *I have got the bed made up in your old room. Wade has been in there. I put fresh sheets on this morning.* I said *thank you, Mama.* She said *the room is still your room.* I said *it has not been my room in twenty-two years.* She said *it is still your room, Della,* and went into the front room and turned on the television, and the television was a station I did not get in Knoxville and I could hear the volume low through the wall, and I went upstairs.

The back bedroom upstairs was the room I had been a child in. The window looked north out at the pasture and at the high ridge behind it. The bed was the bed I had slept in. The quilt on the bed was the quilt my grandmother had pieced in 1972, which had been Vera's last quilt and had been on this bed since the year I was four. The dresser had Wade's things on it now — a wallet, a pocketknife, a Bible, a pack of throat lozenges, a small framed photograph of my mother and my father and Wade and me on the porch of this house in the spring of 1996. I was eleven in the photograph and Wade was seven and our father was forty and our mother was thirty-eight, and my mother had her hand on the back of my neck, and my father was looking off to the right at something I could not see in the photograph and could not now remember.

I sat on the bed in my clothes. I did not unpack. I did not turn the light on. The light from the hall under the door was enough. The propane heater was clicking down the hall in the kitchen. My mother's bedroom door across the landing showed a strip of light under it. She was in there reading the way she always read at the end of the day, which was sitting up in bed with her glasses and a book and a small glass of water on the nightstand.

I lay down. I did not get under the quilt. I put my hand on the pillow next to me and I left it there.

I thought about the hymnal. I thought about the table downstairs. I thought about the second piece of coal on the rock at Sorrow Hollow that was, this minute, exactly where I had left it, the deputies having missed it again on the count of the four days they had now missed it. I thought about Hannah at the diner saying *no blood means no blood, Della, the press doesn't print the no.* I thought about the second sentence my grandfather had said to me twenty years ago that had a small precise word in it I could not yet remember.

I thought, finally, about my mother in the dress on the porch.

I closed my eyes.

In the morning I was going to call Hannah from the front porch where I had room to walk, and I was going to tell her about the second piece of coal. I was going to drive into town and tell Cornett to send a deputy up to bag it. I had not yet decided what I was going to tell Hannah after the deputy left.

The clock above the sink downstairs struck the hour twice with the small chime my mother had finally turned almost all the way down sometime in the early 2000s. I could just hear it through the floor. Ten o'clock. My mother's bedroom light went out under her door. The propane heater clicked off.

I lay in the dark of the room I had been a child in. The quilt under me smelled like a quilt that had been in a closed bedroom and had been brought out today and put on the bed by a small woman in a dark blue dress with white flowers on it, and I lay on the quilt and I listened to the house, and I did not, for a long time, sleep.

---

# Chapter 8 — Two Pieces of Coal

I called Hannah from the porch at seven minutes to seven, before the kitchen was light. My mother was already up — she had been up since five for fifty years — and she had handed me coffee without speaking. I had taken it out onto the porch and dialed the number Hannah had given me on the back of a Brass Lantern receipt.

She picked up on the third ring. *Della. It is six fifty-three.*

*I know.*

*Are you on the porch.*

*I am on the porch.*

*Are you about to tell me something you should have told me yesterday.*

I did not answer.

*All right,* she said. There was a soft sound on her end like a cupboard closing. *I am in my kitchen in Pikeville. I am pouring a second cup. I am going to put you on speaker. Talk to me.*

I told her. I told her in the order I had walked it, the way I had told her about Wade. I told her the trace, the lip of the tape, the patch of dark in the laurel, the seep at the back, the flat sandstone outcrop east of the body the deputies had walked past six times. I told her the size of the slab and the wipe on the top face and the placement of the piece dead center, square to the long axis, two inches from the high edge and four from the low. I told her cannel coal, three and a half inches across, glossy. I told her placed.

She did not interrupt. When I finished she was quiet for a count.

*Della,* she said.

*Yes.*

*Why are you telling me at six fifty-three on Thursday what you saw at ten forty-six on Monday.*

*Because I did not know what to do with it until last night.*

*Are you telling me you do now.*

*I am telling you I am going to call Cornett at eight when his office opens, and I am going to ask him to send a deputy up the trace to bag it before any more leaves come down on it. I am telling you I want you in the room with me at the Brass Lantern at six tonight because I need to tell you the rest.*

*The rest.*

*Yes.*

She breathed in once.

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*Is the rest the thing that makes you the only person between Knoxville and the courthouse who can put a piece of coal on a piece of paper in front of a magistrate and have him understand what it means.*

I did not answer for a second longer than I should have.

*Yes,* I said.

*All right,* she said. *Six. The Brass Lantern. I will be in the back booth.*

*Six.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*I am not going to ask you on the phone what the rest is. I am going to ask you in person across a piece of cobbler. Do not eat between now and then. I am going to need you a little hungry.*

She hung up.

I sat on the porch with the coffee. The light had come up over the high ridge. The pasture had a faint white on the grass that was not frost yet but was the dew that frost grows out of. The crows up the slope had started in.

I had a hymn in my head. I had not put it there. *Just a closer walk with thee.* I knew the first three lines. The fourth was not in me. I let it go.

My mother came out at twenty after seven with her own coffee in her hand and stood at the rail and did not look at me. *You been on the phone,* she said.

*Yes ma'am.*

*With the lawyer-girl.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*All right.*

She did not ask what about. She stood at the rail and drank her coffee and looked down toward the church-bend in the road for the count of about three minutes, and then she said *come in and eat,* and went in. I sat a minute longer. The cup in my hand had cooled. I got up and went in.

I called Cornett at eight straight up. I called him from the kitchen. My mother was at the sink and did not turn around. He answered on the second ring, in his slow voice. *Combs.* I told him. I told him without preamble. I gave him the location of the slab — *ten feet east of the cleared circle, the slab with the corner sticking up on the south side, the one your boys walked around six times by my count* — and the size of the piece and the shape of it and the placement and the wipe. I told him I had not crossed the tape and I had not touched the piece. I told him the cleared circle on the duff was big enough to make the slab look outside the scene, but the slab was inside the scene by any reasonable read. I told him he should bag it today.

He did not say anything for a count of three.

*All right,* he said. *I am sending Pickett. He will be at the trace head by ten. Della.*

*Yes.*

*You walked into a sealed scene.*

*The tape was sagging. I did not cross it. I looked.*

*You walked into a sealed scene.*

*Avery.*

He breathed in. *You looked, and you did not cross. I hear what you said. I am going to tell Pickett the call came in anonymous because I do not want a paper trail with your name on it on this piece of evidence. The piece is going into the bag as a found-by-deputy on a re-walk of the scene. Do you understand me.*

*I understand you.*

*Do not tell the lawyer-girl what I just said.*

*I will not.*

*I will call you when it is bagged.*

He hung up.

I stood at the kitchen table holding the phone. My mother had not turned from the sink. She had heard the call. She did not say anything. I set the phone on the table.

*Mama.*

*Yes.*

*I am going to be in town a while today.*

*All right.*

*I will be back for supper.*

*All right.*

I went up the back stair and washed my face and brushed my teeth and put on the same clothes I had been in for two days and came back down. My mother handed me a paper sack with two biscuits and a small piece of yellow cheese wrapped in foil. *Eat that around eleven,* she said.

*Hannah said I should be hungry tonight.*

*Eat the biscuits anyway.*

I drove down Bear Branch to town with the manila folder on the seat. I parked at the courthouse and went down to Cornett's basement office through the front this time. Cornett looked up and pushed across the desk a single Polaroid of a flat slab of sandstone in a bed of leaves with a piece of glossy cannel coal on the top face. *Pickett took this twenty minutes ago,* he said. *He's bagging it now. Chain-of-custody filled in by noon. The lawyer-girl will get her copy in discovery this afternoon. Do not be the person who walks her through it. Let her find it.*

*All right.*

*Anything else.*

*Your daddy in Williamsburg.*

He held my eye.

*If I came down there and asked him about the missing-persons file from sixty-one. Would he talk to me.*

He considered me a long moment. *I would call ahead. He may not have a long window with you. I would give it twenty minutes. I would write down what he says with a pen in your hand because he is going to say it once. That is for later. You are going to wait until after the prelim if you can stand to.*

I nodded.

*Go,* he said.

I went.

I had nine hours before Hannah. I sat in the truck in the lot and ate one of the biscuits my mother had packed. I went up to my mother's and read the file twice. I made the list. *Earl Whitt. Roy Slone. Wendell Cornett. Tully Renfro's daughter Sara if Hannah will let me.* I added *the foreman Eldon Hicks at the strip site* because Tully's job had touched the strip operation. I did not weight the name. I would not, for four weeks, know the weight.

I sat at the kitchen table at four with the list and a second cup of coffee. The hymnal sat on the refrigerator. My mother sat across from me grading the choir's roster for the Sunday service. She did not look at the list. I did not show it to her.

At five thirty I drove back into town. I parked at the Brass Lantern at three to six. Hannah was already in the back booth. Two waters on the table. Cobbler on saucer. Coffee.

*Eat the cobbler,* she said.

I sat.

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*Tell me about the second piece of coal,* she said. *Pickett bagged a piece of cannel coal at ten forty this morning on a re-walk of the scene. It came over the wire on my discovery feed at three. I did not call you. I figured you would tell me.*

*I told Cornett at eight.*

*I figured you told Cornett at eight.*

*Hannah.*

*Yes.*

I took a breath. I had been holding the breath since about two thirty.

*I knew the coal on the chest before you told me yesterday,* I said. *I knew the meaning of it before you told me there had been one. I knew it because my grandfather Hollis Combs told me, on his deathbed in April of two thousand five, that he had killed a man named Calvin Tull in Sorrow Hollow in July of nineteen sixty-one and had placed a piece of cannel coal on the man's chest as a sign. He told me what the sign meant. He told me there was a second sign — a second piece of coal, placed on a flat rock at the scene — which meant somebody had witnessed the act. He told me to keep what he had told me and not pass it. I have kept it for twenty years. I have not, in any conversation with any person, named what he told me. I am naming it to you now because the killer of Tully Renfro left both signs. The killer of Tully Renfro knew the signature. The killer of Tully Renfro could only know it if he learned it from somebody Hollis told before he died, or from somebody Hollis told *after* I think he told me, or from somebody Hollis told *that I do not know about,* or because my grandfather told the truth less narrowly than he wanted me to think he had.*

I stopped.

Hannah did not move.

I had said the speech in one breath. The booth around us had not changed. Lily was at the counter taking an order from a man in a yellow hi-vis vest. The framed basketball team looked at nothing on the opposite wall. The fluorescent above us flickered the small flicker it always flickered.

Hannah picked up her coffee. She drank a quarter of it. She set it down. She put her hands flat on the table on either side of the saucer.

*Della,* she said.

*Yes.*

*That is either the thing that saves your brother,* she said, *or the thing that ends him.*

I did not answer.

*Because either,* she said, *the man who killed Tully Renfro is a man your grandfather told. In which case the chain of who knew that signature is a finite list and we walk it down and we find him. Or your grandfather did not kill Calvin Tull either, in which case the chain of who actually did is a finite list and we walk it down. Or your grandfather killed Calvin Tull and also told somebody and that somebody passed it down a line your family has not been tracking. Or the man who killed Tully Renfro is a man your grandfather did not tell directly but who heard it from a man your grandfather told. In all of these cases we have a list. In all of these cases your brother is sitting in a jail four blocks from this booth for a crime he did not commit, because the killer made him look right by leaving a signature that matched a fifty-something-year-old killing the killer thinks is going to scare anybody off who recognizes it.*

*I did not recognize the signature on the body until you told me,* I said. *I recognized the signature on the rock the second I saw it.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*Who else might your grandfather have told.*

I did not answer.

*I am not asking you a rhetorical question.*

*I do not know,* I said. *That is the truthful answer. I do not know. He told me he was telling me alone. He told me to keep it and pass it to nobody. He was a man whose word I would have trusted with my own breath. He may have told one other person before he died. He may have told two. I do not know who.*

*All right.*

*The first person I would ask is my mother.*

*Have you asked her.*

*Not in those words. I have asked her for his papers. She has told me she kept things back. She is keeping them back for reasons of her own. She will not give them to me today.*

*She will give them to you.*

*She will give them to me on her own time.*

Hannah nodded. She picked up the pen and the legal pad and wrote, in her small careful hand, three lines I could not read upside down.

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*I am going to need this in writing.*

*The deathbed.*

*Yes.*

*All right.*

*Not as an affidavit yet. As a memo to my files. I am going to draft it tonight and I am going to send it to you tomorrow and you are going to read it and tell me if every word in it is the way you remember it. If you have to change one word we change it. We do not change two words and then a third and then a fourth. We get the memo right the first time. Do you understand.*

*I do.*

*I am also going to draft a second memo about the chain of custody on the second piece of coal that has Pickett finding it on a re-walk. That memo and your memo do not touch. They are in two different folders in two different cabinets in two different rooms in my life. Do you understand me.*

*I do.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*One more.*

*Yes.*

She set the pen down.

*Why did your grandfather tell you and not your mother.*

I had been waiting for her to ask it because I had been asking myself the same question on the porch this morning while I tried to find the fourth line of a hymn I had not been able to find.

*I do not know,* I said. *I have not let myself ask. I am going to have to ask.*

*Ask,* Hannah said.

*I am going to.*

*All right.* She slid the pad and the pen into the accordion. She closed the accordion. She drank the rest of her coffee. *Eat the cobbler,* she said.

I ate the cobbler. I tasted it. It was peach. There was a small lattice of crust on top that had been cut with a butter knife by a woman in the back who had cut crust on cobbler four mornings a week for thirty years. I ate the cobbler in seven bites. Hannah watched me eat without weighting the watching.

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*You are not flying back to Knoxville on Friday.*

*No.*

*You are going to call your captain tonight and ask for indefinite leave.*

*Yes.*

*I am glad.*

*I will call him after I get back to my mother's.*

She nodded.

We did not say much else. She paid the bill. She left a tip on the table that was larger than the bill. We walked out together into a parking lot that had gone full dark while we sat. The lights along KY-92 had come on. A coal truck rolled through the second stoplight running empty, the bed clean, on its way back to the strip site for the morning load. The smell of diesel sat in the air the way it sits in the air in any small town that has a road through it.

Hannah stopped at her car. The 2014 Subaru. She turned and looked at me a moment.

*Whatever else this is going to be,* she said, *I am glad you are here, Della.*

*I am too,* I said.

She got in. She drove off. I stood in the lot for the count of about a minute.

I drove out Bear Branch in the dark. I passed Verna's house with its porch light on. I climbed the last rise and came up over it and the house was where it had been.

The porch light was on. The kitchen light was on. I parked. I sat in the cab with the engine off for the count of ten. Through the kitchen window I could see, lit from inside, the kitchen table and the propane heater and the galvanized sink and the refrigerator and the hymnal on top of the refrigerator. The hymnal in the warm yellow light of the kitchen looked like a thing that had been waiting on top of that refrigerator a long time and was prepared to wait longer.

I made the call from the cab. Heffern picked up on the second ring. I told him I needed indefinite leave. He did not argue. He said *take what you need, Combs. Call me when you know.* I hung up.

The kitchen window held the hymnal in the warm yellow.

I got out of the truck. I went up the porch steps. My mother was in the kitchen at the table with a cup of tea and the Sunday-school book open in front of her, and she did not look up. The hymnal sat on the refrigerator in the warm yellow light.

I came in. I closed the screen door behind me with my heel so it would not slam. I sat down at the table across from her and put my hands flat on the brace mark Pap had cut into the wood in 1929.

*Mama,* I said. *I am going to be here a while.*

*I know,* she said.

She did not look up from her book.

I sat at the table.

---

# Chapter 9 — The Land Man

Hannah's office was on the third floor of the old Citizens Bank building on Main in Pikeville, and the elevator did not work. The stairwell smelled of paint that had not dried since the Carter administration. I came up it on the Friday morning that closes the first week, with two coffees from the gas station on the corner and a yellow legal pad I had bought the day before at the drugstore in Caldwell because I wanted my own pen on my own paper for what we were about to do.

The door said *H. SOYARS, ESQ.* in stick-on letters and then, underneath, in the same letters, *PUBLIC DEFENDER — ENTER.* Hannah had once told me the *ENTER* was for the man across the hall who collected court-mandated child support and tended to lock his own door against people who needed him. She had stuck her own *ENTER* up so it would be visible from the stairs. *It costs me five letters,* she had said. *It is the cheapest piece of practice I do.*

She opened the door barefoot, in jeans and a UK sweatshirt, hair up with a pencil through it. Behind her on the long folding table she used as a desk, three banker's boxes stood open with their lids on the floor. *Della,* she said. *You bring coffee or you bring breakfast.*

*Coffee.*

*Then you sit there.* She pointed at the metal folding chair on the visitor's side. *And we work.*

I sat. I gave her the cup. She had already set up what I had come for. On the table to her right, in a stack as neat as a deck of cards, were six file folders, manila, labeled in her handwriting in Sharpie: *RENFRO 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6.* On top, a one-page printout I recognized as a copy of the company's public-facing organizational chart. Tully Renfro's photograph occupied a small box near the bottom of the second tier. He looked, in the photograph, like a man who had ordered a club sandwich without irony and asked the waitress how her son was doing in school. He had a wide, sun-burnt face. He had the kind of mustache a man grows when his wife stops asking. He had been alive when the photograph was taken and was now twelve days dead.

*Subpoena pulled six files off his desk,* Hannah said. *His desk at corporate, not his truck. Corporate keeps a cleaner trail.* She tapped *RENFRO 1.* *He had twenty-three years at Blackridge, mostly in lease acquisition and what they call title clearance. Last six years, this was his case load. Six open files at the time of death. Five in Caldwell. One in Pike.* She slid the top folder across. *Read this one first.*

I read it.

The folder was an inch thick. The first document was a one-page summary in the company's letterhead, dated August 1, 2025, on stationery the color of weak tea. *RENFRO, T. — Field Acquisition Report. Property: Combs (Bear Branch). Status: open, refused. Recommended action: subsidence claim filing per 1907 broad-form deed, projected Q1 2026.*

I read it again. I read it a third time. I set down my pen.

*Hannah.*

*I know.*

*My mother.*

*I know.*

I made my hands flat on the table. Twelve years on the job had taught me what to do with my hands when something arrived I did not yet have a place for. You put them flat, you put them visible, you make them quiet. It is what you do when a man's wife is screaming in the kitchen behind you and you are about to put your fingers on his neck to find the pulse.

*Della,* Hannah said. She was watching me the way she watched her older brother in old photographs, in the way I had learned to read in a week of working with her — alert without intervention, ready without crowding. *Take it at your own speed.*

I took it. I read the next page. It was a printed visit log. The first entry was from October of 2024. *Visit 1: subject (L. Combs, owner of record) declined to invite agent inside. Discussion held on porch. Subject stated, "I don't sign anything without my children." Agent left card.* The second entry was three weeks later. *Visit 2: subject (L. Combs) repeated prior position. Agent offered $14,000 against future royalties; declined. Subject stated, "It is not for sale."* The visits went on. November. January. March. May. July. *Visit 8: subject (L. Combs) requested agent leave property. Agent complied. Subject was overheard, departing porch, to say "the Lord don't bargain for what isn't his." Agent has noted possible religious objection to lease release; recommend continued seasonal contact.*

The visit log had nine entries on the Bear Branch file. The most recent was September 22, 2025 — thirteen days before the killing.

*Visit 9: subject (L. Combs) opened the door with a shotgun broken over her arm. No threat made. Agent left card. Subject stated only "you'll have to come back when I am not here."*

I read that one twice.

*That's my mother,* I said. *That is the woman who taught me to write thank-you notes.*

*That is the woman who knew exactly the thing to do with a shotgun in the crook of her elbow,* Hannah said. *Not aimed. Not loaded, probably. But broken over. The country way to say *no* without giving you a reason to call anybody.*

I looked at Hannah. She was watching the page upside down. She had read the file twice before I came in. She was reading me on top of the file now.

*She didn't tell me,* I said.

*No.*

*She didn't tell me he had been at the house nine times in a year.*

*No.*

*She didn't tell me she had stood on the porch with a shotgun broken over her arm in September.*

*No.* Hannah took her own coffee in both hands. *Della. I don't have a daughter. But I have known mothers. There is a thing mothers do where they take the part of the harm they think they can absorb in their own bodies, and they do not pass it on to the child. They believe — sometimes correctly — that the child has their own weight to carry. Your mother was carrying this one. She decided you did not need to know.*

*She decided wrong.*

*She made a choice. You can rule on it after.*

I went back to the file. I made myself slow down. I had been at the wreck on the Camry seven days ago and on the eve of this work I could feel, in a kind of low-level professional way, the part of me that had been a paramedic for twelve years arrive in the chair beside me — the one that has learned never to skip the secondary survey. You don't fix the obvious break and walk away from the head wound. You look at all of it. You look at all of it twice.

I looked at all of it twice.

The Combs file had a second pocket inside it. Hannah had not opened it for me, on purpose. I opened it. It contained a draft of a document on company letterhead, unsigned and undated, headed *NOTICE OF SUBSIDENCE CLAIM AND INTENT TO ENJOIN.* Three pages. Standard form. The fourth page was a hand-drawn site map of the Combs property and the four properties immediately adjacent. Two of those adjacent parcels were Hicks. The hand that had drawn the map was small and careful and had used a pencil first and then gone over the lines with what looked like a fountain pen.

I held the map up to the light. Under the pencil and ink, the paper was Blackridge-watermarked. At the bottom right, in a small box no bigger than a thumbnail, in faded type the new ink had been laid over without quite covering, was a stamped notation: *PRIOR FILING NO. 0317, 7/61. RETIRED.* The watermark was the same the company had been using since the Hoover administration.

*Hannah.*

*Yes.*

*The properties next to ours. The Hicks places. They had been about to file on those too.*

*The day Tully was killed,* Hannah said quietly, *he was carrying this file in his truck. Cornett's deputies inventoried his truck the day after Earl Whitt found him. The file was not there.*

I lowered the map. I set it on the desk.

*The killer took the file.*

*Or moved it,* she said. *Or burned it. Or it never left his desk in the first place and the truck inventory is just an inventory of what was in a truck. But the file is here, on this desk, because Cornett's evidence officer pulled it off Tully's office computer as a backup and printed it. The original paper is presumed missing.*

I looked at her. She was looking back at me the way an emergency-room attending looks at a paramedic who has just brought in a patient that does not quite match the report — not skeptical, just willing to wait while you talk it through.

*All right,* I said. *So. Six open files. One of them is Combs. Two of them are Hicks-adjacent. What are the other three.*

She slid them across. I read them.

The other three were spread across the county. The Boyles place at the head of Tinker Branch — a woman who had been holding out for two generations, fighting on the same 1907 deed, who had buried a brother killed in Mine 4 in '59 alongside Asa Combs and who had not signed anything for anyone in sixty-six years. The Whitt place — Earl Whitt's older sister's farm, three coves over from us, with timber and a sycamore stand that had been left standing through two clear-cuts. The Profitt place — and yes, Mrs. Profitt of the Mountain Laurel Motel, who had inherited a hundred and eight acres on the back side of Honaker Ridge from her father-in-law and who, when Tully Renfro had asked her three years ago about a lease release, had told him to take his name off her motel register. *Not a customer,* she had written on the back of the registration card. *Won't be served.*

I read all six.

The pattern was in the geography. I drew it for myself, on the legal pad, with my pen. The Bear Branch holler. The Tinker Branch drainage. The Honaker Ridge back side. The cove behind Whitt's. The two Hicks-adjacent parcels. The Pike County file was the outlier and looked, on its face, like cover.

The five Caldwell County properties together formed a near-continuous strip up the spine of the ridge — five private holdings whose mineral-rights releases, taken together, would clear about eleven hundred acres for stripping. Whoever had drawn the hand map on the Combs file had drawn the same shape, with the same boundary lines, with the same pencil.

*Hannah,* I said. *This is one operation. Not six files. One operation.*

*Yes,* she said.

*And my mother is sitting on the cornerstone parcel.*

*Yes.*

*And Tully Renfro was killed two weeks before he was supposed to file.*

*Yes.*

I sat back. I drank some of the coffee. It was the kind of coffee a gas station serves to a paramedic at three in the morning and to a lawyer at nine. It was not the point. The point was that there were five families who had reason to want Tully Renfro dead, six counting the Combses on their own. The Commonwealth's attorney had picked the Combses because we were the family with a brother on a trail-cam two hours out from the body and a hammer in a truck bed. The other four families had quieter trail-cams that day and no hammers in any trucks they would admit to.

*Hannah,* I said. *Wade had a motive.*

*Yes.*

*My mother had a motive.*

*Yes.*

*I have a motive.*

*Della.* She did not look away. *You were on a wreck in Tennessee at the time. You have one of the cleanest alibis a working paramedic ever gets given by God. But yes. In the logical sense. You also.*

I made a note on the legal pad. *Combs motive: structural. Five families share it. Commonwealth chose us.* I underlined *structural.* I crossed it out. I wrote *kin-protective.* I looked at the word for a long second. Then I went on.

*Tully Renfro,* I said. *Who was he.*

Hannah turned over the photograph on the org chart and put it face down. *He was a man,* she said. *He had a daughter in Pineville. He had been divorced four years. He had a hunting cabin on the New River in West Virginia and went there twice a year for whitetail and once a year by himself in February when he could not sleep. His employees liked him better than his supervisors did. He had a long file of internal memos saying he was sometimes too quick to back off a contested file — a thing his bosses considered a weakness. He had been told twice this year to stop sending Christmas cards to the families on his open files. He was, the people who knew him said, not cruel. He was a man who had developed a habit of treating very hard things as if they were not hard, and that had begun to wear his face down.* She took her hand off the org chart. *He did not deserve to be killed.*

*I did not say he did,* I said.

*I am saying it for me.* She looked tired. *I have to say it out loud for me. I am defending the only Combs the Commonwealth has named, and I am about to spend three weeks arguing that five other families had structural motive too, and I want my own voice on the record before I do it that I do not think any of them deserved to do it to him. I do not. He was a person.*

*All right,* I said. *Yes. He was a person.*

*Good.*

I went back to the Combs file. I looked at the visit log again. I looked at the *Lord don't bargain for what isn't his.* I had not heard my mother say those words. I had heard her say things like them. I had heard her say, when I was nine and had asked her why we did not sell the back pasture to the man who came up the road in a Cadillac one Tuesday, *Della. There are some things in this life a person doesn't put on the market. The Lord didn't put a price tag on them and I'm not going to.* I had thought, at nine, that this was a thing she said because she had not been to college. I understood now, at forty-one, that it was a thing she said because she had been listening, all her life, to her own mother. And to her mother's mother. And to the women on the porches up Bear Branch who had taught her how to mean a sentence.

I closed the Combs file.

I looked at Hannah.

*She didn't tell me,* I said again, because I wanted to hear myself say it one more time.

*She didn't tell you because she did not believe you would come back over a piece of paper,* Hannah said. *Or she did not believe you would come back at all. Or she wanted to keep the line clean between the daughter who left and the woman who had to stand on the porch with the shotgun. I don't know what she was thinking. I am not going to make it up for you. You are going to have to ask her.*

*Yes,* I said. *I am.*

I picked up the legal pad. I wrote, in my run-report hand at the top of a fresh page, *Combs (Bear Branch). Owner of record: L. Combs. Surface only. Mineral subject to 1907 broad-form deed, Blackridge Coal Holdings. Active subsidence claim drafted, unsigned, undated, undelivered. Victim's last visit September 22, 2025. Subject opened the door with a shotgun broken over arm. Daughter of the subject — me — was unaware of any of the above as of October 17, 2025.* I underlined *unaware.*

Then I wrote, in my own hand and not my run-report hand, *Why didn't she tell me.*

I did not underline that one.

I looked up. Hannah had picked up the Combs file. She was holding it at the edges, the way you hold a thing you do not want your fingerprints on more than they already are. She was reading the company's last note on the cover sheet, where someone in the home office had penciled a small block-letter comment in the margin: *COMBS — keep pressure on. Old woman. Will fold by winter.*

I asked her to read it aloud.

She did.

*Old woman,* she read. *Will fold by winter.*

She closed the manila cover.

We sat in the room a while. Through the wall I could hear the man who ran the support-collection office talking on the phone in a low patient voice to somebody who was crying. Outside, the courthouse clock on Main struck eleven. Hannah did not move. I did not move. The file lay between us on the third-floor desk with the word *Combs* on the spine in her Sharpie hand, sitting there like a piece of coal somebody had set down deliberate on a flat rock, the way they used to do up the holler when they wanted you to know they had seen you, and had decided what to do about it later.

---

# Chapter 10 — The First Witness

Earl Whitt lived at the head of Tinker Branch in a single-wide trailer set crossways on its lot the way trailers get set on lots whose owners cared more about the view than the road. The view was the head of the holler — a stand of tulip poplar climbing east up the slope into a notch where the sky in October was the cold clean blue of well water held up to a window. The road was a quarter-mile of gravel that had been graded since Carter and not since. I parked Hannah's Subaru behind his pickup because mine had a Tennessee plate and there was no reason to ride in with a Tennessee plate when a Kentucky one would do.

It had rained in the night and the air still smelled of it. A cardinal called twice from the chinkapin at the fence corner. The grass at the edge of the gravel pad was beat down in a path to a bird feeder shaped like a small church. Earl had filled it that morning; there was a smear of dark sunflower seed on the splash board.

He opened the door before I knocked, which was the country way to tell a person you had been watching them come up the road and were not ashamed of it. He was a tall man on the way to being a stooped one — six foot two becoming six foot, gray hair cut by a wife and parted on the same line for forty years, eyes that had spent enough time underground to have a permanent narrow set to them even now, in the morning sun. He wore a button-up shirt the color of weak tea and brown work pants and slippers.

*You're Hollis Combs's grand,* he said.

*I am, Mr. Whitt.*

*Earl.* He held the door open. *Come on in. Lou's gone to her sister's for the morning. The coffee is the kind you make in a Mr. Coffee. You want some.*

*Please.*

The kitchen was a galley off a small front room with a propane stove and a rocker recliner and a television playing the morning news with the sound off. There was a Bible on the side table beside the recliner, open to Job, with a folded church bulletin holding the place. A radio on the kitchen counter was on at a low whisper, tuned to the AM gospel station out of Hazard. *I'll Fly Away* was playing, slow, the way the old congregations do it. Earl did not turn it off. I did not ask him to. He poured two mugs of coffee — Pyrex, with the chip you get from a thousand dish drainings — and set them down on the small table between us. He sat. I sat.

*Becca Couch said you'd be coming,* he said.

*Becca's a friend.*

*She is my niece. Her mama and my wife were sisters. *I hadn't put it together. Becca's hug at the jail door rearranged itself in my memory in the small way these things rearrange themselves when you are in a place where everybody is some kin of everybody else and you are the one who left and forgot the lines. Earl did not make me work for the connection. He let it sit on the table and moved past it. *I will tell you what I told the sheriff,* he said. *And I will tell you the part I did not know to tell him that morning and have thought of since.*

*All right.*

*All right.*

He took a careful drink. He set the mug down with both hands. He kept his hands on the mug.

*I take Bird out of a morning,* he said. *That's the dog. Beagle, twelve years old, deaf in the right ear, blind in the left eye, the only animal I have left that has earned the privilege of being slow. We go up the trace on Tuesdays and Fridays of an old habit. Tuesday the seventh I had her up to the mouth of Sorrow Hollow by seven. There was frost on the laurel.* He paused. *You know the place.*

*I do.*

*Then I won't paint it for you. I will tell you only the things that mattered.*

*Yes, sir.*

*I came up the trace,* he said. *And before I came to the flat I saw a truck parked at the turnout where the trace bottoms out by the slip rock. It was a green Ford F-250, late nineties, with a Blackridge sticker on the rear window. The truck was running. There was no one in it.*

I had taken out my pad. I was writing. I stopped.

*Earl,* I said. *The truck was running. With no one in it.*

*The truck was running. With no one in it.*

I wrote that down. I wrote it twice.

*I made Bird sit,* he said. *I did not call out. I have lived long enough to know what a running truck and no driver means in a holler off this road, and it does not always mean anything good. I waited. I listened. I did not hear anybody up the trace toward the flat. I did not hear anybody coming down. I stood there with Bird at heel for a count I would put at four minutes by the way the cold was getting at the tops of my ears. Then I heard a door slam up on the flat — a tailgate, I'd say now, but at the time I thought it was a cab door — and then I heard the truck door of the F-250 open and close, and then I heard the engine put in gear and the F-250 backed out of the turnout and went down the trace and was gone.*

*You did not see who got in.*

*I did not. The turnout's around the bend from where I was standing.*

*Was it one man or more.*

*One door slam. One man, would be my guess.*

I wrote *one man* and then I wrote *guess.*

*And then you went up.*

*And then I went up.* He shifted in the chair. His hands stayed on the Pyrex mug. *Bird went up ahead of me. She is old enough she does not chase but she is still a beagle and she will tell you, by the way she puts her nose, that there is a thing she does not want to put her mouth on. She did that. I came around the slip rock and onto the flat and I saw Tully Renfro.*

He stopped. He did not look down. He looked, for the first time since I had come in, directly at me — the look of a man who knows the next thing he is going to say will lodge in a person and who wants to make sure he gives it to her in the way it will lodge with the least harm.

*He was on his back, Della. He was on his back ten feet east of the seep, on a patch of beat-down laurel. His head was turned to the right and there was a piece of cannel coal on his chest, the size of an apple, set there on purpose, polished on the outer face. His hands were at his sides. Whoever had laid them at his sides had laid them careful. His eyes were open. The crows had not been at him yet, which means he had not been there long, or he had been there long enough that the morning had not yet come up enough for them. I knelt down because I have been the man who finds the body twice before in my life and you make sure first that the body is a body. He was a body. He had been one a while. The blood at the temple had dried.*

He stopped again. He took a drink of the coffee. The hand he lifted it with shook once and steadied.

*I closed his eyes,* he said. *I know the sheriff don't like you to touch a scene. But I closed his eyes. I do not know that man Tully Renfro. I knew him to see at the IGA. He had a daughter, somebody told me once. I closed his eyes because I was not going to leave him laying there with the morning sun coming through the laurel and his eyes open. I will answer for that when I have to.*

*Earl,* I said. *You don't have to answer for that to me.*

*I know I don't. I am telling you anyway.*

I wrote *closed eyes — Earl* on the pad. I underlined it. I did it because I wanted to remember, when this was over, the part of the morning where a seventy-one-year-old retired union mechanic had refused to leave a stranger uncovered.

*And then,* I said.

*And then I walked Bird back down the trace,* he said. *I drove home. I called Avery Cornett from this telephone here on the wall. I told him what I had seen. He came up with a deputy and two of the EMTs from Pineville and I rode with them back up. I showed them the spot. I gave my statement to the deputy in the front seat of the cruiser. I went home. That was Tuesday morning, and from Tuesday to Friday I did not go up that road. On Friday they came and got me at the IGA — they had a question about the way the body was lying. I went up with them again. I stood on the trace this time and they walked it. They did not need me up at the flat the second time. They needed me to confirm the spot.*

I let him drink. He drank. I let him sit. He sat.

*The thing I did not tell the sheriff,* he said.

*Yes.*

*Two things. I am going to tell you the smaller one first.*

*All right.*

*The cannel coal on his chest,* he said. *I did not pick it up. I did not move it. I would not have. But I will tell you. I have been a mineworker thirty-eight years in this county, and I have seen a great deal of cannel coal in my time, and that piece on his chest was not cannel from McCreary. It was cannel from the Pond Creek seam. Pond Creek runs through the Hicks property up Bear Branch. You know that.*

I had not known it. I wrote it down. *Pond Creek seam. Hicks property.*

*That is the smaller thing,* he said. *The bigger one I have thought about every morning since.*

*Earl.*

*I will say it plain,* he said. *Whoever killed that man knew the hollow well enough to walk in and out without being seen by anybody on the trace below, and knew the work well enough to put coal on a chest like he meant it, and was patient enough to wait there for the man he was meeting and not get spooked by a running truck at the bottom. That is not a stranger to that holler. That is somebody who has been up there a great many times in his life. There are not many people in this county who that describes. I am one. Avery Cornett is one. Your brother is one, because Hollis took him up there as a boy. Your cousin Lem is one. You are one, Della. Don't think you're not. There would be six or seven others, most of them my age, most of them not up to climbing the trace anymore. The rest would all be Combses or Hickses or Slones.*

He looked at me a long moment.

*I do not have a name for you,* he said. *I have only the shape of the room.*

*Earl,* I said. *The truck. The green Ford. You said Blackridge sticker.*

*Blackridge sticker on the rear window. The big oval kind. The new design they have been using since 2018 or so. Whoever drove that truck off the trace was a man with company business at his back.*

*Caudill Boggs drives a truck that fits that description.*

*Caudill Boggs drives that truck,* Earl said. *Or one of three trucks that look like it. The company has a small fleet of them. I am not naming Caudill to you. I am telling you what I saw.*

*Yes, sir.*

He took another long drink of the coffee. He set the mug down. He sat back in the chair. *I have to ask you a thing now,* he said.

*Yes.*

*Your granddaddy.*

*Yes.*

*I worked under Hollis Combs in '76. I was twenty-two. I had been on the surface six weeks and they sent me down to the McCreary section as a low-voltage mechanic because the man they had on it had bent his back. I was scared. I was not going to tell anybody I was scared. Your granddaddy met me at the gate the first morning and he walked me down and he did not say a word about being scared. He talked to me about the wire. He talked to me about the rock above the wire. He talked to me about where to set my feet. He did this for two weeks of mornings.* He paused. *Your granddaddy was the only foreman I ever had who'd put his own body between you and the rock. That don't sound like much. It is.*

I wrote that down. I wrote it slow. *Body between you and the rock.* I did not underline it. I did not have to.

*I have wanted to tell that to somebody in your family for a long time,* he said. *I never came up the holler to do it. I am telling you now because you are sitting here. Your brother is in the jail and they have got the wrong man in there, and you have got the right name on you to be the one who fixes it. I do not know whether you can. I am telling you what your granddaddy was to me in 1976 because you should not have to walk into this without that knowledge in you. He was a good man. He was a hard one. He would have put his body between Wade and the rock if he was here to do it. He is not. You are. That is what I came to say.*

I put my pen down. I did not say anything for a while. *I'll Fly Away* had ended on the radio and a quieter hymn had started, and I did not know its name, and I did not turn to look. Earl did not move. The cardinal at the feeder called twice more from the chinkapin.

*Earl,* I said. *Thank you.*

*Don't thank me. Drink the coffee.*

I drank the coffee.

When I stood up to go, he stood up with me. He walked me to the door. At the door he put his hand in his shirt pocket and brought out something he had been carrying, I think, since he had first heard I was coming up the road — small, dark, polished. He took my right hand in his left and pressed it into my palm and closed my fingers over it.

*That,* he said, *is a piece of Pond Creek seam cannel I have had on my workbench since 1979. I gave one to my boy when he turned ten and I gave one to my grandson when he made Eagle Scout and this is the last one I held back for myself. I am giving it to you because I told you a thing five minutes ago about the coal on Tully Renfro's chest, and I want you to know by your own hand and your own eye what I was talking about. There is no Pond Creek cannel in McCreary and there is no McCreary cannel in Pond Creek and once you have held the one in your hand you will know the other on sight. I do not want you taking my word for it without the thing in your hand.*

I opened my hand. The coal lay in my palm. It was clean. It had been polished by his thumb. It was lighter than ordinary bituminous and the outer face had a quiet shine to it that did not look like the shine you see in a museum case. It was set on my palm like a small black egg.

*Earl,* I said. *This is yours.*
