*It was,* he said. *It is yours now. Go look in the photographs in your sheriff's file when you go in next, and you will see the piece on his chest, and you will know it. That is all I wanted you to have. The rest of it I have told you.*

He closed my fingers over it a second time, gentle, as a man closes the eyes of a stranger he has found in the laurel. He let go of my hand. He held the door for me. I went down the steps to the Subaru with the coal in my fist and the cardinal calling once more behind me from the chinkapin at the corner of the lot, and I did not open my hand again until I was back on the blacktop with the engine running and the heater on, and even then I held it up to the windshield and looked at it for a long minute before I put it carefully in the breast pocket of my flannel and drove down the holler with one hand on the wheel and one over the pocket, the way you carry, with the second hand, the thing that matters.

---

# Chapter 11 — The Mother's Silence

I came up Bear Branch in my own truck on the Sunday afternoon because the conversation I was driving up to have was the kind a woman does not have in another woman's car. I had been at the Mountain Laurel that morning. I had not slept much. I had stood under the shower in the cinderblock bathroom long enough that Mrs. Profitt had knocked once on the door at half past eight and asked, in her small voice through the wood, *Honey, you need a biscuit*, and I had said *Yes, ma'am* and she had said *I left two on a paper plate outside your door* and I had heard her sneakers go back up the walk before I had finished saying *thank you.*

I had eaten the biscuits in the truck on the way out of town. They were soft. They were the kind of biscuit a woman makes who has been making them so long she does not have to think about them. The paper plate was on the bench seat beside me with two crumbs left on it when I came up over the rise past the church and saw my mother's house from the road.

The house from the road in October looks the way every photograph I have ever taken of it in my head looks. White clapboard, dark green shutters, the dogwood at the corner of the porch turned the color of dried blood at the leaves and gone bare at the top, the tin roof catching the slant light of four in the afternoon. The yard sloped the way it had always sloped. The barn behind the house stood the way a barn stands when it has not held an animal in twenty-seven years, which is to say a little crooked at the south corner where the foundation had given an inch. Behind the barn the upper headwater of Bear Branch went on doing what it had done since I was small, which was move quietly downhill over a bed of round stones and not particularly mind anybody.

My mother was on the porch.

She had a book in her lap. She was sitting in the green Adirondack chair I had given her for her sixtieth birthday in the only year I had bought her a birthday gift in person, which had been the year I had also done what I had done. She had a sweater over her shoulders and her good shoes on. She had heard the truck coming up the road from a half-mile down and had positioned herself, because that was what she did when she knew I was coming. She would not be caught in her housecoat. She would not be caught indoors. She would be on the porch with a book that she would not be reading.

I parked. I came up the walk. She watched me come.

*Mama.*

*Della.*

*I brought you nothing,* I said. *I should have brought you something. I will next time.*

*Bring me yourself,* she said. *I have already got what I need.*

I sat down on the porch swing because the second Adirondack chair had been moved into the kitchen for Wade. The chains of the swing creaked the way they had creaked when I was twelve. I put my hands flat on the boards beside me. The paint had been done in the spring; I could feel under my palms the small dimples where the painter had not quite worked his roller into the grain. My mother was watching me. She had set the book — Wendell Berry, of course; she had been reading him in chapters since 1991, and she would die reading him still — face down on the arm of the chair.

*Mama,* I said. *I need to ask you about Tully Renfro.*

She did not move. She looked at me. She held my eye. She said nothing for the time it took the katydid in the dogwood to clear its throat once and then think better of it.

*I knew you were coming up here today to ask,* she said.

*I think you did.*

*I did.* She straightened her sweater across her shoulders. *Come inside. I am not having this on the porch.*

*All right.*

I followed her inside. The screen door fell to behind me with its small grateful sound. The kitchen was the kitchen — propane heater off, woodstove going low in the front room, the smell of coffee that had been made at seven in the morning and not since, the hymnal on top of the refrigerator the way it had been every time I had come into this room since before I could see the top of the refrigerator. She got two cups down from the cabinet by the sink. She poured. She set one in front of me at the table and sat with hers across from me. She did not offer cream. She knew I took it black on a difficult conversation.

I took a drink. The coffee had been sitting nine hours and tasted the way coffee tastes that has been sitting nine hours. I did not say anything about it.

*He came up here nine times in a year,* she said. *I am going to tell it to you in the order I want to tell it, Della, and you are going to listen and not stop me. When I am done you can ask me what you want.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*He came up here the first time in October of last year,* she said. *He had a manila folder and a clean shave and a tie. He stood on this porch and he told me Blackridge wanted to talk to me about a release on the mineral rights, and he said the words *good faith offer* and the words *fair to your family* and the words *a sum certain* like a man who had been to a class to learn them. I told him I did not invite agents into the kitchen and I did not sign anything without my children. He left his card. He left.*

I had not picked up my pen. I would not. I was not in Hannah's office. I was in the kitchen I had run through barefoot at eight.

*He came up again three weeks later,* she said. *He raised the offer. I refused. He came in November. He came in January. He came in March, after the dogwood. He came in May. He came in July. He came on a Tuesday in September. The last time he came I had a shotgun broken over my arm because he had been on this porch the time before in May and had stayed on the porch after I had told him to leave, and I had been thinking about that May visit through the summer, and I had decided that whatever it was I had not done the last time, I was going to do this time.*

She paused.

*The gun was not loaded, Della,* she said. *I am not a woman who points a loaded gun. I broke it over my arm and I came out onto the porch and I told him he could leave or he could see what would happen if he did not. He left. He left the card. I think he heard me say a thing I had said with the shotgun open on my arm because he didn't come back. He may also have not come back because somebody put a piece of rock to his head two weeks later. I do not know the order of those two thoughts in his last month. I am not going to pretend to.*

She drank her coffee. She set the cup down.

*Now,* she said. *Ask me.*

*Mama,* I said. *Why didn't you tell me.*

She had been waiting for that one. She had been holding it. I could see her shoulders drop the small inch they had been holding above their resting place since I had come up the walk.

*Della,* she said. *I figured you'd left to get away from things like this. I wasn't going to drag you back over a piece of paper.*

*A piece of paper.*

*A piece of paper.*

*Mama.* I put my own cup down. I made my hands flat on the table the way I had made them flat in Hannah's office. *That was not a piece of paper. That was your land. That was our land. That was the land Pap built this house on.*

*I know what it was.*

*Then —*

*Della.* She did not raise her voice. She did not have to. *I have been keeping this land out of that company's hands for twenty-four years. I started the day your daddy died. I will keep on. You did not come back when your daddy died. You did not come back when your granddaddy died, except by yourself four days late. You did not come back when I fell down the porch steps in 2018. You came back for Annie Couch and you left before the lunch. I did not believe you would come back over a piece of paper, even one that mattered. I was wrong. I see that now, with your brother in the jail. I was wrong. But it was not because I did not love you. It was because I had been wrong for so long about you in the small way that I had come to take the wrongness for the truth. I had come to think of you as a woman who did not want to be asked. I asked you nothing because I was afraid you would say no.*

She drank her coffee.

I did not say anything for a while. The clock above the sink was the same clock my grandfather had hung in 1968. It had a quiet tick. It had stopped twice in my life and Hollis had wound it both times and it had not stopped since.

*Mama,* I said.

*Yes.*

*I need to ask you the next question.*

She nodded. She had been waiting for that one too. She closed her eyes for a count and opened them.

*Yes.*

*Mama. Where were you on the fifth of October.*

She did not flinch. She did not look away. She held my eye.

*I was in the basement of the Bear Branch Methodist Church,* she said. *I was helping Verna Hicks lay out the funeral lunch for Doyle Sturgill. I was there from nine in the morning until past four in the afternoon. Verna and I made the coffee. We laid out the casseroles. We washed the cups after. We did the breakdown when the family had gone. I drove home at four-thirty. The funeral was at one. There were a hundred and forty people in that basement that day. Verna can tell you what I wore. The bulletin will tell you who I sat by.*

I had known the answer before she gave it. I had needed to ask. The asking was the part that cost. The answer was just the answer.

*Mama,* I said. *I am sorry I had to ask.*

*I am not,* she said. *You are doing your job. You are doing your brother's job for him. You came home to do it. Do not apologize for doing it well. You are not the first Combs to ask a member of this family where she was when something happened. You will not be the last.*

I looked at her. There was a thing under the sentence I had not heard her say before. Something turned a small quarter-inch under the table between us, the way the boards of an old house will turn a quarter-inch in a hot summer and not turn back.

I did not ask her what she meant. I drank the rest of my coffee. I stood up. She stood up. She walked me to the door.

At the door I stopped on the porch and turned and looked at her in the slant late-afternoon light through the screen. She had her hand on the door frame. Her hand looked smaller than a hand that small had any business looking. The light came across her knuckles and showed me, in a way I had not allowed myself to see in twenty-two years, the way her mother's hands had looked, in the one photograph I had of her mother — Vera, who had died when I was six, whose hands I had not been told to remember.

*I will be back tomorrow,* I said. *I will call before I come.*

*You don't have to call. Come on up. I will be here.*

*Mama.*

*Yes.*

*Thank you for telling me.*

*I should have told you in May.*

*Yes,* I said. *You should have. But you told me now.*

She nodded. She did not smile. She did not move from the door frame. The dogwood at the corner of the porch made its small dry sound in the slight wind that comes off the upper headwater of Bear Branch when the sun is going. The katydid in the laurel below the barn started up. I went down two steps. I went down the third. I had my hand on the truck door when she said, from the porch, the thing she had not been planning to say and had decided in the moment she would.

*Della.*

I turned.

*Your granddaddy used to ask me where I was, too,* she said. *I always told him.*

She held the door frame. She did not say anything else. She watched me until I had got the truck door open, and then she went back into the house and closed the screen behind her.

I sat in the cab a long minute before I started the engine. I sat with my hands on the wheel. I thought about Hollis in this kitchen at sixty, asking my mother at thirty where she had been on a particular afternoon — and my mother, at thirty, telling him. I thought about my mother at sixty-seven telling me, just now, where she had been on a particular afternoon. I thought about the women in this family asking the women in this family where they had been and being given the answer because the women in this family had decided, generations back, that this was what the women in this family did.

I did not yet have a word for what I had been given. I did not have a name for the small turned quarter-inch under the table. I would learn it. I would learn it in a kitchen, on a Sunday, three weeks from this one, with a hymnal open between me and the woman who had just stood at this door frame and put her small hand against it and watched me go.

I started the engine. I backed out into the yard. I turned the truck around in the gravel pad in front of the barn the way I had turned cars around in this yard since I was sixteen. I went down Bear Branch in the long copper light with the windows up and the heater on low and my pulse, when I took it at the radial with one finger on the wheel, holding steady at seventy-two.

---

# Chapter 12 — Mine Records

I left Caldwell at five-thirty in the morning on a Wednesday in the dark. Mrs. Profitt had a paper bag waiting for me on the office counter with two biscuits and a banana and a small jar of strawberry jam she had put up herself in May and labeled in pencil with the year. *You bring me the jar back,* she said. *I don't make these for a refund.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*Drive slow on the parkway. It is not built for the trucks they put on it.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

I drove out east on KY-92 with the heater on and the bag on the bench seat and the world a quiet predawn dark and the road empty except for a coal truck that passed me westbound at the second curve out of town, going faster than it had any business going, hauling sized cleaned coal to the powerplant on the Tennessee line — the kind of load the strip site sent down on the night shift. The driver lifted two fingers off the wheel at me as he went and I lifted two fingers back and the truck went on around the curve and was gone, and then it was just me and the road and the eastern sky beginning to think about being lighter than the western sky behind me.

Eight miles east of Caldwell, on the long straight where KY-92 runs along the base of the ridge before it climbs the gap to the parkway, there is a place where the road bends gently to the left and a turnout shoulder opens up on the right. There is a battered sign nailed to the ironwood at the back of the turnout that reads, in faded company paint, BLACKRIDGE MINING AND COKE COMPANY · MINE NO. 4 · NO TRESPASSING. Behind the sign, set back forty feet into the sandstone face of the ridge, is a concrete plug. The plug is square. The corners have been chipped by something in the years since they were poured. A drainage pipe runs out of the bottom and a small trickle of rust-colored water comes out of it constantly because the mine has been giving back its own water for forty-six years and will go on giving it back longer than I will be alive.

I pulled into the turnout. I did not get out of the truck. I sat with the engine running and the heater going and I looked at the plug for a count I would put at three minutes.

Hollis had walked into that portal for thirty-one years. He had walked out of it for thirty-one years. He had carried a lunch pail. He had carried a stick of light. He had carried, on a particular morning in September of 1959, the body of his older brother Asa Combs out of it on a stretcher with three other dead men, and he had walked four hundred yards down the holler to where the trucks were waiting and he had not said one word the whole walk down, my mother told me once, because he had been the one whose hand was on the corner of the stretcher at Asa's head, and he had been making sure, with the part of him that could still pay attention, that the stretcher did not tip.

I did not get out.

I put the truck back in drive. I went on.

I came up the parkway in the daylight by the time I had cleared the gap, and I drove the long four hours to Frankfort with the radio off because I needed the inside of the truck to be quiet for the work I was driving to do. I made one stop, at a Wendy's outside Mount Sterling, for a coffee I drank in the parking lot looking at a woman who was teaching a small boy to walk a beagle on a leash on the strip of grass between the parking lot and the road. The boy was not yet good at it. The beagle was patient. The woman watched both with the kind of attention a mother gives the slow learning of a child she is going to love either way. I sat in the truck and watched them for the time it took me to drink the coffee. I went on.

The Kentucky State Mining Archives sit in a low brick building on the back side of the capitol complex, set far enough from the dome that the tourists do not find it. The lobby has a coffee urn that has been on continuous duty since the Carter administration and a black-and-white photograph the size of a door, on the back wall, of a single piece of cannel coal in a glass case at the 1948 state fair. The coal in the photograph is round and dark and has a hand-polished face. There is a card under the case that says, in the typewriter font of 1948, THE OLDEST CANNEL IN THE COMMONWEALTH. EXTRACTED 1903, MINE NO. 1, BLACKRIDGE MINING AND COKE, CALDWELL CO. I stood in the lobby for the small time it took me to look at the photograph and then I signed in at the desk and told the woman behind it what I had come for.

She was a small woman in her sixties with reading glasses on a beaded chain and the kind of practical hair you get when you have stopped sitting for a stylist and started cutting it yourself in a bathroom mirror. She asked me my name. I gave her my name. She asked me what I was researching. I told her I was looking for the 1959–1963 records of Blackridge Mining and Coke, specifically the strike year and the missing-person file for a land agent named Calvin Tull. She did not write any of this down. She wrote nothing about my truck plate. She did not ask me what county I was from. She looked at me over the reading glasses and said, *Honey. The Blackridge files are in the back. Sit at table four. I will bring you what we have. We don't make copies for free, but we do make them.*

I sat at table four.

She brought, on a flat library cart, four banker's boxes and a flat-file folder the size of a road map. She set them down. *Holler when you are ready for the photocopier,* she said, and went back to her desk and to a paperback she had laid face down by the coffee urn, a Walter Mosley I made a small note of because I had been meaning to read it.

I opened the boxes.

I worked the way I had been taught to work a chart. You do not look for the thing you are looking for first. You look at the whole document. You read the labels. You read what is on the cover before you turn the cover. You make a list of what is there before you make a list of what you want. I made the list. I put the boxes in the order of date. I started in 1959.

The strike file was the thickest.

I will give you what I made of it, the way I gave Boyd the chart-back of a difficult run.

In April of 1959 a wildcat strike began at Mine No. 4 over a series of safety grievances. The grievances were named. Three were named at the top of the document, which is the lawyer's habit. The first was bad rock above the McCreary section. The second was a refusal by the company to maintain a clean second egress in the lower west drift. The third was the company's practice of paying a "production bonus" that incentivized crews to skip the timbering recommended by their own roof bolters. The strike spread to the surface within four days. It went to the wider UMWA local at the end of the second week. Three men were named by the company as the named leaders of the strike. Their names appear in a typed list, dated April 17, 1959, on a piece of company letterhead in the strike folder. The first name on the list was Hollis L. Combs.

The second was Buddy Slone.

The third was a man named Riggs Begley, who had been killed in 1964 in a different mine and had been buried up the holler behind his mother's place.

I read the list twice. I read the second name twice more.

I went on.

In May of 1959 Hollis was arrested in Harlan on a charge of incitement to riot and was held in the Harlan jail for six weeks on a count he would not name, which was the rock thrown by an unnamed man at the jaw of a deputy in the first week of the strike. He was released the second week of June without a charge filed. He returned to Caldwell. He went back on the picket line. The strike continued through the summer.

In September of 1959 the McCreary section roof failed. Four men were killed. Three were named in the file in the order the company recorded them: Asa T. Combs, age 23. Marlon J. Honaker, age 41. Otis Profitt, age 47. The fourth name had been entered later, in a different typewriter ink, after a chain-of-evidence on the body had been established three days later: Lloyd Boyles, age 19. The company's internal memo on the fall was three pages. It was signed at the bottom of the third page by a man identified as *C. Tull, Senior Land and Safety Agent.* The memo's conclusion was that the section had been cleared as safe on August 21, 1959, by an inspection conducted by the company's own staff, that the rock above the section had given for reasons "not foreseeable from prior inspection," and that the company was not at fault. There was a settlement schedule on the fourth page. The Combs family received eight hundred and fifty dollars.

I made a note of *eight hundred and fifty dollars.*

I underlined it. Then I made myself stop, because there is a kind of underlining that is for the page and a kind that is for you, and I had begun to do the second.

I went on.

There was a second internal memo, this one not in the strike file but in the company's safety folder for the year. It was dated August 4, 1959 — seventeen days before the McCreary clearance. It was a one-page memo from a man identified as *F. Hoskins, Mine Inspector,* addressed to *C. Tull.* It said: *McCreary section — bolt count below standard; cover rock fractured at the third pillar; recommend continued idle until secondary timbering installed. Do not clear.* At the bottom of the page someone had written in pencil, in a hand that was not the hand of the memo, the single word *DEFER.* The pencil was Calvin Tull's. The hand matched the signature on the September memo.

I sat with the document in my hand a long minute. The lobby clock ticked. Through the wall of the archive office I could hear the small woman talking in a quiet voice to somebody on the phone about her grandchildren. The Walter Mosley was where she had left it. The black-and-white photograph of the cannel coal stared at me from the far end of the room.

I went on.

I came to the missing-person file. It was four pages. It was the file of the company's report to the Caldwell County sheriff on the disappearance of Calvin H. Tull, age 57, on or about July 19, 1961. The report was filed July 21. It noted that Tull had been on personal business in the area of Bear Branch on the day of his disappearance and had not returned to his hotel. It noted that his vehicle had been found at the Phillips 66 lot on KY-92 on the morning of the 22nd with the keys in the ignition and his briefcase in the front seat. It noted that the matter had been referred to Sheriff Caleb Honaker. It noted that the company had retained a private investigator out of Knoxville for sixty days. The PI's brief final report was clipped to the file. It said, in two paragraphs, that no body had been recovered, that no witness had been found, and that the matter was closed for lack of body or evidence on October 14, 1961.

The file did not contain the name *Letty Anne Hicks.* The file did not contain the year *1932.* The file did not contain the word *first wife* or the word *drowning* or the word *Cumberland.* I will say this clearly because Della at the table at the archive did say it, in her head, to herself, as a matter of professional discipline: *the absence of a thing in a file is itself a thing in a file. Make a note of what isn't there.*

I made a note of what wasn't there.

I went back to the strike file. I went to the back of it. There was a personnel folder for each of the three named strike leaders. I went to Hollis's first.

Hollis was terminated on April 22, 1959, four days after his name appeared on the company's named-leader list. He was re-hired on November 13, 1959, after the UMWA arbitration. His re-hire was processed by the personnel office in Pikeville. The personnel manager who processed it was a man named E. Halsey — Becca's grandfather, I would learn later, by way of a thing my mother said to me at the kitchen sink three weeks from this day. E. Halsey had handled all three re-hires.

On the inside cover of Hollis's folder, E. Halsey had written a personal note in fountain-pen ink, dated November 13, 1959. The note read, *Re-hired this date per arb. Will work the lower west. Stay clear of McCreary. Combs is the one who'll come back for it.*

I read the note three times.

I did not move for some count of seconds.

I read the other two folders. Buddy Slone's personnel folder had no note from E. Halsey, only the date of re-hire and the section he was assigned to. Riggs Begley's folder had a note that said *Watch this one.* Hollis's folder was the only one that had been written about as a man who would *come back for it.*

I made a copy. The small woman ran the photocopier for me. She made three copies of the personnel note for fifty cents. She did not look at the page. She gave me the three copies in a brown manila envelope from the supply drawer and made my receipt with a carbon book and asked me, when she handed it across, *Honey, you don't look like you have eaten today. There is a Lee's down the road. Get yourself a leg and a biscuit before you drive home.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*The drive is long.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

I drove to the Lee's. I ate a chicken leg and a biscuit in the truck in the parking lot in the gray light of three in the afternoon. The biscuit was not as good as Mrs. Profitt's biscuit. The chicken was the chicken. I drank a Coke. I drove home.

I came back across the parkway in the falling light and made the Mountain Parkway and came down through the gap and was on KY-92 east of Caldwell by eleven at night. The mine portal was invisible in the dark when I came past the turnout. I did not look for it. I did not have to. I knew where it was. I drove past it the way you drive past the house where you used to live — your shoulders know.

I pulled up to the Mountain Laurel at five past midnight. The neon sign with the burnt-out *L* was on. The office light was off; Mrs. Profitt had gone to bed at ten as she did every night of her life. I sat in my truck with the engine running. I had the manila envelope on my lap. I opened it. I took out the top copy. I held it in the cab light and I read it again.

*Re-hired this date per arb. Will work the lower west. Stay clear of McCreary. Combs is the one who'll come back for it.*

I sat with the page in my hand. I thought about Earl Whitt's piece of cannel coal in the breast pocket of the flannel I had left at the Mountain Laurel that morning. I thought about how, four days before, I had held a small dark thing in my hand and known it was a key without yet knowing what it opened. I had a second key now. It was a yellowed photocopy of a sentence written in fountain-pen ink by a man named E. Halsey on the inside cover of a personnel folder on November the thirteenth, nineteen fifty-nine. The two keys were on me at the same time. They did not yet fit the same lock. I knew, sitting in the truck at midnight in the parking lot of the Mountain Laurel, that they would, and I knew I would have to walk them around for a while before I found the door.

I shut the engine off. I took the envelope with me into the room. I locked the door behind me. I set the envelope on the dresser next to my keys. I did not turn on the overhead light. I sat on the bed in the dark and took my boots off and put my head in my hands and held it there for a long count, and when I lifted it the room was still dark and the envelope was still on the dresser and the company's word about my grandfather, written in November of 1959 by a man who had been dead forty years, was still waiting.

---

# Chapter 13 — Verna Hicks

Verna Hicks lived a half-mile past the up-the-holler Methodist church on Bear Branch, in a frame house with a green metal roof her son had put on her in 1998 over her objections. The roof had outlived her son. *The Lord is patient,* she had said to me once when I was nine and standing in her kitchen, *and so is the roof.*

I drove up on the Friday afternoon. The two Adirondack chairs on her porch had been painted fresh white in the spring, and there was a small table between them with a doily on it and on the doily a glass pitcher of sweet tea sweating in the sun. She had made the tea before I got there. She had set out two glasses. She had been listening for my truck since the morning.

She came out as I came up the walk. She was eighty-eight and was the kind of small her grandmother had been before her — small as if the smallness was something the family had been cultivating four generations on purpose. Her hair was white and put up in a knot the way she had put it up at the schoolhouse in 1955. She wore a flannel shirt buttoned to the throat and a long pleated skirt and the brown lace-up shoes she had been wearing in 1992.

*Della.*

*Mrs. Hicks.*

*Don't you Mrs. Hicks me, girl. Sit.* She pointed at the chair on the porch. *I have been waiting for you to come up this road. You are slower than your granddaddy.*

*I am.*

*Sit.*

I sat. She sat. She poured my tea without asking and set the glass in front of me with the kind of slowness that lets you know the conversation is going to be longer than you came for. I let it be. She had earned a long conversation. Half the women who had taught me to spell were in the ground now, and the other half were on this porch. She poured her own. She set the pitcher between us. She put her hands flat in her lap.

*I will tell you,* she said, *what I think you came to ask. I will tell you in my time. You drink the tea.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

I drank the tea. It was strong. She had used three bags. The sugar had been put in while the tea was hot, the proper way, and stirred until it had gone in. The water in the ice was her own well water, which came up out of the rock at the back of the holler at the same constant cold the seep at Sorrow Hollow ran at, because they were the same water the same mountain had been holding since before any of us. I held the glass in both hands.

Across the road, in the little frame house where her husband's nephew Lonnie had lived alone since 2014, a radio played out an open kitchen window. The radio was on the AM gospel station out of Hazard, the same station Earl Whitt had had on low in his trailer six days before. *Will the Circle Be Unbroken* was playing, the way the old congregations do it, slow, with the lead voice an alto woman who had been singing it for fifty years.

Verna listened a moment. She made, in her chair, the small involuntary motion of a woman whose body knows a hymn. She did not sing. She did not hum quite. She moved her right hand on her knee in the quarter-time the alto was holding.

*That is the hymn we sang at your granddaddy's burial,* she said.

*I know.*

*You weren't there for the burial.*

*I know.*

*You came four days late and stood at the grave by yourself at noon. I drove up that morning to see if you would come and I sat in my truck at the gate. I did not get out. I did not want to crowd you. You stood there twenty minutes. Then you got back in your car and drove away.* She looked at me. *I have been carrying that for twenty years.*

I did not say anything.

*I am telling you now because I do not want to die carrying it,* she said. *And because I want you to know that I knew you had come.*

*Thank you, Mrs. Hicks.*

*Verna,* she said.

*Verna.*

She nodded. She picked up her tea. She drank a small swallow. She set the glass down. The hymn across the road moved on to the second verse, the verse about the long brown box and the hearse coming up the lane, and the alto held a note that did not waver. Verna did not look across the road. She looked at me.

*Now,* she said. *Tully Renfro.*

*Yes.*

*Your brother is in the Caldwell County jail. He did not kill that man. I have known your brother since he was three days old. He is a Combs, which means he is more capable of cruelty than he looks, but he is not capable of *that* cruelty, and the people in this holler who think otherwise are people who do not know what the difference is. You will have to take my word on that or you can take Avery Cornett's, when he comes around to it.*

*I take your word.*

*Good.* She drank again. *I will tell you what I know. Some of it is old. Some of it is older than that. I will tell you what I know because you are the one who has come to ask, and because you are Hollis Combs's granddaughter, and because there is no one left in this county to ask but me.*

She paused. She looked, for a small second, at her own hands in her lap. They were knotted the way old apple wood is knotted, the joints turned slightly out of true by what arthritis had done over forty years. She turned them palms up and then back down again, as if she were taking inventory of them.

*Your granddaddy,* she said, *was a man who would do a thing himself before he asked anybody else to do it. He was not a man who held a grudge unless the grudge was earned, and then he held it forever. He drank, before he met Vera. He did not drink, after. He went to church because Vera went, and because the church had been part of how the strike was held together in '59, and because — I will tell you, Della, because you should know this — because he believed in *something* enough to do the thing he did, and he did not parse it out for himself or for anybody else, and he would not have called it God on most days, but he was not the kind of man who walks past the building either.*

She paused.

*He had hands the size of a shovel head,* she said. *He had one good eye after '53. He kept his voice low. I have heard him raise it twice in my life. Once at a deputy who was trying to push my mother off the church steps in 1964, and once at a foreman in '78 who had sent a boy of nineteen down into a section Hollis had told him not to. The deputy stepped back. The foreman cried. Your granddaddy was a man who did not have to make himself big. He was the kind of man who could make a room narrow around him by standing still in it.*

I drank. I did not interrupt.

*I will ask you a thing,* she said. *Did your granddaddy have a grudge against Calvin Tull.*

I did not speak.

*Honey,* she said. *Calvin Tull killed Hollis's brother.*

I held the tea. The condensation was wet on my palms. The hymn across the road had ended; the alto was talking now to the host, in the small AM-radio interval between songs. I could hear the laugh in her voice. Lonnie's radio was loud enough I could hear the cadence and not the words. Verna did not look across the road.

*Asa Combs,* she said. *Twenty-three. Going to be married in November. The section they sent him into in McCreary on the second of September of 1959 had been cleared as safe by Calvin Tull eleven days before. There was a mine inspector's note. There was a pencil mark on the inspector's note. There was a roof fall. There were four men. Your granddaddy got Asa out himself. He carried him down on the stretcher with his hand at the head end. He came up out of the holler that afternoon and washed at the pump in his mother's yard and did not speak for three days, and on the fourth day he walked into town and stood in front of the company office on Main and looked at the door for ten minutes and turned around and walked home. I will tell you what your great-grandmother Effie said to my mother that week, because my mother told it to me when I was a young woman and I have kept it. She said: *Hollis isn't going to do anything fast about it. Hollis is going to do something slow.**

She paused.

*That was September of 1959,* she said. *Your granddaddy did not do anything fast. He did not do anything for two years.*

*Verna.*

*Yes.*

*In July of 1961, Calvin Tull disappeared.*

*Yes.*

*The sheriff at the time was Caleb Honaker.*

*Yes.*

*He closed the case for lack of body or evidence in October of '61.*

*Yes.*

I had set my tea down. I was leaning forward. I did not know I had been leaning forward until she put her own glass down with the small careful sound of a woman who is about to say a thing she has been holding sixty years.

*Della,* she said. *You know what I am about to tell you. Some part of you has known it since you were old enough to keep a secret. I want to be the one to give it to you, not because you didn't know, but because I would rather it come from somebody who loved your grandfather than from a courthouse file. So I am telling it to you on my porch with sweet tea between us, and you can do with it what your judgment tells you to do. Hollis Combs did not kill Calvin Tull alone, and the way Calvin Tull was killed was not the way Hollis Combs would have done it.*

I felt the small turn under the boards under our chairs that I had felt under the boards under the kitchen table at my mother's six days before. I held the tea in both hands. I did not say anything.

*Hollis would have shot him at the company gate at noon,* she said. *He would have stood there and let them put the cuffs on him after. He would not have taken him up a hollow and put a piece of coal on him. That was somebody making a point. That was somebody trying to make it look like it meant something. That was not your granddaddy's hand.*

She looked at me.

*I am not going to tell you whose hand it was,* she said. *I do not know. I have an idea, the way an old woman has an idea, and I will not put it in your head. I am only telling you what was not your granddaddy. And I am telling you that whatever your granddaddy did in that holler in July of '61, he did it as a man who had helped do something else, not as the man who had done the doing. I have known that for sixty-four years. I have not said it to a living soul until this afternoon. You are the one I have said it to.*

*Verna.*

*Yes.*

*Why are you telling me.*

*Because I am eighty-eight years old. Because your brother is in the jail. Because the man who killed Tully Renfro put a piece of coal on his chest like a man who had been taught what a piece of coal on a chest is for. And because there are not three people alive in this county who were taught that, and one of them is sitting on this porch.*

She looked at me. The light through the maple at the corner of the porch had gone slant copper. The bell at the Bear Branch Methodist down the road would ring at five and at six. The wind was beginning to come down off the ridge the way it does in October when the heat of the day starts to give up.

*Hollis told one person about what he had done besides his Lord,* she said. *He told it on a porch in the spring of 1962 to a man named Buddy Slone. Buddy was on the strike committee with him in '59. Buddy held the secret all the way to the day he died in '99. I knew he held it because Buddy's wife, who was my own first cousin, told me one Christmas when she had had two glasses of the wine her sister sent her, and she did not tell me what was in the secret, only that Buddy had it on him and was made smaller by it. Buddy died in '99. He had a boy.*

I made a note. I made it in my head. I did not have my pad.

*Buddy's boy is Cal Slone,* she said. *Sixty-four now. Welds at a shop in Pineville. He has his daddy's quiet on him and his daddy's eye for what a thing means. I am telling you, Della. So you know to listen to a man named Cal Slone when he turns up. He will.*

I looked at her. She was not smiling. She was not not smiling. The slant light had come in through the maple and was lying along the line of her jaw and along the line of her flannel collar.

*Verna.*

*Yes.*

*I will not contradict any of this for you,* I said. *I do not know what I am going to do with it.*

*I am not asking you to know yet. You will. You are Vera Sturgill's granddaughter. You will work it out.*

She said the name *Vera* the way you say a name when you mean for the person hearing it to feel it land. I felt it land. I had not heard Vera said aloud, by somebody who had known her, in twenty-five years. I did not say anything for a moment. The hymn from across the road had come back; the alto was a guest on the program that afternoon and *Circle* was her closer. Verna moved her hand on her knee in the quarter-time. She did not sing.

*Your grandmother knew what your granddaddy had done,* she said. *She knew it the same week he did it. She did not speak of it to me ever. I am telling you because you should not go forward thinking your grandmother did not know. The women in your family have always known. They have not always told.*

I felt that one in the chair. I felt it land in the chair where my mother had been sitting in the green Adirondack six days before. I felt it land in the kitchen on top of the refrigerator where my grandmother's hymnal had been sitting since before I was born.

I did not say anything for a long count. I drank the last of the tea. I set the glass on the doily on the painted-white table between us. Verna did not move. She watched me the way a schoolteacher watches a child who is working out a sum in her head and is to be left alone until she comes up with it.

I did not come up with it. The thing was bigger than a sum.

*Verna,* I said. *I am going to need to come back.*

*I will be here.*

*If I do not come back —*

*You will come back. You are Hollis Combs's grand. You come back to a thing once you have been told.*

I stood up. She stood up. She walked me to the door of the porch. The bell at Bear Branch Methodist down the road struck a quarter past five. Verna stopped at the door and put her hand on the screen frame and looked at me. The light was orange now and was full on her face and was kind to her in the small way that the late slant October light is kind to old women on porches in this county, which is not to flatter them but to show them. She was eighty-eight. She had taught me my times tables in 1991. She had stood in the cemetery in 2005 in her good shoes and had not come down to me because she had not wanted to crowd me.

*Della.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*Carry it down careful,* she said. *The fire don't ask you whether you wanted it.*

I went down the steps to my truck. I did not look back. I knew she was at the door. I would have known it if she had been an acre away. I started the engine. I sat with my hands on the wheel a moment because I did not yet trust them to drive. I pulled out onto Bear Branch and drove down past the church and the trace and the bend where the road turns, and the orange light went out of the trees, and the dusk came down on the holler the way it came down on every holler in this county every evening in October at quarter past five, even when nobody was watching, even when somebody had just been given a thing she did not yet have a place to put.

---

# Chapter 14 — The Green Truck

The plate was Kentucky 47-RKB and the truck was a 1998 Ford F-250, Forest Service green, with the Blackridge Coal Holdings hardhat sticker peeling at the corner above the rear wheelwell. I had driven past it three times since the morning I came in on KY-92, the first time without seeing it and the second and third with the kind of seeing that comes after a thing has been put on a list. The list was in Hannah's office on a sheet of yellow legal paper. The truck was item one. The driver was the gap.

The county clerk's office sat in the basement of the courthouse, across the hall from the sheriff's. The woman at the counter was named Joletta Boggs — *no relation to Caudill,* she said without my asking — and she had her own grandmother's red-frame readers on a chain around her neck and she squinted through them at the license plate I had written down. She typed it into the system the slow methodical way of a person who had been typing the wrong key for forty years and had decided she did not have to learn the right one. She turned the screen toward me. She did not say anything. She did not have to. The name on the screen was *Caudill Wayne Boggs.* The address was a P.O. box at the post office in Bear Branch and a street address up Possum Trot off KY-92 between town and the Phillips 66.

*Joletta,* I said.

*I figured.*

*You know him.*

*I know who he is. So do you, honey. He was a deputy here when you were in high school.*

I remembered him then. The way you remember a teacher you did not have but whose name was always in the announcements. Caudill Boggs had been the sheriff's nephew or the sheriff's wife's cousin or some third thing that meant he had ridden in a cruiser at twenty-three with the kind of attitude a county does not survive twice. He had broken a man's wrist with a flashlight in 2003 against a Phillips 66 ice cooler that I had walked past every day of my senior year. The county had paid the settlement quietly. The county had let him quit and called it quitting. He had come back four years later wearing the soft jacket of a man who worked for a company, and the county had let that happen, too, and not asked.

*Thank you, Joletta.*

She nodded. She took her glasses off. *Della.* She said it in the voice the older women in this town used on me — the voice that meant *I will not pry but I am going to be holding the door for whatever you bring back through it.* *You be careful what you turn up about that man.*

*I'm careful.*

*He was a careful man when he was twenty-three, too,* she said. *Careful enough to know which side of the cooler to swing on.*

I went out into the hall and called Hannah from the bench under the window. She picked up on the second ring. I said *Caudill Boggs* and she did not say anything for a second and then she said *Get in your truck and meet me at the Marathon at the Bell County line. I'll be there in forty minutes.*

I got in the truck. I drove. The maples along the Caldwell road were as red as anything in this country ever got. The sky was the cold blue of a sky that has decided about winter. The narrative section of the run report — what a paramedic wrote when the boxes weren't enough — was the one I did not yet have the words for.

Hannah's Subaru was at the Marathon when I got there. She was inside paying for two coffees and a sleeve of peanut-butter crackers. She handed me one cup and the crackers without speaking and we walked back to her car and got in. She said *Drive me down KY-92 a piece and turn up Possum Trot a quarter mile before the house and pull into the second turnout. It's high enough you can see the driveway. Park nose-out.* I did. She drank her coffee. She watched the road the way you watch a dog you are not yet sure of.

*You worked a stakeout in Lexington,* I said.

*Once. With my professor in his Volvo.*

*This is not a stakeout.*

*No,* she said. *This is two women in a Subaru looking at a truck.*

The truck was there. The green F-250 was parked under a metal carport beside a single-wide that had been a single-wide for thirty years and had been painted recently white. There was a Trans Am up on blocks in the side yard with a tarp half off it. There was a dog chain stretched between two pin oaks with no dog on the end of it. There was a satellite dish on a pole. There was a hand-lettered sign at the foot of the drive that said *NO HUNTING.* The sign was new. The paint was still bright.

*He's home,* Hannah said.

I had not said he wasn't.

We sat. Hannah finished her coffee. I drank mine in three swallows because I had been a paramedic for twelve years and a paramedic drinks coffee the way a soldier eats. The turnout was at the lip of a low ridge above the trailer, and below the trailer the land dropped away toward the run of KY-92 and beyond it the bottom of Big Cane Creek and beyond that the line of ridges that ran east toward Sorrow Hollow as the crow flew. The crow's flight from Caudill Boggs's carport to the place a man had died in laurel three weeks ago was about eight miles. By road it was twenty. He had been on the long road that day, by Earl Whitt's account. He had been at the mouth of the trace. He had been in a position to see who went up.

*Tell me what we are doing,* Hannah said. She said it quietly, the way a doctor asks a patient to identify a pill.

*We are not approaching him.*

*Good.*

*We are establishing he uses this truck. We are establishing his pattern. If he goes anywhere today, we see where he goes.*

*And if he goes to the IGA.*

*Then we know he goes to the IGA.*

*Della,* she said, *if he goes to a person, we have to think about what that means. He is not on trial. Wade is on trial. The Commonwealth doesn't have to chase Caudill Boggs because we say chase. It has to be given a reason it can't refuse.*

*Yes.*

*I'm saying I see why we are here. I'm also saying I am not yet sure how this becomes something I file.*

*I know,* I said. *I'm not asking you to file anything.*

*What are you asking.*

*I'm asking,* I said, *who taught Caudill Boggs to leave a piece of coal on a rock.*

She was quiet for a long minute. The wind worked the dry leaves in the oaks above us. A redtail went over from south to north, low, riding the ridge thermal. Hannah took her notebook out of her bag and wrote the date and the time and the plate number and the address and our position and nothing else.

*All right,* she said. *We watch.*

We watched. Watching, I learned that afternoon, is not waiting. Waiting is what you do for an ambulance. Watching is the form of attention you give a thing you have already decided is going to fail in front of you, and you do not want to be looking away when it does.

For an hour the carport did not move. Then a screen door opened and Caudill Boggs came out.

He was bigger than I had remembered and he was smaller than the file had let me imagine. He came out in a tan canvas jacket and Carhartt pants and boots a half-size too big — I noted the boots without yet knowing what they meant. He had a paper bag in one hand. He did not look around. He went to the truck and opened the door and put the bag on the passenger seat and shut the door and went around the front and got in and started it. The engine had the loose low rattle of a truck of a certain age and the exhaust came up gray. He let it idle for a minute. He sat with his hands on the wheel. The way he sat with his hands on the wheel was the way a man sits when he has been a deputy and has had to sit in a parked vehicle as part of the work; he sat upright, both hands at ten and two, looking at the dashboard. Then he backed out of the carport, came up the driveway, turned right onto Possum Trot, and went past us, downhill, toward KY-92.

He did not look at the Subaru.

Hannah did not start her car for a count of ten and then she started it and pulled out and we went after him at a distance I would not have been able to articulate and which she clearly could. *Don't get closer than three vehicles,* she said. *If he turns onto a road with nobody on it I drop back further.* I said *Copy.* I said *copy* because that was the word my body knew to say. We took KY-92 south through town. We passed the IGA. We passed the courthouse. We passed the Brass Lantern. We passed the Mountain Laurel where I had been sleeping for two weeks. He did not turn at any of these. He went on south on KY-92 toward Pineville.

*Pineville,* Hannah said.

*Tully Renfro's daughter.*

*Yes.*

We rode in silence the twenty-two miles. The road from Caldwell to Pineville runs alongside the upper Cumberland through a country of pinched bottoms and ridges that close in on the river the way the ridges do on this side of the state. We crossed three single-lane bridges. We passed a hand-painted plywood sign for tomato plants outside a house that had stopped selling tomato plants in 2009. We passed a propane company. We passed a man on a riding mower. The truck stayed three vehicles ahead of us the whole way, neither speeding nor slow.

Sara Renfro's house was on a side street off the main drag of Pineville, a small white bungalow with a chain-link fence and a wooden ramp built over the front steps for a stroller. There was a car seat visible through the back window of the Subaru wagon in the driveway and a Halloween skeleton taped to the inside of the storm door. Hannah went past the house, turned, came back, and parked across the street under a maple two doors down. We watched Caudill park his truck in the gravel apron next to Sara's driveway. He got out. He took the paper bag out of the passenger seat. He went up the wooden ramp and stood on the porch and rang the bell.

The storm door did not open.

He rang it again. He stood with the bag in one hand and the other on the door frame, which is the way a man stands at a door he has stood at before. The way a man stands at a door of a person who has been told not to open it. He waited. He stood for what my watch said was a hundred and twenty-three seconds.

He set the bag on the porch beside the door.

He turned and went down the ramp and got in his truck and started it and sat with his hand on the gearshift for another count and then he looked across the street at us.

Not at the Subaru. At us. At Hannah and me, in the Subaru, in the shade of the maple, two doors down. His head turned and his eyes found ours through Hannah's windshield and across the asphalt and through the gap between the two parked cars between us, and they stopped on us with the particular settling of a man who had known where to look. He was not surprised. He had known we were behind him for the last twenty-two miles. He had let us be behind him the way a man lets a dog walk along beside him while he goes about an errand. He had brought us to this porch in this town so that we would see him come up the ramp and set down the bag and come back to his truck, and he was looking at us now to see whether we had understood what we had been shown.

He did not lift a hand. He did not nod. The right corner of his mouth moved the width of a fingernail.

Then he put the truck in gear and pulled out and drove back the way he had come, north on KY-92, toward Caldwell.

Hannah did not move. Her hands stayed on the wheel of her parked car. She watched the green truck go up the road and become a green dot and become nothing.

*Della,* she said.

*Yes.*

*That was not a man scared of being followed.*

*No.*

*That was a man showing us he could be followed and that it cost him nothing.*

*Yes.*

*What was in the bag,* she said.

*I don't know.*

We sat. The Halloween skeleton looked black against the door. After a minute the storm door opened a hand's width and a young woman in a sweatshirt looked out at the bag on her porch and then up and down her street and then she stepped out and picked the bag up by the handles and went back inside. The deadbolt threw with the small dry sound of a deadbolt thrown by a person who had been throwing it three times a day for three weeks.

Hannah started the car.

*We don't follow him,* she said.

*No.*

*We go back to my office and we write down what we just watched.*

*Yes.*

She made the U-turn and we went back the way we had come. The light over the road was the long copper light of late afternoon in October. Neither of us said anything for several miles. I watched the road the way I had stopped watching the road three weeks ago when I came in on this same KY-92 from the other direction and had not yet been a person who knew the truck I was now hunting. I watched the dropped hubcaps in the ditch. I watched the silo behind the Baptist church at Frakes. I watched a hawk on a fencepost let us go by without lifting.

*Caudill knows Eldon,* I said, finally, because it was the sentence that had built itself in me on the way down and was now finished. *Caudill works for the company. He was at the mouth of the hollow that day. He didn't have to kill Tully Renfro for the company to want a person under the body. He just had to be there and to know what he was looking at. He has known for three weeks. He knows what Eldon used. He knows what Wade did and did not do. He is sitting on it.*

Hannah did not answer for a long time. The Subaru hummed. The leaves in the median dropped in handfuls every time a truck went by us in the other lane.

*Della,* she said. *If Caudill Boggs knows, then the question is not whether the company knows. The company knows. The question is what they intend to do with the knowing.*

*Yes.*

*And the answer is they intend to let your brother fall.*

*Yes.*

*And Caudill smiling at us in that driveway was a man enjoying the proximity of a fall he is not going to be in.*

*That,* I said, *or a man who is starting to figure out he might be.*

She looked at me. The road went under us. She looked back at the road.

*All right,* she said. *We hold that one. We don't say it out loud yet. We watch it.*

I did not answer. I rolled the window down a hand's width and put my fingers on the cold metal of the door frame and let the wind go over my knuckles. The same hand had taken Madison's wrist in a folded Camry two weeks earlier and had held my mother's hymnal three nights ago without yet knowing what was inside it. It had just been seen, in a parked Subaru in Pineville, by a man in a green truck who had wanted to be seen by it.

The sun went behind the ridge. Hannah drove and did not push the speed limit. The smile at the end was the one thing he had not been careful about. The smile had moved on his face without his permission.

The motel sign came up over the rise. The *L* was burnt out.

Hannah pulled in and put the Subaru in park. She did not turn it off.

*Sleep,* she said. *I'll write tonight.*

*All right.*

I got out. I shut the door. I stood in the parking lot with my hands in the pockets of my jacket. The neon hummed. Mrs. Profitt's office light was on. The Subaru backed out and went up the road and was gone.

I went inside. I did not turn the room light on. I lay on the bedspread in my clothes and I looked at the ceiling and I thought about a paper bag set down on a porch in Pineville and I thought *what was in it,* and the answer that came back was *money*, and the answer that came back after that was *something his daughter is going to need that the company has decided he is no longer going to be able to give her.*

I closed my eyes.

I slept four hours, the way a paramedic sleeps, and woke at the first gray on the ceiling.

---

# Chapter 15 — Wade Again

Becca Couch was on the desk when I came in and she did not say my name. She slid the visitor sheet across the laminate. *He's in,* she said. *They moved him to B. New cellmate. Don't ask.*

*I won't.*

She buzzed me through. The deputy at the visitation door was Curtis Whitt — Earl's grandson, by the look of him. *Twenty minutes,* he said.

He let me in. Wade was already on his side of the plexiglass when I sat down. He looked thinner than he had two weeks ago, the way a man looks thinner who has been moved from a job his body knows to a chair it does not. His hair was cut. Someone had cut it for him — too short over the ears, the line at the back uneven. He saw me looking at it. He put a hand to the side of his head and let it fall.

*Mama send you the cookies,* he said.

*She did.*

*Did Curtis take them.*

*He's got them.*

*I'll get half.*

*You'll get all of them. Curtis isn't going to eat your mother's cookies.*

He almost smiled. The almost did not get there. He laid his right hand on the steel ledge below the glass and looked at it. The hand was shaking, low and steady, the way his hands had been shaking since the second day. It was not withdrawal. The doctor at the jail had given him something for that the first week. This was the other thing. This was the long thin tremor of a man in a small room.

I picked up the handset on my side. He picked up his.

*Dell.*

*Wade. I have to ask you something.*

*All right.*

I had thought, the whole drive down from the motel, about how I would put this. I had thought about laying it out like a run report: *Subject Boggs, observed at — subject smiled at — subject's actions consistent with — request you tell me what you know about subject's relation to —* and I had thought about laying it out like a sister: *Wade, please.* In the end I did neither. I asked him the way Hannah would have asked him, which was the way a person asked a person when she did not want the answer to be the easy one.

*Tell me what Caudill Boggs has to do with this.*

His shoulders went down a quarter of an inch. He did not look up. He looked at his hand on the ledge. He took a slow breath in through his nose and let it out the same way and his hand did not stop shaking but he did not pretend it wasn't.

*Della.*

*Wade.*

*Listen to me.*

*I'm listening.*

*Whatever Caudill did,* he said, *he didn't do alone. And I'm not going to tell you who he did it with.*

He said it the way a man says a thing he has practiced in his head for nineteen nights. He said it with the small dry care of a man who has decided that this sentence is the one sentence he is going to be able to keep saying without changing the words. He had said it once already, to himself, and now he was saying it to me, and the saying of it to me was not a confession or a request. It was a setting-down.

I did not say anything for a long minute.

The handset was warm against my ear from the man before me who had used it. There was a smudge on the plexiglass at the level of Wade's mouth that had been there since my first visit. Curtis had not wiped it. Curtis did not wipe it because nobody wiped it. The smudges built up over weeks and the cleaning crew came on Saturdays and wiped them all at once and the next week the smudges came back. I was looking at the smudge at the height of my brother's mouth while he told me he was going to take a charge for someone he would not name.

*Wade,* I said. *They are going to put you away.*

*I know.*

*Do you.*

*I know,* he said. *I have known for two weeks. Hannah came in here Friday and laid out twenty-five to life. She laid out the deal too. She laid out everything. I listened. She is a good woman. She is doing the best she can with what I am giving her, which is nothing.*

*Wade.*

*Dell.*

*Look at me.*

He did not look at me. He kept looking at his hand. His hand was the thing he was keeping his eyes on the way I had kept my eyes on Madison's chest the morning before all of this started, because the hand was the one true thing in the room that would tell him whether he was still alive in the way he meant to be.

*Tell me one thing,* I said.

*All right.*

*Was it him.*

*Was who him.*

*Caudill. Was Caudill the one.*

He was quiet for a count of four. His hand on the ledge made a small involuntary movement and stilled.

*No.*

I had not thought it had been. I had thought, since the smile in Sara Renfro's driveway, that Caudill was something else — a man too careful to do the careful thing, a man too proud of his carefulness to take the necessary mess of an actual killing onto himself. But the *no* from Wade landed heavier than I had thought it would. The *no* meant that my brother knew who had done it, knew it was not Caudill, and was choosing not to say.

*Wade.*

*Dell.*

*Whoever it is, they put a piece of coal on a dead man's chest. They put another piece on a rock. They did a thing that has been done in this family before. They did it on the same ground.*

He said nothing.

*You know that,* I said. *You know I know. That is why you have not asked me what I have been doing for two weeks. That is why, when I came in the third time, you did not ask whether I had found out anything. You knew what I was looking at. You knew what I was likely to find. You knew what your silence was costing.*

He said nothing.

*Wade. The thing in the family. The thing Granddaddy carried. You knew.*

He shook his head once. *I knew the shape of it,* he said. *I did not know who. I did not know the whole. I knew there was a shape, and I knew the shape was ours.*

*Who told you the shape.*

He looked up then. He looked through the plexiglass at me, and the smudge of someone else's mouth was between our faces, and his eyes were our eyes — the same brown-with-green at the lower edge, the same set, the same lashes — and they were the eyes of a tired man who was not going to give me what I had come for.

*Dell,* he said. *I am not going to tell you that either.*

I let the handset rest against my shoulder for a second. I closed my eyes. I opened them. The fluorescent over our heads made a small clicking sound that the fluorescents in this building had been making for as long as I could remember. I had stood in this hall at sixteen waiting for a friend's father to come out and sign a paper for her. The fluorescent had clicked then too. The county did not replace the bulb. The county replaced nothing it did not have to. The county let a clicking fluorescent click until the bulb went and then it left the bulb dark for a week.

*Wade,* I said. *I am going to ask you the worst thing I have asked you.*

*Go.*

*Did you do it.*

*No.*

*Were you there.*

He did not answer.

*Wade.*

He looked at his hand. *Dell. I am not going to lie to you. I am also not going to put a sentence in this room that the wall in this room can carry out into the next room. Do you understand me.*

*I understand you.*

*Don't ask me again,* he said. *Not in here.*

I sat for a second. The clicking fluorescent. The smudge. Curtis Whitt on the other side of the wall behind me, listening or not listening, paid the same either way. The handset in my hand.

*All right,* I said.

*All right.*

*Wade.*

*Dell.*

*If I find it,* I said. *If I find it without you, and I bring it to Hannah, and Hannah brings it to the Commonwealth — is there a way you tell me, before I have to do that, that there is something I am missing.*

He thought about this. He took it seriously. He took it as seriously as he had taken anything Hannah had said to him on Friday. *I will tell you,* he said, *one thing. I have not told it to her. I will tell it to you because you came down from Knoxville in the middle of a shift and slept in a motel for two weeks and have been doing this for me. The one thing is this. The man who did it did not ask me whether he could do it. He told me he was going up there to talk. He did not tell me what was going to happen. When it happened, I should have stopped it. I did not. After it happened, he asked a thing of me. I did the thing. I should not have done the thing. I did it because of who he is. Not because of what he was. Because of who.*

*Wade.*

*That is what I will tell you. Do not ask me for the name in here. I will not say it in here.*

*Will you say it somewhere.*

He looked at his hand for a long time.

*If you bring me out,* he said, *I will say it in the kitchen.*

It was the closest thing to a hope I had been given by a person in any room in fourteen days. I did not let my face change. I put it where I could find it again. *All right,* I said.

*Dell.*

*Yes.*

*Don't get yourself killed.*

*I'm not going to get myself killed.*

*The man we are talking about,* he said, *is not the kind of man who would kill you. The man we are talking about is also not the kind of man who will be able to live with the fact that I am in here. The danger is not what you think it is. The danger is to him.*

*Wade.*

*Dell.*

*That is the most useful thing you have said to me yet.*

He almost smiled again, and this time the almost got there for a quarter of a second, and then it went. *Mama said you been driving back and forth like a fool,* he said. *Eat something. Sleep in the room she made up for you. She is sixty-seven years old and she is making your bed every morning like you are coming home from school.*

*I'll sleep in it tonight.*

*Tonight.*

*Tonight.*

The handset speaker over our heads said *Two minutes.*

*Wade.*

*Yes.*

*I love you.*

*I know it.*

*Say it back.*

*Dell.*

*Wade. Say it back.*

He looked at me. He put his hand flat on the plexiglass without lifting it off the ledge — a small move, fingers spread, palm against the smudge. *I love you,* he said. *Get out of here.*

I stood up. I put the handset in the cradle. I put my hand on the plexiglass on my side for the count of a second, against the place where his hand was on the other side, and I felt the cool of the glass and the warmth that does not pass through it, and I turned and went out.

Becca Couch was at the desk. *Della.*

*Becca. If he asks for anything.*

*I'll tell you. Get on home.*

I left.

I drove out of the courthouse parking lot at five to four. The light over Caldwell was the long copper light of late afternoon at the end of October, with the gold pulled out of it. The road north out of town climbed and bent and ran the creek for two miles and turned right at the Caldwell road and climbed again. I drove with both hands on the wheel because both hands on the wheel was the thing I could do.

I thought about Hollis. About him as the man my brother had sat with the last six weeks of his life. About him in Harlan jail in 1959, refusing to name the man who broke a deputy's jaw with a thrown rock. About him in a cell the size of the cell my brother was in.

I thought about a phrase my grandfather had used once, sitting on the porch with a piece of kindling in his hand, watching Wade and me carry a coal scuttle from the smokehouse to the back door. He had said, *You don't haul fire careless. You carry the fire down and you don't drop it. You drop it, you start something you can't put out.* He had been talking about the literal coal scuttle, and about us being eight and ten, and about kindling. He had been talking about other things too. *Carry the fire down.*

I had been thirteen. I had let go of my end of the scuttle on the back step. The coals had spilled into the laurel. We had put it out with a five-gallon bucket and a wet quilt. My grandfather had not yelled. He had said *Next time, Della, you ask Wade to slow up before you put the thing down. Don't drop it. Tell him.* He had said it without looking at me. He had said it the way he said most things — once, level, in a voice that did not require an answer.

He had carried the fire down. He had carried his brother's death, and Pap's reason for it, and his own week in a cell, and the rumor of a thing he had not done. He had not dropped it. He had kept his hands on the bucket the way you keep your hands on a wheel.

Wade was carrying the fire down. Wade was sitting in a cinderblock room with a smudge of another man's mouth between him and his sister, and he had a man's name in his mouth that he would not let go of, and he was going to take the weight of it because the man whose name it was was a man he could not put down without putting down what he had been raised to hold.

I was — I did not know what I was. I was driving the Caldwell road north in a 2017 Toyota Tacoma with both hands on the wheel and a long sentence finishing itself in my head. *We don't tell, in this family,* the sentence said. *We carry the fire down.*

The sentence finished. It sat in my chest for a mile.

It was not a beautiful sentence. It was not a sentence I would have written down. It was the sentence the place had given me, in the voice of my grandfather, in the body of a thirteen-year-old who had dropped a scuttle in laurel and been spoken to once. It was the sentence the family had been carrying, on the lips of its men and in the silences of its women, for ninety years.

It was the sentence that had put my brother in a cell.

The sun went behind the ridge above Bear Branch. The light on the road turned from copper to bronze. I came up past the Methodist church, past Verna Hicks's place with the green metal roof and the lit kitchen window, past the turn for the trace up to Sorrow Hollow on the right.

I pulled into the trace turnout and put the truck in park. I did not turn it off. I sat with my hands on the wheel. The trace went off into the rhododendron at a slight uphill grade and disappeared at the second bend. The light at the bend was the last of the day.

I thought *We don't tell. We carry the fire down.*

I thought *I am going to have to be the one who tells. There is no one else.*

The sun was off the ridge. The shadow of the trace bend came toward me down the slope and put the truck in cold. My hands on the wheel were cold. I let them be cold for a count.

I put the truck in drive. I drove the quarter mile up to my mother's house. The light over the porch was on. She had turned it on at four because she had not turned it off all day.

I parked. I sat with my hands on the wheel for another count.

Then I cut the engine and got out and went up the steps and inside, and Ladonna was at the stove stirring soup beans with a wooden spoon, and the kitchen was warm, and she did not turn around to look at me, and she said, without turning, *Wash up, honey. Supper in five.*

I washed up.

I sat down at the table.

I ate.

Tomorrow I would carry the fire somewhere it had not been carried before.

Tonight I let my mother put a bowl in front of me and I ate.

---

# Chapter 16 — The Boy at the Funeral

Doyle Sturgill had been seventy-nine and a deacon and a man who had cut Ladonna's grass since 2011 without ever letting her pay him for it. He had died at the post office on a Wednesday afternoon with a money order for his grandson in one hand and a roll of stamps in the other.

Ladonna had told me the night before, sliding a casserole across the kitchen table on a hot pad. *I told Bea you'd come up to the basement if you were still here. She'd like to see you.* I had been still here. I went.

The funeral was at one. Ladonna wore the gray dress she had worn to my father's service in 2007, with the cardigan she had bought to wear over it. She had let the cardigan go shiny at the elbows in the years since and had not replaced it. She did her hair in the twist. She put her mother's ring on her right hand. She put my grandmother's pearl earrings on. She put her good shoes on. She came out of her room at twelve-thirty and stood in the kitchen doorway and said, *All right. Let's go bury Doyle.*

We drove the half mile to the church in my truck because her car was acting up and she had been meaning to take it to Ronnie's. The Bear Branch Methodist sat at a bend of the road where the creek came under a culvert and the dogwoods made a low arch over the ditch. White clapboard, white steeple, a brass bell in the steeple that still rang on Sundays. The lot was already full. The hearse was at the side door. Men in dark suits were standing in clumps on the gravel smoking. I knew most of them by face and half of them by name and one of them, before I could put a name to him, turned to look at me with the look of a man who had been waiting to be looked at, and the look was Caudill Boggs.

He was at the back of a clump near the door with three men I did not know, in a dark sport coat and a black tie, and he was holding the cigarette down at his side the way a man holds a cigarette at a funeral. He saw me see him. He did not nod. He looked at me for the count of a second and then he turned his head a quarter inch and was looking at the steeple, and he stayed looking at the steeple while we walked up the steps and went in.

*Caudill Boggs,* Ladonna said, low, in the vestibule. She had not turned her head. *Was he kin to Doyle.*

*No.*

*He's here, though.*

*He's here.*

She did not say anything else. She took the bulletin from the Halsey grandson at the door, and we went down the right aisle and sat in the Combs pew six rows from the front.

The stained glass behind the pulpit was a Slone-family memorial from 1953 to their boy who had died in Korea. The casket was open at the front. Doyle was laid out in the navy suit he had bought for his daughter's wedding in 1992, his hands on his chest in the position of a man holding the hat he had never in his life worn. A candle at the head of the casket was burning slow and even in the still air.

The hymn before the message was *Amazing Grace*, which the bulletin called for and which Bea Sturgill had asked for and which the woman at the upright piano played in the key Bea's mother had played it in for forty years before her. Ladonna sang. I did not. I have not sung in a church since I was nineteen. I stood with the hymnal open at the right page and I let the room sing around me and I watched the candle at the head of the casket make the small flickering movement that a candle makes when there is no draft and the wax has begun to pool at the lip.

Preacher Hatton was thirty-eight now and had been the preacher at Bear Branch Methodist for six years and was good at it. He said the things you say. He said them well. He said the part about Doyle being a man who showed up. He said the part about the post office and the money order and the grandson, which was the part Doyle's grandson would carry the rest of his life. He read the Twenty-Third Psalm in the version we used and prayed the prayer and asked us to bow our heads. Ladonna's hand found mine on the pew between us and held it. Her hand was the size of a child's hand. I had forgotten how small. I had also forgotten the thing her hands had always done in church which was to go very cold even in a warm room. The cold of her hand against my hand was the first thing in two weeks that had moved through my body without being a thing I had named.

We sang the closing hymn. We carried Doyle out to the hearse. The men carrying the casket were Doyle's two sons and his brother and a son-in-law and the grandson at the foot and a man I did not know at the head. Caudill Boggs was not one of them. I had been watching to see whether he would be.

The burial was at the cemetery up the ridge behind the church and the bulletin said the family would receive friends in the church basement after, and so Ladonna and I went around to the basement door and went down the stairs and into the long low room with the linoleum and the folding chairs and the smell of percolated coffee and macaroni and warm sweet potato casserole, and that was where I saw Lem.

Lem was at the corner of the long table by the kitchen with a paper plate in one hand and a paper napkin in the other, and he was looking at the banana pudding the way a man looks at a thing he has decided he is not going to do today, and when he saw me come down the stairs his face changed in a way I had not been prepared for.
