He set the plate down without putting anything on it. He came across the basement to me. He did not stop at any of the women he passed. He came across the linoleum the way a man crosses a room to a sister he has not seen in nine years and which he had not been sure he would see today.

*Della.*

*Lem.*

He hugged me. He hugged me the way a man who has been sober a year and a half hugs a person he loves. He hugged me long enough that I had time to register the smell of him — Irish Spring and pipe tobacco he was not smoking, no whiskey, the faint creosote smell of a roofer at the end of a long week.

He let me go. He took a step back. He did not meet my eyes.

*You look like Aunt Donnie,* he said.

*You look like Hadley.*

He smiled a little at that. He looked at the floor. *Mama said you was home.*

*I've been home three weeks.*

*She said.* He looked at the floor. *I should have come up to see you. I didn't know how. I figured Wade.*

*Wade.*

*I went down to the jail on Saturday. Becca let me in. I sat with him fifteen minutes. He didn't say nothing. I didn't either, mostly. We sat. I wanted you to know.*

*Thank you, Lem.*

He nodded. He did not look at me. He looked at the floor and at the table behind me and at Bea Sturgill across the room and at the door behind Bea, and he did not look at me. His hands were in the front pockets of his good Wranglers. He took one out and ran it through his hair and put it back.

*Eighteen months,* he said. *In December.*

*I know. Mama told me. Lem. That is something.*

*It's something.*

*How is your mama.*

*Mean as ever. Knees gone. She'd have come today, Doyle put a new roof on her house in '04, but the knees.* He nodded toward the table. *I brought her green beans. I cooked 'em myself. Don't tell.*

*Won't tell.*

*Della.*

*Lem.*

*I don't know what to say to you.*

*You said it.*

He looked at me then, finally, for the count of a second. He looked at me the way a person looks at a person when the look is the saying. His eyes were our grandfather's eyes, which were my brother's eyes, which were my eyes, all four of us threaded onto the same line through the four generations Hollis had thrown his hand around. Lem's looked tired. They had the slight pink-rimmed look of a man who had been keeping eighteen months of being awake at four in the morning in his sleep. He looked away again.

*I'm gonna get some banana pudding,* he said.

*Get me some.*

*Don't want the meringue.*

*I want the meringue.*

*Della.*

*The meringue is the whole thing.*

He went. I watched him go. He took two plates. He put pudding on both. He put the meringue on mine. He brought them back. He handed me mine. He stood beside me eating his and not looking at me. The light through the basement windows was the high gray light of late October at one o'clock in the afternoon, and Ladonna was across the room with Bea Sturgill holding both of Bea's hands, and the kitchen behind us had the smell of green beans being kept warm in a roaster oven, and Lem was eating banana pudding without the meringue beside me without looking at me, and I had not seen him in nine years.

He set his plate down on the table.

*I gotta go,* he said.

*Already.*

*Got a roof in Pikeville at three. Joel needs me on the tear-off.*

*Lem.*

*Della.*

*Will you come up to Mama's for supper Sunday.*

He looked at the floor. He took a long breath. He let it out.

*I will,* he said. *Sunday. I'll come.*

*Bring your mama.*

*I'll bring her.*

He hugged me again, shorter, and went up the stairs. I watched him go. Lem was the first name my mind put on the table when I was not looking at the table. Lem and Wade had been each other's only brother since they were six. Lem who had hugged me too long. Lem who would not meet my eyes.

I stood with my plate in my hand and tried not to do what I was doing. The pudding tasted of vanilla and the small extra cold of the dishpan it had sat in. The meringue was browned brown not gold, with the small ridges left by the spatula still visible at the surface.

It was while I was tasting the meringue that the old man came up to me.

He was, by the look of him, sixty-five or close to it. He was wearing a brown sport coat and a yellow shirt and a brown tie and brown loafers polished and re-polished. He had a paper plate with a chicken leg on it that he was not eating. He had the long careful walk of a man whose knees were going but who was not yet using a stick. He stopped a little to my right at a distance of perhaps three feet and waited until I looked at him.

*Ma'am.*

*Sir.*

He looked at me. He looked at me a long time. He looked at me the way a man looks at a face he is matching to another face.

*You favor your granddaddy something terrible,* he said.

I looked at him. I looked at his face. I had not seen it before that I could place. He was not a Sturgill and he was not a Hicks and he was not a Combs. He had a flat brow and a long jaw and the kind of pale eyes you see in this country and the kind of mouth that looks like the mouth of a man who keeps a thing in it. He had a small scar at the right temple that was the kind of scar a child gets from a stove handle and keeps for life.

*Thank you,* I said.

He nodded. He looked at the chicken leg on his plate. He did not eat it. He did not say his name. He looked back at me and he did not quite smile but his mouth moved a fraction at the corner the way a man's mouth moves when he is about to say one more thing and decides not to.

*Take care, ma'am,* he said.

He turned and went. He went up the stairs behind me the way Lem had gone up them. He did not say goodbye to anyone on the way. I watched him go and I had the small careful sense of a person who has just been spoken to by someone who has been waiting in this room for me to be in it.

Ladonna came up beside me with a plate of green beans she had not had the strength to refuse. *Who was that you was talking to,* she said.

*I don't know.*

She looked at the stairs. She looked at me.

*The one in the yellow shirt.*

*Yes.*

She thought for a count of two. Her eyes did the small narrowing they did when she was working a name down out of the loft of her head. *Slone,* she said. *Cal Slone. Buddy Slone's boy.*

*Buddy.*

*The man on the porch your granddaddy used to go set with after church.* She was not looking at me. She was looking at the stairs. *Cal lives over to Whitley now. Has for years. He don't come up much. Doyle was his cousin by marriage. He used to set with your granddaddy in the front room on a Saturday in the fall to watch the Wildcats. He hadn't been to anything of ours in a long time.*

*Why is he here today.*

*Della,* she said. She said it without taking her eyes off the stairs. *That's a question you ask the man.*

She turned and went to the table and put her plate down without eating from it and went back to Bea Sturgill, who was crying without making any sound and which Ladonna was not going to leave her doing alone. I stood with my own plate and my own pudding and my own piece of meringue and my own pulse beating in my throat the way it did when I came up on a wreck on a road I had been driving fast.

Cal Slone. The man my grandfather had told a partial version of the truth to on a porch in 1962, and who had carried it for thirty-seven years, and who had told his boy, and whose boy had now come three counties to a funeral and stood three feet from me and said *you favor your granddaddy something terrible* and gone.

I did not go after him. I stood. I ate the meringue. I made the small decisions a person makes when she has just been given a piece of information she is not yet equipped to spend. I would find Cal Slone in his own time. He had come to find me. He would do it again. The way he had said *take care, ma'am* had been the way a man says it who intends to.

I went up the basement stairs and out into the gravel lot. The hearse was gone. The men had gone up the ridge to the cemetery. The lot was emptier than it had been at one.

The green F-250 was at the back of the lot, nose-out, with its driver's door open and Caudill Boggs standing beside it lighting a cigarette with a cupped match. He saw me come out. He did not move toward me. He stood beside his truck and smoked and looked across the lot at me with the same long settling look he had used at the head of the casket.

He had been at Doyle Sturgill's funeral. He had no reason to be. He had come to be seen by me. He had also come, I understood now in a way I could not yet have put words to, to see who else came. He had stood at the steeple looking at Lem Combs in the basement window. He had stood at the steeple looking at Cal Slone come up the gravel.

He finished his cigarette. He dropped it. He stepped on it. He got in his truck. He started it. He backed out and rolled across the gravel and onto the road and turned right, north, up Bear Branch, away from town.

He had no business up Bear Branch. There was nothing up Bear Branch past my mother's house but the trace to Sorrow Hollow.

I stood in the lot with my mother's keys in my pocket and the taste of meringue in my mouth, and I watched the green truck go up the road. He tapped the brakes once at the bend by the dogwoods. The brake lights came on red against the brown of the leaves and went off and the truck rolled around the curve and out of sight.

---

# Chapter 17 — The Pressure

I had been at the courthouse most of the day pulling tax records on the Bear Branch parcel and on the three parcels that joined it on the east, north, and south, and I had come back to the Mountain Laurel at six-twenty in the evening with a paper folder of plat copies and a takeout box of pulled pork from the place beside the IGA that I was not going to eat. The sky over the ridge had gone the gray of November on this latitude, which was not yet a winter gray but was the gray that tells you winter is two weeks out and is on time. The neon at the motel had already come on. *Mountain  aurel,* the sign said, the *L* burnt out since 2009.

I was not thinking about the parking lot because the parking lot had no truck in it that I was not used to. The pickup belonging to the man in unit four was there. The Camry belonging to the salesman from Knoxville in unit nine was there. The Tacoma I had been driving for eight years was where I had left it. The car that did not belong to the parking lot was at the back of the lot under the dogwood, nose-out, and was a dark blue Buick Encore with a Hertz sticker visible on the back glass. I clocked it the way you clock a vehicle that has been put deliberately at the place in the lot you would put a vehicle if you wanted to see who came out of room six and seven and eight without being in the line of sight of the office window. The driver was in it. The driver had been in it for some amount of time I could not know.

I put the truck in park. I sat with my hand on the gearshift. I did the thing Hannah had begun to do with the Subaru, which was to watch a parked vehicle for what it was doing without doing anything yourself. The Buick did not move. The driver did not move. He was a shape in the front seat with his hands at ten and two on a steering wheel.

I got out. I locked the truck. I took the folder of plats and the takeout box and walked across the gravel toward my room, which was unit six, and the driver of the Buick opened his door at the moment my hand was on my key in the lock and got out and came around the front of his vehicle and crossed the lot toward me at a measured walk.

He was a man of fifty or so. Dark suit, dark overcoat unbuttoned, no hat, hair the color of an iron skillet. He carried a briefcase in his left hand and held the right one open and slightly forward, the way a man holds his right hand when he wants to make sure you can see that he is not holding anything in it. He stopped six feet from me. He smiled with his mouth.

*Ms. Combs.*

*Yes.*

*I apologize for surprising you. My name is Reed Whitlow. I'm an attorney with Whitlow, Pruitt and Lacey out of Lexington. Our firm represents Blackridge Coal Holdings. I was hoping for a few minutes of your time this evening.*

I set the takeout box down on the concrete walk in front of the door. I did not put the plats down. I held them at my side and I looked at the man.

*Mr. Whitlow.*

*Ms. Combs.*

*Did you call ahead.*

*I did not. I had been advised that you have not been answering your phone. I was in the area on other matters and chose to take the chance you'd be in.*

*The area being.*

*Pikeville,* he said. *I had a deposition at three.*

*That's a four-hour round trip to take a chance on, Mr. Whitlow.*

*It's been a long week, Ms. Combs.*

*Mr. Whitlow,* I said. *I do not want to have a conversation in my motel parking lot at six-thirty in the evening with a lawyer for the company that has been pressuring my mother to sign over the mineral rights on her house for two years and is currently watching my brother be arraigned for murder. If you want a meeting, call my brother's attorney and ask for one. Her name is Hannah Soyars. She is in the book. She returns calls.*

He nodded. He nodded slowly, the way a man nods when he has heard exactly the response he expected and was prepared with a second sentence for. *Ms. Combs. I am not here on the criminal matter. I am here on the civil matter.*

*There is no civil matter.*

*There is the matter of your mother's land,* he said. *And the position my client finds itself in.*

I did not say anything. The neon over our heads buzzed. A passing truck on KY-92 went by behind him with its lights coming on and the lights raked the side of his face in passing.

*Ms. Combs,* he said. *Let me speak plainly. My client is aware of the difficult position your family is in. My client is also aware that the criminal matter, however it concludes, will have made the continued ownership of the Combs property — given the proximity of the alleged crime, the public attention, the family stress — an additional burden on your mother that she may not wish to carry. My client is prepared to offer a substantial settlement for the mineral rights, with an additional consideration for the surface interest if your family chose to be relieved of it. The figure I have been authorized to discuss is well above any prior offer. The offer is being made now as a gesture of condolence given recent events.*

I had not, in twelve years on the road, heard a stranger try to convert a killing into a transaction in the same breath that he asked me to call it condolence.

I felt the blood go up the back of my neck and into my ears in the slow even way it does when you have been a paramedic for twelve years and have trained your blood to go where it goes without taking the rest of you with it. I did not move my hand on the folder. I did not change the line of my mouth. I looked at Reed Whitlow in the place between his eyes where I had been taught at twenty-three to look at a hostile patient who is making it clear he is about to take a swing.

*Mr. Whitlow,* I said.

*Ms. Combs.*

*Get the fuck off this property.*

It came out very quiet. I had not planned the *fuck.* I had planned *off.* The *fuck* came in front of *off* because the body sometimes makes editorial choices the brain has not approved. Mr. Whitlow did not flinch. He had heard people say *fuck* to him in parking lots before. He held his right hand a little further out, palm slightly upturned, and he reached into the inside pocket of his suit jacket with his left hand and removed a business card. He extended the card toward me at the level of my chest. The card was cream. The print on it was raised. The font was a serif font. I did not move my hand to take it.

*Ms. Combs,* he said. *I understand. I would also like to ask you to consider the offer on its merits for a period of forty-eight hours and then call me. The number on the front is my office. The number on the back is my mobile. If I do not hear from you by Monday evening, I will assume the offer has been declined, and I will advise my client accordingly. My client's options at that point may include other procedures it has not, to this point, elected to pursue.*

*Subsidence,* I said.

He blinked. Once.

*The procedure your client has not elected to pursue. Subsidence. Surface condemnation through the abuse of the broad-form deed. The procedure your client has been waving over my mother's head through the man who is now dead.*

*Ms. Combs.*

*Mr. Whitlow.*

He held the card a half-second longer. The neon buzz of the *L*-less sign sounded between us. I took the card. I did not look at it. I held it in the same hand as the folder of plats. He nodded once. He stepped back. He started to turn.

*Mr. Whitlow,* I said.

He turned.

*One question.*

*Yes.*

*Did your client send Mr. Boggs to my mother's church.*

His face did not move. It did not need to. The micro-second pause before he answered told me more than the answer.

*I would not know who you are referring to, Ms. Combs.*

*Yes,* I said. *You would.*

He looked at me for the count of two seconds. He had stopped smiling some sentences ago. The lift of his mouth was now the lift of a man making the small involuntary expression of a person who is reassessing the dimensions of the room he had thought he was in.

*Good evening, Ms. Combs.*

*Good evening, Mr. Whitlow.*

He walked back across the lot to the Buick. He got in. He closed the door with the soft solid sound of a rental-car door closing. He started the engine. He sat for ten seconds with his hand on the gearshift the way I had seen Caudill Boggs sit with his hand on the gearshift in Sara Renfro's driveway four days before.

He backed out. He rolled across the gravel. He turned left, west, onto KY-92, toward Pineville and Pikeville and Lexington and the office where he would, by ten-thirty, be writing the memo on this visit to put in the file under the heading the company called *Bear Branch — Combs.* The taillights went up the road and went down the dip past the IGA and were gone.

I stood in the lot. I had not moved my feet. The card was in my hand under the folder. The takeout box was on the walk in front of my door.

I took a breath. I let it out. I bent and picked up the takeout box. I unlocked the door. I went in. I closed the door behind me and threw the deadbolt. I set the folder and the box on the cigarette-burned dresser. I put my back against the door.

The bedside lamp was on its low setting. The room was the size and shape of every motel room I had ever stayed in for a fire conference. Two queens. A nightstand between them with a Gideon Bible in the drawer. A television. A scratched-up dresser with a coffee maker and four packets of Folgers and a paper cup. A bathroom with a wall heater that would not turn off.

I sat on the foot of the bed by the door.

I held up the card.

The front said *REED WHITLOW / ATTORNEY / WHITLOW, PRUITT AND LACEY* in the raised serif font, with a Lexington address and a phone number with a 859 area code. I turned the card over.

The back of the card had a sentence on it, written in the careful slanting hand of a man who used a fountain pen and who took his time with the letters. The ink was a wet dark blue. The downstroke of the *l* in *call* had a small bead of ink at the bottom of the stroke where the pen had paused, which is what a fountain pen does when the hand holding it has hesitated for a second on the way down. He had written the sentence in the car before he got out. He had written it before he had decided whether he was going to give it to me.

*Call me before the hearing.*

I looked at it a long time.

The hearing was the preliminary hearing on November 18, seventeen days off. The hearing was not the trial. The hearing was the procedural pivot at which the Commonwealth either bound the case over to grand jury or did not. The hearing was the date on the calendar of Wade's life around which Hannah was building every brief she would write between now and Christmas. The hearing was the date on the calendar of the company's life around which the company was building, evidently, the procedure that would proceed when Wade was bound over to grand jury and Ladonna was buried under a daughter-in-jail and the lawyer in the parking lot could call back in March and have his offer accepted.

*Call me before the hearing.*

The sentence was a thing a careful man wrote down because he did not want to say it out loud in a parking lot where someone might hear. The sentence was the company's quiet thing, said into ink, handed to me under the front of a card that bore the company's loud thing.

I set the card on the bedspread beside me.

I put my hands on my knees.

I had not cried since 2007 in the chapel at Baptist in Corbin when my mother had come up the aisle and put her hand on my shoulder and said *they could not get him back.* That was the floor I had been keeping under my crying.

I did not cry now. The body did not want to. The body wanted to do the thing I had been trained to do, which was to take a clean breath and write the report.

I took a clean breath.

I went over to the dresser. I opened the folder of plats. I took out the page that showed the Combs parcel and the three parcels east, north, and south. I laid it out on the bed. I laid the card on top of the plat, in the place where my mother's house was. The card covered the house and a hundred feet of yard on each side. The card was the size of the house at that scale.

I sat back down.

I picked up my phone. I pulled up Hannah's contact. I typed *Whitlow of Whitlow Pruitt Lacey was at my motel. Offered to buy the rights "as a gesture of condolence." Wrote a sentence on the back of his card with a fountain pen: "Call me before the hearing." Pictures coming.*

I sent it.

I sent her a photo of the front of the card. I sent her a photo of the back. I sent her a photo of the back at an angle that caught the bead of ink at the bottom of the downstroke of the *l*.

She wrote back in forty seconds. *Don't call him. Don't reply. Don't move the card. I'm in Pikeville. I will be at the motel in two hours. Eat something.*

*All right,* I wrote.

I put the phone down. I went to the dresser and opened the takeout box. The pulled pork was cold. I ate it cold from the box at the dresser standing up the way a paramedic eats, with my right hand on the plastic fork and my left flat on the wood, and the card on the bedspread behind me in the place where my mother's house was on a piece of paper from a county clerk's office two miles down the road.

The neon sign outside the window said *Mountain  aurel.*

The pulled pork had vinegar in it. The vinegar bit at the cut at the corner of my mouth from where I had bitten the inside of my lip in the parking lot without knowing I was doing it.

I made the small inward decision, in the room with the cold pork and the low lamp and the card on the bedspread, that the next thing I would do tomorrow was drive the half-mile up Bear Branch and ask my mother what she had not told me. I had been asking her the wrong question for two weeks. I had been asking her about Wade. I was going to ask her, tomorrow, about the company. I was going to ask her what the man with the fountain pen had not been the first man to put on the table she had been keeping a hymnal above.

I wiped my hand on a paper napkin.

I picked the card up off the plat. I put it on the nightstand next to the Gideon Bible. I left it face-up so I would see it when I woke.

---

# Chapter 18 — The Hymnal

I drove the half mile up Bear Branch in the early dark with the heat in the truck on its lowest setting. The cold from the parking lot the night before had stayed with me and I did not want to lose it yet. It was the small accurate temperature of what Reed Whitlow had set down in front of me.

The porch light was on. The kitchen light was on. The light in the front room was on. My mother had turned the lights on for me before five because she had known I was coming and had wanted me to see them from the road. I parked and sat for a count of three with my hands on the wheel and got out.

She had the door open before I was up the steps. She was in the dress she had worn to church that morning and she had her good shoes on. She had not changed because she had been waiting. *Della.*

*Mama.*

*Come in. The roast is in. Lem and Treva couldn't come. Treva's knee. They send love. Wash up.*

She had said this in one breath and turned and gone back to the kitchen by the time I had got my jacket off. The house smelled of the roast and of the rosemary I had brought her from the IGA on Thursday and of the coffee she had been keeping warm on the back of the stove. The hymnal was on top of the refrigerator. It had been on top of the refrigerator for as long as I had been a person who could see it. It was on top of the refrigerator now.

I washed up at the kitchen sink. The water came cold from the tap the way it always had and I drank a swallow of it before I put my hands under it and let the cold go up to my wrists. Ladonna was at the stove with her back to me. Her shoulders were the shoulders of a sixty-seven-year-old woman who had been carrying a thing for forty years and had decided, somewhere on the drive home from church this morning, that she might be done carrying it.

I dried my hands on the cup towel. I sat down at the table.

She turned. She had the wooden spoon in her hand. She looked at me a long moment with the expression of a woman who has been preparing a sentence for a year and a half and has found out, today, that she is going to need to say it.

*Della.*

*Mama.*

*You eat first. Then we talk.*

*Let me say one thing first.*

She looked at me. She set the spoon on the rest beside the burner. She did not turn the burner off. She stood with her hand on the edge of the stove.

*A man came to the Mountain Laurel last night,* I said. *A lawyer for the company. He stood in the parking lot and offered to buy your mineral rights and your house as a gesture of condolence. He told me if I didn't call him before the hearing the company would file the subsidence procedure they have been holding over you.*

She did not move.

*Mama. Tully Renfro was not the first man they sent.*

She closed her eyes for a second. She opened them. She nodded once. She turned back to the stove. She turned the burner off. She moved the pot off the heat. She came over to the table and pulled out the chair across from mine and sat down. She put her hands on the table in front of her — her small hands, the wedding ring on her right and her own on her left, the small dry hands of a woman whose body had been the body of this kitchen for forty-six years.

*No,* she said. *He wasn't.*

*Who was.*

*The first one was a man name of Maynard,* she said. *He come to your granddaddy in 1968. The week after Pap died, almost. Granddaddy run him off the porch. That one I don't remember much; I was ten. Then there was a Pruitt in '79 and a Pruitt again in '88 and a man named Robideaux in '95 and a man named Foley around 2003. Tully Renfro started coming in 2018. Hard, after your granddaddy was gone twelve years. They wait us out, Della. They wait until the man they can't run off is gone and they come for the woman.*

*And you have never told me any of this.*

She looked at me. She did not flinch. *Della. You was gone.*

*I would have come.*

*You would not have. You would have called Hannah Soyars and Hannah Soyars would have written a letter and the company would have come back in three years when Hannah was busy with a deposition in McCreary. I have been telling the company *no* for forty years. I have not needed a daughter to tell it for me. What I have needed a daughter for, I have not been able to ask one for. That is the part I will not say twice.*

I did not say anything. The clock above the sink ticked. The clock above the sink had been ticking since 1971 and had been the same clock and had been wound twice a year. It was a yellow plastic clock with black numbers and it had the cord that ran down the wall behind the refrigerator. It was the clock my grandfather had bought my grandmother in Pineville the year before he made foreman. It would, I knew without thinking it, outlast my mother. I would have to decide what to do with it. I had not yet thought about it. I thought about it now.

*Mama,* I said.

*Della.*

*The card he gave me. The lawyer. He wrote a sentence on the back of it with a fountain pen. He wrote *Call me before the hearing.* He wrote it before he got out of the car. Mama. The company is going to come at us before the eighteenth. They are not going to wait for Wade to be bound over. They are going to use the hearing as the moment.*

She nodded slowly. She had her hands flat on the table. She looked at the table. She looked at the table for a long time. Then she stood. She did not look at me. She crossed the kitchen and went to the refrigerator and stretched up on her toes and took the hymnal down from the top.

It was a softcover Cokesbury hymnal, the 1957 edition, the kind they used at the church up the road and the kind a hundred churches in this country had used until 1989 when somebody decided to update the wording in *Onward Christian Soldiers* and started a quiet war among Methodists that had not been settled by the time half of them died. The cover was dark blue with the title in gold blocked letters that had worn at the corners to the brown of the cardboard underneath. The spine had a long crack that had been mended with masking tape and the masking tape had been on the spine since I was a child. The book was, in the way old hymnals are in a house, a thing my eyes had stopped seeing some years before they had stopped seeing it. I had not picked it up since I was eleven.

She brought it to the table. She set it down between us. She put her hand on it.

*Della.*

*Mama.*

*Your granddaddy did not kill Calvin Tull.*

It was the sentence the room had been waiting for. It was the sentence the hymnal had been holding on top of the refrigerator for sixty-four years and the sentence the women in this house had been holding in their mouths for sixty-four years and had not, until this evening, been able to set down on a table in front of the right person. The sentence laid itself on the wood of the table between us and did not move.

I did not say anything. I could not have said anything. The blood went up the back of my neck the way it had gone up in the motel lot, in the slow even way it goes up in a paramedic when the situation has rearranged itself and the body is taking a second to update.

She opened the hymnal. She opened it to page 363. The page was *Just a Closer Walk with Thee*, with the music staff and the words and the third verse, which the Methodists in this church had always sung and which most other Methodists in most other churches had not. The page had been opened to often enough that the spine had cracked at it long ago and the page wanted to lie flat. Tucked between the page and the page before it was a folded envelope, yellowed at the edges to the color of strong tea, with a piece of typing-paper-color thread of something — the sort of fold paper makes after sixty years of being where it was put — visible at the seam.

She lifted the envelope out. She set it on the table in front of me. She closed the hymnal but did not put it back. She left it on the table beside the envelope. She sat down across from me.

*This is what your granddaddy wrote your grandmother in August of 1961,* she said. *Sixteen days after Calvin Tull disappeared. He give it to her the day he wrote it. She tucked it in this hymnal that night. She kept it sixty years. When she died in December of '91 she said to me that morning — clear, knew who she was talking to, knew what she was saying — she said, *Donnie, the hymnal stays on top of the refrigerator. It don't come down. Sometime your girl might need this. I never knew if she meant you or Della.* I have known what was in it since the night your grandmother died. I have not opened it since. I have not had to. I am opening it now.*

I picked the envelope up. I did not unfold it for a second. The paper was thinner than modern paper, with a soft tooth to it. It was the sort of envelope a man bought a box of in 1959 at the IGA and used the rest of his life. The flap had been opened and closed enough times to be soft at the gummed edge. The address line on the front was blank. There had been no need for an address. He had handed it to her at the kitchen table.

I unfolded the letter.

The paper was a single sheet of typing paper, folded in thirds. The handwriting was a careful slanting hand in pencil — I could see the small graphite shine at the strokes — that I had not seen since I was twenty-one and standing at the foot of my grandfather's bed reading the labels on his prescription bottles in the lamp light. The hand was unchanged from the hand on those bottles, which is the way a man's hand stays his hand from his twenties to his death-bed.

I read it.

*Vera,*

*I want you to know I didn't kill him. I want you to know who did, in case it ever falls on me, because I have been thinking on this and I believe it will. It was your father. It was Pap. He came to me afterward and told me. He had reasons that are his. He had a reason I knew about. He had a reason I did not know about that I have learned of since and that I will not put in this letter. He came up the trace the night of the 19th. I was at the deep mine until six. I went up after. He had it laid the way he had told me he would lay it. The piece on the chest and the piece on the rock. I helped him carry the man into the rhody and we put him under the deadfall and we covered him with stone enough that the dogs won't get to him and the company won't find him with what they have got to look with.*

*I helped him because what else was I going to do. He is your father. He has been a father to me since my own went. I have eaten his food at our table forty years. He has held our children. He has held you. If I had said no, he would have done it alone and gone home that night and sat at our table the next morning and asked you to pass him the molasses, and I would have known and you would not, and that is not the kind of marriage I am going to be in with you. I went up. I did what I did.*

*I did not pick up the rock. I want you to know that. I did not pick up the rock and I did not put the coal on the chest and I did not pick the spot. The spot was his. The code was his. He invented the code. I had never seen it. He told me what it meant after, in our front room, late, with the woodstove going. The piece on the chest is the man, that he broke a vow to the rock. The piece on the rock is the witness, that one man saw and will not speak. I am the witness. I will not speak.*

*Vera, I am writing this in case the witnessing is what they come for. I am the man in this county who they will think of when they need a man to put under it, because of the strike and because of Asa and because they have been wanting a hook for me since 1959. If they take me, they take me. I am writing this so that the truth is in your hand and not in the air. If they take me and you have to use it, use it. If they take me and you do not have to use it, then keep it, and one day if a child of mine or a grandchild of mine asks you straight, you let them see this. Don't volunteer. Let them ask.*

*I love you. I will be home for supper at six tomorrow. Save me the heel of the cornbread.*

*Hollis*

*August 4, 1961.*

I read it through. I read it through again. I read it through a third time because the third time my eyes went slow enough to see the part about the heel of the cornbread. The heel of the cornbread was the part that made the rest of it real.

I set the letter on the table beside the envelope.

I put my hands flat on the table on either side of the letter.

I did not cry.

I had not cried since 2007 and I did not cry now. The thing in my chest was the long slow rearrangement of every sentence I had been carrying since April of 2005. The sentence I had been carrying was *my grandfather killed Calvin Tull and told me on his deathbed.* The new sentence was *my grandfather helped my great-grandfather bury Calvin Tull and gave me a version on his deathbed in which he was the man with the rock because he did not want me to grow up knowing the version in which Pap was.*

He had given me the easier part. He had hoped I would not need it.

I had needed it now. I had needed it the night Eldon Hicks killed Tully Renfro in Sorrow Hollow with a piece of seam rock, because Eldon's father had taught him a code, and Eldon's father had learned it from Pap, and Pap had invented it.

Ladonna sat across from me with her hands on the table. She was watching my face. She was not interfering with what was happening in it. She had given me the letter tonight because tonight was the night the company had told me the procedure was about to begin, and the procedure was the thing the letter had been waiting for.

*Mama,* I said.

*Della.*

*The other thing in the letter. The reason he didn't know about and that he wrote he had learned of since. What was that.*

She did not look away from me. *Letty Anne,* she said.

I had known the name from the family tree the way one knows a name on a tree, which is not at all. Letty Anne Hicks. Pap's first wife. Died young. *Drowned,* the family tree had said. *Cumberland River, near Pineville, 1932.* The line at the bottom of the box around her name had been the line that meant *no children survived* and which I had read at fifteen and not asked about.

*Mama. What about Letty Anne.*

*That is the other letter,* she said.

*The other letter.*

*There is another envelope in this book. Your grandmother's hand. October 1973. She wrote it to you. She held it the rest of her life. She did not give it to you. She died with it in this book on top of this refrigerator. I have not read it. She told me not to. She told me you would.*

I looked at the hymnal on the table between us.

I looked at my mother.

The clock above the sink ticked.

The woodstove in the front room cracked once with a piece of oak shifting in its bed and the cracking went through the small house and into the kitchen where we were.

I picked up the letter. I read the part about the heel of the cornbread one more time. I folded the letter back into thirds along its old folds. I slid it back into the envelope. I opened the hymnal to page 363 and laid the envelope between the pages where Vera had laid it sixty-four years ago. I closed the hymnal. I held my hand on the cover for a count of three. The cover was warm where my hand was on it from the kitchen and was cool around the edge.

*Mama.*

*Della.*

*I'm staying.*

She did not say anything for a long time. She looked at the hymnal. She put her hand on top of mine on top of the hymnal. Her hand was small. Her hand was the size of a child's hand. Her hand was the hand of a woman who had been keeping this kitchen for forty-six years and who had been keeping this hymnal for thirty-four of them and who was now, finally, going to be helped to keep it by another woman in this same room.

*I know it,* she said.

*Tomorrow I'll bring my things from the motel.*

*Wade's room is made up. Yours has been since the night you got into town.*

*Mama.*

*Della.*

The woodstove cracked again. The clock ticked. The hymnal sat on the table with my hand and my mother's hand on its cover, with sixty-four years of paper between the cover and a folded letter that had finally said its sentence out loud.

I sat with my mother at the kitchen table at the end of Bear Branch on the second of November of 2025 and I did not get up for a long time.

When I did get up, I put the hymnal back on top of the refrigerator. I put it back at the angle my grandmother had put it at in 1961.

I went and turned the burner back on under the roast.

We ate at seven. We did not talk about the letter while we ate. We talked about what time Lem and Treva would come up next Sunday and whether the gutter on the east side of the house would last until spring.

I cleared the table. I washed the dishes. My mother dried.

I went to bed at ten. I slept. I did not dream of my grandfather. I dreamed of a kitchen.

---

# Chapter 19 — Re-Reading the Case

We worked through it on the floor of Hannah's office because the desk was no longer large enough for what we were looking at.

She had come back from the bar on the corner with two cans of Diet Coke and a paper bag with two crullers in it from a gas station that closed at nine, and she had set the bag on the floor between us without comment and gone to the whiteboard and erased everything that had been on it since the second week of October. She erased the names. She erased the timeline. She erased the bracket of arrows that had connected *Tully Renfro* to *Wade Combs* to *Caudill Boggs* in the configuration we had been working with for three weeks. When she was finished she stood with the eraser in her hand and looked at me and said, *All right. We start over.*

It was a Monday night in early November. The third floor of the old Citizens Bank building in Pikeville had the smell it had every night past nine — toner, floor polish, the cold corner where the radiator did not reach. The window behind Hannah's desk gave onto Main Street and a stoplight that had no traffic to direct. The light worked anyway. Green. Yellow. Red. Green again.

I had driven to her office straight from my mother's. I had the hymnal letter folded inside a plain manila envelope on the passenger seat the whole way and I had not looked at the envelope, not once, the ninety miles east. The road had done what the road does in the dark on those mountains — it had narrowed me down to two lanes of attention and a song on the radio that I could not have named two hours after hearing it. I had pulled into Hannah's lot at a quarter past seven and sat in the truck for ten minutes before I went up. When I came in she took one look at me and said, *Della. Sit down. Tell me what you know now that you didn't know yesterday.*

I told her. I gave her the letter to read. She read it twice, the second time with her finger moving under the lines the way a child reads, which is the way a lawyer reads a document she does not want to misremember in court. When she was done she set it on the desk and put her hand flat on it for a second and then she got up and went to the whiteboard and erased it down to nothing.

*All right,* she said now. *If your granddaddy did not kill Calvin Tull, then everything we have been thinking about who knew the signature is wrong.*

*Yes,* I said.

*And if Pap killed him, then the signature begins with Pap. With your great-grandfather.*

*Yes.*

*And the chain that comes down from Pap is not the Combs chain. It is the Hicks chain. Because Pap's son — the one by his second wife — was a Hicks.*

*Roy Hicks,* I said. *Pap married Eliza Hopkins in 'thirty-four. Their boy was Roy. Roy took his mother's surname. He died in 'three.*

*Why did he take her name.*

*Because of a piece of land in Bell County that came down through the Hopkins side. You had to have the name to take the deed. It was easier to give the boy the name than to fight the title company about it. My mama explained it to me one time, but I was little. People did that here.*

*All right,* she said.

She wrote on the whiteboard. *PAP.* And under it, *HOLLIS — witness/accomplice. Knew the code. Dead 2005.* And below that, on the other side of the line she drew, *ROY HICKS — son of Pap by second marriage. Knew the code? — likely. Dead 2003.* And below that, *ROY'S CHILDREN — ???*

She turned and looked at me. *Della. Who are Roy Hicks's children.*

I sat on the floor with my back against the file cabinet and I made myself think. The dead men in my family I could name without trying. The living were harder. I had been gone twenty-two years and what I had not paid attention to in those years was the shape of a generation past mine in the houses up Bear Branch and Tinker Branch and the back roads off the Caldwell road that were not on any map any more. I had a paramedic's memory of names; that is to say, I could remember the name of a sixty-eight-year-old woman in pulmonary edema I had run on twice in 2019 because I had written it down both times, but I could not without effort tell you who lived in the house across the field from my mother's. I closed my eyes. I thought about my mother's kitchen and the wall by the back door where she had pinned the church directory for as long as I could remember, two pages stapled on cardboard and updated by hand in pencil when anybody moved or died.

*Eldon,* I said. *Eldon Hicks. He is forty-nine. Lives over on the back side of Tinker.*

*Just Eldon.*

*No. He has a sister. Marsha Hicks Boggs. She is older. She lives in Lexington — moved when her husband took a job at the parts plant in 'eighty-eight. They had no children that lived. She had two miscarriages.*

I had not remembered I knew that. The way it came up surprised me. It came up the way a vital sign comes up off a tape — you do not know you have read it until it is in your hand.

*Two children of Roy,* Hannah said. *And Eldon has children of his own?*

*Two. A boy and a girl. The boy is in the Marines or was. Camp Lejeune. The girl is in high school still. Maybe a freshman now. Her name is Karis. The boy I do not remember.*

*Anybody else in the Hicks line. Cousins. Half-brothers. Adoptions.*

*Roy had a brother,* I said. *Pap and Eliza had two boys. The other one was named Conley. He died at sixteen in 'fifty-three. There were no children.*

*Anybody by Pap's third wife.*

*There wasn't a third wife. Eliza died in 'forty-seven. Pap moved into Mama and Daddy's back bedroom after that. He lived there till he stroked out in 'sixty-eight. He never remarried.*

She wrote it on the board. The Hicks line, as it ran down, narrowed to a single man in Caldwell County who was old enough to have known the code from his father's mouth. *Eldon.* She wrote it bigger than the rest.

*Tell me about Eldon,* she said.

I tried. I told her what I had. Eldon was eight years older than I was. I had a memory of him at a church picnic when he was maybe seventeen and I was eight, throwing a softball overhand at the back of the schoolhouse for some boy who was catching for him with a piece of cardboard wrapped around his hand. The throw had been very hard and very straight. The boy had caught it and laughed and looked at the place where the ball had hit the cardboard. I told her this and then I felt foolish telling her this and said, *That is what I have. He was a kid. I left when he was twenty-seven. I have not seen him in nine years and the last time I saw him was at Annie Couch's lunch and he did not speak to me and I did not speak to him.*

*What does he do for a living.*

*He's a foreman at Blackridge,* I said. *The strip site up on the Cumberland Plateau. Twenty miles north of the holler. He's been there fifteen, sixteen years. He started as a haul-truck driver out of high school.*

She set down the marker. The cap clicked against the tray. She put both her hands in her hair and pulled it back from her face and held it there with her fingers and looked at the whiteboard with the look I had seen on her face exactly once before, which was the day she had told me, in this same office on the floor, that there was a path to win this case and the path went through a place she did not want to ask me to go.

*Della,* she said. *Your killer works for the company.*

*Yes.*

*Your killer is kin to you.*

I did not answer that one with a word. I put the diet Coke down on the floor between us. The can went on the carpet without a coaster and I let it.

*Your killer learned how to leave a piece of coal on a chest from his father, who learned it from his father, who learned it from the man your grandmother kept a hymnal for.*

*Yes.*

*This is not a stranger who happened to know the signature. This is a member of your family who inherited it.*

I picked up the cruller she had put in my hand and set it back on the napkin without eating it. I nodded once.

She was quiet for a long time. Then she sat down on the floor across from me with her back against the desk and she put one of the crullers in her hand without eating it, and she said, *I keep waiting for this to feel like a break. It is a break. It is the break. But I cannot make it feel like one. Can you.*

*No,* I said.

*Why not.*

*Because it does not make Wade less guilty if he was there with his cousin. It makes him more entangled, not less.*

*That is true,* she said. *But it does mean somebody else was there. It does mean there is a man we can name now.*

*A man,* I said.

*Della.*

I waited.

*Your granddaddy did not pass the signature down to you to indict your brother. He passed it to you because he knew it might come back. He was trying to put a fire extinguisher in the right hands.*

I did not say anything for a count. I looked at the cruller in her hand and the way her thumbnail had pressed into the glaze. I thought, *He gave me the strike-and-brother version. He let me carry it as if it were his. He did not tell me Pap. He told me a story he could stand to have me know, in the only voice he had left, and he told it five months before he died, in April, with his oxygen tube laid across the sheet like a small white snake. And he had a reason to do it like that. He thought the day might come when I would have to use it. He could not give me the whole load because he did not want it inside me unless I needed it. He thought he could trust me to be where I would be needed.* And here I was, in November, on the floor of a lawyer's office in Pikeville, holding a manila envelope with the truer version in it, and the man my granddaddy had been preparing me for was a foreman at the same company my granddaddy had spent thirty-one years inside of, and the strip site that man worked at was lit up at night across the valley from my mother's house like a row of low stars that did not move.

*All right,* I said. *Walk it for me. What does it look like if it is Eldon.*

She was already on it. She pulled the legal pad over and started writing in the long thin hand she wrote in when she was thinking out loud. *Motive: company has been pressuring the Combs for the mineral rights. Eldon works inside the company. Eldon knows the company is preparing a subsidence claim that would force the strip-mining of Bear Branch. Eldon is a Hicks; his cousins live on Bear Branch and would also be condemned. Eldon's motive is kin-protective. Tully is the instrument; the company is the engine. Eldon does not want the engine; he wants the instrument removed and the procedure stalled. He kills Tully to slow the claim and to send a signal to the company that this land is not free.*

I watched her write it. It was the cleanest framing of a motive I had ever heard somebody draw at speed. Hannah at her best was a person who could read a situation the way I could read a patient — fast, without ornament, lethal.

*Opportunity,* she went on. *Eldon works the area. He knows the back trace up to Sorrow Hollow because his daddy took him up there as a boy the same way your granddaddy took Wade. The hollow is on Combs land and the Hicks are tenants in everything but title here. He could be there on a Sunday afternoon and nobody would think a thing.*

*Means.*

*Means he has too. Seam rock. There is seam rock on every flat in that drainage. He picks one up on the walk in. He doesn't need to bring anything but his hands.*

*The hammer in Wade's truck.*

*Misdirect. A truck like Wade's has a hammer in it. The fact that there is no blood on it is the only thing keeping the Commonwealth from being able to put a weapon in our boy's hand. We've already known it was a misdirect. We just didn't know who planted it. Eldon could have. Or Tully had a roofing hammer in his own truck and they were going to talk about a roof — anything. We'll know more when we know more.*

She put the marker down again. She looked at me.

*Della,* she said. *Tell me you've considered that Wade knows.*

*I have considered it.*

*Tell me what you think.*

*I think Wade went up there because somebody asked him to come. He told me himself. *I went up there to see about a thing.* He has been saying that to me for three weeks. He is not going to tell me what the thing was. He is protecting somebody. If it is Eldon — if his cousin asked him to come along — Wade might have been there. Wade might have watched.*

She let that sit.

*And the second piece of coal,* she said.

*I have considered that too.*

*Della. If Wade placed the second piece of coal —*

*Then Wade is, under our statute, an accessory after the fact at a minimum. He may also be construable as a principal in the second degree, depending on what the Commonwealth thinks they can prove about prior knowledge. I have looked it up.*

*You looked it up.*

*I am a paramedic,* I said. *I look things up.*

She did not smile. She rubbed her eyes with the heels of her hands. *Okay,* she said. *Let me think about that one.*

I let her think.

The room was very quiet for a long minute. Somebody down on Main Street ran a stoplight. I heard the engine open up through the gear change and go off east toward the river. The radiator in the corner clanked twice and was still.

*You know what I keep coming back to,* she said.

*What.*

*That whoever left the second piece of coal in October left it for a witness, by the rules of the code as you have just laid them out to me. The first piece is for the dead man. The second piece is for somebody alive who saw it. Somebody who is now bound, by the code, not to speak. And if Eldon killed Tully and Wade was the witness, then Eldon left the second piece of coal *for Wade.* As a binding. As an inheritance.* She looked at me. *He was trying to make your brother carry it.*

*Yes.*

*And your brother won't tell us because the code says he can't, and because if he tells us he indicts himself, and because he is your brother and he is going to do what your family has always done in this kind of fix.*

*Yes.*

*Which is what.*

*Which is carry it. We don't tell. We carry the fire down.*

The phrase came out of my mouth before I had decided to let it. I had not said it aloud to her before. I had said it in my head, in the truck driving back from the jail at the end of October, and I had not said it since.

She wrote it on the whiteboard. *We carry the fire down.* She wrote it small, in the bottom corner, the way you write a name you intend to remember.

She put the marker in the tray. She came back and sat across from me and finally bit the cruller and chewed it and swallowed and washed it down with Diet Coke that had gone warm in the can.

*Della,* she said. *We are not going to break Wade tomorrow.*

*No.*

*We are going to build the picture around him. We are going to build it so complete that when we put it in front of him he can see we already know, and the only thing he is choosing is whether to let his cousin walk out of the holler with this on him, or to put it down himself. We are going to go talk to Eldon's wife. Not Eldon. Marlene. She is a dialysis tech in town. I went to school with her sister. She will sit with you for coffee.*

*All right.*

*We are going to talk to Wendell Cornett, the old deputy. He may remember things about 1961 that nobody else does. He is not going to live forever.*

*All right.*

*We are going to go down to the library and pull microfiche on Letty Anne Hicks. Pap had a reason of his own for Calvin Tull and it was older than the strike. I want to know what it was, because whatever Eldon thinks he's avenging by killing Tully Renfro for what the company is about to do — that pattern in your family did not start in 'fifty-nine. It started before that. We need to know how far back.*

*All right.*

She looked at me. *Della. You all right.*

*No,* I said. *But I am the one who has to be.*

She did not argue. She got up. She went to the whiteboard and across the top of it, big, she wrote three words. *FIND ELDON HICKS.* Then she went around her desk and sat in her chair and pulled the legal pad over and started a new list, and the list had on it the people I was going to have to talk to and the order to talk to them in and what I was going to need from each of them before I sat down across a plexiglass window from my brother and asked him to tell me, finally, what he had been refusing to tell me since the night my mother called me on the bay floor of Station Fourteen in Knoxville at five-thirty-eight in the morning on the twelfth of October.

I drove home at midnight. The stoplight on Main was on a blinker by then. The mountains east of Pikeville were a darker dark in the dark, and the road through them was empty for fifty miles, and the moon was a knife.

I thought about the row of work lights across the valley from my mother's house. I thought about whose hands had put them up there. I thought about whose hand was on a switch tonight, maybe, and whose hand had been on a piece of coal on the fifth of October, and how those hands belonged to the same man.

I thought, *He is family. He is family and the same blood I am.*

I rolled the window down for the last twenty miles and let the cold come in and clean me out for what I was going to have to do in the morning.

---

# Chapter 20 — Eldon

I did not go to Eldon. I went to his wife.

Marlene Hicks worked Tuesday through Saturday at the dialysis clinic in the strip building on KY-92 that had been a hardware store when I was a girl and was a Family Dollar between the hardware store and the clinic and was now, for the second decade, the only place in Caldwell County where you could get your blood cleaned without driving an hour to Pikeville. The clinic was open from six in the morning until seven at night and ran four shifts of patients through eight chairs, and Marlene had been a tech there for nineteen years. I had not known any of this on Monday night in Hannah's office. By Tuesday at noon I knew it from Becca Couch at the jail, who knew it because her aunt was on dialysis three days a week and Marlene was the tech she liked. Becca had given me the information across the dispatch counter with a Styrofoam cup of coffee in her hand and a look that said she knew I was not asking about Marlene because I wanted my aunt put on her chair.

*She's been having a hard fall,* Becca said. *I won't say more than that to you.*

*You don't have to.*

*Della.*

*I know,* I said. *I am being careful.*

I drove the half-mile to the clinic and parked in the back lot under the dogwood that had lost most of its leaves in the rain the week before. I had not eaten lunch. I had not eaten breakfast either. I sat in the truck with the engine off and looked at the brick wall of the clinic and the door beside the dumpster that the techs used for their breaks, and I thought about how I would have done this in my own register — assess, stabilize, hand off, leave — and how I was about to have to do it in another one, which was the register of a woman about to ask another woman whether her marriage had broken and, if it had, what had broken it. I rehearsed nothing. Rehearsing was for paramedics who did not know their job. I had not rehearsed a conversation with a patient since 2014 and I was not going to start.

Marlene came out at one-fifteen for her lunch. I knew her at thirty yards. She had been in my brother's class in school and she was the kind of woman who had been pretty at fifteen and was something better at forty-seven, which is to say her face had organized itself around the work it had done. She had on lavender scrubs and a fleece zipped to the throat and she was carrying a Tupperware container and a Diet Mountain Dew, and she stopped when she saw me and she stood for a beat at the curb the way somebody stands when they have just decided not to pretend they don't recognize you.

I got out of the truck. *Marlene,* I said.

*Della Combs.* She did not move toward me. *I heard you was back.*

*I'm back.*

*For Wade.*

*For Wade.*

*Becca said you were at the jail this morning.*

*She did.*

She looked at the Tupperware in her hand. She looked at me. She said, *I don't take a break with anybody usually. I sit in my car. There's an Adirondack chair around behind the building somebody's husband made for the techs, and there's an extra one. If you want to sit a minute we can do that. I have thirty.*

*I'd be obliged.*

We went around the building. The chairs were where she said. The wood had silvered to the color of November sky. She sat in one and I sat in the other and the dumpster was over to the right and the kitchen window of the clinic was over to the left and the back lot ran to a chainlink fence that looked over a vacant lot where there had once been a body shop and was now a stand of sumac and a refrigerator somebody had hauled out and left.

She opened the Tupperware. It was tuna fish on white bread. *Half?*

*Thank you, no. I ate.* I had not. I did not want her sandwich in my hand making me feel like a person she had fed. I wanted my hands free.

*Suit yourself.* She took a bite. She chewed it the way a person chews when she has been chewing alone all week and would rather not start performing the act for company.

I let her finish the first half before I said anything. There is a way you sit with a patient when you have asked her to lie down and you are waiting for her pulse to settle before you take it for the second time. It is the same posture as sitting with a person you do not know well in autumn light behind a clinic. You are not absent. You are quiet. The quiet says, *I have time. I will not push you. Whatever you want to tell me, I will hear.* Marlene did not say anything for a while. The wind came up. A Doordash car cut through the lot looking for the road and turned around at the dumpster and went out. A starling sat on the lip of the dumpster and looked at us and went.

She wrapped the second half of her sandwich back in the foil. She set the Tupperware on the arm of her chair. She put her hands in the pockets of her fleece. She said, *Della. What do you want to know.*

I did not pretend. *I want to know how you are.*

She made a small sound that was not a laugh and was not a cry. She looked at the sumac at the back of the lot.

*I am all right at work,* she said. *I am all right with the patients. I am all right driving home if I take the long way through the church bottom because it gives me time to come back into the house from the road slow. After that I am not all right and have not been since the third week of August.*

*The third week of August.*

*The Tuesday. He went up to his daddy's old cabin one Sunday and he never come back the way he went up. He come back quiet. He has not been loud in twenty-some years, but he has not been *that* kind of quiet ever. After that Sunday he started sleeping in the basement. He told me his back was bothering him and our mattress was a torment. He did not have a back complaint before that Sunday. He has not slept in our bed since.*

I did not say anything.

*That's eleven weeks tomorrow,* she said. *I counted.*

The wind moved in the sumac. I thought, *Eleven weeks ago is the third week of August. The killing was the fifth of October. He did not start sleeping in the basement after the killing. He started sleeping in the basement six weeks before the killing.* I sat very still and let the thought walk all the way to the back of my mouth and stop.

*Marlene,* I said. *Has he been going up to his daddy's cabin since.*

*Every Sunday. The four Sundays in September. The Sundays after.* She thought. *He missed the Sunday after they found that man. He did not go up on the twelfth. He went up on the nineteenth and every Sunday since.*

*He never went up there before August.*

*Not in five years that I know of. He hated his daddy. Roy used to take him up there and beat him with a leather belt for things he had not done. Eldon was the one of the two boys who stayed. He stayed for me. He stayed because we got married when he was twenty-two and I was twenty and I would not leave my mama. He stayed because that is what he does. He is the staying kind. But he was the staying kind in spite of his daddy, not because of him. He had not been to that cabin since Roy died.*

*When did Roy die.*

*Oh-three. November of oh-three. I was in there with him at the end. Eldon was not. Eldon was at home.*

*And now Eldon goes up every Sunday.*

*Every Sunday since the middle of August.*

I let it sit. I watched her face. There was a thing happening in it that I had seen happen in faces in trauma bays and on shoulders of country roads — the face of a person who is finally telling another person a thing she has been holding by herself, and the face is half relieved and half terrified at the relief, because the relief means the holding was real and she had not been making it up.

*Marlene,* I said. *Has he said anything to you about Tully Renfro.*

She looked at me for a long second. Then she said, *No.*

*Has he said anything to you about Wade.*

*He said it was a sorry thing. He said it twice. The day they arrested Wade and the next day. Both times he was at the kitchen sink with his back to me. He has not said it again.*

*Has he said anything to you about my mother.*

She blinked. *He went to see Ladonna on the sixth of October. That was the Monday. I remember because it was the day after that man was killed up the holler and I did not know that yet, none of us did, the body was not found till the Tuesday. Eldon come home from work on Monday and changed his shirt and got back in his truck and drove down to your mama's. He was gone two hours. He come home, he ate supper, he did not say a word about it. I asked him at the table where he had been and he said, *I went to see Aunt Donnie.* That was all. I asked him how she was. He said, *She's holding up.* I asked him if it was about the lease. He said, *No, it wasn't about the lease.* And he went out and split kindling till it was dark.*

I sat with my hands flat on the wooden arms of the Adirondack chair. I felt the grain of the wood under my thumb. The wind moved.

*Marlene,* I said, *had Tully Renfro ever come around your house.*

*Once. Last fall. He talked to Eldon outside on the porch for maybe twenty minutes and Eldon come back in the house and would not eat his supper. I asked him what they had talked about and he said it was company business and I did not need to know. I did not push him. I have not pushed him about a thing in our marriage and that is one of the things wrong with our marriage, and I am here telling that to you on this chair on this dumpster lot.*

*You are not wrong to push him.*

*I am not wrong to push him. I am only late.*

The starling came back. It went away again.

She looked at her watch. *I got ten minutes before I have to go back in. Della. Why are you asking me about Eldon.*

I had been waiting for it. I had decided on the answer in the truck. I had decided to tell her the truth because she had given me the truth and because lying to her would have been the worst kind of theft. I said, *Marlene. My brother did not kill that man. The Commonwealth thinks he did. I am trying to figure out who else was up there. I am asking about Eldon because Eldon works for the company. Because Eldon is kin to me. Because somebody I trust told me last night that whoever killed Tully Renfro left a sign at the scene that nobody else in this county outside of two families would have known to leave, and one of those families is yours.*

She did not move. She looked at me.

*I am not the police,* I said. *I am not going to go to the sheriff with what you tell me. I am going to use it to figure out what happened and I am going to use it to keep my brother from going to prison for what somebody else did. That is all I am going to do. If what you tell me ends up sending your husband to a jail cell, I will not lie to you and say it could not. But I will tell you straight: if I were you and what I had been carrying was what you have been carrying, I would want somebody to come to me on a chair behind a building in the middle of the day and ask. I do not know if that is fair. I am asking anyway.*

She breathed in once, very deeply. She breathed out.

*I have known something was wrong with him for eleven weeks,* she said. *I did not know what. I do not know now. I have a guess. I am not going to say my guess out loud because if I say it out loud I will have to do something with it and I have not figured out what I am willing to do.*

*That is fair.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*When you do whatever you are about to do, you remember he has a daughter still in school. Her name is Karis. She is fifteen. She is the only thing on this earth that man has gotten right. You do whatever you have to do for your brother. I am not asking you to spare my husband. I am asking you to think about Karis.*

*I will think about Karis.*

She nodded once. She picked up her Tupperware. She picked up her Diet Mountain Dew. She stood up. She looked at me a long second from above.

*One more thing,* she said.

*Yes.*

*He keeps a thing in the basement now. Wrapped in an oilcloth, in a milk crate, behind the water heater. I went down there one night last week looking for the propane lighter and I about put my hand on it. I did not unwrap it. I do not want to know what it is. I told myself it was tools. It is not tools. Tools do not get wrapped in oilcloth and put behind a water heater. Whatever it is, it is up there now. He went down to the basement the morning they arrested Wade and he was down there a long time. He put it there then. I think.*

*Marlene.*

*I am not going down there again. I am telling you because I have told nobody. I am telling you because if my husband did the thing I am afraid he did, then whatever is in that oilcloth is the thing I will be asked about in two years by a man in a suit and I am not going to be able to say I did not know. I am telling you so somebody else knows besides me.*

She turned and walked across the lot. At the door she stopped and turned back and said, *You favor your granddaddy too. I never thought so when we were kids. You do now.*

She went inside. The door swung shut behind her on the slow piston the door had. The lot was quiet. The starling did not come back. I sat in the chair for another five minutes because I did not trust my hands on the steering wheel, and then I got up and walked to the truck and got in and drove out of the lot and turned south on KY-92 and went the long way through the church bottom because Marlene was right that it was the way a person came back into the world slow.

In the truck I made a mental list and I made it in run-report cadence because that was the cadence I had left.

*Subject: Eldon Hicks, 49, foreman Blackridge Coal Holdings, lives 1413 Tinker Branch.*
*Behavioral change: third week of August 2025, approximately Aug 19.*
*Onset: returned from one-day visit to deceased father's cabin, Tinker Branch upper drainage.*
*Sleep pattern altered, basement, persistent.*
*Spousal communication: minimal, withdrawn.*
*Recent activity: weekly Sunday return to father's cabin since onset.*
*Contact with potential witness Ladonna Combs: October 6, 2025, two hours, undisclosed subject matter. (Sixteen hours after estimated time of death of T. Renfro.)*

I drove and I added it. I added that he had begun going to his father's cabin six weeks before the killing. That meant he had been preparing for something. That meant the killing was not impulse. That meant he had taught himself, for six Sundays of August and September, in a cabin where his father had once taught him a code with a belt, what his father had taught him about what to do with a man at a seep and a piece of cannel coal.

I added it. I drove the long way home. The sky over Bear Branch went pale at the edges and a wedge of geese went south above the church bottom calling once and once.

I thought about a cabin I had never been inside and a milk crate and an oilcloth and a fifteen-year-old girl named Karis who did not know yet what her father had done. I thought about a Tuesday in August when a man had gone up a hollow alone and had decided, sitting in his dead father's cabin, to do a thing his great-grandfather had done in 1961 because he had figured out — alone, on a Sunday, with the door shut — that the company was about to take the whole holler and the only way to stop it was to take a piece of it back.

I drove past the IGA. I drove past the Phillips 66 where the windows were lit and a man I did not know was filling a Ford Ranger. I drove past my father's parking lot. I did not look at it.

I went to my mother's. I came up the porch. Ladonna was in the front room reading the paper with her glasses on the end of her nose, and she looked up when I came in and she said, *You all right, honey,* and I said, *Mama. I need to ask you something and I need you to tell me the truth.* She set the paper down on her lap. She took her glasses off. She folded them and laid them across the paper.

*All right,* she said.

*Eldon came to see you on the sixth of October. The Monday. Two hours.*

She did not move. She looked at me.

*Mama.*

*I will tell you about that,* she said. *I will tell you all of it. But not tonight. Tomorrow, Della. Tomorrow morning. There is one person I want to go see first.*

She had not made the answer a refusal. She had made it a date. I sat down on the arm of the couch and I looked at her. She looked at me. The clock on the wall above the television ticked. The propane heater kicked on in the kitchen and ran for a minute and kicked off.

*Mama,* I said. *Who do you want to go see.*

*Wendell Cornett,* she said. *In the home in Williamsburg.*

I had been about to say the name myself.

She picked up her glasses. She put them back on. She folded the paper and laid it on the arm of the chair. She got up. She said, *I will leave with you at seven,* and she went into the kitchen, and I heard the cabinet open and the rattle of two cups taken down for the morning, and I sat on the arm of the couch and listened to it the way a person listens to a sound she has known her whole life and has only just now understood is a sound.

---

# Chapter 21 — Old Honaker

We left Bear Branch at seven sharp and did not talk on the way down except about the weather and how Wade was eating since they had him in the county jail. She had brought a thermos and poured me a cup at the Phillips 66 in Pineville without asking how I took it. We were going to a man.

Cumberland Manor was off US-25W behind a Tractor Supply and a Hardee's and the parking lot had been resurfaced in the summer. Inside the home smelled the way they all smell — warmed-over peas, antiseptic, lavender from a plug-in air freshener somebody had put in the lobby to do work it could not do. The woman at the desk knew Ladonna. I had not known my mother visited Wendell Cornett. I learned it in the hallway from the way she walked it. She did not look at the room numbers. She turned at the second hall and went to the fifth door on the left and knocked once on the open jamb and said, *Wendell. I brought somebody.*

He was in the chair by the window. He was eighty-four years old and his shoulders had pulled in toward his sternum the way a man's shoulders do when he has spent forty years bending into a desk and twenty more sitting in chairs that did not fit him. His hair was the white of a turkey feather. The skin on the back of his hands was the color of wax paper. He wore a flannel shirt buttoned to the throat and a pair of gabardine slacks and bedroom slippers, and on the table by his elbow there was a paperback I knew the cover of without reading the title and a glass of water and a little plastic Communion cup with a wafer in it that he had not yet eaten.

*Donnie,* he said. *Lord have mercy. And.* He looked at me. He looked a long time. *Wendell's girl?*

*His daughter,* Ladonna said. *Della.*

*Della.* He sat with the word in his mouth. *I remember now. You was eleven the last time I seen you. You was at the picnic.*

*I was.*

*You favor him a little. You favor your granddaddy more.*

*People keep telling me that.*

*They will keep telling you. Set down, honey. Set down, Donnie. They got two chairs in here so the priest can sit with the wife and there ain't been a priest in here all year. The wife is gone too. Set down.*

We sat. Ladonna in the chair nearest the bed. I in the one by the window. The light from the window came at a low angle and laid itself across his lap and onto the side of Ladonna's face and made her look, for one second, like a photograph of herself I had not seen in twenty years and could not have placed.

Wendell looked at his daughter-in-law of sorts — at the woman whose father-in-law had been his friend for forty years — and said, *Donnie. What did you bring her down here for.*

Ladonna had not taken her coat off. She did not take it off now. She put her hands in her lap, one over the other, the wedding ring of her mother that she wore on her right hand catching the light.

*Wendell,* she said. *Della needs to ask you about 'sixty-one.*

He did not look surprised. He looked at me. He looked at her. He looked at the window.

*I knowed somebody would,* he said. *I did not know it would be you all.*

*Wendell.*

*Donnie. Hush. Let the girl ask it.*

I waited a second. I had been a paramedic for twelve years. The thing you learn in twelve years is when to let the person in front of you set the pace. I let him set it.

He set it. *Calvin Tull,* he said. He said the name the way you say a name you have not said aloud in twenty years and have said in your head ten thousand times.

*Yes,* I said.

*Disappeared the nineteenth of July, nineteen-sixty-one,* he said. *I was a deputy then. I was twenty. Caleb Honaker was sheriff. He was not a bad man. He was a tired one.*

He paused. He drank from his water. He set it back.

*Old man Honaker,* he said, *was sheriff when your granddaddy worked the strike.*

*I know.*

*He was sheriff when your granddaddy come out of Harlan jail in 'fifty-nine. He was sheriff when your great-uncle Asa died in the McCreary section in September of that year. He was sheriff when Calvin Tull went up Sorrow Hollow in July of 'sixty-one and did not come back.*

He looked at me. His one good eye was the right. The left was clouded with cataract. He looked at me through the one good one with a steadiness that was the steadiness of a man who had been waiting a long time for somebody to come and was no longer surprised when they did.

*Della,* he said. *Everybody in this county knew Hollis Combs had a reason to kill Calvin Tull. The reason was your great-uncle in a roof fall. The reason was the strike. The reason was Hollis come out of Harlan jail with six weeks of nights to think about the man who had cleared the section that took his brother. And the company knew it. And Caleb Honaker knew it. And me and Buddy Slone and Henley Profitt and every deputy and every bailiff and every man who worked under a foreman in that mine knew it.*

*Yes.*

*And everybody in this county also knew Hollis Combs was not the kind of man to do it that way.*

I sat still.

*Hollis would have shot him at the company gate at noon,* he said. *He would have walked up to the gate with a deer rifle and shot him from twenty yards and he would have set the rifle down and put his hands behind his head and waited.
