He would have done it where everybody could see him do it. He would have done it so the company could not pretend it was about anything else. That was the kind of man your granddaddy was. He was made out of the strike. He was a public man. He did not do things in private if he could help it. He did not do things in the dark.*

*And the killing was in the dark.*

*The killing was up a hollow at the edge of his own land,* Wendell said. *The killing was a man not seen again. The killing was a body never found. The killing was a missing-person report and a sheriff who closed the file in three months for lack of evidence. The killing was somebody trying to make it look like it meant something. *That was somebody making a point. That was somebody trying to make it look like coal-country justice. That was somebody decorating it.* That was not Hollis.*

He sat with it. He looked at the window. A jay landed on the redbud outside. The jay was the color of the sky beyond it and the jay shook itself once and went.

*Wendell,* I said.

*Yes, honey.*

*If it wasn't Hollis. Who was it.*

He looked at me. He looked at Ladonna. Ladonna was very still. Her hand was folded over the other on her lap and her wedding ring caught the light and was the brightest thing in the room.

*Donnie,* he said. *Have you told her about Pap.*

Ladonna did not move. She said, *Some of it. Not all. She has the letter.*

*She has the letter.*

*She has Hollis's letter to Mama. From the August after.*

*Then she knows who.*

*She knows who.*

*She does not know why.*

*Wendell. That is what I brought her down here for.*

He nodded slowly, once. He looked at his Communion wafer in the little plastic cup. He picked it up and set it down. He looked at me.

*Della,* he said. *Pap. He had reasons of his own. Calvin Tull was the man who put Pap's first wife into the river. They never proved it. Pap never forgot it.*

I had been told twice in my life that I had stopped breathing for a second. Once was at twelve when a cousin had pushed me off a rope swing at the deep hole in Big Cane Creek and I had hit the water on my back and the air had gone out of me and not come in for a count of three. The second was at twenty-one in my granddaddy's bedroom in April of 'oh-five with his hand on the bone of mine. Now there was a third. Wendell saw it on my face. He did not look away.

*Letty Anne Hicks,* he said. *Pap's first wife. March of 'thirty-two. The Cumberland River near Pineville. Foul play not suspected. Four lines in the *Bugle.* It was the Depression. It was a young woman in a river. People drowned and people walked off and people did things to themselves we did not have language for then. There was no investigation. There was no body recovered for nine days and when it was, it was a body. The county coroner was a dentist. The coroner ruled accidental. The case was closed in two paragraphs.*

He drank from his water again. He set it down.

*But the talk in the county was that Calvin Tull had been keeping company with her. Against Pap's protest. He was a land agent for Blackridge with the use of a Model A on a Saturday night. Pap had a baby girl at home and a wife who was unhappy. You know how that story goes. It goes that way still.*

*Vera.*

*Vera was the baby girl. She was four. She did not remember her mother by sight. She remembered her by hands. That is what Vera told me once, on a porch in 'eighty-one, when I had drove up to Bear Branch to give Hollis the news his brother Wesley had died in Detroit. Vera said, *I remember my mama's hands. I do not remember her face. I remember her hands giving me a tin cup.* That is what she said.*

I did not look at Ladonna. I did not have to. I could feel her hands fold tighter in her lap.

*Calvin Tull was the last to see Letty Anne alive. Pap knew before anybody else knew because he had come home a day early from McCreary. He went down to the river by himself. He was there when they pulled her out. He took his wife home. He buried her at the church. He raised the baby for two years before he married Eliza Hopkins. And he never spoke the name of Calvin Tull in his life again that I know of, until July of 'sixty-one.*

*He waited twenty-nine years.*

*He waited twenty-nine years.*

The light at the window had moved. It had come off Ladonna's face and was now across the foot of the bed. The jay did not come back.

*Wendell,* I said. *The roof fall in 'fifty-nine. Asa.*

*That was the tipping. That was not the reason. The reason was 'thirty-two. The tipping was 'fifty-nine. A man can carry a thing for twenty-seven years and what he does at the end of the twenty-seventh year is what he was always going to do. What he does in the twenty-eighth or the twenty-ninth is just the last shove. Calvin Tull cleared a section he had been warned was bad and four men died including the brother of the man married to the daughter of the woman Calvin Tull had put in the river. Pap had reason to think the Lord had finally arranged the math.*

*And Hollis helped him.*

*Hollis was the strike. Hollis was the brother. Hollis was the witness. Hollis carried the rumor of it for the rest of his life because Pap was old and would die in eight years anyway and the company was looking for somebody to hang it on and the easier man to hang it on was a strike-leader with a reason than a sixty-three-year-old widower with a reason older than the company. The company would have rather it was Hollis. The company *did* rather it was Hollis. They never went after either one of them. They had a missing-person report and a sheriff who closed the file. But the talk in the county was Hollis. And Hollis let it be. He let it be because his father-in-law was an old man who had buried two wives and a child of his own breath and the rumor was the price for letting him die in his bed.*

*And he died in his bed.*

*He died in his bed in nineteen-sixty-eight. In the back bedroom of your mother's house.*

*Yes.*

*He died,* Wendell said, *quiet.*

I sat with that. The clock on the wall above the door was the kind that ticked. I had not heard it before. I heard it now. Tick. Tick. Tick.

*Wendell,* I said. *The coal.*

*The coal.*

*The piece on the chest. And the second piece on the rock.*

He looked at me a long time. *Honey,* he said. *Where did you hear about a second piece.*

*I found it. Three weeks ago. At the scene of the man who was killed up there in October.*

He was quiet for a count.

*Hollis told me one time,* he said, *that the coal on the chest was a sign the man had broken a vow to the rock. That the rock had taken him back. He told me on a porch in 'sixty-two when he had had two beers and the porch was Buddy Slone's. I was there because Buddy was my second cousin and I was over from Williamsburg for a fish fry. Hollis told the three of us, sitting on the porch, what the coal meant, and we three drank our beer and we did not say a word back to him. Buddy did not ask him a question. I did not ask him a question. The third man — that was Henley Profitt, who is dead now thirty years — Henley did not ask him a question. We let him say what he had to say. He said two things. The coal on the chest is for the dead man. The coal on the rock is for the one who watched.*

*The one who watched.*

*The one who watched. Who would not speak of it. Who carried it as his.*

*Hollis was the one who watched.*

*Hollis was the one who watched. Pap left him the second piece. As a sign. As a binding. As you-and-me-now-share-this. The coal on the rock was Pap's way of saying *I done it, and you saw it, and we two will carry it as one.* And then Hollis carried it for thirty-seven years and on his deathbed he carried it as if it had been his hand on the rock, because the alternative was telling a granddaughter that her great-grandfather had killed a man for a woman dead since the Depression. He thought it was easier on you to think it was him.*

I could not speak. I made myself.

*Wendell,* I said. *Who else knew the coal meant what you have just told me it meant.*

He thought. He worked his jaw.

*Buddy Slone knew because Hollis told him. Henley Profitt knew because he was on the porch. Henley took it to his grave. Buddy told his boy, Cal — Cal told *me* once, ten years ago, in a hospital waiting room. He thought he was the only living person besides me who knew. He did not know I had told my own son, the sheriff now, in 'ninety-two when Caleb Honaker died. Avery was a teenager. He has likely forgot.*

*Anybody else.*

*Vera knew. The whole thing. Pap was her father. Hollis wrote her the letter so they had the knowing in a hand they could pass down if they ever needed to.*

*And the Hicks side.*

He nodded once.

*Roy Hicks knew the coal,* he said. *He did not know the why. Pap raised him on the method but not on the wound. Roy taught his boy the method. I know because Roy and me drank together off and on through the nineteen-nineties and one night in 'ninety-eight in a parking lot at the VFW he told me, *Wendell, I taught Eldon what my daddy taught me about the rock and the coal. I did not tell him why. Some things ain't a child's to know.* I have thought about that sentence for twenty-seven years.*

I looked at the window. The light had moved off the foot of the bed and was on the floor by the door. Ladonna had not moved.

*Wendell,* I said. *On the fifth of October, somebody killed a man up Sorrow Hollow in the same place and put a piece of coal on his chest and a piece on a rock ten feet off.*

He nodded slowly. He looked at me.

*And you all know who,* he said.

*We think we know who.*

*Della.* He looked at me a long second. *I am eighty-four. I will not live to be called to a stand. I have not been called to one in this county since 'ninety-five. If somebody comes and asks me what I have just told you, I will tell them. I will tell them on tape and I will tell them under oath. You tell that lawyer of your brother's that if it would help him I will sit for a deposition. I will do it tomorrow. I will do it this afternoon. I have been waiting on it.*

*Yes sir.*

*I have been waiting on somebody to come ask in the right tone.*

*Yes sir.*

He sat with his hands on the arms of the chair. He looked at his Communion wafer. He picked it up and looked at it and set it back down. He looked at Ladonna.

*Donnie,* he said. *I am sorry I lived this long with this in my head and did not bring it to you sooner.*

*Wendell. You did right.*

*I do not know what I did. I was tired all my life. I am tired now in a different way.*

*You did right. Eat your bread.*

He picked the wafer up. He put it in his mouth. He chewed it slowly. He swallowed. He drank from his water. He set the cup down.

*Della,* he said. *One more thing.*

*Yes sir.*

*Whoever done it in October. He left the second coal for the same reason Pap left it for Hollis. He left it for somebody who watched. You find the one who watched, you find the one who knows. The one who watched is the door. He always was. In 'sixty-one and in this one. Find the door.*

I nodded. I could not say anything.

He looked at the window. The morning light had moved entirely off him and was on the floor.

*Donnie,* he said. *Tell that boy of mine I love him. He don't come down enough but I love him.*

*I will tell him.*

*Tell him to come on Sunday.*

*I will.*

*Y'all go on now. I am tired.*

We got up. Ladonna leaned and kissed his forehead. He closed his eyes. I touched the back of his hand. The skin moved under my fingers like wax paper that had been folded and unfolded too many times.

In the hallway Ladonna did not speak. In the parking lot we sat in the truck for a long minute before I turned the key, and she said, *I knew about the river. I have known about the river since I was twelve. I did not know who all knew about the coal.*

*Mama.*

*I am going to ride home in the back,* she said. *I am tired in my chest. I want to lie down.*

*All right.*

She got in the back seat. She lay across it with her coat folded under her head. I drove. I went the back way out of Williamsburg past the river crossing where Letty Anne Hicks had drowned in 1932 and I did not say her name aloud and Ladonna did not say it either, and we were quiet all the way home. The mountains east of Bear Branch were lit at the tops where the sun had got at the maples that had not yet dropped, and the maples burned the color of cannel coal taking a polish. I did not look at them long.

---

# Chapter 22 — What the Coal Meant

I sat in the truck in the parking lot of Cumberland Manor with the engine off and the heater bleeding warm air out of the vents on residual until the cold came in slow off the asphalt and the back of my neck started to set. Ladonna had gone in to use the restroom and to refill the thermos from the kitchen at Tonya's offer, and she had walked across the lot without looking at me through the windshield because the kindness she was doing me was the kindness of going.

I let my granddaddy back in.

I had not let him in the whole way since April of 2005, which is what I had told myself for twenty years and which was not exactly true. I had let him in the way you let a man in when you have built a small room for him inside your house and closed the door and, every now and then, gone to the door and listened. I had heard him in the door. I had not opened it. I had filed his deathbed talk under *old man's poetry* the way you file a stack of bills under the sugar tin and then forget you have done it, and I had walked out of the house in Lexington at twenty-one with my degree under my arm and moved south to Knoxville and become the kind of person who triaged and stabilized and handed off and left, and I had not opened the door because to open the door was to be in a room with him.

I opened it now.

He was sitting on the edge of the bed. The bed was the iron-frame bed in the back bedroom of my mother's house, the one Pap had died in, the one Hollis was going to die in two months after that April afternoon. His oxygen tube was laid across the white sheet like a small white snake. The morphine pump was at his right elbow on the bedside table. He had on the gray cotton shirt my mother had buttoned for him that morning because his hands were tired. The window was open three inches because he had asked for it open. The curtains were the curtains Vera had hung in 1971. The smell of the room was clove and laundry starch and the kerosene heater in the hall.

I was twenty-one. I was sitting on the chair by the bed. The chair was the church chair from the front room. He had sent me out of the room for an hour while my mother bathed him and he had had me back in for the last hour and a half of the morning. He had a glass of water with a straw bent toward him on the table. He had drunk from it twice while I had been in the room and he had set it back each time without my having to hold it for him, which was the small dignity he was hoarding.

He said, *Honey. Listen.*

I said, *I'm listening, Pappy.*

He took a breath that was longer than it should have been to move the small amount of air it moved.

*I want you to know the shape of a thing,* he said. *So when somebody comes asking, you'll know what to say and what not.*

*All right.*

*A man name of Tull. Calvin Tull. He went up Sorrow Hollow in 'sixty-one and did not come back.*

*All right.*

*He had it coming. The company sent him to clear a section in 'fifty-nine that he knew was rotten. The section come down on four men. One of them was your great-uncle Asa. He had been warned and he cleared it anyway. I done six weeks in Harlan that year for a strike I did not start, and when I come out, the section had come down. The company had thought firing me would shut me up. They re-hired me when the strike ended. They put a note in my file the day they re-hired me that said *Combs is the one who'll come back for it.* I have seen the note.*

*All right, Pappy.*

He breathed.

*The man Calvin Tull,* he said, *went up Sorrow Hollow in July of 'sixty-one to talk about a boundary on Combs land. He was lured up there. He did not come down. The body was never found. The county opened a missing-person file in three days. The sheriff closed it in three months. There was talk in the county that I done it. There was no proof. There has never been proof.*

*Pappy.*

*Hush. Listen.*

*Yes sir.*

*The man Tull was found by no one but God,* he said. *He is in the rhododendron on the east slope above the seep. He is under a deadfall a piece of the way up. The deadfall has not been moved. It will not be moved. He is bones now and he has been bones since the war in Vietnam.*

I had not breathed in for the length of a sentence. I let myself breathe in. He saw it and he waited until I had.

*Della,* he said. *I am telling you this because somebody might come asking one day. The day might not come. If it comes you will know what to say. You will say my granddaddy did not know any such thing. You will say my granddaddy was a foreman at the mine and a strike leader and a man who lost his brother in 'fifty-nine and you will say my granddaddy did not put his hand on a man and a piece of rock. You will say my granddaddy died in his bed in two thousand and five and you will say my granddaddy is in the church cemetery and you will say if there is a thing the family knows about a man missing in 'sixty-one we have carried it down for sixty-some years and we will carry it down for sixty more and you do not have leave to ask us.*

I listened.

*Two things,* he said. *Two more things, and then you are to forget this conversation until the day comes you need it.*

*Yes sir.*

*The man was laid with a piece of cannel coal on his chest. That is one thing.*

*All right.*

*And there was a piece of cannel coal laid on a flat rock ten feet east of him. That is the other.*

He stopped. He breathed.

*The coal on the chest,* he said, *means the man broke a vow to the rock, and the rock has taken him back. The coal on the rock means somebody else saw it done.*

I had been twenty-one. I had not asked him *what man broke what vow.* I had not asked him *who saw it done.* I had thought, sitting in that chair with my hands on my knees, *He is a dying man and he is talking the way a dying man talks, which is in pictures, and I am not going to ask him to be plain.* I had thought, *He is using the language of the place because the place is what he is leaving and the language is the part of him that gets to leave last.* I had filed the two sentences under *poetry* and I had carried them down for twenty years and I had not opened the file.

I sat in the truck in the parking lot of Cumberland Manor in November of 2025 and I let myself open it.

*The coal on the chest means the man broke a vow to the rock and the rock has taken him back.*

It was a code.

Pap had built it. He could not write *I killed this man because thirty years ago he drowned my wife and seven weeks ago a section he cleared killed my son-in-law's brother.* He could not write that on a piece of paper or paint it on a fence or put it in the *Bugle.* He built a sign instead. He put a piece of cannel coal on the chest of Calvin Tull and let the sign say *this man was a man who broke faith with the rock.* The rock was a metonym. The rock was the work he did and the work he asked other men to do and the work he cut corners on and the work that came down on four men in 'fifty-nine. The rock was the river too — the river that had taken Letty Anne in 'thirty-two while Calvin Tull stood on the bank, taken her in the same general kind of way the section had taken Asa, which is to say by the will of a man with a Model A and a wallet and a clipboard.

But the sign was a lie about itself. The sign said *I am an industrial reckoning, I am the mountain taking back its own.* The sign was not that. The sign was a sixty-three-year-old widower with a wife in a river since the Depression and a son-in-law's brother in a hole in McCreary, and a piece of rock in his hand because he had walked a man up a hollow and put his fist around the truth and brought it down. The sign was personal grief disguised as a ritual. A man's hand on a piece of cannel coal so the world would think it had been the mountain.

The coal on the rock.

The coal on the rock was the other half of the lie. It said *somebody else saw it done.* It was a binding. It was a man saying to another man *you saw, and we two now carry this together, and the price of telling is my life and yours.* Pap had left it for Hollis. Hollis had come up the hollow after his shift and had found the body and the chest piece and had walked ten feet east and had found the second piece on a flat sandstone outcrop, and he had picked it up and put it back down where it was, and he had understood. The sign had told him what he was being asked to do. He was being asked to bury the body and carry the truth.

He had done both.

He had done both for forty-four more years and on his deathbed he had not even given it to me as Pap's. He had given it to me as his. He had said *I want you to know the shape of a thing so when somebody comes asking you will know what to say.* He had let me think it was him. He had let me think it because to tell me it was Pap would have been to tell me about a river in 1932 and a young woman with hands a four-year-old remembered, and Hollis Combs at eighty-one in April had not been willing to put that grief into a twenty-one-year-old granddaughter going back to Lexington in three days. He had taken the rumor of it on himself a final time. He had carried it down to me as the strike and the brother because the strike was a story I could put down. The river was not.

*The work is knowing the difference.*

I sat in the truck with my hands in my lap and I thought, *Some things you carry down to bury. Some things you carry down to keep warm. The work is knowing the difference.* The sentence came at me out of the back of my mouth from a place I had not been keeping it on purpose. I did not know yet where it had come from. I would know later. I let it sit.

I thought about October.

The coal on Tully Renfro's chest. The coal on the flat rock ten feet east. Two pieces. The same configuration. The same hollow. The same seep. The same flat. Sixty-four years after the first time.

If the chest piece meant *this man broke a vow to the rock and the rock has taken him back* — then the killer had framed Tully Renfro as a vow-breaker. Tully had been Calvin Tull's professional descendant by sixty-four years, by job title, by purpose, by the wearing of a clean shirt while telling another man's mother to sign a piece of paper she did not want to sign. The killer had put a piece of coal on his chest to say *the company is the rock and you stood between it and the people and you are taken.* The same lie Pap had told. The lie that disguised personal motive as ritual industrial justice. The motive was kin-protective: *the company is about to take the holler and you are the instrument and the instrument has to be broken to slow the engine.* The chest piece said *the mountain.* The chest piece was a personal hand on a personal piece of rock.

And the rock piece.

The rock piece said *somebody else saw it done.*

And the rock piece had not been put there for the deputies to find. The deputies had missed it. The rock piece had been put there for one specific living person who knew the code and who would understand, when they saw it, that they had been bound.

The killer had left the second piece *for a witness.*

He had picked the witness ahead of time. He had walked the witness up the hollow that afternoon. He had asked the witness to come on a pretext. The witness had come thinking he was going to watch a hard conversation about a lease. The killer had killed the man instead. He had laid the first piece of coal on the man's chest. He had stepped ten feet east and laid the second on the flat outcrop. He had turned to the witness and said *Combs and Hicks always carried it together. We carry this one together.* Or words like it. And the witness had stood and watched. And the witness had not been able to speak.

The witness was my brother.

I had been working toward the sentence for three weeks. I had built it slow because to build it fast was to build it wrong and a paramedic does not build a thing wrong if she can help it. I had built it slow. I had not let myself say it. I let myself say it now.

Wade had gone up Sorrow Hollow on the fifth of October because Eldon had asked him to come. Eldon had told him he was going to talk to Tully Renfro about the lease. Wade had gone because his cousin had asked. He had gone because the cousin was kin and a kin asked is a kin gone. He had not known what he was going up there for. He had stood and watched. He had been handed a piece of coal by Eldon afterward and had been told to place it on the rock. He had placed it. He had walked back down to his truck on the trace road, the trace road my granddaddy had walked Wade up as a boy and taught him to set traps on, and he had driven home, and he had not said a word, because the second piece of coal that was now in his hand by metaphor had a sign on it that said *you saw, and we two carry this together, and the price of telling is my life and yours.*

He had carried it down for five days before they had arrested him for it.

He had carried it down through three jail visits in which I had sat across from him through scratched plexiglass and asked him to tell me what he had been doing up the hollow that day and he had said *I went up there to see about a thing* and would not say more. He had carried it for me because telling me Eldon meant incriminating himself under the law, and breaking the code he had been raised on, and telling me my own great-grandfather had killed a man with a piece of rock in 1961, and that the inheritance had moved through the Hicks side and landed in our generation on a Sunday afternoon when his cousin had asked him to take the binding.

He had been carrying the fire down because the family had taught him to carry the fire down. The code had told him *we do not tell.* The code had told him *we two carry this together.* The code had said it with a piece of coal on a rock in a hollow at the back of our own land, and Wade had not been raised to argue with a piece of coal on a rock at the back of our own land.

He had been raised to carry it down.

Tully Renfro was, I understood now, the easy fall for the company. The trail-cam image of Wade's truck. The hammer in the truck bed. The known grievance. The brother who had not been home in years. The mother who would not be able to fight an arraignment for long. The company had not directed the killing. The company had been handed it. Caudill Boggs was the company's hand on the work after, and Caudill's work was being made cheaper by the fact that my brother had been raised on a code that required him to be silent. The whole thing was structurally beautiful the way any system that converts a personal grief into a profit is structurally beautiful, which is to say it is the kind of structure that makes you want to throw up.

I sat in the truck. I had not breathed for a count. I let myself breathe.

I thought, *Pap left the coal for Hollis. Eldon left the coal for Wade. My grandmother kept Hollis's letter for sixty years and did not open the envelope to the air. My mother knew about the river when she was twelve and did not tell me. I knew about the strike since I was twenty-one and did not tell anyone.*

I thought, *Wade is in a cell in the basement of the courthouse and has been there for thirty-three days. He is going to be there until I do something he does not yet know I can do.*

I thought, *I have to go to my brother and I have to put what I know in front of him and I have to ask him to break what he has been raised never to break.*

I thought, *I have to find one more person first. Cal Slone is alive. Cal Slone is the door between Buddy Slone's porch in 1962 and my mother's kitchen in 2025. Cal Slone is the outside witness who can corroborate the meaning of the coal if I have to put it in front of a Commonwealth's attorney or a grand jury. Cal Slone is the man at the funeral lunch who said *you favor your granddaddy* and did not give his name. He had come to the lunch because the Slones had been carrying the fire down with us for sixty-three years and the fire was about to come up, and he had wanted to see, before it did, what kind of woman the Combs had grown by 2025.*

I thought, *I have to find him.*

I thought, *I have to find him today.*

Ladonna came back across the parking lot. She had the thermos. She had her coat buttoned. She got in the back seat the way she had said she would and she said, *Della. Are you all right.*

*Mama,* I said. *I have to make a stop on the way home.*

*Where.*

*Brass Lantern. I need to call Cal Slone from the pay phone.*

*The pay phone don't take coins anymore. They took the box off the wall in 2014.*

*I'll find a phone.*

*Use your cell.*

*I don't have his number.*

*Honey,* she said, and she leaned forward between the seats and put her hand on my forearm. *Cal Slone is in the book. He has been in the book since 1981.*

I had not known. I had not known a single useful thing about how this place actually held its own information in the time I had been gone. I had known how to find a vein on a forty-year-old woman with three teeth and a collapsed circulatory system in the dark in the back of an ambulance at sixty miles an hour. I had not known to look in the phone book.

I started the truck. I backed out of the space. I pulled out of the lot of Cumberland Manor and onto US-25W and headed north.

I thought, *Find the door.*

Old Honaker had said it. *Find the door. The one who watched is always the door.*

My brother was the door I knew. Cal Slone was the door I did not. I was going to walk through both of them.

The mountains ahead of me were lit at the tops the way they had been in the morning, only now the light was the late afternoon light and the maples were burning the color of cannel coal taking a polish, and this time I let myself look at them, and I drove.

---

# Chapter 23 — Pap's Reason

I did not get to Cal Slone that night. I got to the Caldwell County library at four-twenty in the afternoon, and the librarian was Sissy Halsey, who had taught me to read and was Becca's mother's older sister, and she looked at me over her glasses when I asked her for the microfiche of the *Caldwell County Bugle* for March 1932 and said, *Della Combs. What are you up to.*

*Looking up an old story.*

*That what you're doing.*

*Yes ma'am.*

She did not ask which. She did not have to. She had been the librarian in Caldwell County for thirty-eight years and she had pulled the same drawer of microfilm for some woman or some old man or some lawyer from out of town often enough to know what a person was up to when they asked for March of 1932 and did not specify a week. *Reader's in the back,* she said. *Don't bend the film.* And then, when I turned for it: *Della. You favor your granddaddy.* *I have heard that today already.* *You will hear it again.*

The reading machine was in the back corner where it had been when I was twelve and had used it to look up the 1958 west Kentucky mine explosion for a paper I had written for Mrs. Couch in eighth grade. The bulb hummed. The screen was the gray of pond ice in February. I threaded the film and cranked the handle slow and watched the back issues of the *Bugle* go by. The masthead said *Caldwell County Bugle — Established 1894 — D. P. Mullins, Publisher.*

I cranked into March.

*MARCH 4 1932 — Coal Production Down From January.*

*MARCH 11 — Roosevelt Speaks At Lexington.*

*MARCH 18 1932* — page one was about a school burning down at Tinker Branch. Page two was a list of debt-relief auctions in McCreary. Page three was the Methodist sermon for that week. Page four was the want ads and the small items. On page four, in the small-items column under the line that read *Local Notes*, in five lines, in a block of type that had been set by a woman in a brown apron in 1932:

> *Mrs. Letitia Anne Sturgill, age 24, wife of Phineas Sturgill of Bear Branch, was recovered from the Cumberland River near Pineville on Tuesday afternoon last by deputies acting on report of a fisherman. The deceased had been missing since the previous Wednesday night. The county coroner Dr. M. Hampton has ruled the death an accidental drowning. The family asks privacy. Funeral services Saturday at Bear Branch Methodist, Pastor J. R. Profitt presiding.*

I sat back. I read it again. I read it a third time.

*The deceased had been missing since the previous Wednesday night.*

Wednesday the ninth. Recovered Tuesday the fifteenth. Six days in the river.

I cranked back. I cranked back to March 11. I read the front page and the second page and the third page and the fourth page. There was nothing. There had been no item the week of her disappearance. The paper had not mentioned a missing twenty-four-year-old wife from Bear Branch the week she had gone missing. The paper had run a piece about Roosevelt at Lexington and a piece about a debt auction and a piece about a school burning, and it had not run anything about a young woman missing for six days from a holler eight miles up the road from the office where the type was set, because the man who published the paper had been a friend of the company and the woman who had gone missing was the wife of a man the company had no use for and the family the woman had been keeping company with did. The paper had waited until they had pulled her out of the river. The paper had given her five lines in *Local Notes* and not put her name on page one.

I cranked back through the rest of the March 11 issue to be sure. I cranked forward through March 25 and April 1 to be sure. There was nothing else. No follow-up. No second item. No obituary. The *Bugle* had given Letty Anne Hicks Sturgill five lines and the assertion of an accidental drowning and a request for the family's privacy, and it had moved on to a thirteen-year-old boy from Williamsburg who had run for State Senate page that summer.

I sat with the film on the screen for a long count.

I knew now what Wendell Cornett had meant about the dentist coroner and the four-line item. I knew now what my mother had meant when she said she had known about the river since she was twelve. I knew now what the texture of 1932 had been. The texture of 1932 had been a county newspaper that had run an item on page four about an accidental drowning and had not, in any of the three weeks before or the three weeks after, run a single line about a young woman of twenty-four whose husband had walked from Pineville to Bear Branch on a Friday morning to find his house empty and his daughter at a neighbor's and his wife in a river by Sunday. The texture of 1932 had been the silence inside that page-four item. Pap had read that paper on a Saturday in March. He had read those five lines. He had taken them to mean what they meant.

I got up. I went to the front desk.

*Sissy. Is there a reporter's archive for the *Bugle.* For the 1930s.*

She looked up. *There is one box at the historical society. It is in the basement of the courthouse. It is open Wednesday afternoons. They have notes that did not run.*

*Today is Wednesday.*

*They close at five.*

I looked at the clock above her desk. It was four-forty-one.

I drove the three blocks. I parked behind the courthouse. I went in the side door that opened into the records vestibule. The historical society was in a former coal-room in the basement and the door was held open with a brick because the latch did not catch, and the woman at the table inside was Marie Hatfield, age seventy, who had taught at the high school until she retired in 2014 and who had been the historical society's volunteer secretary since. She looked up when I came in. She smiled the way Marie always smiled, which was the smile of a woman who is used to greeting people who have come to look up genealogies and is glad when the person who has come is somebody from town.

*Della Combs.*

*Marie.*

*What can I do for you, honey.*

*Bugle reporter's archive. The 1930s box.*

She did not blink. She got up. She went to a steel cabinet against the back wall. She opened the third drawer. She slid a green Hollinger box out onto the table. She set it down and she opened the lid and she said, *It's mostly slip notes. The publisher's son sold the archive to the society in 'sixty-eight when he closed down the paper. The notes are loose in folders by year. There may be three folders for 1932 or there may be one, I do not remember.*

There were two. I sat down at the table. I opened the first folder. The notes were on small slips of yellow newsprint paper and on the backs of envelopes and on a few half-pages of typed copy paper with handwritten corrections in pencil. The handwriting was the handwriting of a man who had set type six days a week for forty years and who wrote fast in the margins of his own copy because he had to. D. P. Mullins. Publisher and reporter and proprietor.

I read through the folder slowly. I read about a county fair and a stillborn calf and a man named Honaker who had run for sheriff and lost to a man named Profitt. I read about the school burning at Tinker Branch and what had caused it (a stove flue not properly vented). I read about a four-page editorial Mullins had decided not to print about the price of coal that summer.

I came to the slips for the week of March 18.

There were three. The first was the slip for the school burning. The second was the slip for the debt auctions. The third was a single small slip of yellow paper, four inches by six, with a pencil note on it in Mullins's hand.

> *Sturgill drowning. Per Sheriff Kincaid. Body recovered Tues. Cause accidental. Family asks privacy. Five lines, page four.*

Under it, in a different pencil, possibly added later that day or the next:

> *Local talk: Calvin Tull, Blackridge, was the last to see her alive. He had been keeping company with the deceased over her husband's protest. Spoke to Tull on the porch of his boarding house in Caldwell on Friday morning. Tull denies. Says he left her at the bridge in Pineville at ten o'clock Wednesday night and she was walking home. Denies further comment. Not running.*

The slip had been set aside. The slip had been put in the folder with the other slips for the week and had stayed there for ninety-three years until I opened the folder on a Wednesday afternoon in November of 2025.

*Not running.*

The publisher had spoken to Calvin Tull on a porch on a Friday morning in March of 1932 and had asked him about Letty Anne and had been told that Tull had left her at the bridge in Pineville at ten o'clock on a Wednesday night, twenty-some miles from her house, with no car and no money and no coat. *And she was walking home.* The publisher had made a note of the conversation and the note had not run. The publisher had run five lines on page four and the five lines had said *accidental drowning* and *the family asks privacy* and the publisher had taken his pencil and written *not running* and put the slip in the folder.

I read the slip three more times. I copied it longhand into the small notebook I had been carrying since October. I wrote the date at the top — *November 5, 2025, 4:51 p.m., Caldwell County Historical Society basement, file: 1932 / Bugle / loose notes / Mullins.* I wrote it the way I wrote a run-report narrative, which is to say so it would hold up.

I put the slip back in the folder. I put the folder back in the box. I closed the lid.

I sat with my hands flat on the box for a count.

*Pap waited twenty-nine years,* I thought. *He waited until 1961. He chose Sorrow Hollow because the chapel was on his daughter's husband's family's land. And he made it look like the strike. He made it look like the brother. He made it look like coal-country reckoning so it would not be about a river.*

I sat with my hands on the box. I let them shake for a count of ten and then I made them stop and I picked up my notebook and I closed the cap on the pencil.

Marie came back to the table. *You all right, honey.*

*I am. I am going to come back tomorrow for the rest of this box if that's all right.*

*That's fine. We're open ten to five tomorrow.*

*Marie. One more thing.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*Did anybody come look at the 1932 file before me. Ever.*

She thought. *Honey. There's a sign-in card on the back of the box. Take a look.*

I lifted the lid. The card was tucked inside. The card was a piece of three-by-five buff cardstock with a column for date and a column for name. There were six entries. The earliest was 1981. The latest was 1997.

I read them.

*1981 — Hollis Combs.*

*1985 — Roy Hicks.*

*1989 — Hollis Combs.*

*1992 — Hollis Combs.*

*1994 — Wendell Cornett.*

*1997 — Eldon Hicks.*

I read the list twice.

I read the list three times.

My grandfather had come down to this basement in 1981 and 1989 and 1992. He had come down three times to look at the same box. He had read the same slip I had just read. He had sat at this table — or a table like it, in a smaller historical society in an earlier configuration — and he had read the words *Local talk: Calvin Tull, Blackridge, was the last to see her alive,* and he had put the slip back in the folder and closed the box, and he had gone home, and he had sat with his wife at the kitchen table and he had not spoken of it. He had come three times in eleven years and he had not told a soul.

Roy Hicks had come in 1985. Roy had not been raised on Letty Anne. Roy had known the code but not the wound. Roy must have come asking because somebody had pointed him at it. Somebody had said *Roy, you should know.* I did not know who. It did not matter now.

And Eldon Hicks had come on a Wednesday in 1997. Eldon would have been twenty-one. He had been four years out of high school. He had been working at the strip site. He had come down to the basement of the courthouse one Wednesday in 1997 and he had asked Marie or whoever was there before Marie for the 1932 file, and he had read the same slip I had just read, and he had read *Local talk: Calvin Tull, Blackridge, was the last to see her alive,* and he had read *Not running,* and he had put the slip back in the folder, and he had gone home, and he had carried the knowing for twenty-eight years before he had picked up a piece of cannel coal on the flat by the seep on the fifth of October of 2025.

He had not killed Tully Renfro because the company was about to take the holler. He had killed him because the company had been taking the holler for a hundred years and had taken Letty Anne Hicks Sturgill out of a river ninety-three years ago and had taken Asa Combs out of the McCreary section sixty-six years ago and was about to take Marlene's mother's house and his cousins' houses up the back of Tinker, and he had killed him because he had been carrying it down since he was twenty-one and had finally found the moment in which the carrying would not be enough anymore. He had killed him for the same reason Pap had killed Calvin Tull. He had killed him because the love for the people the company was about to take exceeded the patience that had asked him to wait.

He had also killed him because he had been raised on a code that gave him the form for the act. He could not have killed him with a deer rifle at the company gate at noon. He had not been raised for that. He had been raised for cannel coal on a flat by a seep on Combs land where his great-grandfather had done it before him, and the form had told him *this is how a man like you, in a situation like this, does the thing.* The form had been waiting in him since 1997.

And he had asked Wade to come.

I sat in the basement of the courthouse on November the fifth of 2025 and I understood it. I understood why he had asked Wade to come. He had asked Wade because Pap had asked Hollis. The form required a witness. The form required the binding. The form said *we two carry this together.* The form required a Combs to take the second piece. It had been built that way in 1961 and it had been carried down to him intact, and he had used it intact, and Wade had stood on the flat by the seep and watched his cousin do a thing and had been handed a piece of coal and had laid it on a rock because he had been told to lay it on a rock, and he had walked down the trace and had driven home and had not slept in his bed for thirty-three days.

I closed the box. I locked the cabinet for Marie because she had asked me to. I gave her a hug because I could not say anything more, and she did not ask. I went up the stairs and out the side door. The light was the last light of the afternoon and the sun was on the courthouse steeple and a starling was on the bronze plaque of the veterans of the Korean War. Hollis's name was on it. I touched it on the way down the steps the way I had touched the back of Wendell Cornett's hand that morning, the way you touch a thing to say *I know you.*

I got in the truck. I sat for a count. I started the engine. I drove out of the courthouse lot. I turned east on KY-92.

I had Cal Slone to find by morning. I had Wade to break by the end of the week. I had Eldon to face on a porch up Tinker by the end of next week. I had a mother at home who had known about the river since she was twelve and a hymnal on a refrigerator with a letter in *Amazing Grace* I had not yet looked at and a kitchen table that had been the kitchen table Pap had drunk water at after burying a man in 1961 and Hollis had eaten breakfast at every day for fifty-seven years.

I had all of it.

I drove. The sun went down behind the ridge and the headlights came on and the road through Caldwell went two-lane and the white line on the shoulder caught the headlights the way a vein catches a penlight in the back of an ambulance, and I followed it home.

---

# Chapter 24 — Buddy Slone's Boy

He was already in the back booth when I came in, the booth Hannah and I had taken the first time, three weeks before. He had ordered cobbler for both of us without asking and the cobbler was sitting across from him, untouched, the ice cream gone to milk. He stood up when he saw me. He was a tall man, taller than I had registered at the funeral, with the kind of long careful body that had spent forty years welding pipe and had paid for it in the shoulders. He put out his hand. *Della,* he said. *I'm Cal Slone. I don't know if you remember meeting me.*

*I remember,* I said. I took his hand. It was a hand like Hollis's — broad, knotted at the knuckles, dry. *Bear Branch Methodist. Doyle Sturgill's lunch. You told me I favored my granddaddy.*

*You do.* He sat. He waved the waitress over and ordered me coffee without asking and I let him. *I have been working up to this for about three weeks,* he said. *I am sorry I made you find me. I should have come to you.*

*You came to me at the funeral.*

*That was halfway. That was me looking at you to see if you were the one.*

I sat with the cobbler in front of me. The waitress brought my coffee in the heavy white mug they used. The Brass Lantern at nine on a Monday night was almost empty. Two truckers at the counter eating biscuits and gravy. A teenage couple in the front booth not eating anything, the boy with his hand inside the girl's jean jacket, the girl looking at the door. The light over our booth was a fluorescent that had been buzzing for years and would buzz for years more.

*The one,* I said.

*The one Hollis told.*

He had said it without any drama. He was watching me across the table the way an old man watches a creek for trout — without moving and without pretending not to. I had the feeling I sometimes got with the oldest men up here, that there was no point trying to hurry them and no point trying to slow them and the most I could do was sit upright and listen.

*Mr. Slone,* I said.

*Cal,* he said.

*Cal. Tell me what your daddy told you.*

He sat back. He took a breath that he let out slow. He looked at the cobbler in front of him for a second and then he pushed it to one side and laid both his hands flat on the formica. They were a welder's hands and they did not shake. *My daddy was Buddy Slone,* he said. *He died in 1999. He'd been a section boss at Mine Four under your granddaddy. They started out together as buddies and they finished as buddies and there were maybe four people in this county your granddaddy fully trusted and my daddy was one of them.*

*All right.*

*In 1962, in the fall of it, your granddaddy went up to my daddy's porch on a Saturday and asked my mother if he could borrow Buddy for an hour. My mother said yes. My daddy and your granddaddy walked off into the field behind the house and stood at the fence line where the apple trees were and they talked for the better part of two hours. My mother said when my daddy came back up to the porch, his face had gone the color of paste. She asked what was wrong. He said, *Lillie, Hollis Combs has just told me a thing I am going to carry to my grave and I am going to ask you to never ask me to tell you what it is.* And she never did. She lived another thirty-six years and she never asked.*

*All right.*

*My daddy told me when I was eighteen. He took me out to the same fence and told me the same thing your granddaddy had told him. He said, *Cal, I am telling you for the same reason Hollis told me. You don't tell. You remember. In case.* And I said, *In case what?* And he said, *In case anybody ever has to ask, and the asking comes from the right place.* I asked him what the right place was. He said, *You'll know it when it comes. It might not come in my time and it might not come in yours. But if it does, you'll know.* And then he told me.*

He picked up his coffee. He drank from it. He set it down with the deliberation of a man who had used the cup as punctuation his whole life. The waitress walked past us with her pot and looked at him and he shook his head and she went on.

*He told me,* Cal said, *that in the summer of 1961 a man named Pap Sturgill killed a man named Calvin Tull up Sorrow Hollow. He told me the strike of '59 was a piece of why. He told me Hollis's older brother Asa was a piece of why. He told me the company was a piece of why. He did not tell me about Letty Anne. I think your granddaddy didn't tell my daddy about Letty Anne either. I think he told the strike-and-brother of it. That part was the truth as much as the rest, and it was a story a man could carry without being torn up by it.*

I sat very still. I had drunk no coffee. The mug was hot against my palm and I had not moved my palm off it because the heat was an anchor and I needed one.

*He told me,* Cal said, *about the coal. He said: Cal. When you find a man laid out with a piece of cannel coal on his chest, somebody has done a hard thing and meant to say something by it. The thing they meant to say is, *this man broke a vow to the rock and the rock has taken him back.* And he said: when you find a second piece of coal set out flat on a stone ten feet off, that is a sign meant for one specific living person who watched the doing, and it says, *I saw you see this and I trust you to keep it.* My daddy said, *That is the code. There are not many in the county who know it. You don't tell. You remember.**

He stopped. He looked at me. *Have I got it right,* he said, *as far as you know it.*

I sat with my hand on the cup. I had not expected the question. I had been listening to him the way you listen to a witness on a run who has held something in for a long time and is finally letting it out clean, and you do not interrupt that, you let it run. But he was asking me a thing I was going to have to answer.

*You have it right,* I said.

*Then I will tell you the part that comes after,* he said. *My daddy said that a piece of coal on a rock did not always mean the witness was complicit. Sometimes it meant the man who set it had only watched and promised silence. Sometimes the witness had no part in the doing at all. He said the code had two readings. He said, *Don't ever forget that part, Cal, because somebody may set you a piece of coal in your life and you will need to know what it is asking of you and what it is not.**

I looked at him. *Why are you telling me this part.*

*Because you have a brother in jail,* he said, *and the prosecution has a piece of coal on a chest and another piece of coal on a rock, and your brother has gone quiet, and I want you to know there is a reading of the second coal that does not make him a killer or even a man who helped a killing. It makes him a man who saw a thing done and was asked to hold it. That is also a thing this code can mean. I think you should know it.*

I had not moved. I had let go of the mug.

*Cal,* I said. *Tell me why you think Eldon Hicks knows the code.*

He did not look surprised. He had been waiting for the question. He nodded once. *Eldon Hicks's daddy was Roy Hicks,* he said. *Roy was the son Pap had with his second wife, Eliza Hopkins. Roy was the kind of man who would teach a thing like that to a boy because he wanted the boy to feel he had inherited something he could be proud of. Roy worked at the company my whole adult life. He died around two thousand and three, I think it was. Eldon would have been thirty maybe by then. Old enough. Roy would have told him.*

*Do you know that for certain.*

*I do not know it for certain. I know Roy. I know the kind of thing Roy would have wanted to give his boy. I know Eldon is a foreman at the strip now and I know Tully Renfro was found laid out up Sorrow Hollow with a piece of coal on his chest, and I know your brother is the one in a cell. I am sitting in a diner with Hollis Combs's grandbaby telling her this because I think a stone has been thrown that was meant to land a particular way, and your brother is the one it landed on, and I think Eldon Hicks did it and I think your brother saw it and I think Eldon set him a second piece of coal to say, *brother, you saw, and you will hold.* And your brother has held.*

I looked down at the formica. There was a coffee ring on it from someone before us, perfect as a wedding band. I looked at it because I could not look at Cal Slone for a minute.

*Cal,* I said. *How long have you been carrying this.*

*Forty-six years.*

*And you have not told another person until tonight.*

*Not one. Not my wife. Not my children. Not my pastor.*

*Why now.*

He breathed in. He looked at his hands and then he looked at me and his eyes were the watered blue of a man who had spent his life squinting at sparks. *Because Hollis told my daddy and my daddy told me so that if a day came when this needed to come out, somebody outside the families would be able to say it,* he said. *That was the whole point of him telling. He was setting a relay. He didn't want it sealed up inside one bloodline. He told my daddy so somebody could speak who wasn't a Combs and wasn't a Hicks. He told you so somebody in the family would know it was hers to tell or to keep. He gave both halves to people he trusted. I have to think he meant for them to meet someday.*

I did not say anything for a while.

*Mr. Slone,* I said.

*Cal,* he said.

*Cal. If I needed you to say what you have just said to me — to the sheriff, to my brother's lawyer, to a grand jury — would you say it.*

He did not hesitate. *I would,* he said. *I would not enjoy it and I would lose some friends over it and I would be the first Slone since 1962 to break a thing my daddy told me to hold, and I would say it. That is what your granddaddy was setting up when he told my daddy. He was setting up a man who could break the seal when the time came. I am that man. I have known it for a long time. I have been waiting on the time. You came back. The time is now.*

I looked at him. I had to make myself do it.

*Cal,* I said. *Did my granddaddy ever say my name to you. Or to your daddy. Was I — was I on this register.*

He looked at me a long second.

*You were on it,* he said. *My daddy told me your name when you were a baby. He said, *Hollis says he's got a granddaughter coming on. He says if this thing ever comes to the table, it'll likely come through her hand. He says the boys won't do it. He says the women in his line will.* He said it the way a man tells you about a horse he's watching come up.*

*Jesus, Cal.*

*I know. I am sorry.*

*No.* I shook my head. *Don't be sorry. I —*

He waited. He let me have the silence. He was a man who knew how to let another person have a silence.

I picked up my cobbler spoon and I did not eat. I held it. I looked at the buzzing fluorescent. I looked at the truckers at the counter, both of whom had paid up and were getting their jackets. I looked at the teenagers — the girl had finally gotten what she wanted out of the boy, which was for him to be looking at her, and they were eating a single piece of pie now between them with two forks. I looked at Cal Slone's hands on the formica. I had spent the last three weeks thinking my granddaddy had handed me a thing on his deathbed in April of 2005 because he was running out of breath and didn't have anyone else to hand it to, and I had been wrong. He had been setting a thing in motion since before I could spell.

*Cal,* I said. *Will you come to Hannah Soyars's office tomorrow morning.*

*I will.*

*I will tell her you're coming.*

*All right.*

*Eat your cobbler.*

*You first.*

I ate a bite. It was good. He ate a bite. The waitress came back with the pot and refilled us both and Cal said *much obliged* and she went on. He hummed something briefly under his breath while he stirred his coffee — the chorus of *Will the Circle Be Unbroken*, half a line of it, the way a man hums what's been in him since he was a boy. He did not appear to know he was doing it. He stopped after one phrase and went on stirring.

*Cal,* I said.

*Yeah.*

*Did you ever know my granddaddy to carry a piece of coal in his pocket.*

He looked up at me. His face did something — the smallest movement at the eyes.

*Funny you ask that,* he said. He reached into the front pocket of his work pants. He brought out a small flat piece of cannel coal, polished black, the size of a half-dollar. He set it on the formica between us. *I have carried this since 1979. Hollis gave it to me on my wedding day. He said, *Cal. This is a token. It means I trust you. It does not have to mean anything else.* I have carried it in my pocket every day for forty-six years. I have not understood it any better than that. I have just carried it.*

I looked at the coal on the formica. I did not pick it up. I did not need to.

We finished the cobbler. He paid the check over my protest. We walked out together. In the parking lot the air had the cold of the second week of November, the kind of cold that came up out of the ground rather than down from the sky. He stood by his truck — a clean older Ford, white, dent in the tailgate — and held the door open and looked at me across the cab roof. *Della,* he said.

*Yes sir.*

*Your granddaddy was the best man I ever knew and the most cornered man I ever knew. He spent forty-four years trying to hand off a thing and almost ran out of time to hand it. You came back. You let him.*

He got in. He drove off. I stood in the parking lot and watched his brake lights bend out onto KY-92 and head west toward Putney, and then I got in my own truck and I did not start it. I sat with my hands on the wheel. Through the front window of the Brass Lantern I could see the waitress wiping down our booth, lifting the cobbler plates, lifting the mugs. I could see the spot where the small flat piece of coal had sat on the formica. She would not see it. There was nothing there to see.

I had been on the register my whole life and I had not known.

I thought, of an evening, my granddaddy had set me a piece of coal across forty-one years.

I started the truck.

---

# Chapter 25 — Caudill at the Door

I came back to the Mountain Laurel a little before midnight. The neon out front had the *L* burnt out so it read MOUNTAIN AUREL and had read it the whole month I had been there, and I had stopped noticing it the way I stopped noticing the buzz of my own refrigerator at home. I came up the highway from the east, did the long slow turn into the lot, and saw the truck before I saw him.

It was parked nose-out in the second row, the row by the chain-link, where guests parked when they wanted to see the highway. Green Ford F-250, late nineties. Blackridge sticker in the back window. Same truck I had clocked in the IGA lot the night I came in. Same truck Earl Whitt had seen at the mouth of the hollow. I knew the rear bumper now the way you know a face you have looked at across a courtroom.

I did not pull in next to him. I parked in front of my own unit, number seven, three rows up. I shut the engine. I left the lights on.

He got out. He took his time about it. He was a big man and he moved like a big man who had been taught somewhere — military, maybe, the deputy years, maybe both — to take his time on purpose so that whoever was watching him would have a second to register he was not in a hurry. He shut his door soft. He walked toward the front of my truck in the wash of my headlights, not fast, hands at his sides, palms turned a quarter outward so I could see he had nothing in them. He stopped about ten feet off the bumper. He looked at me through the windshield.

I shut the headlights down. I let my eyes adjust. I rolled the window halfway.

*Della Combs,* he said.

*Mr. Boggs.*

*Caudill is fine.*

*Caudill.*

*I'm not here to do you any harm. I want you to know that first. I am going to stand right here and talk to you for about three minutes. I am going to keep my hands where you can see them. You don't have to get out.*

*I won't,* I said.

*All right.*

He stood with his hands out a little from his sides in the dark of the parking lot. The neon was a wash of pink-orange across the right side of his face. He was bigger than I had read him from the turnout on Highway 92 — not heavier, just longer in the limb. His face was sharper than the body, the way some of these men are. He had a thin mouth and the kind of clean small ears that did not go with the rest of him.

*I have been hoping to catch you alone,* he said.

*Here I am.*

*Yes ma'am.* A small smile. *Here you are.*

A semi went by on KY-92 behind him, westbound, running its jake brake down through the gears as it came up on the stoplight at the IGA. The brake worked through us like a freight train. He waited it out. He did not look at the road.

*I want you to know two things,* he said. *I'll say them and then I'll go. I'm not the killer. I want you to know that first because I know what it has looked like, and I know my truck was up there, and I know what Earl Whitt said about it, and I know what your lawyer is figuring on saying about me in front of a judge. I am telling you straight. I did not kill Tully Renfro. I have done a lot of things. I have not done that.*

I watched him.

*All right,* I said.

*The second thing. The company has known for about three weeks that Eldon Hicks did it. I am the one that figured it out for them. That is the work I do for them. I went up to the cabin on Tinker Branch about ten days after they pulled Tully out of the hollow, and I sat on Eldon's porch with him, and we talked. He did not say *I did it.* He did not have to. He said five things in a row that any man who has done this work for twenty years would understand the same way. I came back into town. I went to a lawyer the company keeps on retainer in Lexington and I gave him my read. The company has known since. They have made a choice not to act on it. They have made the choice to let your brother fall.*

The semi was gone. The highway was clean behind him.

*Why,* I said.

*Because if Wade Combs goes down for it, the company gets the Bear Branch land in a settlement to your mother that has a quiet release clause on the subsidence claim. Your mother is sixty-seven. She is the only Combs at the table. She buries her boy in a state prison and signs the paper to make the money he doesn't get to spend mean anything. They have done this exact deal three times in this county in the last twenty years. They know how it goes.*

*And if Eldon goes down.*

*If Eldon goes down, the company has a foreman in prison for a murder committed in service of a corporate subsidence strategy and they have a federal grand jury on the lease desk by January.* He paused. *They are not going to let Eldon go down. They were not going to let me let Eldon go down. I work for them. They pay me. I do what they need. I'll be honest with you, Della, I would have let your brother fall. I was prepared to.*

*What changed.*

He looked at me a long second.

*I was at a desk in the company office in Pikeville last Thursday,* he said, *and I overheard a thing I was not supposed to overhear, which was a phone call between the lawyer in Lexington and the lawyer in Frankfort about what to do with me when this case is over. I am the only person outside the family that knows Eldon did it. I am the only person outside the family that knows the company knows. They have decided I am cheaper to retire than to keep paying.*

*Retire,* I said.

*A word they used.*

*All right.*

*A word a man can take more than one way.*

The neon buzzed behind me. A pickup pulled into the lot two slots down from Caudill's, a couple I had seen the day before at the IGA, and they did not look at us, they went straight to their unit and closed the door. Caudill did not turn his head. He was watching me. I was watching his hands.

*Caudill,* I said. *Why are you telling me this.*

He breathed out through his nose. A small slow breath. *Because I have a daughter in nursing school in Lexington,* he said. *And because if I am going to flip — and I am going to flip — I would rather flip to somebody who is going to use the lever to take down the lawyer in Lexington than to a Commonwealth's attorney in Pikeville who is going to use it to take down me. You and your brother's lawyer are going to use it the way I want it used. I am offering you what I have. You are going to be on the witness list before this is over. So am I. I would rather we coordinate.*

*Coordinate.*

*A word.*

*Eldon does not get to walk.*

*No ma'am. Eldon does not get to walk. I am not asking you to let him walk. I am asking you to put the company in the same dock he is in, and I am offering to give you what you need to do it.*

*What do you have.*

He paused. He looked at the neon over my shoulder. He looked back.

*I have,* he said, *a business card with a fountain-pen note on the back. *Call me before the hearing.* It was signed by the lawyer in Lexington and handed to a Combs woman at this motel five weeks ago. I have a memo signed in the same pen, three days before Tully Renfro died, between that lawyer and Tully, about the subsidence strategy. I have been asking around about the pen. The pen is the link. The pen puts the lawyer and Tully in the same room three days before the killing, in a meeting that does not appear on any company calendar, on a memo that was never filed. If the pen makes it onto a witness list, the lawyer makes it on too. The company will not survive the lawyer being on it.*

I did not move.

*Caudill,* I said. *You're saying this to me in a parking lot at midnight with no recorder running.*

*I am.*

*Why should I believe a word.*

*Because tomorrow morning your brother's lawyer is going to get a manila envelope at her office that has a copy of the memo in it and a sticky note that says *anonymous source.* You will know I sent it. She won't. The day after that I am going to make a particular call I have been putting off. The day after that the company is going to know I have flipped. They will know because they will see I went to the wrong man. After that, Della, I am going to need somebody to be willing to say in court that I came to them first.*

*You want me to vouch for you.*

*I want you to be willing to say I came to you on the tenth of November in the parking lot of the Mountain Laurel and I told you the company had known about Eldon Hicks for three weeks and I told you I had decided to make the company answer for it. I am willing to do hard time. I am not willing to do hard time and be the only one. The only thing that makes my time bearable is the lawyer in Lexington doing his.*

He was watching me again. The pink wash of the neon on his cheek. The thin clean mouth.

*One more thing,* he said.

*Yes.*

*Your brother did not lay a hand on Tully Renfro. He did not even leave a mark up there. He just stood and watched. I want you to know that. I read scenes for a living. I have read enough of them to read this one. Wade Combs was up there because somebody asked him to come and he did not understand what was being asked of him until it was being done in front of him, and then he was a man standing in a hollow with his cousin and a body, and he did what a man in this family does, which is keep his mouth shut and walk down. I have a daughter, Della. I read it pretty clean.*

The phrase landed on the concrete between us in a way the rest of what he had said had not. *He just stood and watched.* I had been turning the same sentence around in my head for forty-eight hours in different words. To hear it said by a man I had been afraid of an hour ago and was now standing in a motel parking lot with at midnight was a thing I would think about for a long time.

I said, *All right.*

*All right means what.*

*All right means I'll be at Hannah Soyars's office tomorrow morning when your envelope arrives and I will know what it is and I will not say. If you flip the day after, I will be available to say what you came and said to me tonight. I will not lie for you. I will say what happened.*

*That is what I am asking.*

*Caudill.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*If you are setting me up, if any part of this is the company sending a softer face at me to see what I will move on, I will know. I have been doing my job too long not to.*

*I know you will.*

*And.*

*Yes.*

*If you are telling the truth, I owe you nothing in exchange.*

*I know.*

He nodded once. He took a half step back. He put his right hand into the side pocket of his coat — not fast, slow, deliberate, a man telling me he was reaching for keys, and he pulled out keys, and he held them up so I could see them, and he turned. He walked back to the green truck without hurrying. He got in. He started it. The engine turned over with the easy heave of a truck that had been kept up. He backed out of his slot, swung the nose west, and took the exit onto KY-92 slow.

I watched the brake lights to the stoplight at the IGA. They held there at the red. Then they bent left and were gone.

I sat in my truck. The neon buzzed. I did not start the engine. After a while I got out. I locked the truck. I walked to the door of unit seven with my key in my hand.

I stopped at the door.

I looked at the parking lot. I looked at the row where the green truck had been. I looked at the highway. I looked east, the way I had come in from the diner. I looked, beyond the highway, at the dark mass of the ridge that ran up behind the IGA — the ridge that put its back to the road and its front to the seep at the back of Sorrow Hollow eight miles up.

The cold had come up out of the ground the way it had at the diner. It was not weather cold. It was November cold. Ground-cold. The kind that comes for the marrow and not the skin.

I went inside.

I turned on the lamp by the bed. I sat on the bed with my coat on. I looked at the wall above the headboard, which had a print of a covered bridge in autumn that the motel had hung in every unit, and I picked up the Mountain Laurel envelope off the nightstand — the kind with the address printed in the corner that Mrs. Profitt had laid out in every room for guests who might want to write a letter, which nobody ever did — and I took the pen from the lamp table and I wrote one sentence on the back of the envelope. I wrote it in the run-report register. I wrote it the way I wrote vitals at three in the morning on a bench seat with a patient who was going to make it.

*Nov 10, 23:58 EST. Subject self-presented in lot, MAUREL Motel. Stated company knew Eldon Hicks killer since approx Oct 20. Stated intent to flip. Stated Wade Combs witness only. Subject's truck consistent with prior IDs. No threat displayed. Hands visible throughout.*

I pinned the envelope to the wall above the headboard with a thumbtack I had brought from the office at the Combs house. The thumbtack went into the drywall easy. The covered bridge in autumn was unbothered.

I lay down on the bedspread in my coat. I did not pull the covers back.

I had been on the register since I was a baby. I had a Mountain Laurel envelope above my head with the only contemporaneous evidence of a conversation that had just changed the case. I had a man named Cal Slone who was going to walk into Hannah's office at nine a.m. tomorrow with forty-six years of carried weight in his pocket. I had a brother in a cell in the basement of the courthouse who had not laid a hand on a dead man and had not yet, in his life, been able to say the word *cousin* about Eldon Hicks without something old kicking up under the word.

I had eight days to the preliminary hearing.

The neon buzz was the only noise in the room. After a while it became indistinguishable from the buzz inside my own head and I could not tell which I was hearing.

I shut my eyes. I did not sleep.

---

# Chapter 26 — Wade Tells Her

The visitation room was the same. The same plexiglass. The same scratched *J* at chest height that some boy had given up on years ago. Wade was already in the chair when Becca buzzed me through. He had on the orange and was clean-shaven for the first time in three weeks, and his hands were flat on the counter on his side and were not shaking yet but were going to.

I sat. I picked up the phone receiver.

*Dell,* he said.

*Hey.*

*Mama bring you down.*

*I drove myself.*

*Did you sleep.*

*Not much.*

*Me either.*

*You look better than yesterday.*

*Beard was itching me.*

We sat with the phones to our ears. Becca, behind the glass at the dispatch desk down the hall, was answering a call about a domestic up at Rockhouse Branch and her voice carried faintly along the hard-tiled corridor. The booth smelled the way all visitation booths smell — disinfectant, hot fluorescent, and the layered breath of every person who had sat where I was sitting and not been able to swallow.

*Wade,* I said.

*Yeah.*

*I have been to a man named Cal Slone.*

He looked at me. He did not say anything.

*Buddy Slone's boy,* I said. *From Putney. He sat with me last night at the Brass Lantern and he told me what his daddy told him. Which is what our granddaddy told his daddy. Which is what I think our granddaddy told me. Which is what I now think Pap's son Roy told Eldon. The same thing, told down four lines.*

*Della.*

*Wade. Listen.*

*I am listening.*

*I have been to a man named Caudill Boggs in the parking lot of the Mountain Laurel at midnight. He told me the company knows Eldon did it. He told me he knows. He told me you did not lay a hand on Tully Renfro. He told me you just stood and watched. He told me I needed to know that. I needed to know it from a third party that did not love either of us and had no reason to lie.*

Wade had stopped looking at me. He was looking at the counter on his side of the plexiglass — at his own hands, palm-down, which had begun to do the thing they did when he was tired or sober or both. The right one a little worse than the left. He kept them flat against the formica anyway, the way a man keeps his hands flat on a hood when a trooper has asked him to.

*All right,* he said.

*All right means what.*

*All right means it is true.*

*Wade.*

*Della.*

*Tell me. Tell me all of it. Start at where Eldon called you and end at where you came down the trace. Don't leave anything. I am not going to interrupt. I am asking you to tell me the way you would tell our granddaddy if he was sitting where I am sitting.*

He was quiet for a long time. He looked down at his hands and lifted his right one off the counter and dragged it through his hair. He kept the receiver against his ear with his other hand. He set the right hand back down on the formica and he started.

*Eldon called me on a Saturday,* he said. *That'd have been the fourth. Late afternoon. I was at the house. Mama was at the church. He said, *Wade. I need to see you up at the hollow tomorrow afternoon around two-thirty. I am going to be talking to a man and I would like you with me.* I said, *What man.* He said, *Tully Renfro. He has been on Aunt Donnie about the lease and I am going to ask him to back off. I want you there because the both of us standing there is more than the one of us.* I said, *Why up the hollow.* He said, *I am asking him to meet me at a place where he understands what kind of conversation we are having.* I said, *Eldon. Are you going to do something stupid.* He said, *Wade. I am going to ask him to back off. That is what I am going to do. I would like you with me. If you are not coming, say so now.*

*And.*

*And I said I would come.*

*Why.*

*Because he was the only family I had outside this house that ever asked me to do anything that wasn't a roofing job. Because I had been sober eight months and the only person besides Mama and Lem who had said *good for you, Wade* to my face about it was Eldon. Because he was kin. Because I am stupid, Della. Because I thought it was a thing two grown men were going up a hollow to do, which was to put it to a third grown man that he needed to leave their aunt alone. I thought it was a *talk.* I thought *we'll go in there in our work boots and stand around him and he'll get it.* I thought that.*

*All right.*

*I drove up at about two. I left the truck at the trailhead and walked up. Eldon was at the flat by the seep. Alone. He had a piece of rock in his hand the size of a cantaloupe and he was looking at it. I came up. He said, *Wade. I want you at the pine. Let me handle the talking. He'll be up in a minute. He likes to be on time.* I said, *Eldon, what is the rock for.* He looked at me. He said, *Wade. It is a piece of rock. The man is a land man. The man is going to understand he is in a place where there is a great deal of rock.* I went and stood at the pine.*

He stopped. His right hand had started shaking. He lifted it off the counter and put it down again on his knee under the counter where I could not see it. He kept the left on the formica.

*Tully came up the trace by himself,* Wade said. *About a quarter past two. Carhartt on him over his suit pants. He looked like a man who had walked too steep a grade in the wrong shoes. He stopped an arm's length off Eldon. He said, *Eldon.* Eldon said, *Tully. I want to know what the company is going to do about the subsidence claim.* Tully said, *The company has not made a final decision and even if they had, this would not be the conversation.* Eldon said, *Tully. I am asking you. As one Caldwell County man to another. Is the company going to file.* Tully looked at him. He looked at the rock. He looked at me at the pine. He said, *Eldon. There is a young man at the pine.* Eldon said, *That's Wade Combs. My cousin. He's here because this involves his mother's land.* Tully said, *Then this should be at the courthouse.* Eldon said, *Tully. Is the company going to file.* Tully said, *Eldon. I'm walking back down.
