You and I will do this Monday with the lawyers.* Eldon said, *Tully.* And Tully turned around and Eldon hit him in the side of the head with the rock.*

*Wade.*

*Della. Let me finish it.*

*Finish it.*

*He hit him once and Tully went down. Tully was on his side. He was breathing. He was making a sound I have heard once before in my life. Eldon hit him twice more on the left side of the head while he was on the ground. The second one was the one that stopped the sound. The third was just a man making sure. Eldon stood up. He set the rock down. He turned around and he looked at me at the pine.*

I had not breathed.

*He said, *Wade. Come over here.* I went. I stood next to him. He was looking down at Tully. He said, *Wade. Do you know what I just did.* I said, *Eldon, you killed him.* He said, *Wade. Do you know why.* I said, *Eldon. I don't.* He said, *I did it because Tully was going to take the holler. I am the only one in the family in a position to know what they were about to do. Nobody else was going to. You go home and sit at that table and eat your mother's cornbread and you understand that this hollow is still going to be here when you are dead because I did this.* And I stood there.*

*Wade.*

*I stood there. I did not say anything. Eldon bent down and he picked a piece of cannel coal out of his coat pocket and he laid it on Tully's chest. He laid it square in the middle of his sternum. He did it like a man laying a flower on a casket. He stood up. He reached into his other pocket and he took out a second piece of coal — smaller, polished — and he held it out to me and he said, *Wade. I need you to put this on the flat sandstone yonder. Don't argue. Put it there.* I said, *Eldon.* He said, *Wade. Combs and Hicks have always carried it together. We carry this one together. Put it on the rock.* And I took it.*

He stopped. He was crying without sound. The tears were running into the receiver and he was not wiping them. He kept the receiver against his ear.

*I took it,* he said. *I walked the ten feet to the sandstone and I set it on the flat. I set it down the way a man sets a quarter on a counter. I came back. He said, *Now we walk down.* We did. He was ahead of me. I don't remember if we said anything. At the trailhead he got in his truck and I got in mine and I went home. Mama was at the church. I sat on the bed in my work clothes for an hour. Then I washed my face and went down and made supper for me and Mama because she was going to be tired when she came back from the church. We ate. I didn't say anything. She didn't ask. Three days later they came and arrested me.*

He was quiet. His left hand on the formica had begun to shake to match the right.

*Eldon hasn't said a word,* he said. *Eldon hasn't even looked sorry. I have not seen him since I came down the trace that day. He has not come to visit me. He has not sent a message through Mama. He laid that piece of coal in my hand and he said *we carry this one together* and then he went home and went on with his life. And I have been sitting in this room for thirty days because of a piece of coal I set down on a rock with my own hand.*

*Wade.*

*Della.*

*Why didn't you say.*

He looked at me for the first time in a long time. His eyes were the eyes of a thirty-six-year-old man who looked twenty-five and fifty at the same time. He was the same color we both were — the same skin, the same hair, the same line at the corner of the mouth that came down from our father.

*Because the second I say it,* he said, *I am a man who placed evidence at the scene of a homicide under instruction from his cousin. Hannah told me that the first day. *Wade,* she said, *if you ever tell anybody you put that piece of coal on the rock, you will be charged with accessory after the fact, and you will go to a state institution for at least a year, possibly five.* I have already been in this room thirty days. I can do a year. I'd rather do five for that than thirty for the other. I would. But the second I say it, Della, I am not the only one I am taking down. I am taking down Eldon and I am taking down what Eldon thought he was doing, which was protecting the hollow, which I am not sure he was wrong about. And I am taking down the code itself. And once you take down the code there is nothing in this family that keeps any of us from being a tool the company picks up next.*

*Wade.*

*I know.*

*You are taking it down anyway.*

*I know.*

*Hannah is going to ask you to.*

*I know she is.*

*And Cal Slone is going to walk into her office at nine and corroborate the meaning of the second piece of coal, and Caudill Boggs is going to flip on the lawyer in Lexington who was running the subsidence strategy, and Eldon is going to be the one we offer the Commonwealth, and they will want you to corroborate, and you will, and you will plead to a misdemeanor obstruction and you will do six months at a county facility and you will come home and Mama will still be at the church and the hollow will still be there. That is what is happening, Wade. Cal Slone has set this in motion. Caudill has set this in motion. I have set this in motion. You sat in this room thirty days carrying it and the door has been open the whole time. We just couldn't find the latch.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*If you tell, you have to know what you are doing. You are not just sending Eldon away. You are breaking something we have all been holding up. I don't know what falls down when you do.*

*I know.*

*Granddaddy carried it for forty-four years.*

*I know.*

*He carried it for the same reason I have been carrying it.*

*Wade. He carried it because he didn't know how to set it down. Cal Slone told me last night our granddaddy was the most cornered man he ever knew. Granddaddy spent forty-four years trying to hand it off and almost ran out of time to do it. I am not going to let you carry it forty-four years. I am not going to let you carry it five.*

He looked at me. He was crying still but the shake had gone out of his hands. They sat flat on the formica again. He was not wiping the tears. He let them come.

*All right, Dell,* he said.

*All right means.*

*All right means I will tell Hannah today.*

*Today.*

*I will tell her today. I will tell her exactly what I told you. I will not soften it. I will not protect Eldon. I will not pretend I did not put the coal on the rock. I will not pretend I did not know what was being asked of me when I took it. I will say it the way it happened. I will let her use it the way she needs to.*

*Wade.*

*Yes.*

*I love you.*

*I love you, Dell.*

*I will be back this afternoon.*

*I know.*

*I will tell Mama.*

*She knows.*

*She knows what.*

*She knows it was Eldon. He told her. The day after.*

I sat with the receiver against my ear. I did not say anything for what would have been a count I could not have given anyone.

*Wade,* I said.

*I know, Dell.*

*I will see you this afternoon.*

I hung up. I stood up. I walked out of the booth, down the corridor, past Becca who looked up from her dispatch desk and said *Della, honey,* and put her hand out toward the air between us, and I touched her fingertips with mine as I went by, and I went up the basement stairs and out the front door of the courthouse into the November light.

I walked across the lot to my truck. I unlocked it. I got in. I sat with my hands on the wheel.

I had not cried in my professional life. I had not cried in my personal life since I was twenty-one in the spring of 2005, in the parking lot of the funeral home in Caldwell, four days late, alone. I had wondered, sometimes, in my fifth year and my eighth year and my tenth year on the job, whether the thing was still in me. I had decided not to think about it.

I cried in my truck for two minutes. I cried with my hands on the wheel and my mouth shut and my eyes open. I let the tears go down my face and onto the collar of my jacket and onto my hands on the wheel. I did not make a sound. I am a paramedic and I have been a paramedic for twelve years and even at the lowest moment of my life I cried like one — quiet, hands at ten and two, eyes on the windshield. I cried until the thing that needed to come out had come out. I drew a breath. I wiped my face on my sleeve. I keyed the engine.

I pulled out of the lot. I turned left on Main toward KY-92. I drove west toward Hannah's office in Pikeville with my brother's voice in my ear and Eldon Hicks's voice in his ear and Pap Sturgill's voice in Eldon's ear and Hollis Combs's voice underneath all of them, the way a creek runs underneath a road you have driven for years and never seen.

The hollow was still there. It was going to be there when we were all dead. That was, I thought, going down the four-lane, the only thing in any of this that any of us had ever been right about.

---

# Chapter 27 — The Question

I left the house at five-thirty in the dark. Mama was up — she had not slept, was at the kitchen window with the propane on. She did not turn around when I came down the stairs. She said, *Della.* I said, *I'm going up to the hollow.* She said, *Take the small thermos. The big one's filthy.* I took the small thermos. She had filled it with coffee made on the stove the old way, with eggshell in the grounds. She had laid a buttered biscuit on a paper towel under a fold of waxed paper. I picked them up. I did not say *thank you.* In this house you did not say *thank you* for that. I said, *I'll be back by the middle of the morning.* She said, *I know.* I went out.

The light at the back of the house was the no-color light it is before the eastern ridge is gray. The cold was the cold the ground gives after a clear night, the air of November in this part of the county and the air of nowhere else. I drove up Bear Branch the way Hollis used to drive it, in second at the curves, in third on the straight stretches, the headlights catching nothing for whole seconds and then catching the white tail of a deer crossing into Verna's pasture and gone. Verna's porch light was on. It was on every morning at this hour and had been for sixty years.

I parked at the trailhead. I did not turn on my flashlight. The light was coming up enough behind the ridge that the trace was its own faint grayer line through the dark. I started up.

The walk was forty-five minutes by daylight and the same by half-light. It cut left around the rib of the second knob, climbed the long pull above the McComb spring, leveled at the saddle where the chestnut stump still sat like a low table at the side of the path, then dropped into the back of the hollow proper. I had walked it as a child with my granddaddy. I had not walked it in twenty-five years. My body remembered the place under my right foot where the trace canted slightly toward the drop and you had to set your weight a touch inside. It remembered the smell of laurel in the cold. It remembered that at the saddle the wind always came up out of the south no matter what it was doing in the valley.

I came down off the saddle. The flat opened up below me. I had been here at the edge in the daylight three weeks ago. I had not crossed in. This morning I went down the trace and across the flat and I sat down on the log six feet from the flat sandstone outcrop, the log the deputies had sat on eating ham sandwiches the day they processed the scene, the log that had been there since 1976 when a windstorm took the white oak above it down.

I sat. I set the thermos on the log beside me. I put the biscuit on top of the thermos. I put my hands in the pockets of my coat. The cold came up through the wood of the log.

I watched the flat come light.

The first light at the back of the hollow does not come the way light comes other places. The hollow is east-west on its long axis and the ridge to the east is high, so the sun does not enter the hollow until it has been up for a long time over the rest of the county. What comes first is a slow gray that is not sun, an underwater gray, the kind of light a creek has at dawn. The shapes that have been dark take edges in it. The pine. The seep. The flat sandstone outcrop where Wade set the small piece of coal that Eldon laid in his hand. The patch on the laurel where Tully Renfro had gone down. The leaves on the patch were dark with three weeks of weather and rain. I could see them. I could not see them as anything I needed to act on. The deputies had taken what they were going to take.

The seep made its small water-sound at the back of the flat. The seep was always making that sound. It had made it for Hollis. It had made it for Pap. It had made it for me when I was eight years old and my granddaddy had handed me a tin cup with a cracked handle and a single drop of cold water sliding down the outside of the cup onto his thumb. *Try this water and tell me if it tastes like anything else you have ever had,* he had said. I had drunk. It had not. He had nodded once. He had not said anything else about it. We had gone home down the trace at noon and Mama had made grilled cheese for our supper and Hollis had eaten his at the table without taking his cap off and Mama had not made him.

I sat on the log. I did not drink the coffee yet.

I had six days. I had Hannah's office to come back to at noon. I had Cal Slone in the conference room at nine, and Caudill's envelope on the desk, and Wade's full account that Hannah had taken last night in three pages of legal-pad longhand, and a Commonwealth's attorney in Pikeville who was going to be told today at two p.m. that there was a witness with corroborating outside-line knowledge of the meaning of a coal-on-the-chest signature and a foreman at Blackridge who had killed a land agent and a brother who would plead to a misdemeanor in exchange for testimony, and the lawyer in Lexington whose fountain pen was about to walk into evidence on the back of a business card. I had a lever. I had three of them. I had a kitchen table with my mother at one chair and my cousin at another at seven the night before the preliminary, if I asked for it, which I was going to.

I had everything except the answer to the question.

The question was *what kind of person are you going to be when you do this.*

I had been turning it under my hands for forty-eight hours. The medic in me said: stabilize, hand off, leave. The medic in me had been saying that for twenty years and it had served me. It had built me a life in Knoxville with a Type I rig and a captain who said *take what you need, Combs* and a one-bedroom over a vet's office on Sutherland Avenue with a window that looked at a sweetgum in the parking lot and a man named Hamp who waved at me in the lot at shift change and did not look twice. It had built me a body that could cross any threshold and come out the other side. It had built me a profession in which I had not, until yesterday in my truck in the courthouse lot, cried. It had been a discipline. It had been a wall. It had been the wall I had needed.

It was the thing that was going to fail me here.

You cannot triage and hand off a thing that is yours. You cannot stabilize a family. *Stabilize, hand off, leave* was a sentence I had used for twenty years to mean *I will not stay inside the pain long enough for it to know me.* I had walked out of my granddaddy's bedroom in 2005 and got on I-75 south and not looked back. I had walked out of my father's funeral in 2007 the same way. I had walked out of Annie Couch's lunch in 2020 the same way. Every time I had walked, the place had let me walk. It would let you go. It would not pull you back. But if you came back the way I had come back, on a phone call from your mother at five-thirty-eight in the morning, it required of you a thing it had not required when you left. It required that you sit in it.

I unscrewed the thermos. I poured. I drank. The biscuit was cold and it was good. I ate half. I broke the other half and held it in my lap, because if you sat at the edge of a hollow with a biscuit and a wren came up out of the laurel at first light, you offered. The wren did not come. I ate the other half.

The pine at the back of the flat was taking shape now. It was the size it had always been — a white pine four feet through at the breast, older than any human bone in the family, older than the deed. I had told my granddaddy once when I was nine that the pine was bigger than the house. He had looked at it. He had said, *Honey, the pine has been bigger than the house since before there was a house. The pine does not measure itself against the house. We measure the house against the pine.* I had thought it was the kind of thing he said because he liked the sound of it. I thought now he had meant it as instruction.

I stood up. I left the thermos and the cap on the log. I walked across the flat to the seep.

The seep came out of the sandstone at the back of the flat about waist-high. It came out where Hollis had stacked the stones — flat creek stones, set without mortar, the way you set a basin if you had grown up making basins. The water came out at fifty-four degrees. It came out at fifty-four degrees in August and at fifty-four degrees in February. The mountain held the temperature inside itself and gave it back at the rim of the rock without any change. The basin was small. It held maybe a quart. The water filled the basin and the excess slipped over the lip on the downhill side and ran a thin clear channel ten feet through the moss and disappeared.

I crouched at the basin. I cupped my hands. I waited for the cup of my hands to fill.

The water came up cold. It came up cold against the skin of my palms and it came up cold against the inside of my fingers and the part of my wrists that was bare between my coat sleeve and my hand. It filled the cup of my hands. I lifted it. I drank.

It tasted the way it had tasted when I was eight. It tasted the way it had tasted the day Pap drank from it after he killed Calvin Tull, which was July of 1961, in the heat, his hands maybe shaking, maybe not, an old farmer at sixty-three who had waited twenty-nine years for a single afternoon. It tasted the way it had tasted when Hollis drank from it after he had helped bury Tull and walked down the trace alone at dark, my grandmother waiting on the porch in Bear Branch with the lamp lit because he had told her he might not be home before nine, and she had said *all right* the way she said *all right* to many things in their forty-two years. It tasted of the rock. It tasted of the inside of the mountain. It did not taste of anything that had happened in it.

I drank again. I cupped my hands and let them fill and lifted them and drank. I did it three times. I did not say anything to myself in my head about what it meant to drink. It did not mean anything. I had wanted my granddaddy's mouth on this water and my great-grandfather's mouth on this water and my own mouth on this water in the same week, and they were on it now, and the thing the water did was the thing the water had always done, which was come up at the same temperature and leave the basin at the same rate and run the same ten feet through the same moss and be gone.

A line of hymn came up the way they did in this place. *Shall we gather at the river.* I did not finish it. I did not need to. The next line was the next line.

I stood up. I dried my hands on the front of my jeans.

I walked to the pine. I put my left hand on the trunk. The trunk was cold and rough and broken at the bark the way old pine bark breaks — into plates the size of a man's hand, gray-brown, gapped at the edges so you could see down into the deeper bark which was redder and smoother. I had touched this tree at eight years old. I had touched it at fourteen, at sixteen. I had not touched it in twenty-five years. The body of it under my palm had the same temperature as the seep. The same fifty-four. The mountain held it the same way.

At the base of the pine on the east side, where the wind had cleared the duff, a single piece of cannel coal sat in the moss. It was the size of a thumbnail. It was glossy and old. It was not, I thought, evidence. It was not Eldon's and it was not Pap's. It was a piece of cannel that had been lying at the base of the Combs Pine since before Eldon had been born, probably since before Hollis had been born, probably since the company first opened a portal three valleys over in 1907 and the chips and dust of cannel had begun, by the trickle of decades, to surface in places nobody was looking. It was just coal. I left it.

I walked back to the log. I picked up the thermos and the cap. I screwed the cap on. I stood at the log with the thermos in my hand and I looked at the flat sandstone outcrop.

The first sun was hitting it now. The sun came in low and bronze across the top of the eastern ridge and laid down on the wet of the rock — the rock was wet with the night's frost-melt, the way rocks were wet at this hour in this hollow at this time of year — and the wet on the rock under the slanting light made a shape that was not coal-shaped. It was just shine.

I stood and looked at it. I thought about the formica at the Brass Lantern Monday night. Cal Slone had laid a small flat piece of cannel down on the formica between us under the buzzing fluorescent. The light over our booth had hit the polished face of the coal and put a shape on the formica next to it that was not coal-shaped either. It was the same shape. It was the way light moves on anything wet or polished in this part of the world. The two shapes were the same shape. The men were on a different ledger but the light was the same light.

I had not made the decision I had come up here to make. The chapter of it that had been in my head as a question — *do I do this or do I not, do I sit my mother and my cousin at the kitchen table or do I let the case run on a witness-stand testimony with Hannah doing the work, do I use the fountain-pen lever myself or do I hand it to the Commonwealth* — was not the chapter I had been sitting in. The decision had been made the day Mama opened the hymnal in front of me and laid Hollis's letter on the table and I said *I am staying.* It had been made on the porch with Verna in October. It had been made at my granddaddy's bedside in April of 2005 when he had taken my hand and given me a thing he had been carrying for forty-four years and I had taken it without knowing I was taking it. I had come up to the hollow at six in the morning in November to find the answer to a question that had already been answered. I had come up because I wanted to put my mouth on the water and my hand on the pine before I went down and did the thing.

I did not say *I am going to do it* in my head. I did not need to. The water was already in me. The pine was already under my hand. The light was already on the rock. The thing I had to do was, of an evening, going to get done.

I bent and picked up the biscuit crumbs off the log so as not to leave them. I put them in my coat pocket. I shouldered the thermos by its strap. I started down the trace.

At the saddle I turned and looked back. The flat was bright. The light was on the pine. The seep made its water-sound. The sandstone had dried. The patch on the laurel where Tully Renfro had gone down was dark, would be dark another month, would be green by April. The hollow was the hollow. It had been the hollow since before any of us. It was going to keep being the hollow. That was what it did.

I walked down the trace. I made it to the truck by seven-forty.

I drove to Hannah's office.

---

# Chapter 28 — Hannah

Hannah's office was on the third floor of the old Citizens Bank building and the elevator had not worked in a year and a half. I came up the stairs in my coat with the thermos still in my hand. Three weeks had given me the rhythm of these stairs. The tread on the second landing sagged a quarter-inch and you stepped wide. I stepped wide. I came down the corridor past the accountant and the chiropractor and the long stretch of vacant offices the carpet had not been replaced in since 1987. Hannah's door was the third on the left. Frosted glass, *Attorney at Law* in flaking gold, her hours typed on paper taped to the inside.

I went in. Hannah was at her desk in the inner office with the door open, head bent over a legal pad, hair up with a pencil through it. She looked up.

*Della. Come in. Shut the door.*

I shut the door. I sat in the client chair, which was a wing chair with a tea stain on the right arm she had told me on the first day was from a deposition she had given a particularly bad-tempered insurance defense attorney in 2022. I set the thermos on the corner of her desk. I took my coat off.

*Cal Slone was here at nine,* she said.

*I know.*

*He stayed until quarter of eleven. He gave me four pages on a legal pad in his own hand. He said he is willing to come to Pikeville on the day of the preliminary, sit in the gallery, and be on the witness list as a corroborating party. He brought his daddy's Bible and a notebook of his daddy's that had Hollis's name in it twice in 1962. He had thought of these as exhibits in advance. Della, your Mr. Slone is a person.*

*I know.*

*He is also,* she said, *what I have been looking at the ceiling for at three in the morning for two weeks. He is the missing piece. He puts the meaning of the coal on the chest in front of a jury without making me put your grandfather on the stand for a job your grandfather can't be on the stand for, which is to testify to his own death-bed confession to a man I never met. He carries the chain of meaning from Hollis to my courtroom. He carries it cleanly.*

I sat. I had not yet told her about Caudill. I had not told her about Wade. I had not told her about the seep.

*Hannah.*

*Tell me.*

I told her. I told her in the order it had happened. The diner at nine, the cobbler nobody finished, the small flat piece of coal on the formica, the chorus of the hymn Cal had hummed under his breath at the cup of his coffee, the moment he had said the words *He says the women in his line will.* I told her about coming back to the Mountain Laurel and finding the green truck. I told her about Caudill standing in the wash of the neon with his hands a quarter outward at his sides. I told her what he had said. I told her about the envelope. I took the envelope out of my coat pocket — I had brought it down off the wall above the bed at the motel before I came up the stairs — and I laid it on her desk in front of her, the thumbtack still through the corner. She read the sentence I had written. She did not say anything for a minute. She set the envelope inside a manila folder that had Wade's name across the top and she folded the folder shut and she set her pen across the top of it.

*The manila envelope came at ten,* she said.

*From Caudill.*

*Sticky note, anonymous source. Copy of a memo on Blackridge letterhead dated October second, signed in fountain pen, between counsel and Tully Renfro about the subsidence strategy on Bear Branch. A memo that does not exist on any company calendar and was, by everything I know about how a firm like that papers a file, never meant to leave a particular drawer in a particular office in Lexington. The pen on the memo is the same pen on the back of the business card you gave me Monday. I had the card on my desk. I lined them up. I sat in this chair for thirty minutes looking at the two strokes of the descender on the *y* in *call* and the *y* in *yours.* Same pen. Same hand. The lawyer in Lexington is in this.*

*Hannah.*

*Caudill is going to flip when he flips,* she said. *I am going to tell the Commonwealth on Friday that I have an inbound witness who I expect to make himself available by Sunday at the latest. I am not going to name him until he is in my office. I am not going to put him in your mother's lap. He's a separate engine running on a separate track. I will manage him.*

*All right.*

*Tell me Wade.*

I told her Wade. I told her what he had told me through the receiver. I told her the rock by the seep, the *Tully* and the *Eldon* and the *I am asking you,* the way Eldon had set the rock down and turned around to look at Wade at the pine, the words *we carry this one together,* the small polished piece of cannel in Eldon's other coat pocket that Wade had taken out of his cousin's hand and walked ten feet across the flat with and set on the sandstone like a quarter on a counter. I told her Wade had said he would tell her today. I told her what Wade had said about the door being open and us not finding the latch. I told her my brother had cried into the receiver with his hands flat on the formica on his side of the plexiglass, and I had not, but I had cried in the truck in the lot at the courthouse for two minutes afterward, and I had not cried in twenty years, and I was telling her because she should know.

Hannah took it without writing. She had her pen down. She was watching me. When I finished she sat for a second with her eyes on the desk between us.

*All right,* she said. *Della. Here is the legal landscape.*

She pulled the legal pad over. Fresh page. She drew a small box at the top and inside it wrote *Wade.* A line down. *Deal.* Beside *deal,* a fan of three boxes: *talk* / *names* / *places coal.* Off the right, a long line to a circled word at the bottom of the page: *Eldon.*

*Here it is,* she said. *Wade names Eldon. Wade corroborates the placement of the coal — including his own. He agrees in writing that he was present at the killing, did not strike a blow, was instructed to place the second piece of coal, and complied. He pleads to a misdemeanor obstruction. He does six months at a county facility, with credit for time served on the present charge. He is out by next May. The Commonwealth moves to drop the murder against him. The Commonwealth proceeds against Eldon Hicks for second-degree murder, supported by Wade's testimony, Caudill's documentary evidence, and Cal Slone on the meaning of the signature. Eldon's options are to plead or to go to trial against a Commonwealth case that has, on the day it opens, a confessed accessory, a fixer with paper, and a hand-witnessed-by-a-county-elder coal code. Eldon pleads. He does ten to twelve.*

*All right.*

*That,* she said, *is path A. It is the path I have been building for two weeks.* She drew, on the lower half of the legal pad, a second tree. Wider. More boxes. *Path B is wider. Path B uses the same testimony from Wade, the same Cal Slone, the same Caudill — but it also puts the 1961 case on the table. Not as defense. As context. I walk into the Commonwealth's attorney's office and I lay out the chain. Pap Sturgill in 1961. Hollis Combs as accomplice and silent partner. Calvin Tull as company man. The 1932 Letty Anne file. The 1959 strike, the McCreary section, Asa Combs. The newspaper item. The microfiche. The personnel note about Hollis Combs from the 1959 termination file: *Combs is the one who'll come back for it.* I put the whole 1961 case on the table and I show the Commonwealth that the company has been benefiting from a pattern of family code-keeping in this county for sixty-four years. I show the Commonwealth that in 2025 the company knew Eldon Hicks had killed Tully Renfro three weeks before they did anything about it and chose not to. I show the Commonwealth that the lawyer in Lexington was running a subsidence strategy and that a foreman killed the land agent who was going to execute it and that the company kept the strategy moving while a Combs man sat in a cell for the killing. I open the door to a grand-jury inquiry on Blackridge.*

She looked up at me.

*Path B does not save Wade any harder than path A,* she said. *Wade still pleads. Wade still does six months. Path B costs Wade nothing that path A does not also cost him. But path B is the path that puts the company at the table next to Eldon. Path A puts only Eldon. Path B is the one your granddaddy was setting up when he told Cal Slone's daddy at the fence in 1962. He didn't set up a relay for a single homicide prosecution. He set up a relay to put the structure in front of the structure.*

I sat with my hand on the wing of the chair.

*Path B,* I said.

*Hold on. Hold on. There's a price.*

*Tell me.*

*The price is that the 1961 case becomes a public matter. The Bugle prints it. The AP picks it up. The Combs name and the Sturgill name and the Hicks name and the Slone name are in print together for the first time. Your mother's last name is in a newspaper article about a 1961 homicide. Your cousin Lem's last name is in a newspaper article about a 1961 homicide. Verna Hicks's last name is in a newspaper article about a 1961 homicide, and Verna Hicks is eighty-eight and has not asked to be in any newspaper. Path B asks four families that have carried something quietly for sixty-four years to carry it loudly for the rest of their lives.*

*Hannah.*

*I am not making the choice for you, Della. This is a choice for the Combs and the Sturgills and the Hickses and the Slones. Cal Slone has already said yes by walking in here at nine. Caudill has said yes by walking into your motel parking lot at midnight. Your brother is going to say yes by talking to me at two o'clock when they bring him over from the courthouse. Your mother has not been asked. Verna has not been asked.*

*Verna is going to say yes.*

*Probably. But she has to be asked.*

*Hannah.*

*Yes.*

*Path B is the path I want.*

*All right.*

*I want to ask my mother tonight. I want to ask Verna in the morning.*

*Do it.*

*And then.*

She set her pen down. She looked at me across the desk.

*And then,* she said, *there is a thing I have been thinking on for two days. The right thing for you to do is to sit Eldon Hicks down before this all moves and give him the chance to be the one who tells. Not to spare him. To make the telling come from him and not from us. The Commonwealth will treat him better if he comes forward. Your family will survive better if he comes forward. The hearing on Tuesday will be cleaner. I am going to tell you something I should probably not tell you as your brother's lawyer, but I am going to. You have a kitchen table. You have a mother who has known about Eldon for a month and not said. You have a cousin who is family to your mother on his daddy's side. You should put the three of you at the table on Monday night and you should have the letters in front of you and the business card in front of you and Caudill's flip behind you, and you should ask Eldon to do the thing he ought to do. If he says no, the prosecution still moves on Tuesday. If he says yes, it moves cleaner.*

*Monday night.*

*Monday night.*

*The night before.*

*The night before. I am his lawyer's adversary, Della. I am not his lawyer. But I am also a person who lives in this place. I have a kitchen table too. I know the work it can do.*

We sat. She was looking at me. I had the cup of paper coffee she had handed me at some point in the previous hour without my registering it, and the coffee was cold now and the cup was making a wet ring on the corner of her desk. I lifted the cup and set it on a coaster. The coaster said *PIKEVILLE BAR ASSN ANNUAL DINNER 2019.* I noticed it for the first time.

*Hannah.*

*Yes.*

*Will you come to the kitchen table.*

*No.*

*Why.*

*Because it cannot be a lawyer at the table. It has to be the three of you. The minute I am at the table he leaves. You and your mother and Eldon. Three people on a single bench of inheritance. That is the table.*

*All right.*

*Della.*

*Yes.*

*I am proud of you. I do not say that to clients. I am saying it as a person in this town to another person.*

*I know.*

*Go ahead and call your mother.*

I picked up the thermos and my coat and went out. I closed the door. Came down the corridor past the chiropractor's hallway radio on a country station playing a song I could not place. Stepped wide on the landing. Came out onto Main Street.

It was four-forty-five and the light was going. The sycamores on Main were almost bare. The river behind the bank smelled of cold sand and wet rock. I got in the truck. I sat with my hand on the wheel.

I dialed Mama.

She picked up on the first ring.

*Della.*

*Mama. I need you to do a thing.*

*Tell me.*

*Will you ask Eldon to come up to the house Monday night for coffee.*

She did not say anything for a count.

*Just for coffee.*

*Just for coffee.*

*Della.*

*Mama.*

*This is the one.*

*This is the one, Mama.*

*All right, Della.*

*Just you ask him.*

*I will call him in the morning. I will tell him I want to fix him a piece of pie. I will ask him to come at seven. He will come.*

*All right.*

*Della.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*Your granddaddy used to call me from the booth at the IGA when he had something he wanted me to do that he could not ask for at the supper table. I am sitting in this kitchen and you are in a parking lot in Pikeville and we are doing the same thing he used to do.*

*I know.*

*All right.*

*Mama.*

*Yes.*

*Get some sleep.*

*I will try.*

I hung up. I sat for a second with the phone in my hand. The sycamore at the corner of the lot was bare enough now I could see the little red ribbon someone had tied around one of the high branches in October as a marker — there was a tree-trimming crew due, it was a city ribbon, I remembered it from Monday — and the ribbon was still there. The crew had not come yet. The ribbon was very small and very high and very red against the gray.

I keyed the engine. I drove back in the last of the light, KY-92 east, four-lane to two-lane at Jenkins, the slow drop through Letcher into the valley, the curve where you start to see the smokestack of the old plant at Caldwell two miles ahead. My body knew the curves better than the mind did.

The hollow was up the trace. The pine was at the back of the flat. The seep was making its small water-sound. Mama was in the kitchen and she was, I knew, going to start cooking once she got off the phone, because she did not know any other way to wait. Wade was at the courthouse in a room with Hannah and a legal pad. Cal Slone was at his house in Putney. Caudill was at a turnout somewhere on US 23 with his hands at ten and two on the wheel of the green truck. Eldon was at the strip on the Cumberland Plateau in a hardhat with the missing joint of his right index finger inside a glove.

We were all of us moving toward Monday night.

The light went. The headlights came on by themselves at the sensor. I drove on home.

---

# Chapter 29 — Ladonna

I drove home from Hannah's a little past nine and the night had the cold in it that a Caldwell County night gets when the wind turns and the leaves up the ridge start letting go in handfuls. The road from Pikeville to home is two hours of dark and one good gas station, and I had not eaten since a sandwich at noon. I did not eat then either. I stopped once where the road comes over the gap above Hellier and shut the truck off and sat with my hands on the wheel the way Boyd had sat the morning of the first chapter of all this and I tried to find the sentence I was going to say to my mother. I could not find it. I started the truck back up and went on.

The kitchen light was on at the house. Of course it was. She had been waiting up since before dark. I came in through the side door and took my coat off and hung it on the peg I had hung my coat on since I was nine years old. The hymnal was on the refrigerator the way it had been on the refrigerator since 1961, give or take the few times Vera had carried it to her chair and back. Ladonna was at the table with a cup of tea gone cold beside her elbow and a Bible open in front of her that she was not reading.

She looked up. *Della.*

*Mama.* I sat down across from her. The table between us was the table. The same boards, the same scarred legs. The propane heater behind me ticked once and was quiet.

She did not ask. She did not ask because she already knew. I watched her face for the second it took her to decide whether she was going to let me speak first or whether she was going to spare me the trouble, and I saw her decide. She closed the Bible. She put both her small hands flat on it as if she meant to hold it down.

*Tell me what you know,* she said.

I told her. I told her plain, the way you tell a husband on a porch step that his wife did not make it on the table, because the only mercy left at that point is not to make him work for it. I told her Eldon killed Tully Renfro. I told her Wade was up the holler with him. I told her Wade had placed the second piece of coal himself and was sitting in a jail cell because the family code said he could not name his cousin, and the law would say he was an accessory if he did, and the two of those together had locked his mouth and the lock was Pap's lock and it had been on every Combs mouth since 1961 in one form or another. I told her about the hymnal letter I had read here in this kitchen two weeks ago. I told her I had been to Eldon's cabin and that Eldon had told me what he did and would not turn himself in. I told her Hannah said the law would let Wade out if Wade told it, and Wade would not, and I had six days.

I said it in the medic voice. I did not let it tremble. I sat across from my mother and gave her the run report.

She listened. She did not move while I spoke. The only thing that moved on her was her left thumb, very slightly, against the cover of the Bible — the thumb that wore her own wedding band, not Vera's. When I was done she did not look away. She looked at me the way she had looked at me one time, when I was eleven, and I had broken a thing of her mother's and she had wanted to know if I was going to tell her the truth about it before she asked. She had told me, after I confessed, that she had already known and had been waiting on me. She had not punished me. She had given me a piece of cornbread out of the pan and gone outside to sit on the porch alone for an hour.

She said, *Della.*

*Yes, ma'am.*

*I knew about Eldon. I have known for a month.*

I had been ready for a lot of sentences. I had not been ready for that one. The kitchen tilted very slightly the way a porch tilts in a dream and then it righted.

*Mama.*

*He came over here the day after. October the sixth, a Monday. Wade was at Lem's. Eldon come up the steps and asked could he sit down and I made him coffee because that's what you do, and he told me what he had done.* She said it level. *He asked me to keep it. I told him I would.*

I made myself breathe in. I made myself breathe out. *Mama. Wade was in jail by Friday.*

*I know when Wade was in jail. I know what week we are in. Don't you talk to me like I am simple.*

*I am not.*

*You are.* She did not say it cruelly. She said it the way she had said *Della. Eat your supper* for forty-one years. *You are talking to me the way you talk to a person who you think is about to fall apart, and I am not about to fall apart, Della, I am the person who has not fallen apart while you have been gone. Don't.*

I sat back. *All right,* I said.

*All right.* She put her hand flat on the Bible again. *Now you listen to me. I would have let your brother go to prison, Della. I would have. I want you to hear me say it. I am not going to say it twice but I am going to say it once and then I am going to tell you why, because you are owed both.*

*I am listening.*

Her voice did not crack. *Because if Eldon goes, the company is going to come for everything. Bear Branch and the Hicks side and the Combses up at the head of Tinker and Roy's old place and all of it. That is not a paranoia, Della, that is what is in the file. Tully Renfro brought me a draft of that subsidence claim in this kitchen in July. He left a copy. It is in the pie safe behind the canning jars. They were going to file it in October. They would have filed it but Eldon stopped them. He stopped them the wrong way, with the wrong man's blood on him, and I am not saying he was right, but I am telling you what he was trying to do. He was trying to keep the children that are not even born yet from inheriting nothing but a pit. There are eleven houses on this water, Della. There are forty-some children if you count Hicks kin. If the company comes in here we are over.*

I said, *Mama. Wade is one of those forty.*

*I know it.*

*You were going to let him —*

*I was.* She looked at me steady. *I was going to. And I want you to know I knew exactly what I was choosing. I am not a woman who chooses anything by accident, Della. I made the choice my mother made and her mother before her. The women in this family have been making this choice for a hundred years. We have been keeping our boys' wrong things quiet because the alternative was watching a whole holler get put into the ground, and we have been making that math one boy at a time and I am telling you tonight that I made it for your brother and I did it with my eyes open.*

The propane heater behind me ticked twice and stopped. I could hear the seam where the storm window had loosened in October ticking faintly against the trim. The clock above the sink read nine forty-eight.

*Mama,* I said.

*I am telling you now,* she said, *because you are about to make a different choice. And I want you to know what you are choosing against. I am not asking you to choose mine. I am telling you the weight of it so you do not pick yours up easy.*

I looked at her hands on the Bible. I looked at Vera's ring on the right and her own on the left.

*Mama.* I said it the way I had said it on the phone in the bay at Station Fourteen at five-thirty-eight in the morning on the twelfth of October. *Did your mother know.*

*Lord, Della. Of course she did. She knew it in 1961. She knew it from Hollis the night they buried the man. She kept the letter in the hymnal where you found it because she meant for it to keep being found. She talked to me about it all my growing up. Not direct. She did not put it in my mouth. She put it where I would find it and she trusted me to know what to do with it when the time came. I have known what Pap did since I was nine years old, Della. Mama would sit at this table and look at that hymnal and say things that did not have a place to land in the conversation, and I learned to catch them. I caught them all.*

*And you never told daddy.*

*No.*

*And you never told Hollis you knew.*

*No. Hollis knew Mama knew. They had their own understanding. They did not need me in it.*

*And you never told me.*

She looked at me a long time. She looked at me until I had to look down. *I never told you because I thought you would go,* she said. *And I thought you would stay gone. And I thought it was a thing that wanted a daughter who would stay. I was wrong about you, Della. I was wrong about what would stay and what would not. I am telling you that too tonight.*

I sat with that. I sat with it the way you sit with a vital sign that has just dropped twenty points and you have not yet decided whether the cuff is wrong or the patient is going. The kitchen was very still. Outside, a long way down the holler, a dog barked twice and stopped.

I said, *Why didn't Vera tell Hollis to tell me. In '73. Why didn't she just tell him to put it in my hands when I was grown.*

*Maybe she did.*

*Maybe she did what.*

*Maybe she did tell him. And maybe he meant to. Della. Your granddaddy was a slow man about a thing. He took a long time on what he was going to say. The deathbed sentence to you might have been the one he had been working on since 1973. He picked the strike version because he could not bear to give you the river version. He thought he was sparing you the worst of it. He thought wrong, but he thought it for love.*

*He gave me the wrong sentence.*

*He gave you the sentence he could carry to you. The right one Vera wrote. It is in the hymnal still.*

I looked up. *In the hymnal still.*

*The first envelope is the one I gave you. There is a second one in there. Different hymn. I have not opened it in fifteen years. I do not know what it says now. I know what it said when I read it the first time, when I was thirty. I think you should read it tonight. After I go to bed.*

I did not say anything for a count.

*Mama,* I said.

*Don't,* she said. *Don't say I am sorry. Don't say thank you. Don't say anything you would have to take back in the morning, Della. Just go upstairs and read the letter and then come sit on the porch with me tomorrow at first light and we will talk about what happens next.*

*Mama. There is something else I have to ask you. Tomorrow night I am going to ask Eldon to come here. I want him at this table. With us. I am going to put both letters down in front of him and I am going to put the lawyer's card down with them and I am going to tell him Caudill Boggs is about to flip on him, and I am going to ask him to walk in to the sheriff at first light Tuesday morning. I cannot do it without you. I cannot do it without you because he will not come if I ask him. He will come if you ask him. I am asking you to make the call tomorrow. I am asking you to set the chair.*

She closed her eyes. She closed them for the time it took the propane heater to tick once. She opened them.

*All right,* she said.

*All right.*

*I will set the chair, Della. I will set three chairs. You and me and him. And if your brother is out of jail by suppertime Wednesday I will set four. And if he is not, you and me will sit at this table with what we have to sit with, and we will not say one word about it for a year, and after a year we will start saying what we can.*

I said *All right* again because I had nothing else to say. We sat. Neither of us got up. The clock above the sink moved a minute. The dog barked once more, down the holler, and was silent for the night.

*Della,* she said.

*Yes, ma'am.*

*The choice your grandmother made in 1973 was not the choice I made. I want you to know that going in. You read what Vera wrote you and then you make your choice. Mine has been mine. Vera's was Vera's. Whatever yours turns out to be, I am not the one to set it for you. Go on up.*

I stood up. I took the hymnal off the refrigerator. The cardboard cover was worn smooth in the places her mother's hands had been on it. I held it under my arm with the careful loose grip you use to carry a thing that has somebody else's grief inside it. At the kitchen door I stopped and turned back. She had not moved. She was at the table with her hands flat on her own Bible and her face turned toward the window above the sink where if the light had been right she could have seen past the porch rail and past the dogwood and across the headwater creek and up the dark folded back of the ridge above what we called Sorrow Hollow.

*Mama.*

*Yes.*

*I know what it cost you to tell me.*

She nodded once. She did not speak. I went upstairs with the hymnal under my arm.

I sat on the edge of the bed in my old room — Wade's room now, the bed made tight the way he had learned to make it in the eight months he had not been drinking — and I held the hymnal on my knees and I did not open it. I sat with it. I sat the way I had sat in the parking lot of Station Fourteen on the morning of October twelfth holding a phone and listening to my mother's voice come down across a wire from the kitchen I had just left. I thought of Vera and her ring on my mother's right hand and Vera in 1973 at this same table where she had also sat as a girl in 1932 the spring her own mother went into the river. I thought of how a hymnal had carried the truth across sixty-four years on top of a refrigerator in a kitchen at the end of a road in a county nobody outside it could pronounce, and how the truth had waited there as patiently as the seep at the back of Sorrow Hollow waits for whoever next comes to it with a cup.

The clock downstairs struck the half hour. I heard my mother get up from the table and turn off the kitchen light and go down the hall to her room and shut the door.

I opened the hymnal to *Just a Closer Walk with Thee* and Hollis's envelope was still there where I had put it back. I turned to *Amazing Grace* and the second envelope was where she had said it would be. It was yellow at the edges and lighter than I had expected and it had my name on it in a hand I had only ever seen on the spelling chart Vera had taught me from when I was four. The hand was an old woman's hand even then, even in 1973. I held the envelope without opening it. I held it until my own hands stopped shaking and the room went very quiet around me and I could hear, far down the holler, the headwater creek behind the house running the way it had run when my grandfather drank from it and his grandfather drank from it and a long time before either of them, when the rock itself was younger and the water had not yet found the channel it now keeps.

I would open it. Not tonight. I needed to open it once and then act on it, and if I opened it tonight I would not sleep, and I needed to sleep tonight so that I could sit at a kitchen table tomorrow at a quarter past seven and ask my mother to call her sister's boy and invite him to coffee on the night before the law was going to take her other boy away from her for keeps.

I put the envelope back where Vera had put it. I put the hymnal on the windowsill above the head of the bed. I lay down in my clothes. The propane heater downstairs cycled on and off twice before I slept. I slept the way the body sleeps when it has been carrying a thing it did not know yet how to lay down, which is to say not deeply, but deeply enough.

---

# Chapter 30 — Eldon's Cabin

Hannah called me at six-fifteen Saturday morning to tell me not to do what I was about to do. I let it go to voicemail. I drank a cup of coffee on the porch with my mother and did not say where I was going. My mother knew. When I came back into the kitchen for my keys she said, *You take a long way and you take your time getting there,* which meant *be careful,* in a kitchen where *be careful* would have sounded too much like a prayer she did not feel entitled to.

I went down Bear Branch and out KY-92 past the IGA and the Phillips 66 and the Mountain Laurel with the L still burnt out, and three miles past the town line I turned north onto Tinker Branch. The leaves were mostly down on the north faces and still hanging copper on the south, the way they do the third week of November. The blacktop gave out to gravel and the gravel gave out to rutted hardpan with frost still on it where the trees had kept the sun off.

I had not been on this road since I was a child. The cabin sat in a clearing where the head of Tinker came down off Honaker Ridge in a series of small flats. I remembered the place dimly: a porch, a tin roof, a dog on a chain, my uncle Roy in a barn coat the color of nothing. The dog was gone. Roy had been dead twenty-two years. The cabin was smaller than I remembered.

Eldon's truck was in front. His real truck — a 2008 Chevy with the bed scuffed to bare metal — not the company F-350. He was on the porch in a flannel coat and a hunting cap, his hands wrapped around a coffee mug that said HICKS in faded white letters.

*Della.*

*Eldon.*

*Aunt Donnie call you and tell you I was up here.*

*No.*

He nodded slowly. *Figured.* He gestured at the porch step with the mug. *Sit. I'd offer you coffee but I only brought one cup.*

*I had mine.* I sat on the top step. He sat in the rocker behind me. I had positioned myself so my back was to him and I would have to turn if I wanted to see his face. I had done it on purpose. The medic in me had taken statements from a hundred men sitting in the rear of an ambulance after they had hit something they should not have hit, and the medic in me knew you do not put your eyes on them. You make the eyes the road in front of them. The road takes some of the weight.

The wind came down off the ridge and a handful of leaves came with it across the clearing. Yellow poplar. The cabin smelled faintly of woodsmoke and of the kerosene lamp he had not put out from the night before.

*I was looking for you,* I said.

*I know it.*

*I went to your house Friday. Marlene said you had not been home since Wednesday.*

*Marlene don't know where I am most nights.*

*She does. She just don't want to be the one telling.*

He took a sip. He did not say anything to that.

I said, *Eldon. I came up here to say what I came to say and I do not want to do it twice. So I would like to say it once and have you listen to all of it before you answer. Can we do that.*

*We can.*

*All right.* I put my hands on my knees the way Hollis used to put his hands on his knees when he was deciding whether to say a thing or not. I had not noticed I did it. I noticed it then. I let my hands stay there.

*I know you killed Tully Renfro,* I said. *I know how you did it and where you did it and what you put on him and what you handed Wade to put on the rock and what you said to him after. I know it was October the fifth a little before three. I know you asked Wade to come with you because the code said you needed a witness and you wanted him bound to it and you wanted it to be him because he is who Hollis was to Pap and the family math has rhymes in it the company does not understand. I know you went to Aunt Donnie the day after and you asked her to keep it. I know she said she would and I know why she said it and I know the math you were both doing.*

I let that sit. He did not move.

*I went to Frankfort,* I said. *I went to the microfiche at the county library. I went to Wendell Cornett at the nursing home in Williamsburg. I went to Verna Hicks's porch and I went to Cal Slone in a booth at the Brass Lantern. I know what Pap did in 1961 and I know why he did it. I know about Letty Anne in the river in 1932. I know about Asa under the rock in '59. I know the coal on the chest is for vow-breaking and I know the coal on the rock is for a witness who promises silence. I know the code is real. I know it was Pap's invention and I know it was a thing he made up to disguise a man taking a private debt for the woman he loved. I know your father taught it to you. I know he did not teach you all of it because Roy did not have all of it. He had the part about Asa. He never had the part about the river.*

I heard him set the cup down on the boards of the porch. I did not turn.

*I know Caudill Boggs is on a road somewhere this morning and he is figuring out how to stay alive,* I said. *I know the company has decided to let you take the fall and to let him take the rest of it, and Caudill has figured that out about a day ago, and he has been asking around about a fountain pen.*

*He has,* Eldon said.

*That fountain pen is real, Eldon. It belongs to a man named Reed Whitlow, lawyer for Blackridge out of Lexington. He wrote a memo on it three weeks before Tully died. He used it to write me a card too, the night he came to the Mountain Laurel, and I have got the card and I have got the ink on it and Caudill has been looking for the memo. The memo is in a folder in a filing cabinet in Whitlow's office and Caudill is going to find it inside a week and when he does he is going to walk it into the U. S. Attorney's office in Lexington and trade it for himself.*

*All right.*

*I am telling you this not because I am proud of knowing it but because you are about to be in a small room with a man you did not choose. He will not protect you. He never has. He is going to put you in front of the company by Wednesday at the latest and the company is going to put you in front of a grand jury by Christmas. You can be in front of it Tuesday morning instead and you can be the man who told it. You can do it from a chair that you walked into on your own.*

The wind came down again. I watched a leaf turn over twice on the air in front of me before it settled on the lowest porch step.

He said, *Della. I am not going to apologize for what I did.*

*I did not come up here to ask for that.*

*I want you to hear me say it anyway. I would do it again.* His voice was level. *Tully Renfro was on his way to put eleven houses on this water into a pit. I know what that company does once it files. I have watched it three times since I started with them. They file. Then they buy at thirty cents on the dollar. Then they take the top off the ridge and they leave a slurry pond. There ain't water in the holler ten years after. There ain't a creek. There ain't a fish in the creek that ain't. I knew what was coming and I knew none of you would believe me about it until it was here, and once it was here it would be too late. Tully Renfro was not the worst man at that company. He was just the man who was going to file the paper. If it had been Whitlow I would have done it to Whitlow. I would have liked it less. I would have done it the same way.*

*I know.*

*Do you.*

*I do, Eldon. That is what I am telling you. I have spent a month understanding it. I am not stupid and I am not new to this and I am not from Knoxville the way you would like to think. I am from this. I know what you were trying to do. I have got no quarrel with what you were trying to do. I have got quarrel with the way you did it and I have got quarrel with the man you asked to come along.*

He was quiet. The rocker squeaked once.

*Wade,* he said.

*Wade.*

*Wade should not have come. That was on me. That was me being scared and not wanting to be up there alone with the thing afterward. I asked him to come because I did not want to be the one standing on that rock by myself when Tully went down. I wanted somebody else to be there. I wanted somebody whose people had stood there before. I asked him because he was Hollis's, and Hollis had been Pap's, and that is the only thing I am sorry for. He did not know what he was coming to. I knew. I made him part of it without his knowing. That was the wrong thing.*

*That is the only wrong thing you will name.*

*It is the only one I am sorry for. I am not sorry for Tully. I am not sorry for the company. I am not sorry I picked the rock I picked or the place I picked. I have been over it. The only thing I will lay down for is what I did to your brother.*

I sat. The wind had stopped. The clearing was very still and the chimney of the cabin was putting up a thin white smoke that climbed straight a little way and then bent west.

*Are you going to turn yourself in,* I said.

*No.*

*No.*

*No, Della. I am not. I have thought about it three weeks. If I walk in I lose the holler. The company files. The judge they bring don't recuse. The whole thing goes the way they planned it. If I do not walk in, Wade goes down for something he did not do, and I will live with that. I have already chosen which thing I am going to live with. You did not come up here this morning thinking I would not have chosen.*

*I did not.*

*Then we are square on that.*

*We are not square,* I said. *Because I am going to make you do it anyway.*

He laughed once. It was not a real laugh. It was the sound a man makes when somebody says a thing he wishes he had not had to hear come out of a woman's mouth.

*Della.*

*Tomorrow night,* I said. *Aunt Donnie is going to call you. She is going to ask you to come up to the house for coffee. She is going to set the chair. You are going to come because she asked and because you owe her the courtesy of saying yes to that one thing. When you get there I am going to be at the kitchen table. I am going to put what I have got down on the table in front of you. I am going to put down both letters and I am going to put down Whitlow's business card and I am going to put down a list of what Caudill is going to do by Friday. Then you and Aunt Donnie and me are going to sit at the table and we are going to decide what comes next. I am not deciding for you. Aunt Donnie is not. We are deciding together because that is the thing the code was supposed to be all along and never was. Pap decided alone. Hollis decided alone. You decided alone. I am asking you not to die alone too. I am asking you to come up to my mother's house and sit at her table and let the three of us figure out how this ends. That is all I am asking. I cannot make you. You can come or you can not come. I am telling you the question. The answer is yours.*

He sat for a long time. I heard the cabin settle, a board somewhere inside the wall taking the morning warmth.

*Aunt Donnie know you came up here to say this.*

*She knows I came up here. She does not know I was going to say it this way. I had not decided.*

*And Wade.*

*Wade knows I am working on something. He does not know what.*

*And the lawyer girl. The Soyars girl.*

*She told me not to come up here. She is going to be angry with me when she finds out I did. She would not stop me. I told her last night.*

*All right,* he said.

*All right what.*

*I will think on it, Della. I am not going to tell you tonight or in the next hour. I am going to think on it. Aunt Donnie can call me tomorrow. I will give her the answer when she calls. I will not lie to her about it. That much I owe her. That much I owe Pap.*

*That is all I came for.*

*That is not all you came for.*

I did not answer that. He was right and he knew it. I had come for more. I had come to look at him and see whether he was a man who could be turned. I had what I had come for. I stood up.

I turned around then. I looked at him. He was older than I had thought he would look in the morning light. The gray at his temples was more than I had remembered. The missing joint on his right index finger lay along the rim of the mug exactly where it would have curled around if he had had it. He looked back at me. He did not look ashamed and he did not look defiant. He looked tired and clear, the way Hollis had looked the morning before they put the oxygen on for the last time.

*Eldon.*

*Della.*

*I am not the law. I am not Hannah. I am not the U. S. Attorney. I am your cousin. I am at this table because I am your cousin. If you come to my mother's house tomorrow night you are coming because I am your cousin. If you do not come you are answering me as your cousin too, and I will take the answer as that and I will go to Hannah Sunday morning with what I have, and Hannah will go to the Commonwealth Monday, and you and Wade will both stand at the bar by Wednesday. I want you to know I will do it. I will not pretend otherwise. I am asking you to choose differently. I am not bluffing about what I will do if you do not.*

*I have never thought you was a bluffer, Della.*

*Then we are square on that one too.*

*We are.*

*I am leaving.*

*Drive careful.*

I went down the porch steps and across the clearing and got in my truck and turned around in the ruts. He was still on the porch when I came back past in the direction I had come. He did not raise his hand and I did not raise mine. He had not picked up the mug. The leaf I had watched settle on the porch step was still there.

I drove down off Tinker Branch in the dropping morning. About halfway down I had to pull off into a turnout to let a coal truck go up. A driver I did not know lifted his hand off the wheel at me and I lifted mine. After he was past I sat in the turnout a minute and looked through the windshield at the bare oaks on the ridge above me and I thought about Pap walking out of Sorrow Hollow on the evening of July 19, 1961, and getting in whatever vehicle he had got in that night and driving back to the kitchen that was now my mother's kitchen and drinking a cup of water out of the galvanized sink and going to the back bedroom and lying down. I thought about him lying down knowing what he had done and not regretting it and never speaking of it again in his life and dying in that same bedroom seven years later with the secret folded under his head like a pillow he had brought from home. I thought about the way he had passed it on without speaking of it — the way he had laid it into the house itself, into the table and the sink and the seep at the back of the hollow, where it had waited sixty-four years for one of us to come along and put a hand on it.

I started the truck. I pulled out of the turnout. I drove home.

The light was coming clear of the ridge by the time I came back down Bear Branch and the woodsmoke from my mother's chimney was going straight up in the no-wind of the late morning. She was on the porch with the second coffee already poured for me. She looked at me as I came up the steps and she did not have to ask. I shook my head once. She nodded once. We sat. We drank the coffee. After a while she said, *You think he will come.*

I said, *I do not know. I think he might. I think he might not. I think he will think about it because I gave him a way to think about it that he had not been given. I think that is all I could do.*

*That is all anybody could,* she said.

I drank my coffee. Down the holler somewhere a chainsaw started up and then stopped. The dogwood at the corner of the porch had let its last leaf go in the night and the leaf was on the boards between our two rockers, dry and curled.

*I will call him at noon tomorrow,* she said.

*All right.*

*I will be the one to ask.*

*I know you will.*

*I have not asked him for anything since he was fifteen years old, Della. He came to me then for a pair of pants because his mother could not afford new ones for school and he was ashamed to come. I bought him three pair and gave him a Bible and never told his mother. He has remembered the Bible. He does not remember the pants. I am hoping the Bible is the part that carries him to my kitchen tomorrow night.*

I did not say anything to that. There was nothing to say. We sat on the porch and watched the morning come full up over the ridge and we drank our coffee and we did not look at each other and we did not need to.

---

# Chapter 31 — The Second Letter

I did not open the envelope on Saturday night and I did not open it Sunday morning. I made coffee. I sat on the porch with my mother and watched the ridge come up out of the dark. Wade was still in the jail. Eldon was still on his father's porch up Tinker Branch or had moved by then to wherever he was going to be when my mother called him at noon. Hannah was in Pikeville. Cal Slone was in his shop welding a thing for his nephew. Caudill Boggs was on a road somewhere south. All of them were the people I had been carrying in my head for six weeks, and on Sunday morning I sat on the porch and let each one of them be exactly where they were and did not try to move them with my thinking.

At eleven-forty-five my mother went inside to make the call. I stayed on the porch. I did not need to hear it. I had heard her make calls of that kind my whole life. They never sounded like what they were. She would say *Eldon, honey,* and then *I have got a thing I would like to talk over with you tonight if you can come up,* and then *Six o'clock would suit me,* and then *Bring your appetite, I am going to put something on,* and she would mean every word at the level of the words and several more things at the level beneath them, and Eldon would hear the under-things or he would not. I had decided not to try to hear.

She came back out at twelve-oh-two. She sat down in the rocker. She picked up her coffee, which was cold by then, and she drank a sip of it as if it were hot.

*He is coming,* she said.

*All right.*

*He did not ask what for.*

*All right.*

*He said he had been figuring on coming anyway.*

*All right.*

She set the cup down. The dogwood was bare. The last leaf had blown off the boards in the night.

*Della,* she said.

*Yes ma'am.*

*Go on up and read it.*

I went upstairs. I sat on the bed in my old room — Wade's room now — and I picked up the hymnal from the windowsill and I took the second envelope out. I held it the way I had held it Friday night. It was lighter than the first one. The paper was thinner. My name in her hand. *Della Combs.* The two words she had set down in 1973 in a kitchen eleven years before there was a Della Combs to put a name on.

I slid my thumb under the flap. The glue gave the way old envelope glue gives — without resistance, the way a piece of bread crust gives when it has been sitting all day on the counter.

There were two folded sheets. I opened the first.

*Della,*

*Hollis says he means to tell you this himself when you are old enough. I am writing it in case he does not, and in case you ever need to read it after he is gone.*

I sat for a minute holding the sheet. Vera had been forty-five when she wrote it. She had been the age I would be in a year. She had written it knowing she might not live to give it to me, and she had been right about that — she had died when I was six and the spelling chart was the only piece of her hand I remembered. I had had her ring on my mother's right hand my whole life. I had had her hymnal on the refrigerator the whole of my growing up. I had never had a sentence in her own hand addressed to me.

*Hollis will have told you the story by then in his way. He will have told you about the strike and about Asa. He will have told you about Pap. He may have told you that he himself was the one who did it. He was not. I want you to know that from me, in case it matters to you.*

She had set it down level. No flourish. The way Hollis would have set it down. They had written the same hand in this regard, even though their hands themselves were different — his big and square and hers narrow and slanted.

*My father did it. I knew it then and I have known it the whole of my life and I have never said a word of it out loud to anybody except Hollis. The reason was Letty Anne. The reason was always Letty Anne. I was four when my mother died and the only thing I have of her is that my father loved her enough to wait twenty-nine years to do what he did about the man who took her from him. I do not know how to feel about that, Della, and at forty-five I have stopped trying to feel any one thing about it. Some days I am grateful that a man loved my mother that much. Some days I think he is the worst man I ever knew. Most days I think both of those at once and go on with my morning.*

I read it again. The line about going on with her morning. I had wondered what Vera sounded like when she was thinking. She sounded like my mother. She sounded like my grandfather. She sounded a little, I noticed with the part of me that was not sitting on a bed in her son-in-law's house reading her hand sixty-two years late, like me.

*I want you to know one other thing, which Hollis will not tell you because he does not believe it. The code is not the family. The family is what the code has been hiding. We have lived under the code in this house since 1961. We have lived as if the secret were the thing that bound us to each other. It was not. We were bound to each other before the secret and we will be bound after it. The secret is a wall the men in this family built around the kitchen, and we have all been living in the kitchen all these years and calling the wall the house.*

I put the page down on my knee.

*If you ever have to choose between the code and one living person, Della, choose the person. Do not even take a long time about it. Choose the person and burn the code in the sink and watch it go and do not look back at the ashes. We chose the code. We chose it because we did not believe we had anything else. I want you to know we were wrong. Everybody we kept the secret from is dead now and we are still keeping it. Don't.*

The room was very still. I could hear my mother downstairs at the sink running water for something.

*I have written this once and once is enough. Hollis will burn it if he reads it. I am going to put it in the hymnal in Amazing Grace and I am going to leave it there. I am going to write your name on the envelope so that whoever opens the hymnal next will know who it goes to. If you are reading this, Della, then I am dead and Hollis is dead and your mother is the one who put the envelope in your hand, and that means she has decided you are ready for it, and that means I trust her decision. She is a careful woman. She has been careful her whole life. Take her seriously.*

*If you choose right, write to me. Burn the letter. I will know.*

*— Vera Sturgill Combs, October 1973.*

I sat with the page on my knee. I unfolded the second sheet — and there was only the second sheet because there had been two pages of the letter, and the second was the back of the first. There was nothing else in the envelope. I had thought there might be. I had thought there might be a thing for me to find. There was no thing. There was the letter and Vera's hand and the date.

I put both pages down on the quilt beside me. I sat. I did not cry. I would cry later, on the porch the morning after the hearing in a way I could not name in advance. In the room I only sat with what she had said and let it find the place in me it was going to find.

The line that found the place was *the secret is a wall the men in this family built around the kitchen, and we have all been living in the kitchen all these years and calling the wall the house.* I had been in this room since I was nine. I had not seen the wall. I had seen the house. I had loved the house. I had loved my mother in this house and I had loved Hollis in it and I had loved Wade in it and I had walked out the door of it at nineteen and not come back inside it for any reason that lasted more than four days. I had thought I had walked away from the house. I had not. I had walked away from the wall. I had not known they were two different things.

I got up. I picked up both pages. I went downstairs.

My mother was at the sink. She was washing a single coffee cup. She had a fresh dishtowel folded on the counter beside her and she was washing the cup the way you wash a thing when you are doing it because you need to be doing a thing with your hands. She did not turn when I came in.

I stood at the table. I read her the last paragraph aloud. I read her the line about writing to her. I read her *burn the letter, I will know.* I did not read her the rest. I would later. Not tonight.

She had stopped the water by the time I got to *I will know.* She stood at the sink with the cup in her hand and the towel beside her and the fading sunlight from the window coming across her face. She did not turn around.

*She told me once,* my mother said, *that she had written me a letter too. I never found it.*

*Maybe it was for you to know it existed without finding it.*

*Maybe. Or maybe Hollis found it and burnt it.*

*Maybe.*

She turned. She set the cup down on the towel and dried her hands on the front of her apron.

*Write your sentence, Della,* she said. *I will lay a piece of paper. There is a box of matches in the second drawer.*

I sat down at the kitchen table. She tore a half sheet off the back of a Methodist church bulletin from the pile on the windowsill. She put it in front of me with a ballpoint pen she had been using to write down something for the choir director earlier in the week. She got the matches from the drawer and set them on the table next to the paper. She went back to the sink and turned her back to me, not to spy on me, to let me have the table alone. I appreciated her for it more than I had appreciated her for any one thing in twenty-two years.

I sat with the pen. I thought about what to write. I thought about a long time, because Vera had said *write to me* and I wanted to write to her, and I had never written to a dead grandmother before and I did not know what the proper form was for that.
