I thought of the line my mother had said to Eldon when she made the call. *Bring your appetite, I am going to put something on.* I thought of the line Hollis had said to me on his deathbed. *I want you to know the shape of it.* I thought of the line Vera had written about going on with her morning. I thought of how my hand was about to write something my hand had never written before, in a kitchen I had not eaten supper in for twenty-two years, on a piece of paper that had borne the announcement of choir practice on its other side, with a pen my mother had used to take down a phone number for a woman who needed an alto.

I wrote my sentence. It was one sentence. It was short.

I am not going to give the sentence to you. I am going to keep it.

I struck a match. I held the corner of the paper to the flame until the flame took. I carried the paper to the sink — my mother had stepped aside without my asking — and I held it over the drain until the flame ate the writing and ate the white of the page and ate down to my fingers, and then I dropped what was left and it fell into the basin and curled black and went out. My mother turned the water on for a half-second and rinsed the ash down. We watched the gray go.

*All right,* she said.

*All right.*

She put her arm around my shoulders. She did not hold on. She put her arm around me the way she had put her arm around me when I was eight and had come down with a fever in the night, exactly that loose and that complete, and after a count of three or four she took it back and went to the stove and lit the burner under the kettle.

*I am going to make us a piece of supper before he gets here,* she said. *He don't need to eat. He will not eat. But we need to. We have got a long night ahead.*

*Yes ma'am.*

I went out on the porch. The light was almost gone. The wind had come up and the bare branches of the dogwood were moving against the rail. I stood at the rail with both hands on it and I looked west, the way the wind was coming from, and I did what I had been waiting to do for I did not know how long. I said her name out loud. I said *Vera,* not loud, into the wind, the way you say a thing not to be heard but to test whether you can put the word in the air without breaking. The word stayed put. The dogwood went on moving. Somewhere across the holler a dog barked once and was quiet.

I stood at the rail a minute longer and I thought, with the part of me that I had been trying not to listen to all day, about the words on the burnt half-sheet in the sink. I thought about whether Vera had known. I did not know whether Vera had known. I knew I had written what I had written and that I would have to live the rest of my life inside it, the way she had lived hers inside what she had not been able to put down.

I went back into the kitchen. My mother was at the stove. The kettle was on. She had taken three plates out of the cupboard and set them on the table — three plates not because she thought he would eat but because she was a woman who set the number of plates that matched the number of bodies that would be in her kitchen, and the number tonight was three. She did not look at me. She said, *Get the bowls down, Della. I am going to do soup beans and a piece of skillet. He used to like skillet. I have not made him a piece of skillet since he was a boy.*

I got the bowls down. I set them out. I laid the spoons. I went back to the door to the porch and looked out one more time at the dogwood moving in the wind, and at the road dropping away toward town, and at the place down the road where Eldon's headlights would appear in about thirty-five minutes if he came when she had asked him to come. I closed the door. I went back into the kitchen and helped my mother with the supper.

The hymnal was on the table where I had set it down when I read her the line. It would stay on the table all evening. We would not put it back on the refrigerator that night. We would not put it back on the refrigerator after, either. The refrigerator had carried it for sixty-four years. The table was going to carry it for the rest of the night. The refrigerator had earned a rest, and the table was about to be the table again, the way it had been the table for Hollis at breakfast and for Pap when he came in off the porch in 1961 with the rock dust still on him and the hymnal had not yet been brought in from her chair.

---

# Chapter 32 — Kitchen Table

He came at six minutes past six. We heard the truck come up the road and we heard the tires on the gravel pull-off below the house and we heard his door shut. Neither of us moved. My mother was at the stove and I was at the table laying out the things I was going to lay out, and we kept on doing what we were doing while he came up the steps. He knocked once, the way a man knocks when he is not sure whether he is family that night or company. My mother said, *Come on in, Eldon,* without turning from the stove, and he came in.

He had not taken off his work coat. He stood on the rag rug inside the door with his hands in his pockets and he looked at the table. The hymnal was on it. Both envelopes were beside the hymnal. The lawyer's business card was beside the envelopes. I had laid a yellow legal pad and a ballpoint pen at the third place. I had set the cups out. The coffee was on. The kitchen smelled like soup beans and like the skillet cornbread she had pulled from the oven ten minutes before.

He looked at the table and then he looked at me. Then he looked at my mother.

*Aunt Donnie.*

*Eldon.*

*Smells good in here.*

*Take your coat off.*

He took his coat off and hung it on the peg where I had hung mine on Friday night. He sat at the chair I had set for him without looking at the chair. I poured his coffee. He took it without saying anything and put both hands around the cup and looked at the cup.

My mother set a bowl of beans down in front of him. She set a piece of cornbread on a saucer. She did the same for me and for herself. She sat. She bowed her head. She said the blessing she had said over every meal in this kitchen since she had been the age I was now. *Be present at our table, Lord. Be here and everywhere adored. These mercies bless and grant that we may feast in fellowship with thee. Amen.* She did not put the *we have done a thing tonight that is going to undo a thing we have been doing for sixty years* into the blessing. She did not need to. It was in the bowl. It was in the cornbread. It was in the chair Eldon had sat down in.

He ate. I had not thought he would and he did. He ate two bites of the beans and a piece of the cornbread and then he set the spoon down and looked at me.

*Della,* he said.

I had been waiting for the cue. I took it.

I picked up the first envelope. I slid the letter out. I set the letter in front of him, between the bowl and the saucer with the cornbread, oriented so he could read it. I did not say anything. He read it. He was not a fast reader. He read it the way Hollis had read a thing, with his finger moving along the lines without quite touching the paper. He read it through. He came to the end. He did not look up.

I picked up the second envelope. I slid that letter out. I set it down on top of the first. He read it too. Slower than the first. He read *the secret is a wall the men in this family built around the kitchen, and we have all been living in the kitchen all these years and calling the wall the house* and his thumb stopped on the word *kitchen* for a count of two and then went on. He read to the end. He sat with both letters in front of him.

I picked up the business card. I set it on top of both letters. It looked smaller than it had felt in my pocket when I had put it there.

*This is from a man named Reed Whitlow,* I said. *He is Blackridge's lawyer out of Lexington. He came to the Mountain Laurel three weeks ago and gave me this card. The ink on the back of it is fountain pen. The pen is a Sheaffer. There is a memo in a folder in Whitlow's office, written with the same pen, on October the second, three days before you went up the holler. The memo lays out the subsidence claim that you were trying to stop. Whitlow used to keep memos like that for himself. He has been at the company nineteen years. He has been keeping memos that long.*

I let that sit a beat.

*Caudill Boggs has known about the memo since the second week of October. He has been figuring out how to get it. He went to the Lexington office on Friday the fifteenth on what he told them was a courier run. He had an hour in the office unsupervised. Whitlow's secretary went to lunch at twelve-thirty and came back at one-fifteen. Caudill was in the file room at one-oh-five. I know this because Caudill told me last night. He called my cell from a number I did not have. He has been thinking about turning up at the Lexington U. S. Attorney's office sometime in the next four days with the memo and with what he knows about how the company tried to use Tully Renfro's death to push the subsidence claim through under condolence terms. The company has decided to let him fall for Tully. Caudill has decided not to fall.*

Eldon had not moved. I did not stop. I was on the line of it now and I was not going to step off until the run was over.

*If Caudill walks into Lexington with that memo and with what he knows about your conversation with him on the porch of your father's cabin in October — yes, he has notes from that afternoon, and yes, he is willing to testify to what you said — the federal piece of this comes first. Whitlow gets indicted. The company gets sat down in front of a federal grand jury by Christmas. You go in front of a Kentucky grand jury by January. Wade goes too because his trail-cam print and the placed coal will surface in federal discovery. He goes as an accessory. He will not see a sentence under three years. The company will not save him because the company has already decided how this story goes. It goes with you and Wade in a cell and Whitlow in Lexington giving cooperator testimony and a settlement on the Bear Branch deed that is half the value of what was on the table before. The holler goes. Wade goes. You go. The company takes a hit on Whitlow and walks. That is the way it lays out if you do nothing.*

I let it sit. I had spoken the way I would speak in a code, level, the way a medic speaks when she does not have time for the doctor to ask her to repeat it. He was looking at the card now. Not at me.

*If you walk in to Avery Cornett at seven tomorrow morning,* I said, *Hannah has it on paper that the Commonwealth will charge you with second-degree murder, will accept a plea of voluntary manslaughter at fifteen years, eligible for parole at five with conduct, and will not file anything against Wade. The federal piece happens anyway because Caudill is going to take the memo to Lexington either way. The difference is that you go in as the man who told it and Wade goes home tomorrow at noon. The holler also goes, Eldon. I am not pretending it does not. The subsidence claim will go through under federal supervision now and the company will not get the deed at thirty cents on the dollar — they will get it at sixty, maybe seventy, with conditions. The holler goes either way. The holler was going either way. You knew that going in. You did not stop the holler from going. You stopped the company from getting it cheap. That is what you did. That is the thing you actually did, when you take the rest of the story away. The rest of the story is what we are sitting here to decide.*

He did not look up from the card. He said, *And Aunt Donnie.*

*Aunt Donnie is here,* I said. *Aunt Donnie can speak for Aunt Donnie.*

She did. My mother sat with her hands on the table on either side of her bowl of soup beans and she spoke to him the way she had spoken to him when he was nine years old and had broken her mother's wash pitcher and had cried in this kitchen and had had to be told he was loved and that the pitcher was already broken and that the second of those two facts was the smaller one. She did not raise her voice.

*Eldon. I told you on the sixth of October that I would keep it. I am not keeping it. I am telling you tonight that I am not. I would have. I want you to know I would have. But Della has put a thing in front of me that I cannot un-put, and Vera has put a thing in front of me that I have been not-reading for thirty years, and the two of those things have set me in a chair I did not sit in before, and from this chair I cannot keep it. I am sorry, honey. I told you I would and I will not. I am telling you to your face so you do not have to find out from somebody else.*

He nodded. He did not look up.

She said, *I am also telling you that I do not regret you. I do not regret the boy who sat at this table when he was nine, and I do not regret the man who sat at it when he was twenty-two and asked me whether his daddy would have liked him, and I do not regret you tonight. I regret what you did. I regret it for Tully Renfro and his daughter and I regret it for Wade and I regret it for you. I do not regret the man you have been to me. You have been a good nephew. You have been a better nephew than I have been an aunt, more than once. I want you to hear me say that before anything else gets said in this kitchen.*

He put one hand over his face. He did not cry. The hand stayed up over his face for the time it took the clock above the sink to move from six twenty-one to six twenty-two. He took the hand down. His face was the way it had been on the porch on Saturday morning. Tired. Clear. Older.

*Aunt Donnie,* he said.

*Yes, honey.*

*Is this what you want.*

She looked at him. She looked at him a long time. I sat between them and did not move. The propane heater behind me ticked once.

*Eldon. This is what we should have done in '61.*

He closed his eyes. He sat with them closed. He sat with them closed for a count I would not be able to give you. When he opened them he looked at me and not at her. He picked up the business card with both hands. He turned it over and looked at the ink on the back. He held it up against the light from the bulb above the sink. He looked at it the way Hollis had looked at the deed map on this same table in 1983 when the company was trying for the first time to claim the strip rights and he had finally understood what he was being shown. He put the card back down.

He nodded once.

*All right,* he said.

*All right.*

*I will go in tomorrow morning. I will not lie about what I did. I will not put it on Wade. I will not put it on the company. I will not put it on Caudill. I will sit in front of Avery Cornett and I will tell him what I did and why I did it and I will sign whatever it is he puts in front of me.*

*Hannah is going to be there at six-thirty,* I said. *She is going to walk in with you. She has spoken to the Commonwealth's attorney about the manslaughter plea. The plea is on paper. You will read it before you sign it. You will not sign anything that is not on the paper.*

*All right.*

*She will also want to talk to you tonight. Before the morning. She is at the Mountain Laurel. She has been there since four o'clock. I told her I would call her after.*

*All right.*

*She is a good lawyer, Eldon.*

*I do not need a good lawyer, Della. I have already lost. I need somebody who knows what the right paper to sign looks like. She is going to be that.*

He picked up his cup. The coffee had gone cold while I had been talking. He drank it cold. He set the cup down.

*Aunt Donnie,* he said.

*Yes, honey.*

*If they let me have a visit Tuesday — *

*I will be there Tuesday. I will be there every visit you have got. I will be there the day they let you out and I will be there the day they will not let you out. I have not failed to show for a single Combs in this family yet and I am not going to fail to show for a Hicks. Not you.*

He nodded. He did not say anything else for a minute. He looked down at the two letters under the card. He read them again, both, the bottom one through the thin paper of the top. I could see him doing it. He came to the end of Vera's. He stopped on the line about the kitchen. He stayed on it.

*Vera knew me as a child,* he said.

*She did,* my mother said. *She held you twice. She told me you had your father's hands.*

*I have got Pap's hands,* he said.

*You have got Pap's hands too,* my mother said.

He laughed once at that. It was not the laugh he had made on the porch. It was a different laugh. It was small and it was a thing more than nothing.

*All right,* he said.

He stood up. He put his coat on. He pulled the collar up the way a man pulls the collar up when he is going out into the cold and is glad of it, because the cold is going to be the only thing he can think about for the next stretch and he is allowed to. He took a piece of the cornbread off the saucer and wrapped it in a paper napkin and put it in the pocket of the coat.

*For the road,* he said. *I might could eat it later.*

*You might could,* my mother said.

He looked at the table one more time. He looked at me.

*Della.*

*Eldon.*

*I do not think I am going to forgive you for this. I want you to know that. I do not bear you a grudge for it. But I am not going to forgive you for it either. I do not know how. I will sit at a table with you at Christmas if Aunt Donnie sets one. I will not be a man you call up to ask after. I want you to know what kind of a thing this is going to be between you and me from here forward. I do not want either of us to pretend.*

*I do not want to pretend, Eldon.*

*Then we are square on that one too.*

*We are.*

*All right.*

He looked at my mother. He looked at her with the particular look I had seen him give her once when he was nineteen and she had carried him out of his grandmother's funeral when his knees had gone. It was the look of a boy who knew there was one woman in the world who was going to keep showing up for him no matter what he did. He did not say anything. He nodded once. She nodded once. He went out the door. We did not hear his footsteps on the porch. We did not hear his truck door. We heard the engine start way down the road and we heard it pull out and we heard it gone.

My mother did not move for a long minute. Then she got up and went to the sink and turned the water on and washed her hands. She washed them three times. She did not say anything. She came back to the table. She picked up his bowl. There was a little of the soup left in the bottom and a small piece of cornbread on the saucer that he had not finished. She did not throw any of it out. She covered the bowl with a saucer and put it in the refrigerator and she covered the cornbread with a piece of foil and she put that beside it. She had done the same thing every time my father had left a meal half-finished for the four years before he died.

She came back to the table. She sat down across from me. She picked up her own spoon. She ate the rest of her beans cold. I ate the rest of mine cold too. Neither of us said anything for the time it took her to do that. After she had set the spoon down she looked up at me. The clock above the sink read six forty-eight. I had thought it was midnight.

*Della.*

*Yes ma'am.*

*Call the lawyer girl. Tell her it is done.*

I called Hannah from the kitchen with the phone pressed to my ear and my mother sitting across the table watching me without watching me. Hannah answered on the first ring. I said two words to her. I said, *He is coming.* Hannah said, *I will be there at six-thirty. He will walk in at seven. I am on my way to him at the Mountain Laurel right now. He needs to sleep four hours minimum. I will not let him sign anything until five-thirty.* I said, *All right.* I hung up.

I sat at the table with my mother. We did not move for a long time. The hymnal was between us with the two letters and the business card on top of it. The cornbread had stopped steaming. The propane heater behind me ticked twice and was quiet. Outside, somewhere down the holler, the wind that had been moving the dogwood had laid down for the night.

We sat there together at the table where Hollis had eaten breakfast for fifty-seven years and where Pap had drunk a cup of water in the summer of 1961 after coming down off the ridge with rock dust still on his hands, and we sat without speaking because there was nothing to be said. The clock above the sink moved a minute. Then another. My mother reached across the table and put her hand on the cover of the hymnal and left it there. I put mine over hers. We sat with our hands stacked on the hymnal at the kitchen table with three empty cups around us, and the night came the rest of the way down over the ridge above what we called Sorrow Hollow, and somewhere south of us on KY-92 Eldon Hicks was driving slow toward the Mountain Laurel Motel to meet a lawyer he had not yet met to talk about a plea he had not yet read, and the holler was as quiet as it had been in 1961 the night Pap and Hollis came down off the trace and washed their hands in the same sink my mother had just washed hers in for the third time.

---

# Chapter 33 — The Hearing

Eldon walked into the sheriff's office at three minutes past seven on the morning of November the eighteenth. He had slept four hours at the Mountain Laurel. Hannah had walked in with him as she had said she would. He sat down in the chair across from Avery Cornett's desk and he said *I am here to make a statement about the death of Tully Renfro and I want my lawyer present while I make it,* and Avery looked at him for the time it took a man to set down a coffee cup and pick up a pen, and then Avery said *All right, Eldon,* and the statement took two hours and seven minutes to take. I know because Hannah called me at nine seventeen and told me. I had been on the porch with my mother since six. The sun had come up by the time the call came in. We had not yet had our second coffee.

*He has read the plea,* Hannah said. *He has signed it. The Commonwealth's attorney has signed it. He is going to be transported to Pike County jail at eleven and arraigned this afternoon. The motion to dismiss the charge against Wade has already been filed. It will be heard at the preliminary at one o'clock. He will be released within an hour of the judge granting it. You should plan to be at the courthouse at twelve-thirty. Bring your mother. Bring whatever clothes Wade is going to want to leave the building in. He has been in the same shirt since the tenth of October.*

I said, *All right.*

She said, *Della.*

*Yes.*

*You did good last night.*

I did not say anything to that. I did not know what the right thing to say to that was. Hannah waited a count and then she said *I will see you at twelve-thirty* and we hung up.

My mother had been on the rocker next to mine the whole call. She had not pretended to drink any of the coffee. She looked up at me when I put the phone down.

*Twelve-thirty,* I said. *He will be out by two.*

She nodded. She got up and went into the house. I heard her go upstairs. I heard her open Wade's closet and take a thing out. I heard her go to her own room and dress.

I sat on the porch a little longer. The light was the cold copper light that the third week of November gets in this part of the state. A nuthatch came down the trunk of the dogwood head first, worked the bark, went around to the back. I drank my coffee. I did not feel triumphant. I felt the flat tired you feel at the end of a run that has gone well and not well at the same time, when the patient is alive and your hands are clean and the other car has not been alive and you have walked away from it because that has not been your job.

We left the house at quarter past eleven. My mother drove. She had not driven me anywhere since I was sixteen. She drove the way she had always driven, two hands at ten and two, the speed limit, signaling at the empty intersection before town. I sat in the passenger seat with Wade's clothes folded on my lap — clean jeans, a blue chambray work shirt, a Carhartt jacket that had been hanging on the back of his bedroom door for eight months, the one pair of his sneakers that did not smell.

We pulled in to the courthouse parking lot at five before twelve. There was a news van from Lexington in the lot, Hannah's Subaru, a county cruiser. A woman with a microphone moved toward us from the van and I lifted one hand at her without looking and said *Not now, ma'am,* and she did not press it. My mother walked ahead of me into the courthouse, head up, no rush, like a person who has already made her peace with the program.

Hannah met us in the hall. She hugged my mother. She squeezed my arm. She had been up since four. Her eyes had the look you get when caffeine has stopped feeling like caffeine and started feeling like a thin tight thread the body is pulling itself along.

*The judge is Belford,* she said. *He is going to grant. Do not be alarmed when the prosecutor speaks. He is going to make a statement for the record about new evidence that has come to light. He will not name Eldon. The plea is sealed until the formal entry this afternoon in Pike. There will be a quote on tonight's news from the Commonwealth's office that will essentially say new information has come forward and the case against Mr. Combs is being dismissed in the interests of justice. You will hear those words. They are bureaucratic words. Do not let them land funny on you.*

*All right,* my mother said.

Hannah looked at me. *Della. The Commonwealth wants to ask you a question. On the record. About the chain of custody on the second piece of coal. They are not going to push you. They are not going to ask you anything you have not been prepared for. They are doing it to put your statement in the transcript so that when the federal piece comes through there is a clean line in the state record about where the coal came from and who found it.*

*All right.*

*One question. They will ask one question. You will answer it. You may answer it in any way you want. You will not be cross-examined. You will be on and off in two minutes.*

We went into the courtroom. It was the courtroom on the second floor with the high windows and the tall walnut bench and the brass railing along the front of the gallery that the WPA had put in in 1936. Becca Couch was sitting in the second row in her dispatcher's uniform. She nodded at me without smiling. There were eight people in the gallery I did not know and four I did. Verna Hicks was on the aisle on the left side, sitting up straight, her hands folded in her lap. Cal Slone was three rows behind her in the same booth-clothes he had worn at the Brass Lantern. Earl Whitt was in the back with his cap on his knee. Wendell Cornett was not there — he was eighty-four and in a nursing home in Williamsburg and the drive would have killed him, but he had asked Hannah to read into the record that he had given a statement, and she had said she would. The judge was already seated. He did not look up when we came in.

I do not remember the order of what happened. The Commonwealth's attorney stood up. He was a man I had not met named Pendell, gray suit. He said into the microphone the bureaucratic sentences Hannah had said he would say. *New information has come forward, the Commonwealth is filing a motion to dismiss the charge against Mr. Combs in the interests of justice and without prejudice to refile.* Hannah said *The defense joins the motion.* The judge said *Granted* the way a man says it when he has granted a thousand. Wade was not in the room. They would bring him out in twenty minutes.

Pendell did not sit down. He said into the microphone, *Your honor. The Commonwealth has one additional matter. We would like to call Ms. Della Combs to the stand briefly for the purpose of establishing chain of custody on a piece of physical evidence recovered at the scene.* The judge said, *Granted. Ms. Combs.* I stood up. Hannah did not look at me. I walked the length of the gallery to the gate. I went through the gate. I stood in front of the bailiff and put my left hand on the Bible and raised my right hand and was sworn. I sat down in the witness chair.

Pendell did not approach. He stayed at his table. *Ms. Combs. You are the sister of the defendant in this matter.*

*I am.*

*And on the thirteenth of October of this year you walked into Sorrow Hollow with the awareness that a homicide had taken place there six days prior.*

*Yes.*

*And while at the scene you observed an object that the sheriff's office had not previously logged into evidence.*

*Yes.*

*Please describe the object and its location, briefly.*

I described it. I described it the way I had described patient findings in run reports for twelve years. *A piece of cannel coal, approximately three inches across, placed on the flat surface of a sandstone outcrop ten feet east of where the body had been found. The piece sat upright on the outcrop in a manner inconsistent with natural placement. I observed it but did not touch it. I reported the observation to Sheriff Cornett the same afternoon. A deputy was dispatched to bag it the following morning.*

*Thank you, Ms. Combs.* Pendell looked at his notes. He looked back up. *Ms. Combs. The Commonwealth has one further question, and I want you to answer it in your own words. How did you know, on the thirteenth of October, what a piece of coal placed on a rock at that distance from a body meant.*

The judge looked up. He had not been looking at me. He looked at me now.

I sat. I had not been prepared for this question. Hannah had not been told they would ask it. I could see from the bench, three rows back, the slight stiffening in Hannah's shoulders that told me she was about to object and was deciding whether the objection would do more harm than good. I lifted my hand a half inch in her direction. She saw it. She did not stand.

I sat with the question. The courtroom was quiet. The high windows on the south side let the cold copper light in across the walnut bench. The brass railing at the front of the gallery had been polished within memory. I looked at it and not at Pendell. I looked at it because I needed the eyes to be on a thing that was not a person while I answered.

I said, *My grandfather Hollis Combs told me, on his deathbed in April of 2005, what such a placement meant. He told me as a thing he wanted me to know in case anybody ever came asking. He did not tell me with reference to any specific crime. He told me as a piece of family knowledge that had been passed down to him and that he believed needed to keep being passed down. I had not had occasion to consider the knowledge in any active way until I walked into Sorrow Hollow on the thirteenth of October and saw the placement and recognized it.*

*Thank you, Ms. Combs.*

I had thought he was going to sit down. He did not.

*Ms. Combs. A final question. The piece of family knowledge you have described — is there anything else about it that you believe the Commonwealth should be aware of, in connection with this case or any case.*

I sat. Hannah's hand was on the table in front of her. She did not lift it.

I thought of Vera in the kitchen in 1973 writing *the secret is a wall the men in this family built around the kitchen, and we have all been living in the kitchen all these years and calling the wall the house.* I thought of Hollis in the bedroom off the front room in 2005 with his hand on mine. I thought of my mother saying *Pap. He had reasons of his own.* I thought of Eldon at the kitchen table the night before with the cornbread in a paper napkin in his coat pocket. I thought of the coal on the rock and the coal on the chest and the two pieces I had seen and the two pieces I had not seen but had reconstructed sixty-four years late from the silence of three generations of my own kin.

I said, *My grandfather was a man who carried other people's secrets for them. He carried one for sixty years. He carried it for a member of his own family. He believed he was protecting his children by carrying it. He was not. He was carrying it because the carrying was what he had been taught a man did. I will not carry it anymore. There are no other secrets from that period of time that I am aware of in this family that bear on any criminal matter currently before the Commonwealth. There is one that bore on a matter from 1961 that the Commonwealth chose to close in October of that year and has no current interest in reopening. I have spoken about that matter to Mr. Pendell's office in writing through Ms. Soyars. I do not have anything further to add about it in this courtroom today.*

I did not look at Pendell. I looked at the brass rail.

He said, *Thank you, Ms. Combs. No further questions, your honor.*

The judge said, *You may step down.* I stepped down. I walked back across the gallery and sat down beside my mother. She did not look at me. She reached over and took my hand and held it on the bench between us. She did not squeeze. She held it. The judge concluded the matter on the record. Wade was brought in through the side door in his jail jumpsuit and the bailiff unlocked him from the chain and a deputy brought him a paper sack of his belongings from the night they had booked him, and the judge said the words that closed the matter — *the defendant is released from custody, the charge is dismissed without prejudice* — and that was all of it. The whole of it. Six weeks of my life. Forty-two days. Ended in eleven minutes of bench time.

Wade looked at me across the gallery. He did not smile. He had a stunned-mule look on him that I had seen on him only one other time in my life, which had been the morning Hollis died, when he had come down the stairs and seen me at the kitchen table and known why I was there. He came through the gate. My mother stood up. I stood up. Wade came up the aisle. He hugged my mother first. He hugged her a long time. He did not say anything to her and she did not say anything to him. Then he hugged me. His hands were shaking against my back. They did not stop the whole hug.

We went outside. Hannah had stepped in front of the news van's microphones and was talking to them in measured sentences, and we walked past her. My mother handed Wade his clothes. He went to the men's room of the gas station across the street and changed and brought the jumpsuit back folded on the sneakers' box, and I put it in the bed of his truck under the toolbox. We got in — Wade driving — and we drove out of town the way we had driven home from town a thousand times when we were children with my mother behind the wheel.

Wade did not talk. About a mile out of town he reached into the jacket pocket and pulled out a pack of Marlboros he had left in it eight months before, and he lit one off the dash lighter with one hand. He took the smoke in the way a man takes a thing in he knows he is not supposed to be taking in and is glad of it anyway. He did not ask me whether it was all right. I did not tell him not to. He smoked it down to the filter and flicked the butt out.

He said, *Dell.*

*Yes.*

*Did Aunt Donnie know.*

*Yes.*

*Did Eldon come up to the house.*

*Last night.*

*Did Mama set a chair for him.*

*She set three.*

He nodded once. He did not say anything else for a long while. We came up Bear Branch in the early afternoon light. The trees on the upper end were bare on the north faces and copper on the south. We came past Verna's place — Verna's truck was not in the drive; she had stayed in town to eat lunch with her sister; she had stopped at the courthouse to see Wade come out — and we came past the Methodist church with its bell rope hanging in the open doorway of the porch. We came past the trace that goes up to Sorrow Hollow. Wade looked at the trace as we went past. He did not slow. He did not speed up. He looked at it the way you look at a thing you do not need to look at to know what is at the end of it.

We pulled up at the house at three minutes past two. My mother got out first. She went up onto the porch and opened the front door and held it for him. Wade did not get out for a second. He sat with both hands on the wheel and his eyes on the dogwood at the corner of the porch.

He said, *I have not been in this house in fifty-three days.*

I said, *I know.*

He got out. He walked up the porch steps. He stopped at the top and put one hand on the rail and looked back at me. I was still in the passenger seat. He did not say anything. He nodded once. He turned and went into the house.

I sat in the cab of the truck a minute longer. The light lay on the porch boards without warming them. On the second floor of the courthouse there was a bronze plaque by the front door I had walked past that morning, listing every man from Caldwell County who had died in the Korean War. Hollis was on it for his service in '52, even though Hollis had come home from Korea alive and had lived to be eighty-one. I had passed it and not stopped. I had thought halfway across the lot that I should have stopped. I would go back. Not today. Later in the week. I would put my hand on his name without ceremony and I would go. I owed him the gesture even though we were now even on what I had agreed to carry. I had loved him my whole life and had spent the last two months understanding the shape of him better than I had understood it when he was alive, and the understanding had not made me love him less.

I got out of the truck. I went up the porch steps. I shut the front door behind me. From the kitchen I could hear my mother already setting plates out for the three of us, the way she had been setting plates for forty years in that kitchen, and Wade saying something low to her that I could not make out, and her low answer, and I stopped in the front room and stood with my back to the door for a count and let myself listen to them. They were the only two people I had grown up with in this house who were still living. They were in the kitchen. Their voices were in the kitchen. It was the third week of November. It was a Tuesday afternoon. The hearing was over. The case was closed. I had said in a courtroom what I had been working two months to be able to say, and I had said it under oath and on a record that would outlive me, and the saying had cost me what I had known going in it would cost me, and what I had not known was that the cost would feel like this — not like loss and not like relief and not like grief but like the thing that comes after grief, which is the long quiet of having to begin again.

I went into the kitchen.

---

# Chapter 34 — The Porch

I was awake before the light. I had slept four hours and then I had lain in the bed in my old room — Wade's room, which Wade had left to me last night without saying so by going downstairs to sleep on the couch in the front room because he had said he wanted to be where Hollis's old bedroom was, and I had not argued — and I had watched the square of the window go from black to the slow blue that comes before color. The headwater creek behind the house was running the way it had been running for sixty thousand years before there was a house. I could hear it through the closed window. I had not heard it the first night I had slept here in October because I had not yet learned to listen for it.

I got up. I put my clothes on without turning the light on. I went down the stairs the way you go down stairs in a house with two other people sleeping in it, on the second tread and the fourth and skipping the seventh because the seventh squeaks. I had not skipped the seventh tread in twenty-two years and my body knew. I went into the kitchen. The propane heater was off. The kitchen was cold. The hymnal was on the table where my mother and I had left it Monday night with both letters on top of it and the business card on top of those, and there was a clean cup beside the hymnal that had not been there when I went to bed. My mother had been up before me. She had set out the cup. She had gone back to her room to give me the kitchen.

I made the coffee. I made it in her old aluminum percolator on the stove the way she had always made it. I waited for it. I held my hands over the burner for the warmth. The light through the window above the sink came up the second slow shade and the sycamore on the bank of the creek behind the house came clear of the gray.

I poured the coffee. I poured a second cup for her. I took both out onto the porch.

She was already there. She had come down the back stairs while I was making the coffee and she had not let me hear her. She was in her own rocker in her good winter coat with the hood up against the morning. She held her hands out for the cup. I gave it to her. I sat down in the rocker next to hers. The dogwood at the corner of the porch was bare. The shriveled berry I had seen yesterday was still on the branch I had noticed it on. A field sparrow was working the dead joe-pye weed along the fence line and was not yet doing the *bouncing-ball trill* of summer, only its winter call, the small dry note it makes when there is nothing to tell anyone but itself.

We drank the coffee. We did not say anything for the time it took the light to clear the top of the ridge above the holler and come down across the pasture and pick out, one by one, the things that had been black shapes a minute ago and were now the things they were. The barn. The smokehouse. The kitchen garden under its blanket of frost. The split-rail fence between her land and Verna's. The post on which somebody — I did not remember who, probably my father in the summer he had been sober — had carved a notch for every spring that the redbud across the holler had bloomed before April first, and the notches went down the post for as far as I could see in the morning light.

She said, *Are you going back today.*

I said, *I do not know.*

She said, *You do not have to know today.*

I did not say anything to that. There was nothing to say to it. I drank my coffee. The sparrow worked its way down the fence and went into the pasture and was gone.

I thought of the things I had been thinking of since I sat down. I thought of Eldon waking up that morning at the Pike County jail in a blue jumpsuit eating an institutional breakfast on a tray and beginning the rest of his life inside a window of time that would not run out until the year I was sixty. I thought of Caudill Boggs on a road south, maybe in Tennessee by now, maybe further. The memo would surface or it would not. The U. S. Attorney in Lexington would call him or someone would not call him, would find him on his daughter's couch in Lexington or in a green truck in the parking lot of a Cracker Barrel at the Tennessee line or would not find him for a long time, and the company would do what the company did, which was draft the next memo, and the next memo would have a different lawyer's name on it and a different pen, and Bear Branch would either be stripped or it would not, and either way the company would still be here in some form when my mother was dead and probably when Wade was, and the holler would either come up out of it slow over the course of two generations or it would not. The work of the company was patient. The county had been patient with it for a hundred and twenty years. I did not know what the answer was on a horizon longer than the one I could see from this porch.

I thought of the seep at the back of Sorrow Hollow that I had drunk from in the predawn six days before, with my mouth on the same water Pap had put his mouth on after he killed Calvin Tull and Eldon had put his after he killed Tully Renfro. The water had not had an opinion. It would not have one. It would come out of the rock at fifty-four degrees this morning the way it had come out of the rock yesterday and the way it would come out of the rock the morning after the Combs Pine fell and the morning after the deadfall over Calvin Tull's grave finally gave way and the bones rolled out of the rhododendron. The water did not care about us. It had been a long time since I had taken comfort from a thing that did not care, and the comfort of it now was a thing I would keep.

I thought of Vera's letter. I thought of the line about the wall and the kitchen. I thought of the half-sheet of bulletin paper I had burned in my mother's sink with one sentence on it that I would not say to anyone and that Vera, if anything of Vera was anywhere, had read the moment the smoke went up.

I thought of Hollis. I thought of his hand on mine in April of 2005 and his one good eye and the careful way he had given me the wrong version of a story he had been carrying for forty-four years, because he had loved me and had thought the wrong version was the kinder one, and had been wrong about that — or had not been wrong about it, I did not know, I would not know, I would let him have the not-knowing — and I thought of the bronze plaque on the wall of the courthouse with his name on it from a war that had not killed him so that another war could not-kill him in this county for the next half-century, and I thought of how he had walked into his eighty-first year without ever saying out loud the name of the man who had done the thing he was carrying for, and I thought that this was the difference between his way of carrying and what I had done yesterday in a courtroom, and I thought that the two ways of carrying were both real and that one of them did not necessarily un-do the other. We had each done what we had been able to do at the time we had had to do it. He had done his with the tools he had. I had done mine with the tools I had had, which were different tools, and different because of him. There was a kind of accounting in that I did not have a name for and would not try to give one.

I thought of Wade asleep on the couch in the front room behind us. I could hear him through the wall faintly snoring the way he had snored when he was sixteen and had fallen asleep watching basketball, the small wet snore of a boy with bad sinuses, and I thought of how he was going to come out of that sleep in an hour or two and how he was going to be a man with a fifty-three-day hole in his life that the rest of him was going to have to grow over, and how the growing-over would take him longer than he thought it would take him, and how my mother and I would be the two people who would be available to him while it took the time it took. I thought of the cigarette in the truck yesterday and I thought he would have to fight to take that back, and I thought he would have to fight to keep not drinking now, more than he had had to fight before, because the thing he had been not-drinking against had got bigger in October and was not going to shrink back to its old size soon. I thought I might could stay long enough to be in the kitchen at suppertime once a week for a while. I did not know whether I was going to. I did not have to know that today either. My mother had said so.

I thought of Hannah. Hannah was thirty-two years old and had three other capital cases on her desk and would have a fourth by Friday and a fifth by Christmas, and the way she fought for those people was the way Hollis had fought for the men in his crews when the company had wanted to cut their shifts in '74, and I thought I was going to keep knowing her now whether she liked it or not, and I thought she might like it.

I thought of Cal Slone with whom I had eaten cobbler twice and would eat it again, and Earl Whitt who had walked his beagle into a hollow on October the seventh and had set the rest of this in motion and did not know it, and Verna Hicks who would be eighty-nine in February and might or might not see ninety, and I thought of how the chorus of this place had carried me through six weeks I had not thought I could survive, and I thought of how I had spent twenty-two years away from that chorus and had not known what I had been missing because I had not known that the missing was a thing.

I thought, finally, of the title phrase. *Carry the fire down.* I had thought it in my truck on the road home from the jail in late October, the first time it had come into my head clean, and I had thought it had meant *we do not tell, in this family.* I had been right about that and wrong about it. The phrase was older than the family code. It was older than Pap. It came out of the country itself, and it had two meanings in it that the country had been holding in the same words because the country was not in the business of pretending the two meanings were not both true. Some things you carry down to bury. Some things you carry down to keep warm. The same hand carried both kinds. The work of being from a place was knowing the difference. The work of being from a family was knowing it some of the time. The work of being a person was knowing it most days and forgiving yourself on the days you did not.

My mother said, very quietly, *I'll fly away.*

I looked over at her. She had her eyes closed against the light. She was singing one of her hymns to herself almost under her breath. It was the one Hollis had liked. It was the one Vera had hated. It was the one I had heard come up out of her three or four times in twenty-two years on phone calls when she had not known I could hear it. She was singing the line and not the whole verse. She was singing only the four words, over and over, in the quiet of her mouth, the way she sang when she was not singing for an audience and not even singing to herself but only singing because the body wanted the song the way the body wanted air, and she did not know I had heard her.

I did not let on. I drank my coffee. I let her have the four words. I sat in the rocker beside hers and I looked out at the ridge above the holler and at the bare branches of the dogwood at the corner of the porch with the one shriveled berry on the one branch and at the post with the notches running down it from a count my father had kept in the summer he had been sober, and I knew with a knowing that did not need to be said out loud that I would be in this rocker again tomorrow morning and that the question of whether I would be in it in a week, or in a month, or in a year, was a question that was going to be answered slowly, and answered by my staying or my leaving on a hundred small Tuesday afternoons that I did not have to plan for now. The medic in me had wanted to triage this and hand it off and leave clean. The medic in me did not get to. I was going to have to be in this whether I left or whether I stayed, because the thing I had walked into in October was not the kind of thing you got to walk out of clean — it was the kind of thing you carried, in some form, whichever direction you walked from it, and the carrying was going to be the long quiet shape of me for the rest of my life.

A jay screamed once from the dogwood.

My mother said, without opening her eyes, *That bird has been mad at this porch since 1989.*

I laughed. It surprised me. I laughed the laugh you laugh on a porch in the middle of November in the third hour after dawn when the worst thing you can think of has not happened and the second-worst thing has, and the second-worst thing is going to keep being a thing for the rest of your life but you are alive on a porch and your mother has just said a thing about a bird, and the laugh is small and it is real and it is the first one that has not had to be paid for.

She opened her eyes. She looked over at me. She did not smile. She nodded once, the way Hollis had nodded.

I sat with her. I drank the rest of my coffee. The cup was warm against my palms. I thought of nothing in particular for a count, and the not-thinking was a thing I had not been able to do in six weeks, and the doing of it now was the body's way of saying *all right* without needing the word for it. The sparrow came back to the fence and worked along it the other direction. The light got up over the hood of the truck in the drive and across the gravel and into the porch boards at our feet. Above us on the south side of the ridge the leaves still hanging were the color a poplar gets the third week of November, and the wind was not on them, and they were not moving, and I sat with my mother on the porch where we had sat together a thousand times when I was a girl and a hundred or so since I had been a woman, and the morning was the way the morning gets at this latitude when the year is going down and the going-down is not a sorrow but only the year doing what years do.

The sun was on the —

---

*END OF THE FIRST DRAFT*
